Fed Leaves Rates Steady As New Data Pose Few Risks

Author: Nasar, Sylvia

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]19 Aug 1998: 1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/431024399?accountid=11752

Abstract: Today's good news about the United States economy was the refreshing absence of bad news: the Federal Reserve did not raise interest rates at this morning's monetary policy meeting. Inflation did not rise much in July. And the trade deficit did not widen further in June, as economists had expected. Despite the lowest unemployment rate in a generation, consumer prices edged up just two-tenths of 1 percent in July, the Commerce Department reported. Prices are up just 1.7 percent from a year ago, an extraordinary performance for the seventh year of an economic expansion. And despite the robust dollar and deepening recession in Asia, the United States trade deficit fell by more than $1 billion in June, to $14.2 billion. In dollars, that is still close to a record high, but as a share of the economy -- a more meaningful yardstick -- the trade deficit is still just two-thirds of its 1980's peak. Exports fell, but at a far slower rate than in recent months. Imports fell as well, as businesses bought less equipment from abroad and oil prices dropped another notch. Against that backdrop, the Federal Reserve announced that it was leaving its target for the overnight lending rate, known as the Federal funds rate -- a benchmark for all interest rates -- at 5.5 percent, and the discount rate, the rate the Fed charges banks for emergency loans, at 5 percent.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Fed+Leaves+Rates+Steady+As+New+Data+Pose+Few+Risks&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1998-08-19&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Nasar%2C+Sylvia&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/00682872&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Today's good news about the United States economy was the refreshing absence of bad news: the Federal Reserve did not raise interest rates at this morning's monetary policy meeting. Inflation did not rise much in July. And the trade deficit did not widen further in June, as economists had expected.
Despite the lowest unemployment rate in a generation, consumer prices edged up just two-tenths of 1 percent in July, the Commerce Department reported. Prices are up just 1.7 percent from a year ago, an extraordinary performance for the seventh year of an economic expansion. And despite the robust dollar and deepening recession in Asia, the United States trade deficit fell by more than $1 billion in June, to $14.2 billion. In dollars, that is still close to a record high, but as a share of the economy -- a more meaningful yardstick -- the trade deficit is still just two-thirds of its 1980's peak. Exports fell, but at a far slower rate than in recent months. Imports fell as well, as businesses bought less equipment from abroad and oil prices dropped another notch.
Against that backdrop, the Federal Reserve announced that it was leaving its target for the overnight lending rate, known as the Federal funds rate -- a benchmark for all interest rates -- at 5.5 percent, and the discount rate, the rate the Fed charges banks for emergency loans, at 5 percent.
As usual, the central bank offered no explanation of its decision, but hardly any economists were predicting a change in policy. With inflation tame, the economy slowing but not in immediate danger of slipping into recession, and turmoil in Russia and elsewhere, the Fed's best action was inaction.
''The Fed has been very successful, and blessed,'' said Robert Di Clemente, co-head of United States economic research at Salomon Smith Barney. ''Most of us feel that they would have been compelled to tighten if it had not been for the shock from Asia.''
Stocks rose sharply for the second consecutive day. But whatever the market was celebrating, it probably was not economic news, which came as no surprise to Wall Street.
Inflation may well be leveling out after declining for much of this expansion. Right now, however, the strong dollar and the growing share of imports are helping to hold down prices. Consumer prices for goods are up just four-tenths of 1 percent from a year ago. It is not just that imports, like gasoline, clothing and consumer electronics, are cheaper. It is also that inexpensive imports are helping to restrain domestic prices by intensifying competition.
The most encouraging news in today's consumer price report is that inflation in the service sector -- which accounts for two-thirds of spending and more than four-fifths of employment -- is a remarkably tame 3 percent. That is half the rate of inflation in services at the end of the 1980's boom.
''Services inflation is very benign," said Maury N. Harris, chief economist at Paine Webber. ''It's higher than goods inflation, but it hasn't accelerated.''
That is true across a wide range of services. From a year ago, for example, housing is up 3.3 percent; car repair, 2.4 percent; haircuts and other personal services, 2.2 percent, and airline and other transportation, 1.2 percent. Even the prices of medical services, a category in which economists expect heftier increases now that the shift to managed care has pretty much been completed, are up just 3.2 percent, faster than a year ago but moderate by past standards.
With labor markets tighter than they have been in decades, employment in services is growing by more than 230,000 workers a month, and wages are rising handily. So the absence of inflation in services is a puzzle.
Like the Fed chairman, Alan Greenspan, Mr. Harris said he suspected that the productivity of workers in services might be rising faster than the official measures were capturing. Also, competition in services is fierce, so businesses are having a tough time raising prices.
And finally, cheaper imports are having an indirect effect on wage-setting in the service industry by holding down the cost of living.
The trade deficit will almost certainly keep getting bigger as the troubles in Asia deepen. Exports to Asia have already fallen 21 percent from a year ago, and exports to the rest of the world are growing very slowly. Imports are apt to bounce back; those from Asia are beginning to head higher. Indeed, the trade deficit is likely to remain the biggest drag on economic growth, as it was during the year's first half.
The unexpected improvement in the trade deficit is a temporary respite. June's smaller trade deficit implies that economic growth in the second quarter, which slowed to 1.4 percent from 5.4 percent in the winter, might have been a bit stronger than first estimated. But one detail of today's trade report -- a sharp decline in imports of capital goods -- implies that business spending on new equipment may have been weakening at the end of last quarter. If that is so, the effects would cancel each other out.
So far this year, the widening trade deficit has knocked 2.5 percentage points off economic growth. The negative effect is likely to diminish over time, though economists expect it to persist well into next year.
''That's bad news for American manufacturers, but good news for inflation,'' said Pierre Ellis, senior economist at Primark Decision Economics, a forecasting firm.
Graph ''Balance of Trade'' tracks dollar amount of imports, exports deficits, from January 1997 through June 1998. (Source: Commerce Department)(pg. D19)

Stocks Rally After Moves To Bolster Yen

Author: Barboza, David

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]18 June 1998: 1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430994926?accountid=11752

Abstract: The stock market staged a powerful rally yesterday after the United States and Japan bought yen to bolster the sagging currency, which has threatened to plunge Asia into a deeper economic crisis. Worries that financial instability in Japan and Southeast Asia would result in a global slowdown this year that would cut even deeper into the already slumping profits of American companies had punished share prices in the United States for the last few weeks. But investors stampeded back into the market yesterday after the United States Government and the Bank of Japan moved to bolster the value of the Japanese yen, partly in the hope of preventing a deeper economic and currency crisis in Japan and the rest of Asia.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Stocks+Rally+After+Moves+To+Bolster+Yen&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1998-06-18&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Barboza%2C+David&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/00674626&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The stock market staged a powerful rally yesterday after the United States and Japan bought yen to bolster the sagging currency, which has threatened to plunge Asia into a deeper economic crisis.
Worries that financial instability in Japan and Southeast Asia would result in a global slowdown this year that would cut even deeper into the already slumping profits of American companies had punished share prices in the United States for the last few weeks.
But investors stampeded back into the market yesterday after the United States Government and the Bank of Japan moved to bolster the value of the Japanese yen, partly in the hope of preventing a deeper economic and currency crisis in Japan and the rest of Asia.
After plunging Monday more than 207 points, or 2.3 percent, on fears of a widening crisis in Asia, enthusiastic buying sent the Dow Jones industrial average soaring 164.17 points, or 1.89 percent, yesterday to close at 8,829.46.
Scores of companies that had been battered recently by a huge wave of selloffs roared back to life in one of the heaviest trading days of the year with more than 744 million shares changing hands on the New York Stock Exchange. While a wide majority of stocks gained, technology and blue-chip issues led the recovery.
''The market had been going down because of Japan -- Japan was the new wrinkle in Southeast Asia,'' said Byron R. Wien, the United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter. ''But now it looks like there's intervention and that Japan's going to deal with its problems, and that is a clear positive for the markets.''
In fact, Wall Street's big rally sparked a comeback in Japan early today. At the midday break, the Nikkei index of 225 issues was up 4.4 percent, or 647.11 points, to 15,362.49.
The Government's decision to buy Japanese yen with dollars, coupled with promises that Japanese officials would address the deepening banking crisis there, also helped lift the value of the Japanese currency, which had fallen close to an eight-year low on Monday at 146.15 yen to the dollar.
Yesterday, the value of the yen reached 136.37.
United States bond prices, however, plummeted after rising sharply in the last week on the prospect that Asia's economic slowdown would benefit bondholders by dragging down commodity prices and helping stave off inflationary pressure. The yield on the benchmark 30-year Treasury, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.73 yesterday from 5.65 on Tuesday.
For investors yesterday's events were not a clear indication that corporate profits, which are expected to be hurt in the coming year by a strong dollar and falling demand in Asia, were no longer in jeopardy.
''The Asia problem is clearly not going to go away easily,'' said Edward Yardeni, chief economist at Deutsche Morgan Grenfell. ''Investors would like to believe it's going to go away, but it's already clearly had an impact on earnings.''
Indeed, in the last few weeks several large multinational companies have warned that the Asian economic crisis would depress earnings growth this year.
Although the United States economy continues to grow at a robust pace, with few signs of inflation, slowing profit growth in the first quarter of the year and worries that Asia's festering crisis would pinch profits even more led to a sharp selloff beginning in mid-April, after the Dow peaked at 9,211.84.
But yesterday, investors helped ignite the Dow, which soared as much as 230 points before a late wave of selling pushed the index lower. Meanwhile, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 19.52, or 1.79 percent, to 1,107.11, and the Nasdaq composite gained 1.33 percent, to 1,776.40.
Oil stocks gained after the price of oil soared on reports that the leaders of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries would move to establish more oil production cuts. Tobacco stocks, which rose modestly, may rise higher today because the Senate voted to reject a tobacco bill aimed at curbing smoking.
To prolong the rally, analysts say that Japanese officials are going to have to do much more than commit to banking reform and stimulus packages. ''This is potentially a watershed event for the financial crisis,'' said Jeffrey Applegate, the chief investment strategist at Lehman Brothers. ''However, the real economy in Japan is still going down. But what was crucial for the markets was to see a policy response. And that's what you're seeing now.''
Mr. Wien of Morgan Stanley added that a big selloff might yet come later this year if things do not change, at home and in Asia. ''This is a rally on the promise Japan will do something,'' he said. ''So we'll have to see what Japan does, and then assess whether it's enough.''

U.S. Trade Deficit Narrowed In November in Surprise Drop

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]22 Jan 1998: 8.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430901993?accountid=11752

Abstract: The United States trade deficit unexpectedly narrowed in November, though analysts said it was likely to be only a brief respite before the Asian economic crisis leads to larger deficits this year. The trade deficit shrank to $8.037 billion in November from a revised $9.091 billion in October, as both exports and imports declined, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. Many economists had expected the deficit to rise. And today, the Japanese Government reported further indications of the trend toward a rising imbalance in trade between the United States and that country. Japan's merchandise trade surplus rose in December by 40.9 percent from a year earlier, and the surplus with the United States rose by 25.4 percent.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+Trade+Deficit+Narrowed+In+November+in+Surprise+Drop&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1998-01-22&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.8&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/00655213&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The United States trade deficit unexpectedly narrowed in November, though analysts said it was likely to be only a brief respite before the Asian economic crisis leads to larger deficits this year.
The trade deficit shrank to $8.037 billion in November from a revised $9.091 billion in October, as both exports and imports declined, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. Many economists had expected the deficit to rise.
The deficits with China, Japan and most Pacific Rim countries also narrowed in November, countering expectations that currency devaluations in Asia would increase United States imports by reducing the cost of foreign-made goods.
Still, despite the declines in the deficits with Japan and China in November, the gaps with those countries over the first 11 months of last year were up 16.7 percent and 24 percent, respectively.
And today, the Japanese Government reported further indications of the trend toward a rising imbalance in trade between the United States and that country. Japan's merchandise trade surplus rose in December by 40.9 percent from a year earlier, and the surplus with the United States rose by 25.4 percent.
For all of 1997, Japan's overall trade surplus expanded by 48.5 percent, the first annual rise in five years, the Ministry of Finance reported.
In a separate report yesterday, the Federal Reserve said that economic ripples from the Asian turmoil were felt in most regions of the United States in recent weeks. ''Manufacturers and agricultural firms report weaker exports to Asia, and there was some evidence of increased competition from Asian products in U.S. markets,'' the Fed said in its regional survey, known as the beige book.
That has helped keep inflation absent from the American economy, the Fed said, noting that ''prices were generally flat'' in the period covered by the survey.
Analysts said the narrowing of the United States trade gap could be accounted for in part by problems with the way the Commerce Department computes the figures.
''We have been arguing for some time that the Government's seasonal adjustment procedure for the fourth quarter produces a narrowing of the deficit,'' said Anne Mills, an economist at Lehman Brothers in New York. ''Today's numbers are a reflection of the seasonal adjustment, not the underlying data.''
Lee Price, Acting Under Secretary for Economic Affairs, acknowledged that there were problems with the adjustment process, but added that the pattern did not show up consistently in every trade category and that Government economists were reluctant to force a general adjustment on the numbers only to try to correct the trend.
The trade figures could give a lift to the fourth quarter's gross domestic product, which economists had been forecasting to grow at about a 3.5 percent annual rate.
Economists said that the trade numbers for January or February should better reflect the actual trade situation and begin to show the effects of the Asian crisis. ''It's the calm before the storm,'' Ms. Mills said.
The trade deficit for all of 1997 is on target to total $111 billion, matching last year's total, which was the largest since a $115 billion deficit in 1988.
''There isn't any doubt in anyone's mind where the trend is going on trade,'' said James Glassman, chief domestic economist at Chase Securities in New York. ''It would defy logic for it to go the other way.''

Treasury Securities Fall in Price

Author: Hurtado, Robert

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]16 Dec 1997: 7.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430889695?accountid=11752

Abstract: Stuart G. Hoffman, chief economist at the PNC Bank Corporation in Pittsburgh, believes rates are currently caught in a tug-of-war. On one side is the Asian financial turmoil, which some market participants believe will slow the United States economy and keep inflation low, not to mention the flight to quality by investors seeking shelter from those markets that have lowered short-term rates here and supported Treasuries for weeks. Also, the shrinking Federal budget deficit, rising stock market -- despite recent volatility -- strong consumer confidence and strong dollar. On the other side, he said, we have a strong United States economy that has the potential to accelerate inflation, with faster wage gains in a tighter labor market, rising consumer debt including delinquencies, along with personal bankruptcies. And in the middle, there is the Fed.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Treasury+Securities+Fall+in+Price&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1997-12-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.7&amp;au=Hurtado%2C+Robert&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/00649131&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Prices of Treasury securities fell yesterday as stocks gained on upbeat news from Korea over the weekend.
The 30-year bond fell 21/32, to 102 6/32, while the yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 5.97 percent, from 5.92 percent on Friday.
Traders said signs of a return to global stock market stability could signal the end of the flight to quality that fueled last week's bond rally.
Bonds also slumped ahead of today's Federal Open Market Committee meeting after a larger than expected increase in factory output. The increase of eight-tenths of a percent last month was higher than the four-tenths of 1 percent some economists had expected. It challenged the notion that the economy is slowing, which some thought would be hastened by the Asian market slump.
Stuart G. Hoffman, chief economist at the PNC Bank Corporation in Pittsburgh, believes rates are currently caught in a tug-of-war. On one side is the Asian financial turmoil, which some market participants believe will slow the United States economy and keep inflation low, not to mention the flight to quality by investors seeking shelter from those markets that have lowered short-term rates here and supported Treasuries for weeks. Also, the shrinking Federal budget deficit, rising stock market -- despite recent volatility -- strong consumer confidence and strong dollar. On the other side, he said, we have a strong United States economy that has the potential to accelerate inflation, with faster wage gains in a tighter labor market, rising consumer debt including delinquencies, along with personal bankruptcies. And in the middle, there is the Fed.
''The balance of these pro and con factors favors continued economic growth in 1998, albeit at a noticeably slower pace,'' Mr. Hoffman said.
Yesterday, yields slipped as market participants grew cautious ahead of today's Federal Open Market Committee meeting and release of the November Consumer Price Index and housing starts, putting buyers on the side lines. Few in the market expect the Fed will take any action today, but even that did not dissuade the market from pricing the risk premium anyway.
Robert Pugliese, senior vice president and director of the fixed-income department at J. W. Charles Securities Inc. in Boca Raton, Fla., thought the market was also concerned about how the Japanese were going to remedy their banking problems. ''That is more important than any of the other stuff,'' he said. ''If no solution is found there is the threat that the Japanese can liquidate some of their Treasury holdings and that could put pressure on prices regardless of what the Fed does. That is the critical issue this week. Even the threat of it happening has put pressure on the market.'' ''Nonetheless, I'm still sticking to a 5.80 yield on the long bond very shortly,'' Mr. Pugliese said.

Crisis in Asia No Big Threat, Fed Chief Says

Author: Stevenson, Richard W

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]14 Nov 1997: 1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430876280?accountid=11752

Abstract: Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, teamed up with the Clinton Administration today to offer soothing comments about the turmoil in global markets, saying the problems in Asia and elsewhere should not threaten prosperity in the United States or the soundness of the nation's financial system. While pointing out possible risks from the crisis to the United States and the rest of the world, Mr. Greenspan said the difficulties that had created such volatility in stock markets around the world could even benefit the United States by helping in a small way to suppress inflation. The Federal Reserve has been increasingly concerned that the strong growth in the domestic economy at a time when unemployment has fallen to a 24-year low could lead to rising prices and thwart the long expansion. The Fed voted on Wednesday not to raise interest rates, concerned that tightening monetary policy would further destabilize markets around the world and betting that the American economy might slow sufficiently to avert any inflationary outbreak.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Crisis+in+Asia+No+Big+Threat%2C+Fed+Chief+Says&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1997-11-14&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Stevenson%2C+Richard+W&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/04797335&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, teamed up with the Clinton Administration today to offer soothing comments about the turmoil in global markets, saying the problems in Asia and elsewhere should not threaten prosperity in the United States or the soundness of the nation's financial system.
While pointing out possible risks from the crisis to the United States and the rest of the world, Mr. Greenspan said the difficulties that had created such volatility in stock markets around the world could even benefit the United States by helping in a small way to suppress inflation.
The Federal Reserve has been increasingly concerned that the strong growth in the domestic economy at a time when unemployment has fallen to a 24-year low could lead to rising prices and thwart the long expansion. The Fed voted on Wednesday not to raise interest rates, concerned that tightening monetary policy would further destabilize markets around the world and betting that the American economy might slow sufficiently to avert any inflationary outbreak.
Mr. Greenspan's comments heartened investors somewhat today. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 86.44 points to close at 7,487.76, partly offsetting Wednesday's 157.41-point decline.
Asked during his appearance before the House Banking Committee today whether the situation in Asia significantly threatened the world economy and international financial system, Mr. Greenspan said no.
''I think we have fallen far short of something where we have serious threats,'' Mr. Greenspan said. ''The reason it requires our attention is that we don't wish it to become something which is a far more serious threat to the system.''
Mr. Greenspan's assessment was largely the same offered by Lawrence H. Summers, the Deputy Treasury Secretary, who appeared alongside him in fielding questions about the crisis.
Mr. Summers said the ''direct and indirect impact on our economy of a prolonged period of lower growth in Southeast Asia, and the large decline in its currencies, is potentially significant.''
But, Mr. Summers said in his prepared testimony, the Administration does not see ''significant risk to United States financial institutions or to domestic financial stability as a whole as a result of the turbulence to date.''
Mr. Greenspan and Mr. Summers said the fallout from problems in Asia could worsen if steps were not taken by the United States and international financial institutions to restore stability.
They made low-key pleas to the Republican leadership in Congress to restore $3.5 billion in additional financing for the International Monetary Fund requested by the Administration. The financing was deleted from spending bills today in the House and Senate because of a political dispute over American support for family-planning programs abroad.
Mr. Greenspan stayed closely to comments he made about the Asian turmoil before a Congressional committee two weeks ago.
He said the trouble in Asia would reduce American exports to the region and diminish profits of companies that operate there. He said there could be indirect effects on the American economy as the problems of Thailand, Indonesia and South Korea put pressure on the Japanese economy.
''To date, the direct impact of these developments on the American economy has been modest, but it can be expected not to be negligible,'' Mr. Greenspan said.
Questioned about whether a $160 million trading loss by the Chase Manhattan Bank in October signaled broader problems in the American banking system because of events in Asia, Mr. Greenspan said the loss posted by Chase was not particularly big.
''The losses that are involved in those trades in no way threaten in any means whatever the financial status of that institution,'' Mr. Greenspan said.
Before the Asian crisis is over, he added, ''there will be lots of other losses recognized, but they are in nowhere near the orders of magnitude which threaten the financial system of the United States nor any of the major banking institutions in the developed world.''
Mr. Summers used much of his presentation to justify American involvement in international efforts to stabilize the economies and financial markets of Asia and, increasingly, Latin America.
But the Administration's policies came under attack from liberal Democrats for supporting countries like Indonesia. They said the Administration was ignoring human rights abuses, corruption and other problems in Indonesia, and demanded that the Administration make financial aid contingent on improvement in those areas.
Photograph The reason the Asian crisis ''requires our attention is that we don't wish it to become something which is a far more serious threat'' to the global financial system, Alan Greenspan told Congress yesterday. (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times)(pg. D3)

The Fed Holds Rates Steady As It Weighs Global Risks

Author: Stevenson, Richard W

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]13 Nov 1997: 1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430877533?accountid=11752

Abstract: Torn between fear of incipient inflation at home and concern about unsettling financial markets around the world, the Federal Reserve voted today to leave interest rates unchanged and ride out the storm for a while longer. But it highlighted the delicate situation faced by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, as he sought to keep the long economic expansion enjoyed by the United States on track while also taking account of his vast influence over markets and economies in the rest of the world. As if to illustrate just how volatile an environment Mr. Greenspan faces, markets tumbled once again today. Sharp declines in Asian and Latin American markets caused jitters on Wall Street, where investors appeared increasingly convinced that slowdowns in foreign economies would hurt profits of multinational companies and slow growth in the United States.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=The+Fed+Holds+Rates+Steady+As+It+Weighs+Global+Risks&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1997-11-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Stevenson%2C+Richard+W&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/00645189&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Torn between fear of incipient inflation at home and concern about unsettling financial markets around the world, the Federal Reserve voted today to leave interest rates unchanged and ride out the storm for a while longer.
The decision had been widely expected by economists and investors, who saw little chance that the central bank would risk upsetting the global markets by raising rates, even though there is mounting evidence that inflationary pressures are building in the American economy.
But it highlighted the delicate situation faced by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, as he sought to keep the long economic expansion enjoyed by the United States on track while also taking account of his vast influence over markets and economies in the rest of the world.
As if to illustrate just how volatile an environment Mr. Greenspan faces, markets tumbled once again today. Sharp declines in Asian and Latin American markets caused jitters on Wall Street, where investors appeared increasingly convinced that slowdowns in foreign economies would hurt profits of multinational companies and slow growth in the United States.
The Dow Jones industrial average fell 157.41 points to close at 7,401.32. It was the steepest drop in the index since the 554-point decline on Oct. 27.
Although the financial turmoil has the potential to do the same thing as a rate increase by the Fed -- slow the economy sufficiently to keep it from overheating -- Fed officials have made clear over the last several weeks that they still view an outbreak of inflation as a real possibility.
Mr. Greenspan said last month that continuing declines in the unemployment rate -- currently at 4.7 percent, a 24-year low -- at a time of strong domestic growth could set off a process in which higher wages would lead to price inflation.
Many analysts believe that if the financial markets were calm the Fed would have seriously considered raising interest rates today, and could still do so in the next three months.
Still, Mr. Greenspan and his colleagues have also been keenly aware that the combination of falling stock prices, tumbling currency values and weak banking systems in other countries, particularly in Asia, holds risks for the global economy and for the United States in particular. Raising rates now could make the situation worse by pushing down stock and bond prices and further weakening foreign currencies relative to the dollar. Mr. Greenspan is to answer questions about the international markets from the House Banking Committee Thursday morning.
Even as the Fed's policy-making committee was meeting today, the stock market in Brazil, the latest nation to be buffeted by the round-the-world financial turmoil, was falling more than 10 percent.
''Against that kind of backdrop, the Fed doesn't have a lot of choice, and in that sense their decision today was not difficult,'' said Ian Shepherdson, the chief economist at HSBC Markets in New York. ''In the longer term it gets much more difficult because they will have to take account of the domestic situation.''
Despite some early warning signs of inflation, including a 4.2 percent increase in hourly earnings over the last year, the Fed has left rates unchanged in recent months because actual gauges of prices have remained stable or in some cases have fallen.
Some economists have even been making a case in the last few months that the real danger to the economy is from deflation -- an across-the-board fall in prices -- rather than from inflation. Their argument is built on the premise that the slowdown in the Asian economies, combined with global overcapacity in many industries, could lead to falling prices around the world.
Other economists believe that fears of deflation are vastly overblown, especially since falling prices are likely to be limited to manufactured goods, while prices for services are likely to continue rising. In any case, the deflation argument has so far received only a cursory hearing with the Federal Reserve, where the focus has been squarely on strong economic growth and the pressures that could ignite inflation in the United States.
''All the dangers seem to be on the inflationary side, not the deflationary side,'' Alice M. Rivlin, the Fed's vice chairwoman, said last week.
The Federal Open Market Committee of the Fed will next meet on Dec. 16, and economists said the chances of a rate increase then, so close to Christmas, were almost nil.
But economists are already vigorously debating what situation the Fed will face at its subsequent meeting, in February. One camp believes the global financial upheaval will slow the American economy enough so that no rate increase will be needed and perhaps a rate cut could be justified later in the year.
''There is as logical a case for a rate cut next year as for a rate tightening,'' said James Glassman, an economist at Chase Securities in New York.
The other camp holds that for the last year or two the economy has always seemed to be just months away from slowing to a more sustainable pace, but then continued to grow at a robust pace and to create new inflationary pressures. With unemployment low and possibly heading lower, consumer spending strong and business investment running at high levels, even a downturn in Asia and Latin America might not be enough to eradicate inflationary pressures in the United States.
''The Fed has erred on the side of caution up to this point,'' said Gail D. Fosler, the chief economist at the Conference Board, a business group in New York. ''As we get into 1998, they may have to take a risk and raise rates to signal that they will take a stand against the inflationary pressures that are present.''
Economists will be looking particularly hard over the next few months for indications of whether productivity -- the output of a worker in one hour -- is rising fast enough to offset the increases in wages. Corporate profits have remained strong enough this year to suggest that productivity continues to outpace wage gains, economists said. But with companies increasingly being forced to pay more to attract the numbers and quality of workers they want, it is unclear how long that situation will continue, they said.
Illustration Graph tracks yesterday's S. & P. 500, minute by minute.
A slide in stocks, initially dragged down by concerns over a global slowdown, accelerated after the Fed announced its decision. Page 10.
To some stock tracers, the Fed's inaction signaled that the central bank was unprepared to do anything to stem the turmoil on world markets. Floyed Norris: Market Place, Page 10.

Rubin Warns of Asia Trade Backlash

Author: Sanger, David e

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 Sep 1997: 18.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430846832?accountid=11752

Abstract: With economic troubles in Southeast Asia and Japan and China's exports booming, Administration officials meeting with finance ministers from around the world said today that they feared the United States was headed toward a revival of huge trade deficits with Asia that could provoke a backlash in Congress. Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin met with his Japanese counterpart, Hiroshi Mitsuzuka, in a session that one Japanese official described as ''quiet but very tense.'' Mr. Rubin urged him to make sure that Japan did not attempt to crawl out of its recession by boosting exports to the United States. Moreover, Mr. Clinton is about to welcome China's President, Jiang Zimen, to the United States for the first state visit of a Chinese leader since before the 1989 Tiananmen Square massacre. With little progress to boast about on the human rights front, Mr. Clinton's aides had hoped to emphasize the foreign policy benefits of an expanding trade relationship with China.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Rubin+Warns+of+Asia+Trade+Backlash&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1997-09-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1.18&amp;au=Sanger%2C+David+e&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/04713125&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: With economic troubles in Southeast Asia and Japan and China's exports booming, Administration officials meeting with finance ministers from around the world said today that they feared the United States was headed toward a revival of huge trade deficits with Asia that could provoke a backlash in Congress.
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin met with his Japanese counterpart, Hiroshi Mitsuzuka, in a session that one Japanese official described as ''quiet but very tense.'' Mr. Rubin urged him to make sure that Japan did not attempt to crawl out of its recession by boosting exports to the United States.
Yet Japanese executives concede that is already happening, and they say they have little choice now that their biggest market -- Southeast Asia -- is reeling from a summer-long currency crisis that is spreading to other nations.
''No one wants to admit it out loud,'' said one senior Japanese executive here for the annual meeting of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund. ''But now there is no place else that still has strong demand for Japanese goods except the U.S.''
According to many estimates circulating here, Japan's surplus with the United States will exceed $65 billion this year, roughly a 20 percent increase from last year.
The sudden revival of trade deficits -- an issue on which President Clinton focused intently for the first three years of his presidency, then scarcely mentioned -- comes at a politically inopportune time for the White House. Mr. Clinton is battling in Congress for a renewal of his power to strike new trade accords around the world.
But he is running into strong opposition on both the Democratic left and the Republican right, much of it rooted in suspicions that American workers have gained far less from from the Administration's embrace of global competition than Mr. Clinton contends.
Moreover, Mr. Clinton is about to welcome China's President, Jiang Zimen, to the United States for the first state visit of a Chinese leader since before the 1989 Tiananmen Square massacre. With little progress to boast about on the human rights front, Mr. Clinton's aides had hoped to emphasize the foreign policy benefits of an expanding trade relationship with China.
That will now be difficult. Most American estimates now suggest that China's trade deficit with the United States will exceed $50 billion for the first time this year, making it nearly the size of Japan's.
Moreover, efforts to speed along negotiations to get China into the World Trade Organization -- which would require it to adopt rules that would further open the Chinese market -- have bogged down. Administration officials are now playing down the potential discussion of that subject at the Washington summit meeting.
''Wherever you look in Asia, America's trade numbers are about to go bonkers,'' said Kenneth Courtis, the global economist at Deutsche Bank in Tokyo. ''And we are just beginning to see the political fallout.''
Large trade deficits themselves are not necessarily bad news for the American economy. A steady stream of competitive imports helps keep inflation in check. America's soaring trade deficit numbers also reflect the fact that the United States has one of the healthiest economies in the world, while its trading partners are not growing fast enough to attract more imports.
But trade politics and trade economics are often two very different things. So today in his meeting with Mr. Mitsuzuka -- a longtime leader of the Liberal Democratic Party and a veteran of many trade disputes -- Mr. Rubin took the unusual step of inviting the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, to join him in arguing that Japan must act now to revive its economy.
Japan's usual response has been to launch major public works programs, but at one American official said recently, ''There are only so many roads and train tracks you can build to nowhere. And they've built them.'
Mr. Rubin said later that he thought that his message had gotten through. ''It is fair to say that they, too, are concerned about domestic demand and sustainable, solid growth,'' he said. But he stopped short of saying that he had urged Japan to cut taxes.
The trade problem has been exacerbated by the currency crisis in Southeast Asia. Those emerging markets have been a major customer for American exports. But Thailand, Malaysia and Indonesia are now canceling major projects, including dams and airports, to trim their own large deficits.
They also have been forced to devalue their currencies, making their goods more competitive on world markets and American goods, priced in dollars, sharply more expensive. Equally important, Southeast Asia accounts for slightly less than half of Japan's exports, and some of those goods will undoubtedly be redirected to the United States.
In the currency markets, this has led to some speculation that the United States would attempt to weaken the dollar, to make American goods more attractive abroad.
But Mr. Rubin has given no hint of that and today at a meeting of finance ministers of the Group of Seven top industrial nations, there was a veiled warning to Japan not to try to drive down the yen to boost its own exports.
Photograph Britain's Chancellor of the Exchequer, Gordon Brown, talked yesterday with the Treasury Secretary, Robert E. Rubin, and the Finance Minister of Japan, Hiroshi Mitsuzuka, at a meeting in Hong Kong. (Reuters)

Subway Brawl Inflames Issue of G.I.'s in Korea

Author: Kristof, Nicholas D

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]24 Aug 1995: A.3.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430274795?accountid=11752

Abstract: "The Korean public is led to believe that these things are getting out of hand, and it's affecting our welcome here," said the American Ambassador in Seoul, James T. Laney. "It's a cause for great concern." The United States, on the other hand, wants South Korea to pick up more of the tab for stationing troops in South Korea. Americans see little reason why they should pay more Korean taxes when they are already helping defend South Korea -- and paying for the privilege. The United States now pays not only all the dollar costs of maintaining troops in Korea, like soldiers' pay, but also two-thirds of the costs incurred in local currency, like payments to support staff. The Seoul Government is willing to increase its payments, amounting to a one-third share, only by the amount of inflation. But the United States argues that South Korea's extraordinary economic growth means that Seoul can now pay more of its own way.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Subway+Brawl+Inflames+Issue+of+G.I.%27s+in+Korea&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1995-08-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.3&amp;au=Kristof%2C+Nicholas+D&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: It all began when an American soldier put his hand on a Korean woman's rump.
The version that has captured the local imagination is that a group of drunken American soldiers were rampaging through the subway, molesting Korean women, and that the soldiers then attacked good citizens who dared protest the errant hand.
That episode, which is expected to lead to the public trial soon of four Americans, has added to an outcry in South Korea against what many Koreans perceive as a wave of violence by American soldiers, and a system that is tilted to allow them to get away with it.
"We strongly condemn U.S. military personnel who do not repent and who continue to commit outrageous criminal offenses," declared a group that calls itself the "headquarters to bring an end to crimes by U.S. military stationed in Korea."
The American understanding of events starts with a fact that the Koreans tend to leave out: The American soldier and the Korean woman whose behind he patted were in fact a married couple.
The Americans say the problems arose when some angry young Koreans on the subway accused the American of sexually harassing the Korean woman. When the Korean woman explained that she was the American's wife, the Korean men allegedly spat at her and slapped her -- leading the woman's husband to punch the man who slapped her.
In any case, the result that evening in May was a huge brawl in the subway. It has reverberated through the country and underscored the delicacy of the mission of the 37,000 American military personnel in bases in South Korea.
South Korea is an intensely nationalist country. Some Korean and American officials alike worry that public irritation about crimes committed by American troops could undermine the public support for the American bases in South Korea.
"The Korean public is led to believe that these things are getting out of hand, and it's affecting our welcome here," said the American Ambassador in Seoul, James T. Laney. "It's a cause for great concern."
Mr. Laney says the problem is not that American soldiers are committing more crimes, but that an irresponsible Korean press is portraying them in a particularly lurid way to an inflamed public.
Many Koreans say that whether or not crime is getting worse, the problem is that American soldiers get special treatment when they commit crimes.
"The problem is not in the crime itself, but in the criminal process," said Kwon Suk Hun, a 23-year-old student council officer at Yonsei University, traditionally a hotbed of social protest. "This process reflects the imperialist characteristics of the U.S. Government."
The United States and South Korea agreed this summer to review the Status of Forces Agreement, known as SOFA, which governs such issues as the treatment of American soldiers charged with committing crimes in Korea. Korean courts have jurisdiction, although soldiers may also face court-martial and other penalties under American law.
"The Korean public's perception is that the SOFA is not meeting with a sense of justice," said Yim Sung Joon, director general of the American affairs bureau in the Foreign Ministry. Mr. Yim said the most important issue is to revise the agreement so arrested American soldiers may be held in Korean custody while awaiting trial.
Now they are released to the American military, although they are made available to the Korean authorities for questioning.
If convicted of crimes, American soldiers are jailed in a special wing of a Korean prison, where eight are now serving sentences. If they are sentenced to death by a Korean court, the Americans could be executed, although this has not happened and none is on death row now.
A measure of the public sensitivity in South Korea is that one of the best-known Americans in the country is Kenneth Markle 3d, an Army private convicted of murdering a Korean prostitute in 1992. The case led to huge demonstrations and anti-American protests, as well as calls for the death penalty for Private Markle, but in the end he was sentenced to a 15-year term, which he is serving.
While cases like the subway incident are seen by Koreans as evidence of the need for protection from rampaging Americans, to Americans they show that they need protection from a skewed local judicial system. After the subway brawl, no Koreans were indicted, but four Americans were.
These kinds of problems, of course, are neither new nor confined to South Korea. They were a factor in the closing of American military bases in the Philippines in 1992.
But in South Korea, the upshot is that with the atmosphere strained because of protests over crime by American soldiers, each side is now demanding more of the other. Lee Jang Hee, one of the leaders of a group that has gathered more than 40,000 signatures seeking to revise the Status of Forces Agreement, says American tax privileges in Korea should be revoked.
The United States, on the other hand, wants South Korea to pick up more of the tab for stationing troops in South Korea. Americans see little reason why they should pay more Korean taxes when they are already helping defend South Korea -- and paying for the privilege.
The United States now pays not only all the dollar costs of maintaining troops in Korea, like soldiers' pay, but also two-thirds of the costs incurred in local currency, like payments to support staff. The Seoul Government is willing to increase its payments, amounting to a one-third share, only by the amount of inflation. But the United States argues that South Korea's extraordinary economic growth means that Seoul can now pay more of its own way.
Japan, in contrast, pays all the costs incurred in local currency for maintaining United States forces stationed in Japan.
Could accusations about criminal behavior and demands for changes in the Status of Forces Agreement lead the United States to decide that it is not worth the bother and expense of defending South Korea?
The Clinton Administration says that despite occasional difficulties, the troops in South Korea are as essential now as they ever were.
Mr. Yim also said a withdrawal was unlikely.
"Keeping troops on the Korean peninsula is in U.S. interests," he added, a bit nervously. "So I'm sure the U.S. won't bring them home."
Photograph American soldiers stroll a back alley of Seoul's Itaewon district, a bar, shopping and entertainment area frequented by soldiers and tourists. (Yuk Sang Soo)

CREDIT MARKETS; Bonds End Mixed After Early Rally Fades

Author: Hurtado, Robert

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]17 Mar 1995: D.16.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430085553?accountid=11752

Abstract: Donald Fine, chief market analyst for Chase Securities, said bond market participants paid close attention to both housing starts and the Philadelphia Fed business outlook because they were "statistics that further indicated a slowdown in business activity, although by now this is news only for those recently arriving from Mars." Mr. Fine says inflation as measured by the consumer index is a lagging indicator and is likely to increase further as the year progresses, though in a nonthreatening manner. "But inflationary expectations, which matter most for bond yields, have clearly lessened in response to the slower rate of economic growth," he said. Some analysts, however, are not as optimistic that the threat of inflation has receded. Anthony E. Spare, chief investment officer of Spare, Kaplan, Bischel & Associates, a $2 billion San Francisco money management firm, sees the economy in transition. "We are going to go from the best of all worlds of rapid growth and low inflation to the worst of all worlds, higher inflation and slower growth," he said. "Everyone has built in their own expectations of a soft landing, but the odds are low for such an occurrence."

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=CREDIT+MARKETS%3B+Bonds+End+Mixed+After+Early+Rally+Fades&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1995-03-17&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.16&amp;au=Hurtado%2C+Robert&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Prices of Treasury securities ended mixed yesterday after a rally set off by signs of an economic slowdown faded.
A series of reports released yesterday indicated the economy had slowed enough to subdue inflation. These reports, which included a modest pickup in consumer prices in February, suggest that the Federal Reserve is unlikely to raise short-term interest rates when its policy-making committee meets on March 28.
The price of the 30-year bond fell 5/32 , to 1032/32 , after being up about a point in intraday trading. At that price, the bond was yielding 7.37 percent, up from 7.36 percent on Wednesday. Prices and yields move in opposite directions. Market participants said the yield had been down as low as 7.28 percent, the lowest in nine months.
In early trading, the market turned higher following the release of the Consumer Price Index for February. The index rose three-tenths of 1 percent. Its core rate, which excludes the volatile categories of food and energy, also rose three-tenths of 1 percent.
In a separate report, housing starts fell 2.6 percent in February, to an annualized rate of 1.323 million from 1.38 million in January.
The Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia, in a survey of regional business activity, said its index fell in March to 3.3 from 11.9 in February. Traders had been expecting the index to slip to 10.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst for Chase Securities, said bond market participants paid close attention to both housing starts and the Philadelphia Fed business outlook because they were "statistics that further indicated a slowdown in business activity, although by now this is news only for those recently arriving from Mars."
The increase in the Consumer Price Index was duly noted, he said, but had little market impact because it was fully anticipated.
Mr. Fine says inflation as measured by the consumer index is a lagging indicator and is likely to increase further as the year progresses, though in a nonthreatening manner. "But inflationary expectations, which matter most for bond yields, have clearly lessened in response to the slower rate of economic growth," he said.
Some analysts, however, are not as optimistic that the threat of inflation has receded. Anthony E. Spare, chief investment officer of Spare, Kaplan, Bischel & Associates, a $2 billion San Francisco money management firm, sees the economy in transition. "We are going to go from the best of all worlds of rapid growth and low inflation to the worst of all worlds, higher inflation and slower growth," he said. "Everyone has built in their own expectations of a soft landing, but the odds are low for such an occurrence."
Bond prices were also supported for most of yesterday by a stronger dollar. But there was late profit taking after word of a large bond sale by a large institutional investor. There was talk among traders that an insurance company had sold substantial holdings in five-year notes and other longer maturities, though the identity of the insurer could not be determined.
In the municipal market, where investors have been hungry for new issues, the Virginia Housing Development Authority competitively priced $100 million of its commonwealth mortgage revenue bonds won by Prudential Securities.
Underwriters said the bonds were insured by the Municipal Bond Investor's Assurance Corporation, giving them a triple-A rating from Moody's Investors Services Inc. and the Standard & Poor's Corporation.
The maximum yield was 6.45 percent for a bond due in 2028, with the next-best yields at 6.40 percent for a bond due in 2022 and 6.35 percent in 2018. For shorter maturities, yields on serials bond range from 6.15 percent in 2011 to 6.30 percent in 2014 and 2015.
Graph "Tax-Exempt Yields" shows average weekly yields for 20 general obligation bonds and 25 revenue bonds, in percent, from Dec. 1994-Mar. 1995. (Source: The Bond Buyer)

MEXICO IS FACING NEW RESTRICTIONS TO GET U.S. HELP

Author: Sanger, David E

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]20 Feb 1995: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/430043316?accountid=11752

Abstract: "This is scary," said Jonathan E. Heath, an economist in Mexico City. "A recession is already unavoidable. The concern for the United States Government is probably that if you start pursuing a very restrictive monetary policy, and things start to get bad, the Mexican Government will want to let up." "We recognize there's not going to be an easy way out," said the official, who insisted on anonymity. "What they're talking about in Washington now is just a difference of degree." When Mr. [Robert E. Rubin] described the bailout plan in Congressional testimony several weeks ago -- after President Clinton abandoned any hope of getting Congress to pass the program and acted on his own to provide Treasury funds for loans and loan guarantees -- he said that "in the unlikely event" that Mexico defaulted on its United States-backed loans, foreign purchasers of Mexican oil would be required to remit their payments to the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. That money, in turn, would be used to pay American investors.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=MEXICO+IS+FACING+NEW+RESTRICTIONS+TO+GET+U.S.+HELP&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1995-02-20&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Sanger%2C+David+E&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
After three days of intense negotiations with top Mexican officials, the United States is preparing to announce details of a plan to bail the country out of a continuing currency crisis, including newly toughened restrictions that are likely to send already high Mexican interest rates soaring.
Details of the plan were still being negotiated here tonight between Secretary of the Treasury Robert E. Rubin and Mexico's Finance Minister, Guillermo Ortiz, who has been in Washington since Thursday. A spokesman for Mr. Rubin said that negotiators were making progress, but it was unclear whether any agreement would be concluded on Monday.
The new conditions, described by Americans and Mexicans familiar with the talks, pose enormous political problems for President Ernesto Zedillo Ponce de Leon.
To get the $20 billion in direct American help that the Clinton Administration announced last month, Mr. Zedillo's Government is agreeing to shrink Mexico's money supply, even if that means interest rates of 50 percent or more on business loans and home mortgages.
Also included in the draft of an agreement here is a toughening of a highly controversial condition that Washington is placing on the Mexicans: The United States would effectively control the flow of the billions of dollars that Mexico earns every year from the export of its oil, starting in the next few months.
Under an earlier plan, the United States would have control of Mexico's oil revenue, its biggest single source of foreign currency, if Mexico defaulted on some of its American-backed obligations.
The newer, even more stringent conditions seem bound to open Mr. Zedillo to charges at home that he has permitted the United States to dictate his country's economic policy, an enormously sensitive matter in Mexican politics.
But Clinton Administration officials, fearful that they will be blamed by both Republicans and Democrats if Mexico requires another bailout later this year, have insisted in recent days that there is no other choice.
Anything short of Draconian fiscal discipline, they insist, will mean that foreign investors continue to pull money out of the country -- and the problem the Administration raced to solve last month could return as a bigger crisis.
If a recession in Mexico follows -- which many Mexican and American economists consider a real risk -- the result could be more social unrest in the country, where a 14-month-long rebellion in a southern state, Chiapas, is already plaguing Mr. Zedillo's weakened three-month-old administration. Last week, his party overwhelmingly lost an election in a big western state, Jalisco, and several more such elections are scheduled later in the year.
At the heart of the negotiations between United States and Mexican officials this weekend was the Clinton Administration's fear that Mr. Zedillo, under growing political pressure, would be tempted to print more money or to lower interest rates before the coming state elections.
The result, in a nation where inflation was running in January at an annual rate of more than 45 percent, could be even steeper inflation. The current level is already a shock to Mexicans, because last year inflation dropped to less than 10 percent for the first time in recent memory.
However, maintaining interest rates at a high level -- they are already more than 40 percent to keep up with the rising inflation -- seems bound to touch off a recession, and with it the possibility of kindling for social unrest.
"This is scary," said Jonathan E. Heath, an economist in Mexico City. "A recession is already unavoidable. The concern for the United States Government is probably that if you start pursuing a very restrictive monetary policy, and things start to get bad, the Mexican Government will want to let up."
That is what Washington says Mexican officials did last year, as the Presidential election neared.
When the Clinton Administration announced its plan last month, Mexican officials knew that it would carry tough conditions that would be difficult to sell at home.
But they were not counting on such a large run-up in inflation in January, making it all the more painful to meet the plan's conditions. Similar conditions apply to about $30 billion in other help promised to Mexico in the last month, including $17.8 billion from the International Monetary Fund.
"Investors are still nervous about Mexico," a White House official said last week, after the value of the peso had again dropped. "And if the markets are nervous, we have to be nervous."
A Mexican finance official familiar with the discussions said tonight that even before Mr. Ortiz left for Washington last week, there was an understanding that with inflation and interest rates above what had been forecast, the Government would have little choice but to tighten the money supply.
"We recognize there's not going to be an easy way out," said the official, who insisted on anonymity. "What they're talking about in Washington now is just a difference of degree."
The official said the Mexican Government recognized the social risks involved in such moves and was preparing several responses, including an emergency jobs plan, using funds from an antipoverty program. The Government will also zero in on areas of high unemployment and political instability with more than 500,000 job-training grants.
The first victims of the tight monetary plan are expected to be Mexico's weaker banks. At least 8 of the 18 major banks are already considered on the brink because many of their clients cannot repay loans carrying variable interest rates that have soared in recent weeks. There are virtually no fixed-rate loans available in Mexico, so everyone from large companies to ordinary homeowners have been hit hard by the fallout from the currency crisis.
Several of those banks seem likely to be merged in the next few weeks, for fear that investors, particularly United States investors, would otherwise remove funds invested in certificates of deposit in the last year or so. The banks are holding about $8 billion in such assets now, and if these are not reinvested, the money is expected to fly to safer havens.
Under previous pressure from the United States, Mexico had agreed to open its banking system to acquisitions by foreign banks.
All this is expected to put great pressure on Mr. Zedillo, an economist by training. In the last few days, he has been on the phone to Mr. Ortiz and to top United States officials, who have tried to lay out the economic and political tradeoffs.
They have told him that while the tight monetary policy would be painful for the next year or so, it was the best hope of stabilizing the economy. At the same time, they are trying to give him some political cover, so that it does not appear he has fully capitulated to American conditions.
One of the provisions carrying the most potential volatility is proposed American control of the revenue from Mexico's oil, a cherished national asset and the source of $7 billion to the Government each year.
When Mr. Rubin described the bailout plan in Congressional testimony several weeks ago -- after President Clinton abandoned any hope of getting Congress to pass the program and acted on his own to provide Treasury funds for loans and loan guarantees -- he said that "in the unlikely event" that Mexico defaulted on its United States-backed loans, foreign purchasers of Mexican oil would be required to remit their payments to the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. That money, in turn, would be used to pay American investors.
But members of Congress including Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, Republican of New York, chairman of the Senate Banking Committee, charged that the plan provided insufficient protection. And Representative Joseph P. Kennedy 2d, Democrat of Massachusetts, told Mr. Rubin it was ludicrous to think that Washington would cut off Mexico's oil earnings, for fear that such a move would cause wide protests.
To mute such Congressional criticism, the new accord would require foreign buyers of Mexican oil to send their payments through the New York Federal Reserve Bank, starting in the next few months, regardless of how well Mexico was paying off its obligations.
It is unclear, however, whether that move alone would satisfy Mr. D'Amato, who, with other members of Congress, introduced a bill last week requiring the Treasury to issue much fuller reports about the details and progress of its bailout effort.

U.S. IS READYING FURTHER BILLIONS TO RESCUE MEXICO

Author: Bradsher, Keith

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]29 Dec 1994: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429956333?accountid=11752

Abstract: The preparations reflect the Clinton Administration's assessment that Mexico is too important to the United States to be left without assistance at a time of severe economic and financial stress. The goal of the package would not be to obtain greater leverage over Mexico on areas of specific dispute like drugs or illegal immigration, but rather to help assure Mexico's long-term economic and political stability. An international monetary official who insisted on anonymity said tonight that all stories of an I.M.F. bailout were false. Because Mexico still owes $4 billion to the I.M.F. that it borrowed in 1989 and the early 1990's, the monetary fund's rules require that any new line of credit come after the negotiation of a comprehensive economic plan it has approved, a process that takes many months. The nation's difficulties should have only modest effects on the overall United States economy, private economists said. Indeed, the peso's drop, making Mexican goods cheaper for Americans to buy, is likely to shave one- or two-tenths of a percentage point off the inflation rate in the United States during the next two years, according to Allen Sinai, the chief economist at Lehman Brothers.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Diferencias+socioculturales+en+la+terapia+de+pareja%3A+La+perspectiva+de+terapeutas+y+parejas+consultantes&amp;title=Psicologia+Cl%C3%ADnica&amp;issn=01035665&amp;date=2017-01-01&amp;volume=29&amp;issue=2&amp;spage=209&amp;au=Ulloa%2C+Valentina%3Bde+la+Puerta%2C+Sofia%3BFossa%2C+Pablo&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=Psicologia+Cl%C3%ADnica&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/2018-17760-004&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The United States is preparing to spend billions of dollars, possibly as soon as next week, to help Mexico in its financial crisis. The money would be used to prop up the peso in foreign-exchange markets and help make payments on Mexico's national debt, an Administration official said today.
The money would be in addition to the $6 billion line of credit that the United States made available to Mexico last week. It would most likely consist of American loans to the Mexican Government and possibly American purchases of pesos in currency markets, the official said.
The Administration official confirmed that discussions were under way on providing assistance to Mexico, but refused to be quoted directly on any details.
The preparations reflect the Clinton Administration's assessment that Mexico is too important to the United States to be left without assistance at a time of severe economic and financial stress. The goal of the package would not be to obtain greater leverage over Mexico on areas of specific dispute like drugs or illegal immigration, but rather to help assure Mexico's long-term economic and political stability.
Lawrence H. Summers, Under Secretary of the Treasury for International Affairs, said tonight that "in approaching this situation, we've recognized the central importance of Mexico to the United States because it is our second-largest trading partner, because we share a 2,000-mile border and because our societies are so closely intertwined."
Bankers and analysts gave a cautious welcome to reports that the United States was preparing a multibillion-dollar aid package for Mexico. But they said that any revival of confidence in Mexico's battered currency and economy would be fragile for a long time to come. [ Page D6. ]
The scale of the assistance is unclear, and would not represent the first time the United States has helped Mexico. In 1982 Washington bought extra oil from Mexico and the Federal Reserve urged commercial banks to reschedule Mexico's foreign debt after it defaulted.
Further assistance from other rich industrial nations -- notably Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Italy and Japan -- remains possible this year, but American officials have had few conversations with them so far.
The Administration official who disclosed the broad rescue plan also confirmed rumors tonight that Mexico had not yet spent any of the $6 billion line of credit to support the peso. This suggests that Mexico may be waiting until the United States is ready to help in a coordinated intervention in the currency markets to drive up the peso.
The country's volatile financial markets started to calm down today with both the stock exchange and the peso improving.
Within the financial markets, attention has focused in recent days on Mexican Government bonds that are falling due in coming weeks. American officials refused to give specifics about the workings of the bailout package.
But it is clear that Washington's efforts to help are not directly aimed at supporting the peso or relieving Mexico of its obligations to pay off the bonds. Instead, the United States is focusing on the overall problem of making sure that Mexico has enough money to meet these short-term needs.
The understanding has been that any American help would have to be repaid.
The peso -- after dropping nearly 40 percent in the last week and a half -- bounced back by more than 14 percent today, partly on a series of rumors of international assistance to Mexico. The main one was that Canada, the United States and the International Monetary Fund would set up a fund of $10 billion to $15 billion to help the Mexican Government make the bond payments.
But an international monetary official who insisted on anonymity said tonight that all stories of an I.M.F. bailout were false. Because Mexico still owes $4 billion to the I.M.F. that it borrowed in 1989 and the early 1990's, the monetary fund's rules require that any new line of credit come after the negotiation of a comprehensive economic plan it has approved, a process that takes many months.
Top Mexican officials were so confident that 1994 would be a year of financial and economic success that they allowed a $5.4 billion line of credit at the International Monetary Fund to expire on May 25, 1993.
The I.M.F. is a 179-nation organization based in Washington that serves as the lender of last resort for countries facing currency crises.
The Mexican bonds, known as tesobonos, pay interest and principal that is calculated in dollars but paid in pesos. If it takes more pesos than before to buy a dollar, as has been the case this week, then Mexico must pay more pesos to redeem these bonds. International bankers said today that $3.6 billion of these bonds would fall due next month.
Mexico's usual practice until now has been to sell new tesobonos, often to foreigners, and use the proceeds to redeem others falling due.
But the collapse of foreign demand for the Mexican bonds has left the Government there with a stark range of choices: It can print more pesos, which risks higher inflation; it can borrow the money at home, which might send interest rates so high that they bring on a recession in Mexico, or the Government can find a foreign country, like the United States, willing either to buy new bonds or to lend Mexico a few billion dollars until other foreigners become interested in these bonds again.
Mexico's situation is made even more difficult because it needs money at the same time to prop up the peso.
Mr. Summers and several assistants have handled the issue in close consultation with Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve. They have exchanged so many phone calls with Mexican officials that they now recognize each others' voices instantly over the phone.
Former Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen, before stepping down on Dec. 22, also spoke frequently with his new Mexican counterpart, Finance Minister Jaime Serra Puche.
Frank N. Newman, the acting Treasury Secretary, and Robert E. Rubin, assistant to the President for economic policy, who has been chosen by Mr. Clinton to replace Mr. Bentsen, are both on vacation this week. They have kept in touch by telephone with developments in Mexico. Members of Congress who scattered on Christmas vacations have largely ignored Mexico's troubles.
The nation's difficulties should have only modest effects on the overall United States economy, private economists said. Indeed, the peso's drop, making Mexican goods cheaper for Americans to buy, is likely to shave one- or two-tenths of a percentage point off the inflation rate in the United States during the next two years, according to Allen Sinai, the chief economist at Lehman Brothers.
But if Mexico fell into a deep recession because of the peso's troubles, that might affect the United States economy by reducing growth here, also by one- or two-tenths of a percentage point in 1995 and 1996, Mr. Sinai said.
These effects are assumed to be modest because the Mexican economy is not big by the standards of industrial powers. While the Clinton Administration has portrayed sales of American-made goods to Mexico as a source of jobs and prosperity, these sales are quite small when seen in comparison with the broad economy of the United States.
Through September of this year, for example, American exports to Mexico totaled $37.52 billion, amounting to only three-quarters of 1 percent of all goods and services produced in the United States in that period.

Subject: FOREIGN AID; UNITED STATES INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS; MEXICO-INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS-US; ECONOMIC CONDITIONS AND TRENDS; CURRENCY; POLITICS AND GOVERNMENT; CREDIT

Location: MEXICO

People: BRADSHER, KEITH CLINTON, BILL (PRES)
