U.S. Weighs Sending Ambassador to Zaire, a Dictator's Fief

Author: French, Howard W

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]25 Oct 1994: A.5.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429904917?accountid=11752

Abstract: Officials in Mr. [Talbott]'s party said Mr. [Kengo Wa Dondo] could not succeed in office without a measure of support from Zaire's traditional foreign partners: the United States, Belgium and France. And if he fails, they said, Zaire, one of Africa's largest countries, with one-quarter the land mass of the United States and 41 million inhabitants, is in danger of disintegration. Mr. Talbott said that for now United States cooperation with Zaire, largely suspended after violent riots in 1991, would remain limited to relief supplies. Even diplomats who support the current opening to Zaire acknowledge that in addition to using Mr. Kengo, Mr. [Mobutu Sese Seko] has repeatedly used the tactic of presenting putatively reform-minded Prime Ministers to the West as a means of buying himself time to refill his coffers and undo his adversaries.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+Weighs+Sending+Ambassador+to+Zaire%2C+a+Dictator%27s+Fief&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-10-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=French%2C+Howard+W&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
In a sign of support for a new Prime Minister's reform efforts in a country led by one of Africa's most corrupt and repressive governments, Deputy Secretary of State Strobe Talbott made a brief visit here today and said Washington was considering sending an ambassador to Zaire.
Mr. Talbott, who is on a tour of several African countries, met with Prime Minister Kengo Wa Dondo during a stopover between visits to Malawi and Ghana.
In carefully worded comments about the visit, Mr. Talbott said United States backing for the new Prime Minister would be closely coordinated with his reform efforts and did not represent an embrace of Zaire's long-reigning dictator, Mobutu Sese Seko.
Officials in Mr. Talbott's party said Mr. Kengo could not succeed in office without a measure of support from Zaire's traditional foreign partners: the United States, Belgium and France. And if he fails, they said, Zaire, one of Africa's largest countries, with one-quarter the land mass of the United States and 41 million inhabitants, is in danger of disintegration.
"We are considering sending an ambassador, which would be a modest step," Mr. Talbott said. "After all, we have ambassadors in a lot of countries where we disagree with things happening there."
Critics of Mr. Kengo say the Prime Minister, who was appointed in July, has already twice led governments under Mr. Mobutu. In the end, they say, Mr. Kengo will prove as unwilling or unable to change Mr. Mobutu's system of tight political control as in the past.
A 1991 conference that sought unsuccessfully to organize a democratic transition in Zaire cited Mr. Kengo for large-scale embezzlement during his two previous stints as Prime Minister in the early and late 1980's. He has also been accused of playing a role in the massacre of students at Lovanium University in Zaire in 1969, when he was Justice Minister.
Mr. Talbott said that for now United States cooperation with Zaire, largely suspended after violent riots in 1991, would remain limited to relief supplies. Even diplomats who support the current opening to Zaire acknowledge that in addition to using Mr. Kengo, Mr. Mobutu has repeatedly used the tactic of presenting putatively reform-minded Prime Ministers to the West as a means of buying himself time to refill his coffers and undo his adversaries.
Western diplomats, including Mr. Talbott's delegation, credit Mr. Kengo with moves to rein in ruinous inflation and to reimpose state authority over a formal economy that has virtually ceased to exist.
Among Mr. Kengo's early reform measures has been a still-unresolved bid to remove what diplomats describe as Mobutu cronies from the Central Bank, and efforts to bring the lucrative diamond mining industry back under state control.
Mr. Mobutu has begun to emerge from his own diplomatic isolation, undertaking a trip to Japan and China in the last week and planning a visit to France next month to take part in the annual French-African summit meeting. Mr. Mobutu was banned from visiting France for more than two years after the French Ambassador to Zaire was killed in the 1991 riots.
In a brief interview at Kinshasa Airport, where he met with Mr. Talbott, Mr. Kengo pledged that he would work toward national elections sometime in the next year. "We are moving toward a rehabilitation of the economy that is capital," he said. "For this we must restore the authority of the state, restructure the army and guarantee the liberty of our citizens."

Subject: UNITED STATES INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS; UNITED STATES-INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS-ZAIRE; DIPLOMATIC RECOGNITION; REFORM AND REORGANIZATION; POLITICS AND GOVERNMENT

Location: ZAIRE AFRICA

People: Dondo, Kengo Wa Mobutu Sese Seko FRENCH, HOWARD W DONDO, KENGO WA (PRIME MIN) MOBUTU SESE SEKO (PRES) TALBOTT, STROBE (DEPUTY SEC)

Title: U.S. Weighs Sending Ambassador to Zaire, a Dictator's Fief

CURRENCY MARKETS; Dollar's Gain Turns to Loss After Greenspan Testimony

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 July 1994: D.15.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429818502?accountid=11752

Abstract: "[Alan Greenspan] may have reopened a can of worms," said Lisa Finstrom, currency analyst at Smith Barney Inc. "It rekindled inflationary expectations. The market had become somewhat more calm and had faith coming into this. The dollar's value reflects overall financial health of the United States and inflation is no good. The expression of concern fed dollar negativity." In his testimony, Mr. Greenspan said the currency's slide was bad for the United States economy and was beginning to have an impact on the Fed's monetary policy because of the inflationary consequences.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=CURRENCY+MARKETS%3B+Dollar%27s+Gain+Turns+to+Loss+After+Greenspan+Testimony&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-07-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.15&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: By The Associated Press
The dollar fell slightly against leading currencies yesterday, following the bond and stock markets, which weakened after the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, rekindled inflation fears in testimony before Congress.
Though Mr. Greenspan indicated concern about the dollar's weakness and its effect on the economy, the dollar still fell against the Japanese yen and the German mark. Such talk usually drives the dollar higher by putting traders on notice that the central bank may intervene in its defense.
Traders said yesterday that sliding securities prices and profit taking from the dollar's recent rally undermined the positive effects of Mr. Greenspan's testimony. Another Rate Increase?
The dollar started higher in Europe but began to fall with stock and bond prices after the release of Mr. Greenspan's prepared testimony. He told the Senate Banking Committee that this year's four interest rate increases might not be enough to rein in inflation.
"Greenspan may have reopened a can of worms," said Lisa Finstrom, currency analyst at Smith Barney Inc. "It rekindled inflationary expectations. The market had become somewhat more calm and had faith coming into this. The dollar's value reflects overall financial health of the United States and inflation is no good. The expression of concern fed dollar negativity."
The dollar was quoted in late New York trading at 98.80 yen, down from 99.30 late Tuesday. The dollar dropped to 1.5650 marks from 1.5682 marks on Tuesday. An Impact on Policy
In his testimony, Mr. Greenspan said the currency's slide was bad for the United States economy and was beginning to have an impact on the Fed's monetary policy because of the inflationary consequences.
A protracted slide could ultimately fuel inflation by pushing up the price of imports, and has already set off investor flight from dollar-denominated assets.
Some market participants shrugged off the comments, which they said shed no new light on the Government's currency policy, and attributed yesterday's dip mainly to profit taking.
"The selloff started before Greenspan opened his mouth. I think it was largely technical," said Hubert Pedroli, manager of currency trading for Credit Suisse in New York.

Currency Turmoil Weighing on U.S. and Japan; Fed Officials Get Confusing Signals on Interest Rates

Author: Bradsher, Keith

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 June 1994: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429767609?accountid=11752

Abstract: Top Fed officials watch a broad 10-currency index of the dollar's value that shows the United States currency has fallen very little this year. The Fed's staff presents the index and other statistics to the Federal Reserve Board each Monday morning at a closed-door meeting on monetary policy. "We're close to that, and certainly the parallels will be drawn, and that will make it more difficult for Secretary [Lloyd Bentsen] and President Clinton," said John H. Makin, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute, a research organization in Washington. "We've been over this ground in the living memory of a lot of people in the market." The Federal Reserve chose in 1978 to raise interest rates fairly gradually, a decision that has been heavily criticized as inadequate since then by monetary economists. When inflation jumped to 11 percent in 1979, the central bank was forced to raise short-term interest rates to 20 percent to bring it under control, provoking a deep recession by 1981 and a long, slow reduction in inflation during the 1980's.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Currency+Turmoil+Weighing+on+U.S.+and+Japan%3B+Fed+Officials+Get+Confusing+Signals+on+Interest+Rates&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-06-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Bradsher%2C+Keith&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
Top Federal Reserve officials found mixed signals in this week's turmoil in the currency markets, which they believe has left their course on interest rates unclear.
When an American-led effort by 16 central banks on Friday failed to prop up the dollar against the German mark and Japanese yen, many analysts wondered if the Fed would be forced to raise interest rates as the only way to stop the slide.
But the Fed sees a far more complicated picture, one that would appear to have it prepared, but not poised, to take action. Little Fear of Inflation
The Fed officials do not see any inherent inflationary pressures stemming from the week's events that would require such an action. They noted that the dollar was stable against many currencies, and actually rose against those of two key United States trading partners, Canada and Mexico.
In addition, they believe that the inflation rate in the United States is falling as economic growth becomes more subdued.
But they worry that the drop against the mark and yen could be a warning sign that international investors have come to expect inflation in the United States and are less willing to hold assets here as a result.
A determination to stamp out such expectations, which tend to become self-fulfilling, was a prime reason for the series of interest-rate increases that the Fed undertook this spring to keep the markets satisfied about the pace of economic recovery. The Effect of Rates
Raising interest rates again might appease the banks, companies and professional traders who have sold billions of dollars in exchange for yen and marks and who on Friday brushed aside an Administration-led effort by the Federal Reserve and 15 other central banks to prop up the dollar.
Higher rates would make American bonds more attractive, providing an incentive for traders to buy the dollars needed to buy those bonds.
But higher rates would also put extra drag on the United States economy, which most economists and many Federal Reserve officials say will grow less quickly in the next six months than it did in the winter and spring.
Another increase in interest rates might also irritate the Clinton Administration, although the dollar's perilous condition could limit that irritation.
Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen said in an interview on June 15 -- before the dollar ran into serious trouble -- that given the recent signs of slower consumer spending and economic growth, "I don't see any pressure on the Fed to increase those rates."
To avoid roiling financial markets, senior Fed officials do not like to discuss publicly their plans for changing interest rates and perceptions of currency rates. But they have let their analysis of the market's conflicting signals be known privately. Keeping Tabs
Top Fed officials watch a broad 10-currency index of the dollar's value that shows the United States currency has fallen very little this year. The Fed's staff presents the index and other statistics to the Federal Reserve Board each Monday morning at a closed-door meeting on monetary policy.
The index shows the dollar's value in comparison to the currencies of Belgium, Britain, Canada, France, Italy, Germany, Japan, Sweden, Switzerland and the Netherlands. The weight of each currency in the index depends on the volume of American trade with each country. Fed officials also keep an eye on other currencies like the Mexican peso.
Even though the yen has been trading at record highs lately, the overall index is higher now than it was in 1987 and during much of the Bush Administration. The index has edged down a couple of percentage points this month -- the latest compilation of the index will be presented today.
A popular rule of thumb among monetary policy experts is that a drop of 10 percent in the trade-weighted value of the dollar produces an increase of 1 percentage point in the inflation rate, but some economists now say the effect is smaller, despite the globalization of the world economy. These economists point to the rising share of American trade with the Latin American and East Asian countries that formally or informally link their currencies to the dollar, and with the oil-producing countries that price their shipments in dollars. Global Factor Played Down
During Congressional testimony on Wednesday, Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, minimized the effect on inflation from the globalization of the economy. "At this point, we have little aggregate evidence that the increased openness of the United States economy over the past several decades has substantially altered the process of domestic price formation," he said.
But during the same hearing, Mr. Greenspan said the Federal Reserve needed to be ready to raise interest rates in response to expectations of inflation, particularly when the expectations were associated with an economy operating at or near full capacity.
Since World War II, Mr. Greenspan said, "tightened markets became increasingly associated with rising inflation expectations and burgeoning credit demands, which we were sometimes too slow to counter."
Clinton Administration officials, including Alan S. Blinder, a member of the White House Council of Economic Advisers who was approved by the Senate on Friday to become the next vice chairman of the Federal Reserve, have estimated that the economy is operating at 98.5 percent of full capacity -- or better.
The Federal Reserve directly controls only short-term rates, but changing them strongly influences the level of long-term rates. Central bankers try to hold down long-term rates to encourage investors to borrow money for long periods of time and build factories, instead of borrowing for short periods of time and speculating in markets for gold, commodities and other goods. Raising short-term rates tends to dampen inefficient short-term bank lending, economic growth and inflation, eventually pushing down long-term rates as lenders conclude that inflation will not erode their returns. Defensive Policies Abroad
To be sure, part of the recent fall in the American dollar reflects the strengthening of the German mark and Japanese yen against currencies all over the world, Wall Street economists say. By keeping interest rates fairly high, the German and Japanese central banks have guarded against inflation in their own countries but drained investment money from other countries like the United States, where slackening growth may make higher interest rates less appropriate.
During the last few years, such an international discrepancy in interest rates has often been handled through coordinated changes in rates by central banks. But Robert E. Rubin, the President's national economic adviser, said on Friday that there would not necessarily be talks on such an effort when the leaders of seven industrial democracies meet next month in Naples. Not-So-Distant Memory
The worst-case chain of events, which no policy makers were willing to discuss publicly, is that the American economy is replaying the events of 1978. As now, the United States economy was then growing faster than those of other large industrial countries and imports soared, so foreigners were being paid more dollars than they were spending for American goods. The value of the dollar began to fall, a problem worsened by an international skepticism about the Carter Administration's foreign policy, similar to the current doubts about the diplomatic skills of the Clinton Administration.
"We're close to that, and certainly the parallels will be drawn, and that will make it more difficult for Secretary Bentsen and President Clinton," said John H. Makin, an economist at the American Enterprise Institute, a research organization in Washington. "We've been over this ground in the living memory of a lot of people in the market."
The Federal Reserve chose in 1978 to raise interest rates fairly gradually, a decision that has been heavily criticized as inadequate since then by monetary economists. When inflation jumped to 11 percent in 1979, the central bank was forced to raise short-term interest rates to 20 percent to bring it under control, provoking a deep recession by 1981 and a long, slow reduction in inflation during the 1980's.
But the current situation is different in that, as Mr. Greenspan noted in his Congressional testimony on Wednesday, the amount of money and loans available in the economy is fairly tight. The money supply was expanding quickly in the late 1970's, and inflation was rising then, rather than falling as it has been during the last year.

New Fuel for the Fed's Rate Fire

Author: Bradsher, Keith

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 June 1994: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429786388?accountid=11752

Abstract: Mr. [Alan Greenspan] first cited the research in Congresssional testimony at the end of last month to argue that further reductions in inflation may be needed. "We don't have enough observations to know that it's conclusively the case," he told the Senate Banking Committee, "but I think it's becoming persuasively the case that not only is it -- which everyone agrees to -- important to bring the inflation rate down from 10 percent to 5 percent, but it's increasingly becoming evident that the lower we get under 5 percent, the more stable and growing the economy is." Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the head of President Clinton's Council of Economic Advisers, disagrees with Mr. Greenspan's argument. She said in an interview that while extremely high levels of inflation might have hurt productivity in foreign countries, inflation in the United States had never been high enough to have a demonstrable effect on productivity, even in the late 1970's. Other research has suggested that high productivity may produce low inflation, rather than the other way around, Ms. Tyson said. Mr. [Glenn D. Rudebusch] and Mr. [David W. Wilcox] tried to show this was not the case by using a statistical technique known as a Granger test. In using the test, the two economists compared each year's productivity with inflation in four previous periods and found a strong correlation. They found no correlation when they compared inflation with productivity in four previous periods.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=New+Fuel+for+the+Fed%27s+Rate+Fire&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-06-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Bradsher%2C+Keith&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan, has found another argument to justify its attempts to shove inflation ever lower with repeated increases in short-term interest rates.
Buttressing his argument with a new Federal Reserve study, he is contending that the central bank can increase the productivity of the nation's people and factories by at some point pushing consumer price inflation even lower than the 2.4 percent for the year that ended in April. The reasoning goes that lower inflation forces businesses to become more efficient because they cannot raise prices, and that when businesses are more efficient, the nation's economic output and standard of living rise.
Mr. Greenspan's fascination with the link is important not only as a defense of past interest rate increases but also as one of many signals about future policy. The research may help to explain why Mr. Greenspan has gradually become more willing than most Federal Reserve officials to raise interest rates this spring, after some initial reluctance. Findings, and Skepticism
A preliminary draft of the study contends that with a reduction of one percentage point in the inflation rate, the nation's productivity and economic growth rates both rise by two- or three-tenths of a percentage point.
But the argument does not impress Administration officials or some private economists, who are skeptical of the Federal Reserve's new claims and worry that in using higher interest rates to slow the economy and therefore push down an already low inflation rate, the Federal Reserve will excessively restrict the economy and needlessly cost the country hundreds of thousands of jobs.
Mr. Greenspan first cited the research in Congresssional testimony at the end of last month to argue that further reductions in inflation may be needed. "We don't have enough observations to know that it's conclusively the case," he told the Senate Banking Committee, "but I think it's becoming persuasively the case that not only is it -- which everyone agrees to -- important to bring the inflation rate down from 10 percent to 5 percent, but it's increasingly becoming evident that the lower we get under 5 percent, the more stable and growing the economy is."
Mr. Greenspan has previously indicated a desire to reduce inflation to 1 or 2 percent, as measured by the Consumer Price Index. The productivity research is the latest of many justifications that the central bank has offered over the years for raising interest rates. In effect, the central bank is arguing that short-term economic pain may be necessary for long-term economic gain.
The new Federal Reserve study found that low inflation has gone hand in hand with high productivity, and high inflation with low productivity, during the last 40 years in the United States. The same correlation was found in similar periods in Britain, Canada, France, Germany, Italy and, to a lesser extent, Japan.
The study also used a much-debated statistical device to make the case that it was low inflation that raised productivity, rather than high productivity causing low inflation. Finally, the study's authors, two Federal Reserve economists, attempted to adjust for the booms and busts in the business cycle, although they acknowledged that their success could be questioned.
Instead of using consumer prices, the new Federal Reserve study uses a different inflation measure -- prices charged by businesses for all goods -- that produces slightly lower numbers and offers a broader comparison to business productivity. The study excluded prices and productivity on farms and in government agencies.
Laura D'Andrea Tyson, the head of President Clinton's Council of Economic Advisers, disagrees with Mr. Greenspan's argument. She said in an interview that while extremely high levels of inflation might have hurt productivity in foreign countries, inflation in the United States had never been high enough to have a demonstrable effect on productivity, even in the late 1970's.
"I don't think it has been well documented at lower levels," she said. Link or No Link?
Barry P. Bosworth, an economist at the Brookings Institution, said earlier academic research had failed to document any clear link to productivity for inflation rates below 20 percent. He suggested that in arguing for the existence of a link at much lower levels, Mr. Greenspan might be looking for a politically palatable explanation for the central bank's interest-rate increases this spring.
"I think it's a bit of throwing everything at the fan and seeing what sticks," Mr. Bosworth said. "He wants to make the argument for the benefits of low rates of inflation."
But Stanley Fischer, an economics professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who will soon become the deputy managing director of the International Monetary Fund, said that his own research in the last two years suggested that Mr. Greenspan might be right, at least for inflation rates of 3 percent or more.
The new Federal Reserve research will be discussed at length on Thursday during a Bank of England conference on monetary and financial policy, he said.
Federal Reserve officials initially expressed reluctance to share the new study even after Mr. Greenspan had mentioned it in his testimony, but they relented last weekend.
Economists at the Federal Reserve said Mr. Greenspan had taken a strong personal interest in the research. Other central bank officials were largely unaware of the work until Mr. Greenspan mentioned a link between inflation and productivity.
"I was taken by that comment myself -- it has some intuitive appeal but I have not seen any of the data," said Robert P. Forrestal, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Atlanta. Computer Modeling
The study consists of highly esoteric statistical calculations and computer modeling, rather than any field research involving interviews with companies or workers. The study also does not address why low inflation may cause higher productivity.
It suggested that temporary shrinkages in the economy might be needed to curb inflation and increase productivity. "Even if a one-percentage-point reduction in inflation boosts the trend growth of productivity (and hence output) only 0.2 percentage point, then an initial sacrifice of, say, two and a half percentage points of G.D.P. is more than recovered within five years," the draft says. The arithmetic is true because of a compounding effect.
Two Federal Reserve economists, Glenn D. Rudebusch and David W. Wilcox, wrote the paper.
Susan M. Phillips, one of the five sitting governors of the Federal Reserve Board, said on Tuesday that when inflation was low, businesses were more willing to judge long-term investments on their own merits and tended to make more efficient decisions, adding to productivity. When inflation is high, she said, businesses tend to time investments to minimize the erosion of returns by inflation.
But other research has suggested that high productivity may produce low inflation, rather than the other way around, Ms. Tyson said. Mr. Rudebusch and Mr. Wilcox tried to show this was not the case by using a statistical technique known as a Granger test. In using the test, the two economists compared each year's productivity with inflation in four previous periods and found a strong correlation. They found no correlation when they compared inflation with productivity in four previous periods.
Some economists have criticized the Granger test over the years as unreliable for examining indicators like productivity that tend to rise and fall with the health of the overall economy.

Chart "Prices and Productivity" shows statistics for the years since 1954 suggesting a link between inflation and productivity. (Source: New York Times analysis of Federal Reserve figures)

The American Economy, Back on Top

Author: Nasar, Sylvia

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 Feb 1994: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429442453?accountid=11752

Abstract: Indeed, no sooner did "The Reckoning" -- the book by David Halberstam that focused on Ford and Nissan to tell "the story of Japan's ascent and America's subtle industrial decline" -- appear in 1985 than did Ford begin its comeback and Nissan begin to falter. Ford's market share has since climbed to 25.5 percent of the American market from 21.3 percent, while Nissan, with 5.0 percent today, never improved on the 5.3 percent it held in the mid-1980's. "More Americans are familiar with these technologies and are using them in everyday life," Mr. [Bruce Steinberg] said. "And more American businesses have learned to use the technology to improve productivity." The Reasons Economic 'Vices' Are Also Virtues So America's back in the lead. But that's no excuse to relax. "Things are going to get different again," said Mr. [Michael Hammer]. "People think the hard work is over, but hard work is going to be a permanent condition."

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=The+American+Economy%2C+Back+on+Top&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-02-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Nasar%2C+Sylvia&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: DURING the Presidential campaign, Bill Clinton often fretted that the United States economy was fast becoming the world's also-ran. But more and more it looks as if he inherited a winner.
A 3 percent economic growth rate, a gain of two million new jobs in the past year and an inflation rate reminiscent of the 1960's make America the envy of the industrialized world. The amount the average American worker can produce, already the highest in the world, is growing faster than in other wealthy countries, including Japan. The United States has become the world's low-cost provider of many sophisticated products and services, from plastics to software to financial services. And after years of decline, the nation's share of the world export market has been rising.
For the most part, these advantages will continue even after countries like Japan and Germany snap out of their recessions. It is the United States, not Japan, that is the master of the next generation of commercially important computer and communications technologies and also of leading-edge services from medicine to movie making. And American managers are not only investing heavily in new equipment, they are also much farther along than those in Europe and Japan in streamlining and re-engineering their companies to make them more competitive. That's one reason that American industries that recently were losers -- like automobiles, machine tools, steel and computer chips -- are back with a vengeance.
The turnaround reflects more than 10 years of sometimes innovative and often wrenching change, driven by recession, deregulation, tougher foreign competition, the threat of takeovers, and, not least, new technology.
The image of the United States as an economic winner, once associated only with apologists for the Reagan era, is catching on. The French newspaper Le Monde and the Japanese newspaper Yomiuri Shimbun have each run long series on the new American competitive threat. Foreign leaders and executives who once lectured Americans on their shortcomings now speak enviously of this country's cleverness in churning out everything from jobs to Jeeps.
While such sentiments may seem more common abroad, there are signs that America's self-esteem is recovering. Some polls show that Americans once again see the United States as an economic giant. Rush Limbaugh, who likes to brag about America's prowess, is one of the most popular radio talk show hosts. By contrast, Bruce Springsteen, the balladeer of dying American factory towns, hasn't had a hit in a couple of years.
"Image and reality have both undergone a remarkable change in the last few years," said Michael Hammer, a business consultant and co-author of the best-seller "Re-engineering the Corporation." "American business -- not just manufacturing -- is again perceived as the world leader."
David Rolley, an international economist at the forecasting firm DRI/McGraw Hill, added: "Everyone thought the U.S. was a basket case, but others turned out to be bigger basket cases. Germany has too many good things, too many vacations. Japan is too much like I.B.M., such a successful model that nobody figured out what to do when it exhausted the model."
None of this is an excuse for giddiness. The gains have come at a high price. Even as the economy created new jobs, tens of millions of people lost their old ones and were forced to pull up stakes, take pay cuts, carve out new careers. The United States is still second best (or third or fourth) in many pursuits. And tomorrow's stars, from Malaysia to Mexico, pose growing challenges for many an American business.
Also, the recovery here and the recessions abroad are bound to highlight American strengths and foreign weaknesses. America's turnaround may look better than it is because reports of the nation's demise were exaggerated to begin with. And, as always, what looks like a lot of improvement to one person looks like too little to another.
Finally, the benefits of economic leadership are mostly psychic. The struggle to make the economic pie bigger and insure that all citizens get a slice is largely a race against oneself, not against other countries. And being the economic leader is no cure for some of the most serious problems American society faces, including crime, inadequate education, the growing isolation of the underclass, the rampant economic insecurity that many middle-class Americans feel, and, for that matter, the malaise that can come with working so hard to do better.
But the changing perception of America is already subtly influencing the way we think. Infatuation with the Japanese or German model of capitalism is becoming less fashionable, and the massive government meddling advocated by so many critics to reverse the nation's supposed decline is less appealing to a lot of people.
For example, despite promises of more government help for so-called strategic industries, the Clinton Administration has not budgeted much money for such an industrial policy. And Washington will probably not lurch toward European-style "managed trade," through which the Government sets targets for how much companies should buy from, and sell to, foreigners -- recent threats to limit Japan's access to the American market notwithstanding. And almost no one expects the big worker-retraining programs proposed during the Presidential campaign to materialize.
In fact, interest is growing in less costly ways to let people roll with the punches -- from getting welfare mothers into jobs to making it easier for workers to change jobs without losing pensions or health coverage.
The climate could still change. A trade war with Japan, while unlikely, is nevertheless a possibility. The Federal Reserve Board could turn a blind eye as the inflation genie slips out of the bottle. There's always the possibility that health care reform, if it happens, will add costs to businesses and therefore stifle job creation. Efforts to protect the people who have suffered in the economic transformation could backfire -- as they have in Europe -- if they take the form of protectionism or laws that make it harder for businesses to shrink payrolls or get out of unprofitable businesses.
But many people, including the Fed chairman, Alan Greenspan, think that the many recent positive signs, especially the rebound in productivity growth, signal that the United States economy will do even better in the 1990's than in the 1980's. That means, they argue, that more Americans will share in the economy's gains. The Evidence Glowing Numbers And More to Come
Some of the evidence of America's impressive economic performance has been around for years but ignored. Most of it is fairly new.
Start with the most basic yardstick of economic health, the growth of productivity, or output per worker. Productivity determines how fast living standards can rise.
The American worker is the most productive in the world by far, producing on average $49,600 in goods and services a year in 1990 -- $5,000 more than German workers, $10,000 more than Japanese counterparts. Moreover, while productivity was growing more quickly abroad than in the United States for most of the 20th century, America has lately been gaining more quickly, as companies here figure out how to provide more and better products and services with new technologies and fewer workers.
Since the start of the economic recovery nearly three years ago, United States productivity has been growing at an annual rate of 2.5 percent, more than twice as fast as the average between 1970 and 1990, and markedly better than elsewhere.
The United States lead is widest in services. Retailing is twice as productive in the United States as in Japan, where laws protect the shopkeeper from the discount chain. The deregulated American telecommunications industry is at least twice as productive as Germany's government monopoly. The fragmented American banking industry is a third more efficient than Germany's banking oligopolies. The American edge in these industries has recently been expanding, not shrinking.
Gains on the factory floor have lately been even more stellar than for the economy over all: Hourly output has been rising nearly 5 percent a year for three years even as it has flattened or fallen in Germany and Japan. The United States lead in manufacturing, which had been shrinking as Germany and Japan caught up or moved ahead in some industries, has lately been widening again.
"You're seeing today the results of actions undertaken in the second half of the 1980's," said Stanley Gault, the chief executive who helped revive the Goodyear Tire and Rubber Company and Rubbermaid Inc.
THERE are at least two reasons for thinking that the American productivity lead won't evaporate when Germany and Japan recover from their recessions. One is that investment, especially in computers, is booming. The other, more important, reason is that corporate America started the painful task of learning how to make more, more quickly and with fewer resources, earlier than many of its overseas counterparts, especially in Europe.
In a recent Harvard Business Review article, Shintaro Hori, a director of the consulting firm Bain & Company Japan, estimated that Japanese companies will have to shed 15 to 20 percent of their white-collar work forces to match the low overhead in America.
"There are far fewer re-engineering efforts under way in Japanese and German companies compared with the United States," said Mr. Hammer, the consultant, whose firm has trained thousands of American managers to overhaul their companies in the last few years but few from foreign corporations.
Hard evidence is the reward American companies are reaping: Despite the moderate pace of the economic recovery, inflation-adjusted corporate profits are close to an all-time high.
In some smokestack industries -- steel, machine tools and especially cars -- the choice was to raise productivity and quality a lot or cede the field to the competition, as American companies did in consumer electronics. American managers chose the former, lifting productivity in the auto plants, for example, by an average of 10 percent every year during the late 1980's, according to McKinsey & Company, the consulting firm.
Indeed, no sooner did "The Reckoning" -- the book by David Halberstam that focused on Ford and Nissan to tell "the story of Japan's ascent and America's subtle industrial decline" -- appear in 1985 than did Ford begin its comeback and Nissan begin to falter. Ford's market share has since climbed to 25.5 percent of the American market from 21.3 percent, while Nissan, with 5.0 percent today, never improved on the 5.3 percent it held in the mid-1980's.
IN the first half of the 1980's, many American products were not only inferior to, but also pricier than, those offered by other nations. The grossly overvalued dollar -- which made American exports expensive and imports cheap -- was largely to blame. But so were a clutch of bad habits -- from executives' granting fat pay increases to Washington's tolerating too much inflation.
These days, by contrast, American factories ship steel to Seoul, transistors to Tokyo, cars to Cologne and Spandex to Siena (for bicycle pants). America's exports have been rising nearly 9 percent a year since 1985, even faster than Japan's 6.6 percent and Germany's 4.2 percent. Even more telling, the United States' share of exports by industrialized countries, which shrank to nearly 10 percent a decade ago, close to a post-war low, has rebounded to its 1980 level, around 14 percent.
Small wonder. Thanks to the weaker dollar, more moderate wage settlements and gains in efficiency, American production costs have been rising more slowly than those in Europe and Japan: 1.1 percent a year since 1985, compared with 2.5 percent in Europe and 1.7 percent in Japan. BMW of North America Inc. and Mercedes-Benz of North America Inc. are building their new plants in the American South. And, to hold down the price of their new models, Japanese car makers with plants in America are resorting to a strategy considered unthinkable just a few years ago: raising as much as possible the content of locally procured parts, as Honda said it would do this year.
Contrast this with Germany's cost problem. At current exchange rates German manufacturing workers are paid about $26 an hour, $10 more than American and Japanese factory hands. The problem is so serious that Chancellor Helmut Kohl devoted the German equivalent of the State of the Union message last October to the problem of German competitiveness.
"It seems as if there is still nothing more important than thinking about how we can expand our recreation time," he scolded. "If we want to secure Germany's future, we cannot organize our country as if it were one big recreation park."
Making things less expensively isn't enough to captivate a world full of ever more affluent, increasingly sophisticated consumers. That requires a prodigious stream of new ideas and invention. A case in point is the converging world of computing, telecommunications and television.
"We're in the midst of the third industrial revolution," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist at Merrill Lynch & Company, "and the United States is leading it."
The European technology industry is in deep trouble, its stagnant revenue, lack of new products and need for billions in government subsidies evident even before the recession. Japan has been a leader in consumer electronics. But, as its retreat in high-definition television shows, that country is still mainly the master of mass-produced components -- flat panel displays, memory chips, fax machines.
Six years ago, the fear was that Japanese dominance would spread to leading-edge technologies. But Japanese electronics giants like the Hitachi Zosen Corporation, the NEC Corporation and the Toshiba Corporation haven't been able to keep up with changing technology and American companies like the Intel Corporation and the Microsoft Corporation are in the lead in virtually all the key ingredients of the business- and personal-computer revolution: software, workstations, laser printers, computer networks and the computers-on-a-chip known as microprocessors.
"Everything important gets invented here," said Charles Ferguson, a Cambridge, Mass., technology consultant who a few years ago was warning darkly of the Japanese threat. The growing demand for sophisticated products will probably play to the strengths of American producers. So might the fact that American consumers are already more familiar with new technology than people abroad. Compared with Japan, for example, the United States has more than three times as many personal computers per worker.
"More Americans are familiar with these technologies and are using them in everyday life," Mr. Steinberg said. "And more American businesses have learned to use the technology to improve productivity." The Reasons Economic 'Vices' Are Also Virtues
Are there reasons for the American comeback other than inventiveness, streamlining, re-engineering, the adaptability of workers and managers, lower inflation and interest rates, and a weaker dollar?
Yes, indeed. Often overlooked is that some of this nation's supposed vices -- from a reluctance to protect and regulate industries, to an obsession with short-term profits -- have turned out to be virtues as well. And the much-praised features of Japanese and German capitalism -- from managed trade to lifetime employment -- have proved to have drawbacks.
The fact that American companies are regularly exposed to the toughest competitors on their home turf is a blessing as well as a curse. Detroit was forced to compete head to head with Japanese cars. European auto makers enjoyed far more protection from Japan. For a while, the jobs of tens of thousands of European auto workers and their bosses were safe, but now a huge downsizing is all but inevitable.
Lighter regulation in the United States than in other nations proved bracing for many American service companies because it encouraged competition. American shopkeepers cannot fall back on the kind of zoning laws that shield mom-and-pop stores in Japan from certain death by would-be Wal-Marts. A.T.& T. has been at the forefront of communications technology in large part because of challenges from the likes of MCI Communications Inc. and the Sprint Corporation. United Airlines gives passengers a better price because Southwest Airlines does. Contrast the American Telephone and Telegraph Company and United with their German counterparts, which remain carefully guarded state monopolies, though things are beginning to change.
Even the focus by investors and bankers on last quarter's bottom line may turn out to be less of a handicap than some argue. Impatient institutional investors blew the whistle when profits shrank, as did bank lenders without the cozy ties to corporate boards that exist in countries like Germany and Japan.
And the deep pain of layoffs has produced important gains. To be sure, the dislocation caused by layoffs in one American industry after another since the early 1980's is staggering. In the past decade, estimates Steven Davis, a labor economist at the University of Chicago, 1 in 10 manufacturing jobs -- two million -- vanished every year, even though the net decline in factory jobs over the decade was only about 1 million.
But it is becoming clear that the ability to move workers quickly from one industry to another also has benefits to the economy -- in the form of increased productivity that is essential to raising the standard of living.
For all the layoffs, the fact is that the number of new jobs has far outstripped the number of jobs lost in recent years. The United States economy has added 18 million jobs in the past decade, a net gain of more than 20 percent, including 2 million in the past year. And the unemployment rate has dropped about 1.5 percentage points in the past 18 months, to just over 6 percent. The economies of the European Community, which combined are the same size as the American economy, have added just 7 million jobs in 10 years, a net increase of 7 percent, and have grappled with unemployment rates, now in the double digits, that haven't fallen below 8 percent in a decade. The Dissent But What About That Trade Deficit?
Perhaps the most compelling counterargument to the notion that America is doing so well is this: Imports are rising three times faster than exports and the trade deficit, which was shrinking during the late 1980's, is swelling. "How can Japan pay wages that are higher, have productivity that is lower and still sell us $60 billion more than they buy?" asked Lester Thurow, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who is much in favor at the White House by dint of his book "Head to Head: The Coming Battle Among Japan, Europe and America."
But the trade deficit is not a particularly reliable yardstick of the health of an economy for two reasons. The first is that how much countries buy abroad depends on their income as well as the attractiveness of the foreign products, and America's income (in short, its gross domestic product) is rising more rapidly than that of most other nations.
The more subtle reason is that critics of American competitiveness have a habit of focusing on trade figures that capture only goods that are traded internationally, ignoring the service sector. Yet America's economic performance depends far more on service industries, which now employ three out of four American workers.
So America's back in the lead. But that's no excuse to relax. "Things are going to get different again," said Mr. Hammer. "People think the hard work is over, but hard work is going to be a permanent condition."

The American Economy, Back on Top

Full text: DURING the Presidential campaign, Bill Clinton often fretted that the United States economy was fast becoming the world's also-ran. But more and more it looks as if he inherited a winner.
A 3 percent economic growth rate, a gain of two million new jobs in the past year and an inflation rate reminiscent of the 1960's make America the envy of the industrialized world. The amount the average American worker can produce, already the highest in the world, is growing faster than in other wealthy countries, including Japan. The United States has become the world's low-cost provider of many sophisticated products and services, from plastics to software to financial services. And after years of decline, the nation's share of the world export market has been rising.
For the most part, these advantages will continue even after countries like Japan and Germany snap out of their recessions. It is the United States, not Japan, that is the master of the next generation of commercially important computer and communications technologies and also of leading-edge services from medicine to movie making. And American managers are not only investing heavily in new equipment, they are also much farther along than those in Europe and Japan in streamlining and re-engineering their companies to make them more competitive. That's one reason that American industries that recently were losers -- like automobiles, machine tools, steel and computer chips -- are back with a vengeance.
The turnaround reflects more than 10 years of sometimes innovative and often wrenching change, driven by recession, deregulation, tougher foreign competition, the threat of takeovers, and, not least, new technology.
The image of the United States as an economic winner, once associated only with apologists for the Reagan era, is catching on. The French newspaper Le Monde and the Japanese newspaper Yomiuri Shimbun have each run long series on the new American competitive threat. Foreign leaders and executives who once lectured Americans on their shortcomings now speak enviously of this country's cleverness in churning out everything from jobs to Jeeps.
While such sentiments may seem more common abroad, there are signs that America's self-esteem is recovering. Some polls show that Americans once again see the United States as an economic giant. Rush Limbaugh, who likes to brag about America's prowess, is one of the most popular radio talk show hosts. By contrast, Bruce Springsteen, the balladeer of dying American factory towns, hasn't had a hit in a couple of years.
"Image and reality have both undergone a remarkable change in the last few years," said Michael Hammer, a business consultant and co-author of the best-seller "Re-engineering the Corporation." "American business -- not just manufacturing -- is again perceived as the world leader."
David Rolley, an international economist at the forecasting firm DRI/McGraw Hill, added: "Everyone thought the U.S. was a basket case, but others turned out to be bigger basket cases. Germany has too many good things, too many vacations. Japan is too much like I.B.M., such a successful model that nobody figured out what to do when it exhausted the model."
None of this is an excuse for giddiness. The gains have come at a high price. Even as the economy created new jobs, tens of millions of people lost their old ones and were forced to pull up stakes, take pay cuts, carve out new careers. The United States is still second best (or third or fourth) in many pursuits. And tomorrow's stars, from Malaysia to Mexico, pose growing challenges for many an American business.
Also, the recovery here and the recessions abroad are bound to highlight American strengths and foreign weaknesses. America's turnaround may look better than it is because reports of the nation's demise were exaggerated to begin with. And, as always, what looks like a lot of improvement to one person looks like too little to another.
Finally, the benefits of economic leadership are mostly psychic. The struggle to make the economic pie bigger and insure that all citizens get a slice is largely a race against oneself, not against other countries. And being the economic leader is no cure for some of the most serious problems American society faces, including crime, inadequate education, the growing isolation of the underclass, the rampant economic insecurity that many middle-class Americans feel, and, for that matter, the malaise that can come with working so hard to do better.
But the changing perception of America is already subtly influencing the way we think. Infatuation with the Japanese or German model of capitalism is becoming less fashionable, and the massive government meddling advocated by so many critics to reverse the nation's supposed decline is less appealing to a lot of people.
For example, despite promises of more government help for so-called strategic industries, the Clinton Administration has not budgeted much money for such an industrial policy. And Washington will probably not lurch toward European-style "managed trade," through which the Government sets targets for how much companies should buy from, and sell to, foreigners -- recent threats to limit Japan's access to the American market notwithstanding. And almost no one expects the big worker-retraining programs proposed during the Presidential campaign to materialize.
In fact, interest is growing in less costly ways to let people roll with the punches -- from getting welfare mothers into jobs to making it easier for workers to change jobs without losing pensions or health coverage.
The climate could still change. A trade war with Japan, while unlikely, is nevertheless a possibility. The Federal Reserve Board could turn a blind eye as the inflation genie slips out of the bottle. There's always the possibility that health care reform, if it happens, will add costs to businesses and therefore stifle job creation. Efforts to protect the people who have suffered in the economic transformation could backfire -- as they have in Europe -- if they take the form of protectionism or laws that make it harder for businesses to shrink payrolls or get out of unprofitable businesses.
But many people, including the Fed chairman, Alan Greenspan, think that the many recent positive signs, especially the rebound in productivity growth, signal that the United States economy will do even better in the 1990's than in the 1980's. That means, they argue, that more Americans will share in the economy's gains. The Evidence Glowing Numbers And More to Come
Some of the evidence of America's impressive economic performance has been around for years but ignored. Most of it is fairly new.
Start with the most basic yardstick of economic health, the growth of productivity, or output per worker. Productivity determines how fast living standards can rise.
The American worker is the most productive in the world by far, producing on average $49,600 in goods and services a year in 1990 -- $5,000 more than German workers, $10,000 more than Japanese counterparts. Since the start of the economic recovery nearly three years ago, United States productivity has been growing at an annual rate of 2.5 percent, more than twice as fast as the average between 1970 and 1990, and markedly better than elsewhere.
The United States lead is widest in services. Retailing is twice as productive in the United States as in Japan, where laws protect the shopkeeper from the discount chain. Gains on the factory floor have lately been even more stellar than for the economy over all: Hourly output has been rising nearly 5 percent a year for three years even as it has flattened or fallen in Germany and Japan. Are there reasons for the American comeback other than inventiveness, streamlining, re-engineering, the adaptability of workers and managers, lower inflation and interest rates, and a weaker dollar?

And the much-praised features of Japanese and German capitalism -- from managed trade to lifetime employment -- have proved to have drawbacks.

The Dissent But What About That Trade Deficit?
Perhaps the most compelling counterargument to the notion that America is doing so well is this: Imports are rising three times faster than exports and the trade deficit, which was shrinking during the late 1980's, is swelling. "How can Japan pay wages that are higher, have productivity that is lower and still sell us $60 billion more than they buy?" asked Lester Thurow, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology who is much in favor at the White House by dint of his book "Head to Head: The Coming Battle Among Japan, Europe and America."

The first is that how much countries buy abroad depends on their income as well as the attractiveness of the foreign products, and America's income (in short, its gross domestic product) is rising more rapidly than that of most other nations.

Yet America's economic performance depends far more on service industries, which now employ three out of four American workers.
So America's back in the lead. But that's no excuse to relax. "Things are going to get different again," said Mr. Hammer. "People think the hard work is over, but hard work is going to be a permanent condition."
Correction: March 6, 1994, Sunday
An article last Sunday about the American economy incorrectly characterized the popularity of Bruce Springsteen. Mr. Springsteen has had several successful recordings in recent years, including his current song "Streets of Philadelphia."

Group of 7 Says Russia Must Do More to Get Aid

Author: Whitney, Craig R

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 Feb 1994: A.12.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429438822?accountid=11752

Abstract: "We urged them to strengthen their efforts to bring inflation under control," Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen said. "We told the Russians that a strong stabilization program is needed to trigger more support from the International Monetary Fund." "We have continued to urge Japan to live up to its commitment to put in place a substantial and effective program to strengthen domestic demand, to reduce its large trade surplus, to open its markets," Mr. Bentsen said. "That is in the interest of all of Japan's trading partners and in the interest of Japan." Pressed on speculation that the United States would try to encourage the continued increase in the exchange-rate value of the Japanese yen to make Japanese products more expensive on United States and world markets, Mr. Bentsen refused to be drawn. "We believe in the fundamentals dictating exchange rates," he said.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Group+of+7+Says+Russia+Must+Do+More+to+Get+Aid&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1994-02-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.12&amp;au=Whitney%2C+Craig+R&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
Finance officials of the seven most powerful industrial nations agreed today that economic aid to Russia could continue as soon as it began credible measures to control inflation and budget deficits, but that Moscow still had much to do, Western officials said.
"We urged them to strengthen their efforts to bring inflation under control," Treasury Secretary Lloyd Bentsen said. "We told the Russians that a strong stabilization program is needed to trigger more support from the International Monetary Fund."
Finance Minister Theo Waigel of Germany said the Western countries had agreed to work with the Russian authorities on measures to help the population cope with the consequences of economic change -- unemployment from bankrupt state industries as they are sold off or closed down, for instance. But he added that the Russians would have to come up with most of the money for such measures themselves.
Two senior Russian officials, Finance Minister Sergei K. Dubinin and Economics Minister Aleksandr N. Shokhin, said they had pledged to bring monthly inflation down from 22 percent to 7 to 9 percent by the end of this year. I.M.F. Talks Expected
Mr. Dubinin also said he expected discussions to resume by mid-March between Moscow and the Monetary Fund about a second $1.5 billion loan to help Russia make the transition to a market economy.
Echoing the caution expressed by European officials at the meeting here, Mr. Bentsen said: "I hope they can make that. It will be a difficult target."
For the American delegation here, trade friction between the United States and Japan came close to overshadowing concern about Russia.
"We have continued to urge Japan to live up to its commitment to put in place a substantial and effective program to strengthen domestic demand, to reduce its large trade surplus, to open its markets," Mr. Bentsen said. "That is in the interest of all of Japan's trading partners and in the interest of Japan."
But a senior Treasury official said that "there was no breakthrough of any kind" before the Japanese left this evening. Bentsen Wary on Yen
Pressed on speculation that the United States would try to encourage the continued increase in the exchange-rate value of the Japanese yen to make Japanese products more expensive on United States and world markets, Mr. Bentsen refused to be drawn. "We believe in the fundamentals dictating exchange rates," he said.
Finance ministers and central bank chiefs of the United States, Germany, Japan, Britain, France, Italy, and Canada , known as the Group of Seven, met in a resort hotel in the hills north of Frankfurt that was built as a castle for the Queen of Prussia. The meeting followed a week of turmoil on international financial markets that has seen long-term interest rates rise steeply and the Japanese yen strengthen considerably against the dollar.
Some of the turmoil was caused by fears of inflation because of unexpectedly strong economic growth in the American economy while most of Europe except for Britain is still mired deep in recession, and some by tensions between the United States and Japan on trade issues.
Mr. Bentsen met his Japanese counterpart, Hirohisa Fujii, in the hotel this morning before the broader discussions began, but he made clear beforehand that he expected no breakthrough.
Originally, Mr. Waigel of Germany convened the gathering so representatives from the seven nations could hear reports from Russian officials about what would happen to plans by President Boris N. Yeltsin to move toward a market economy after the political backlash in last December's elections. Little Money Delivered
At their summit meeting in Japan last summer, leaders of the Group of Seven promised $28 billion to Russia in grants and loans, mainly through the International Monetary Fund and other international institutions, but made the assistance conditional on Russian steps to bring inflation and government deficit spending under control. Only a small amount of money has actually been delivered.
The fund had provided Russia with $2.5 billion in loans since 1992, but has held back on an additional $1.5 billion as Mr. Yeltsin's commitment to change has seemed to waver.
Before today's meeting, officials made clear that no additional Western assistance was under consideration, but that they would like to give the Russians assurances that previous commitments would be met if Moscow continued reform.
"Our response will be dictated by their behavior," a senior Treasury Department official said. No Effect From Spying Seen
American officials said they did not expect American assistance to be affected by accusations that a senior Central Intelligence Agency official had been a spy for the Soviet Union and, after its collapse, for the Russian Government.
Mr. Dubinin, Russia's Finance Minister, Mr. Shokhin, the Economics Minister, and the central bank chief, Viktor V. Gerashchenko, repeated earlier pledges by Mr. Yeltsin to keep moving ahead with privatization of state-owned industries and budget reforms, but not so fast as to suddenly throw millions of Russians out of work or into poverty.
Fears that change was proceeding too fast are thought to be the main reason for the strong showing of ultranationalist opponents of Mr. Yeltsin in the December parliamentary elections, when they won around a quarter of the total vote.
The latest Russian budget target is to reduce the deficit this year to about $30 billion, but only with Western aid and agreement to roll over much of the $80 billion foreign debt, and to reduce annual inflation to 400 percent.

Fed Affirmed Its Bias Toward Higher Rates

Author: Greenhouse, Steven

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 Aug 1993: 1.35.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429197760?accountid=11752

Abstract: As Federal Reserve officials and private economists gathered in this Rocky Mountain resort for an annual conference, the economists were divided over whether the Fed's policy-making committee had maintained the bias when it met on Tuesday. Several economists speculated that the Fed maintained its tilt toward higher rates because it was eager to convince financial markets that it wanted to keep inflation subdued. But others said they thought the Fed adopted a neutral stance because it was pleased by recent inflation reports but worried about slow growth. Federal Reserve officials refused to discuss what was decided at Tuesday's meeting. Several committee members called for dropping the bias toward raising rates, suggesting that a neutral policy was more consistent with the weak growth and the subdued inflation being reported. But those members ended up voting with the majority after being convinced that if the committee abandoned that bias so soon after adopting it in May it "could convey a misleading impression" -- in the report's words -- that the Fed had become less committed to fighting inflation.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Fed+Affirmed+Its+Bias+Toward+Higher+Rates&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-08-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1.35&amp;au=Greenhouse%2C+Steven&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The Federal Reserve's main policy-making committee voted in July to maintain its bias toward raising short-term interest rates, even though some members urged it to drop that bias because economic growth was so weak.
As Federal Reserve officials and private economists gathered in this Rocky Mountain resort for an annual conference, the economists were divided over whether the Fed's policy-making committee had maintained the bias when it met on Tuesday.
The Fed panel meets eight times a year but its deliberations are not disclosed until after the next meeting.
Several economists speculated that the Fed maintained its tilt toward higher rates because it was eager to convince financial markets that it wanted to keep inflation subdued. But others said they thought the Fed adopted a neutral stance because it was pleased by recent inflation reports but worried about slow growth. Federal Reserve officials refused to discuss what was decided at Tuesday's meeting.
According to a summary that the Federal Reserve released today of the July 6-7 meeting, the policy-making committee, known as the Federal Open Market Committee, voted 11 to 1 to continue its bias toward tighter rates. The action gives the Fed chairman, Alan Greenspan, the authority to raise rates somewhat without seeking board approval, although he usually consults board members before acting
At the July meeting, Wayne D. Angell, a member of the Federal Reserve Board, cast the dissenting vote, arguing that the central bank should raise interest rates immediately. He said that there was a disappointing lack of progress in reducing inflation and that monetary policy was overly expansive because the central bank's short-term interest rates, currently 3 percent, were less than the inflation rate.
Several committee members called for dropping the bias toward raising rates, suggesting that a neutral policy was more consistent with the weak growth and the subdued inflation being reported. But those members ended up voting with the majority after being convinced that if the committee abandoned that bias so soon after adopting it in May it "could convey a misleading impression" -- in the report's words -- that the Fed had become less committed to fighting inflation.
The central bank adopted the bias toward raising rates in May, when its members grew upset that inflation was running at an annual rate of more than 4 percent in the first four months of this year.
According to the summary of the July meeting, many Fed officials felt that the statistics pointed to a sustained rate of economic expansion -- a sentiment that may have been too optimistic because in late July the Commerce Department announced that the economy grew by a lackluster 1.6 percent in the second quarter.
The report of the July meeting said, "While current monetary policy seemed likely to support further economic expansion, the committee needed to remain alert to the potential for intensifying inflation."
Some members said that while the need for any change in policy seemed somewhat remote in coming months, the next policy move was more likely to be toward tightening rather than toward easing.
At the July meeting, the policy-making committee decided to put less stock in trying to meet certain targets for growth in the money supply. The members noted that the relationship between economic growth and growth in M-2 -- a broad definition of the money supply that includes cash in circulation and deposits in checking and savings accounts -- had largely broken down. A certain amount of growth in M-2 was no longer producing the expected rate of overall economic growth.
The committee considered, but rejected, relying on an even broader measure of the money supply: M-2 plus bond and stock mutual funds. The central bank concluded that that measure would be unreliable.
Federal Reserve officials said one reason M-2 had became such an unreliable yardstick was that millions of Americans had moved their money from savings and checking accounts, which are considered part of the money supply, into bond and stock mutual funds, which are not considered part of the money supply.
