Brazil Opens Its Borders to Goods From Abroad

Author: Brooke, James

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]16 Aug 1993: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429208701?accountid=11752

Abstract: "Brazil's gross domestic product is twice that of Mexico's, so the potential is enormous," said Richard R. Ades, director of the United States Department of Commerce's trade office here. Brazil "is becoming increasingly attractive to United States companies," he added, with inquiries by United States exporters up by an estimated 40 percent this year over last. Despite this tradition, two Government water pollution control projects, one in Rio de Janeiro and one in Sao Paulo, are expected to provide some of the greatest opportunities for American export companies in Brazil in the 1990's. The Sao Paulo project, a $4 billion effort to clean up the Tiete River, was described this week as Latin America's largest pollution control project by the Sao Paulo state governor, Luiz Antonio Fleury. 'Excellent Chances' "Opening a Market" shows Brazil's average import tariffs (Source: Brazil's Ministry of Industry, Trade and Tourism)

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Brazil+Opens+Its+Borders+to+Goods+From+Abroad&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-08-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Brooke%2C+James&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
"America is Here" proclaims a banner beckoning customers to Radio Shack's new stores here and in Rio de Janeiro, emporiums stocked with goods for which Brazilians previously journeyed to Miami: cellular telephones, laser printers and laptop computers.
"Business is so good that we will open a second store by the end of the year," said Djalma Luiz Crispim, who planted Radio Shack's flag in Sao Paulo, South America's largest city, three months ago by opening a store in Morumbi Shopping Center.
Brazil -- Latin America's largest economy -- is suddenly dismantling import barriers dating back to the 1960's. On Tuesday, for example, Brazilian journalists are to gather here to test-drive the Explorer, a luxury sport-utility vehicle imported from the United States by the Ford division of Autolatina Brasil S.A. The introduction of the Explorer will be the first large-scale promotion of an American car since Brazil banned almost all car imports in 1974.
For the United States, traditionally Brazil's largest trading partner, the opening of Brazil's $450 billion economy is good news.
"Brazil's gross domestic product is twice that of Mexico's, so the potential is enormous," said Richard R. Ades, director of the United States Department of Commerce's trade office here. Brazil "is becoming increasingly attractive to United States companies," he added, with inquiries by United States exporters up by an estimated 40 percent this year over last.
For decades, American exporters wrote Brazil off as a closed shop.
In the 1960's and 1970's, successive Brazilian Governments sought to stimulate local industrial production by banning the import of more than 1,000 products and by imposing tariffs as high as 205 percent on other products.
But in 1990, the list of banned imports was abolished, and the average import tariff has dropped steadily. From 78 percent in 1984, it fell to 14 percent last month in a final scheduled reduction -- and duties could fall even further.
Brazilians now find that their tariff levels are near Latin America's norm. Argentina and Chile impose average external tariffs of 11 percent. Mexico's average tariff is 12 percent. In the United States, the average import duty on industrialized products is 5 percent.
"Fernando Collor was criticized for many things, but he really opened the country up," Soumo Kabakian, trade director of Flowlevel Controls, said of the Brazilian president who resigned last December under a corruption cloud. Mr. Kabakian estimated that his company's annual imports of American and Canadian industrial-control instruments would rise to $500,000 this year -- 10 times the 1989 level of $50,000. Pent-Up Demand
Importers here sense an enormous pent-up demand for imported goods despite Brazil's poor economic situation -- a long-term recession and 32 percent monthly inflation.
[ On Friday, the president of the central bank, Paulo Cesar Ximenes, resigned and was to be replaced by Pedro Malan, who will become Brazil's fourth central bank president in a year. The new foreign debt negotiator is Andre Lara Resende. ]
"Miami Mania" blares advertisements for a national electronics store chain, Photo Mania. Each store now has an imports corner, hawking imported telephones, toaster ovens, stereos, radios and microwave ovens.
Last October, a 10-year ban on imported computers was lifted, and the impact was evident last month at the stands of Fenasoft, Brazil's largest annual computer show.
"The American presence at Fenasoft must have increased this year by 50 to 70 percent," said George Zelenjuk, a director of ABC Dados Informatica S.A., a major importer here of data communications equipment.
In addition to cutting tariffs, Brazil has helped imports by improving its bureaucracy. 'Simply Dreadful'
"We had a customs that was simply dreadful -- they did everything possible to stop imports," recalled Edmundo Klotz, president of Essencial Alimentos S.A., which imports Jack Daniels whisky and Sterling wine from the United States. "But that is changing very quickly, and I am very optimistic. I am investing in new product lines."
As a result of all these sales, the nation's trade surplus with the United States has dwindled, falling to $1.8 billion in 1992 from $5 billion in 1988. During that period, American exports to Brazil grew by 35 percent, hitting $5.7 billion last year. Over all, Latin America constitutes the world's fastest growing market for American exports.
Brazil's imports from all nations hit $11.1 billion during the first half of 1993. This was the highest level on record and an 18 percent increase from the comparable period last year.
Japanese and Korean consumer goods are also entering Brazil's market, but American exporters seem to enjoy an edge based, in part, on Brazilians' long-term cultural affinity with the United States.
Every year, about a million Brazilians study English at private language academies. This year, about 400,000 Brazilians are expected to visit the United States, the most popular tourist destination for Brazilians.
Brazil's economy -- which accounts for about 40 percent of the Latin American economy -- is expected to grow by 3 percent this year; as a rule, every 1 percent of new economic growth in Latin America increases United States exports by about $5 billion, according to Peter Hakim, president of the Inter-American Dialogue, a private study group in Washington. By comparison, every 1 percent of economic growth in Japan yields only a $1 billion increase in imports of American goods.
Hidden behind the flashy new consumer goods filling shelves here, the fastest growing sector of Brazil's imports is capital goods. Brazilian companies have responded to the competition by either seeking Government protection or by investing to make their factories internationally competitive.
Textile manufacturers, auto makers, tire manufacturers and growers of rice and wheat have all demanded import quotas in recent months.
"It seems to be a lot tougher for Brazilians to get licenses to import used tires this year," said Howard J. Levy, president of Tire Source Inc. of Pompano Beach, Fla., a major used tire exporter. He attributed the change to pressure by Brazilian tire manufacturers.
So far, however, the Government has resisted calls for import quotas, and in their absence many companies are cutting prices and improving products to compete with the imports.
Brazil's car industry, dominated by local operations of Ford, Fiat, General Motors and Volkwagen, plans to invest $2 billion to modernize production lines in the 1990's. This year, the industry plans to introduce 10 new models; it normally introduces two models a year. Productivity Gains
With tariffs on imported cars now at 35 percent, down from 85 percent in 1990, car manufacturers have increased worker productivity by 46 percent since 1990, according to a study by Exame, Brazil's leading business magazine.
"Our imports are only to fill niches in our product line," said Cassio Pagliarini, marketing plan manager here for Ford. In addition to the Explorer, Ford plans to start importing Taurus cars from the United States in March. By the end of this year, General Motors do Brasil plans to start importing Calibra cars from its German factories and Lumina APV mini-vans from a factory in Tarrytown, N.Y.
Over all, car imports are expected to hit 30,000 units this year, only 3 percent of Brazil's expected domestic production of one million cars.
In the processed food sector, Brazilian companies reacted to the import threat by improving packaging and by introducing 600 products during the first half of this year -- twice as many as during all of last year.
Prices in the electronics sector have dropped by an average of 40 percent since 1989, according to a survey by Veja, a weekly news magazine, which reported that the price of a locally made Gradiente videocassette player fell to $464 from $1,125. The price of a Sharp fax machine dropped to $630 from $1,600.
But the developments -- a boon for consumers and importers -- have often meant a loss for Brazilian workers. Many of the cost savings have been achieved by importing parts and laying off workers. Over the last four years, Gradiente increased the foreign-made portion of its television from 5 percent to 80 percent, while slashing its work force to 2,700 employees from 9,000.
Although Brazil's Government is resisting calls for imposing import quotas on consumer goods, the state bureaucracy hews to a "buy national" procurement policy, according to the Report on Foreign Trade Barriers, a study issued annually by the Office of the United States Trade Representative.
Despite this tradition, two Government water pollution control projects, one in Rio de Janeiro and one in Sao Paulo, are expected to provide some of the greatest opportunities for American export companies in Brazil in the 1990's. The Sao Paulo project, a $4 billion effort to clean up the Tiete River, was described this week as Latin America's largest pollution control project by the Sao Paulo state governor, Luiz Antonio Fleury. 'Excellent Chances'
"There are excellent chances for American companies to sell water filters and pollution monitoring equipment," said Ricardo E. Rose, trade assistant at the United States Trade Office here.
Despite the gains, corruption and bureaucratic paperwork still slow sales, importers complain.
"Last January, I had to pay $1,500 to get a $25,000 shipment out of customs," said one importer, who asked not to be identified. "The customs inspector was about to be transferred to Rio, and she wanted to get some dollars together for the move."
Aware of importers' complaints, Trade Ministry officials say they plan to reduce, starting in November, the number of documents needed for importing to one, from 30.
Photograph Brazil and its $450 billion economy are becoming increasingly attractive to companies in the United States. At a Rio de Janeiro Radio Shack store, the sign at right proclaims "America is here." (John Maier Jr. for The New York Times)

U.S. Seeks Ways to Isolate Iran; Describes Leaders as Dangerous

Author: Jehl, Douglas

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 May 1993: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429077583?accountid=11752

Abstract: Secretary of State Warren Christopher hinted at the emerging shift earlier this year by denouncing Iran as an "international outlaw" and a "dangerous country" for its support of terrorism and its pursuit of nuclear weapons. But the new policy is far more sweeping than Mr. Christopher indicated when he said the United States would seek to block loans to Iran by international organizations. In March, an initial United States attempt to isolate Teheran failed when the World Bank overrode Washington's objections and approved a $165 million loan to upgrade Iran's electrical power system. But officials who outlined the new policy said the United States intended to mount an aggressive effort to persuade other countries of the dangers Iran poses. Because of high inflation and unemployment, a senior Administration official said, Iran was "more vulnerable than it has been in the past or is likely to be in the future." But he repeated Mr. [Martin S. Indyk]'s warning that "this moment will not last long." The Military Issues

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+Seeks+Ways+to+Isolate+Iran%3B+Describes+Leaders+as+Dangerous&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-05-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Jehl%2C+Douglas&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The Clinton Administration is preparing a broad new effort to weaken Iran by persuading reluctant allies to cut off loans, investment and arms sales to what American officials regard as a permanently hostile Government.
The plan, drafted as part of an intensive policy review, reflects a conclusion that Iran must be isolated if it is to be prevented from emerging as a substantial threat to Western interests. Thus, the plan rejects Reagan and Bush Administration policies that offered to reward Teheran for good behavior. Abandoning a Balance
Administration officials said the new approach aimed at denying Iran access to the money and weapons needed to complete a military resurgence. They said it was based on a decision that the United States and its allies should now treat Iran as harshly as it treats Iraq.
For much of the 1980's, the United States sought to play Iran and Iraq against one another. But with United Nations sanctions imposed on Iraq since the end of the Persian Gulf war, the Administration has concluded that the wiser policy is "dual containment."
Secretary of State Warren Christopher hinted at the emerging shift earlier this year by denouncing Iran as an "international outlaw" and a "dangerous country" for its support of terrorism and its pursuit of nuclear weapons. But the new policy is far more sweeping than Mr. Christopher indicated when he said the United States would seek to block loans to Iran by international organizations.
Administration officials say the isolation of Iran should end only if Teheran halts its support for terrorism, curtails its military buildup, stops its subversion of other governments and ends its quest for nuclear weapons. Catalogue of Accusations
Among the antagonistic activities that United States officials attribute to Iran is active support for efforts by the Hamas and Party of God organizations to use violence to disrupt the Mideast peace talks. The officials say Iran has also helped establish terrorist training camps in Lebanon and the Sudan, and has assisted groups trying to overthrow the Governments of Egypt, Algeria and Tunisia.
The United States already subjects Iran to stiff sanctions that prohibit military and most commercial ties. Administration officials said their new policy could succeed only if other countries change course.
Among the top priorities, Administration officials said, are efforts to convince Russia and China to cancel deals to provide Iran with weapons and nuclear reactors, and to persuade Japan, Germany and Britain to cut off loans.
None of those countries have shown any willingness to sever such lucrative ties, and Japan in particular has argued that commercial links can encourage moderate elements inside Iran. But while the United States once harbored similar hopes, Administration officials now regard all factions in the current leadership as bound to remain hostile toward the West.
While many deals between United States companies and Iran are already prohibited, the new policy would almost certainly lead the Administration to reject a request by the Boeing Company to sell 20 Boeing 737 jetliners to Iran, a deal worth more than $750 million, officials say.
The broad outlines of the new approach were described in a speech last week by Martin S. Indyk, the senior director for Middle East policy for the National Security Council, to the Washington Institute for Near East Policy, a research group.
Mr. Indyk said bluntly, "If we fail in our efforts to modify Iranian behavior, five years from now Iran will be much more capable of posing a real threat to Israel and to Western interests in the Middle East."
The Iran review was one of several dozen studies requested by President Clinton shortly after he took office.
But the atmosphere surrounding it has been intensified by suspicions that Iran might have had a role in the World Trade Center bombing, Government officials say. Investigators have found that tens of thousands of dollars were wired from Iran to bank accounts held by suspects in the bombing. No Help Likely for Rebels
Final details of the plan await White House approval, officials say. But while Mr. Clinton made overtures to the main Iranian opposition group before he took office, his advisers said there was no chance that the new policy would involve closer ties with the group, the People's Mujahedeen.
The Administration has concluded that the Mujahedeen's ties to Iraq and its terrorist attacks against Americans in the 1970's makes it an unacceptable partner.
The officials also expressed little optimism that the organized resistance or other domestic unrest could force a significant change in the Iranian Government. They said they expected President Hashemi Rafsanjani to win re-election to a second four-year term in elections on June 11. The Political Issues
Administration officials also said the United States had rejected seeking an embargo on sales of Iranian oil. The rebels have called for such a ban, but the policy review concluded that it could not be enforced without imposing a military blockade, a step Mr. Clinton's advisers are unwilling to take.
In outlining the effort to isolate Iran, officials acknowledged that the task would be more difficult than the containment of Iraq, which carries the weight of United Nations sanctions.
In March, an initial United States attempt to isolate Teheran failed when the World Bank overrode Washington's objections and approved a $165 million loan to upgrade Iran's electrical power system. But officials who outlined the new policy said the United States intended to mount an aggressive effort to persuade other countries of the dangers Iran poses.
Administration officials have begun to argue that Iran, which has borrowed $25 billion in the last four years, is not a good investment. Teheran has already fallen $5 billion behind on its payments, and the Administration intends to warn countries that go ahead with sales of weapons and nuclear goods that they run the risk of not being paid.
Because of high inflation and unemployment, a senior Administration official said, Iran was "more vulnerable than it has been in the past or is likely to be in the future." But he repeated Mr. Indyk's warning that "this moment will not last long." The Military Issues
The Central Intelligence Agency has warned that Iran may acquire a nuclear weapon by the end of the decade. Its deals with Russia and China for nuclear reactors are regarded with uneasiness by some American analysts, who say the plants may be being used as a cover to acquire sensitive technology.
A simultaneous Iranian buildup of conventional forces has prompted similar concern. Officials at the Defense Intelligence Agency have said Teheran's $2 billion military spending spree may be merely an effort to rebuild forces depleted during its eight-year war with Iraq. But purchases of Russian submarines and a bid to buy top-of-the-line tanks have persuaded other officials that Iran seeks to become the dominant power in the Mideast.

Excerpts From Clinton's News Conference in the Rose Garden

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]15 May 1993: 1.8.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429051772?accountid=11752

Abstract: Q. Mr. President, there's a widespread perception that you're waffling, that you can't make up your mind. One day you're saying, in a few days, we'll have a decision, we have a common approach. The next day, you're saying, we're still looking for a consensus. A. I would say that the month before last, we had virtually no inflation, and you can't run the country on a month-to-month basis. You've got to look at some longer trends. There are some clear underlying reasons for this last inflationary bulge, which don't necessarily portend long-term inflation. I think it's, of course, a cause of concern, we ought to look at it, but we ought to wait till we have some more evidence before we raise interest rates in an economy where industrial capacity is only at 80 percent. I think the American people know one thing: that I'm on their side, that I'm fighting to change things, and they're finding out it's not so easy. But we are going to get a lot of change out of this Congress if we can keep our eye on the ball and stop worrying about whether we characterize each other in some way or another and keep thinking about what's good for the American people.

Full text: Following are excerpts from President Clinton's news conference yesterday in the Rose Garden at the White House, as transcribed by Reuters. OPENING STATEMENT
Three months ago, I presented a plan to our country and to the Congress designed to address what I believe are the significant challenges of this time. For more than 40 years our country was organized to stand up against Communism, to try to help develop the free world, and for most of that time we took our economic prosperity for granted.
It is now clear that at the end of the cold war we must organize ourselves around the obligation we have to be more competitive in the global economy and to enable our people to live up to their full potential.
That means we have to do a lot of things to turn this economy around, beginning with a serious effort to reduce our national debt, to invest in jobs and new technologies, to restore fairness to our tax code and to make our political system work again.
This week I was able to go back again to the American people, to take my case into the country, and to Cleveland and Chicago and New York, and here, in Washington, there were new efforts to break the gridlock and to put the national interest above narrow interests.
The results were particularly impressive in the work done by the House Ways and Means Committee. Achieving over $250 billion in deficit reduction through spending cuts with two dollars in spending cuts for each dollar in new investment in jobs and education, the program provides significantly everything that I presented to the Congress, even though there were some changes. In fact, some of the changes I think made the bill better.
Let me reiterate them. No. 1, significant deficit reduction. No. 2, taking on entitlements issues that have for too long been left on the table. No. 3, real investments for small businesses, and for big businesses, incentives to get people to invest money in this economy to create jobs.
And perhaps most importantly, a break for working-class families, a huge increase in the earned-income tax credit for people with incomes under $30,000, to relieve them of the impact of the energy tax and to say for the first time, people who work 40 hours a week with children in the home would be lifted above poverty.
And finally, of course, the plan was very progressive, 75 percent of the revenues coming from the top 6 percent of the American taxpayers.
I also reiterated that I don't want a penny in taxes without the spending cuts, and I proposed in New York that we create a deficit-reduction trust fund into which all the taxes and all the budget cuts could be put and kept for the five-year life of this budget.
This is a very important thing. I realize some have said it is little more than a gimmick, but the truth is there is no legal protection now for the life of the budget for these funds. This will provide it, in stone, in law.
In every element of this there has been some willingness on the part of those who have supported our efforts to take on powerful vested interests in behalf of the national interest, whether it is in repealing the lobby deduction, or in going for a direct loan program for college loans that will save $4 billion, but which will remove a Government-guaranteed income from several interests who like the system as it is now.
The Congress also moved this week to reinvigorate our democratic process by ending the filibuster and passing the motor voter bill.
These are the kinds of changes that the American people expect of us. They do not expect miracles, but they expect solid, steady progress, and I am determined to stay on this course. It has been a good week, and if we are willing to take more tough decisions, there will be more good weeks for the American people ahead. QUESTIONS AND ANSWERS U.S. Role in Bosnia
Q. Mr. President, you've said that the United States will not go it alone with military action in Bosnia, and yet the European allies have refused to sign on to your proposals. If the allies refuse to follow suit, where does that leave the United States?
A. Let me reiterate what I have said because I think that the United States has taken the right position, and I think that we've gotten some good results. I have said, and I will reiterate, I think that the United States must act with our allies, especially because Bosnia is in the heart of Europe, and the Europeans are there. We must work together through the United Nations.
Secondly, I do not believe the United States has any business sending troops there to get involved in a conflict in behalf of one of the sides. I believe that we should continue to turn up the pressure, and as you know, I have taken the position that the best way to do that would be to lift the arms embargo with a standby authority of air power in the event that the present situation was interrupted by the unfair use of artillery by the Bosnian Serbs.
That position is still on the table. It has not been rejected out of hand. Indeed, some of our European allies have agreed with it and others are not prepared to go that far yet. But we have to keep the pressure up.
And I would just remind you that since we said we would become involved in the Vance-Owen peace process, two of the three parties have signed on, we've gotten enforcement of the no-fly zone through the United Nations, we've been able to airlift more humanitarian supplies there, and we've been able to keep up a very, very tough embargo on Serbia, which I think led directly, that and the pressure of further action, to the statement that Mr. Milosevic made to the effect that he would stop supporting the Bosnian Serbs.
Where we go from here is to keep pushing in the right direction. As we speak here, the United Nations is considering a resolution which would enable us to place United Nations forces along the border between Serbia and Bosnia, to try to test and reinforce the resolve of the Milosevic government to cut off supplies to the Bosnian Serbs.
If that resolution passes, and in its particulars it makes good sense, that is a very good next step. We're just going to keep working and pushing in this direction, and I think we'll begin to get more and more results.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Q. Mr. President, there's a widespread perception that you're waffling, that you can't make up your mind. One day you're saying, in a few days, we'll have a decision, we have a common approach. The next day, you're saying, we're still looking for a consensus.
Will American troops be in this border patrol that the U.N. is voting on? And you know, where are we?
A. Well, first of all, I have made up my mind and I've told you what my position was and I made it as clear as I can. But I also believe it is imperative that we work with our allies on this. The United States is not in a position to move unilaterally, nor should we. So that is the answer to your question. The resolution being considered by the United Nations, I think, contemplates that the Unprofor [ United Nations Protective Force ] forces would be moved and expanded and moved to the border. At this time, there has been no suggestion that we would be asked to be part of those forces. Military's Gay Ban
Q. A domestic question. Could you tell us, how were you affected by the testimony of Col. Fred Peck, whose son is a homosexual, who said that nonetheless he could not in good conscience support lifting the ban?
A. I thought all the testimony given in that hearing, and I saw quite a lot of it from more than one panel, was quite moving and straightforward. I still think the test ought to be conduct.
Q. Do you think that -- do you -- does this allow for the possibility of the "don't ask, don't tell," the compromise that would allow ----
A. You know what my position is. I have nothing else to say about it.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Civil Rights Appointment
Q. Your nominee to head the Justice Department's Civil Rights Division [ Lani Guinier ] has expressed what many regard as rather striking views about voting rights and a number of other areas, including expressing some misgivings about the principle of one man, one vote. And I wonder if you are familiar with all these views and if you support them, and if you do not, why you chose her?
A. I nominated her because there had never been a full-time practicing civil rights lawyer with a career in civil rights law heading the Civil Rights Division. I expect a policy to be made on civil rights laws by the United States Congress, and I expect the Justice Department to carry out that policy.
Insofar as there is discretion in the policy, that discretionary authority should reside either in the president or the attorney general, in terms of what policies the country will follow. I still think she's a very well-qualified civil rights lawyer, and I hope she'll be confirmed, and I think she has every intention of following the law of the land as Congress writes it.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Federal Reserve
Q. Mr. President, what would you say or what do you say to Federal Reserve officials who are arguing for a slight rise in short-term interest rates because they're concerned about resurging inflation?
A. I would say that the month before last, we had virtually no inflation, and you can't run the country on a month-to-month basis. You've got to look at some longer trends. There are some clear underlying reasons for this last inflationary bulge, which don't necessarily portend long-term inflation. I think it's, of course, a cause of concern, we ought to look at it, but we ought to wait till we have some more evidence before we raise interest rates in an economy where industrial capacity is only at 80 percent.
If you look at all the underlying long-term things -- long-term trends in energy prices, industrial capacity, the kinds of things that really shape an economy -- there is no reason at this time to believe that there could be any cause for a resurgent inflation.
Q. The argument is made at the Federal Reserve that higher taxes, higher burdens on business through health care fees or other things like that will indeed raise inflation while the economy stays weak.
A. Well, just a few weeks ago some people were arguing that all this would be deflationary and would repress the recovery. So I guess you can find an expert to argue any opinion, but there is no evidence of that.
The prevailing opinion at the Fed and the prevailing opinion in the economic community has been that the most important thing we can do is to bring down long-term interest rates by bringing down the deficit. You can't have it both ways. We're either going to bring down the deficit or we're not. And everything in life requires some rigorous effort if you're going to have fundamental change.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Opinion Polls
Q. Mr. President, in your opening statement you said this has been a good week for you, but the latest CNN-USA Today-Gallup poll, as you probably saw, shows a 10 percent decline in your job-approval rating since the end of April, from 55 to 45 percent.
Why do you think that's happening? And is it your fault, or what can be done?
A. Well, for one thing, I'm trying to do hard things, and I can't do hard things, and conduct an ongoing campaign at the same time.
You know, I'm doing things that are hard, that are controversial, and anybody who doesn't want to assume responsibility can stand on the sidelines and criticize them. I never expected that I could actually do anything about the deficit without having some hits.
I never expected that I could take on some of these interests that I've taken on without being attacked. And whenever you try to change things, there are always people there ready to point out the pain of change without the promise of it, and that's just all part of it.
If I worried about the poll ratings I'd never get anything done here. My only -- the only thing I'd remind you is, for 12 years we've seen politicians in the Congress and the executive branch worry about their poll ratings every month, and then at the end of every four years things are a lot worse.
If things are better at the end of the period that I was given to serve, then the poll ratings now won't make any difference, and if they're not, they won't make any difference. So my job is to do my job and let the chips fall where they may. Yes?
Q. There seems to be a Catch-22 emerging on Bosnia. One would be that you have consistently said that you want to have a consensus with the U.S. allies, but until that consensus is formed, you've found that it seems very difficult to explain to the American people precisely how that war should be defined. Is it a civil war? Is it a war of aggression? And also not necessarily what the next step should be, but what are the principles, the overriding principles that should guide U.S. policy?
What can you tell the American people right now about that?
A. First, that it is both a civil war and a war of aggression. Because Bosnia was created as a separate legal entity, it is both a civil war, where elements of people who live within that territory are fighting against one another, and there has been aggression from without -- from the Croatians and from the Serbs, principally from the Serbs. That the inevitable but unintended impact of the arms embargo has been to put the United Nations in the position of ratifying an enormous superiority of arms for the Bosnian Serbs that they got from Serbia. And that our interest is in seeing, in my view at least, that the United Nations does not fore-ordain the outcome of a civil war. That's why I've always been in favor of some kind of lifting of the arms embargo -- that we contain the conflict and that we do everything we can to move to an end of it and to move to an end of ethnic cleansing.
Those are our interests there, and those are the ones I'm trying to pursue, but we should not introduce American ground forces into the conflict in behalf of one of the belligerents, and we must move with our allies.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Youth in the White House
Q. Mr. President, one of the charges leveled by critics of you in Arkansas and now at the beginning of your term as President is that you've surrounded yourself with too many young people and put them in too many senior positions. How do you respond to that criticism?
A. Like Lloyd Bentsen and Warren Christopher?
I mean, who are you referring to? Mr. McLarty, Mr. Ruin, Ms. Rasco and Mr. Lake, to name four, and I, are all I think older than our counterparts were when President Kennedy was President.
There are a lot of young people who work here but most of the people in decision-making positions are not particularly young. And I'm amazed sometimes that, you know -- you think I ought to let some of them go?
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Cutting the Deficit
Q. Mr. President, you've been talking a lot recently about deficit reduction, the deficit reduction trust fund. You're talking now about having to stretch out your investment programs, postpone some of the things. What do you say to people in urban areas, some of the liberal congressmen on the Hill, who say, wait a minute, we're the ones who elected this guy and now the programs that have been starved for 12 years that we need aren't going to be able to get money? What sort of political position does that put you in with your core supporters?
A. Well, I ask them, first of all, to look at the five-year budget. The enormous squeeze on domestic spending, including investment spending, began 12 years ago. I can't turn it around overnight. And I ask them to look at the five-year budget and look at in light of the fact that the deficit numbers were revised upward after the election by $50 billion a year in three of the next four years.
And I ask them also to consider this: until we can prove that we have the discipline to control our budget, I don't think we'll have the elbow room necessary to have the kind of targeted investments we need. I think the more we do budget control, the more we'll be free to then be very sharply discriminating in those things which actually do create jobs. I don't think we have any other option at this time. Crisis of Hope
Q. Mr. President, in your New York speech this past week at Cooper Union, you spoke of a crisis of belief and hope, and earlier Mrs. Clinton in a speech talked about a crisis of meaning. How do you see these crises manifesting themselves, what are the causes of them, and how severe do you see this?
A. Well, I think they manifested themselves in people's honest feelings that things are not going very well in this country and that they haven't gone very well in a long time, in the alienation people feel from the political process, and in the alienation they often feel from one another in the same neighborhoods and communities.
There are real objective reasons for a lot of these problems. After all, for most people the work week is lengthening and incomes are declining, the job growth of the country has been very weak, the crime rate is high, and there's a sense of real alienation there. And I don't think we can speak to them just with programs. I think that in our different ways that's what both Hillary and I were trying to say.
And the thing I was trying to say to the American people at the Cooper Union that I want to reiterate today is that you can never change if you have no belief in the potential of your country, your community or yourself, and that the easy path is cynicism, the easy path is to throw rocks. The better path is doing the hard work of change.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

As I said at Cooper Union, when President Kennedy occupied that office, nearly three-quarters of the American people believed that their leaders would tell them the truth and that their institutions worked and that problems could be solved.
So there was a lot more elbow room there. You know, a year or two years could go by, people could be working on something with maybe only slightly measurable progress, but the country felt that it was moving forward. That is what we have to restore today, a sense that it can be done, and it cannot be done by the President alone, but the President has to keep saying that, that faith is a big part of this.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Justice Dept. Nominee
Q. Okay. I'd like to go back to your Justice Department for just a second, Mr. President, since during the campaign you said it was a mistake and in fact apologized for playing golf at an all-white country club in Little Rock, shouldn't it disqualify your nominee for associate attorney general, Webb Hubbell? Is there an exception because he's a family friend and, and are the local ----
A. Absolutely not.
Q. ---- are the local civil rights leaders wrong when they say that his attempts to integrate the club appear to have been a last-minute political conversion?
A. No, as a matter of fact, if you go back -- first of all, let me, let me -- the first question is no, he should not be disqualified. The second question is, is it a last-minute conversion? The African-American who joined the club testified that Webb Hubbell had been trying for years to get him to do it and he had not agreed. That's what the record shows.
Thirdly, my belief is that the overwhelming majority of African-American leaders in my state would very much like to see him confirmed.
He's always had a reputation as being a strong advocate of civil rights whether as Mayor of Little Rock or chief justice of the Supreme Court of my state.
Â 
Â 

Â 
Â 
. . .

Strength of Policy
Q. Mr. President, I want to go back to a question that Helen asked earlier about your indecisiveness over Bosnia. I'm wondering how you think that's affected perceptions of you as a leader. There is a concern reflected in polls and in some comments from Democratic members in Congress that you are indecisive and perhaps not tough enough to tackle all the problems.
A. Well, I'd just like to ask you what their evidence is. When Russia came up the United States took the lead and we got a very satisfactory result. When I took office, I said we were going to try to do more in Bosnia. We agreed to go to the Vance-Owen peace process, and two of the three parties signed on. We got enforcement of the no-fly zone. We began to engage in multinational humanitarian aid.
We got much, much tougher sanctions. We got the threat of military force on the table as a possible option, Milosevic changed his position all because this Administration did more than the previous one.
And every time I have consulted the Congress, they say to me in private this is a really tough problem, I don't know what you should do, but you're the only president that ever took us into counsel beforehand. Instead of telling us what you were going to do, you actually ask us our opinion. I do not believe that is a sign of weakness. And I realize it may be frustrating for all of you to deal with the ambiguity of this problem, but it is a difficult one.
I have a clear policy. I have gotten more done on this than my predecessor did, and maybe one reason he didn't try to do it is because if you can't force everybody to fall in line overnight, the people that have been fighting each other for centuries, you may be accused of vacillating. We are not vacillating. We have a clear, strong policy.
In terms of the other issues, who else around this town in the last dozen years has offered this much budget cutting, this much tax increases, this much deficit reduction, and a clear economic strategy that asks the wealthy to pay their fair share, gives the middle class a break and gives massive incentives to get new investment and new jobs in the small business community and from large business as well?
I think -- you know, I don't understand what -- on one day people say he's trying to do too much, he's pushing too hard, he wants too much change; and then on the other day says, well, he's really not pushing very hard.
I think we're getting good results. We've been here three months, we've passed a number of important bills, and I feel good about it.
I think the American people know one thing: that I'm on their side, that I'm fighting to change things, and they're finding out it's not so easy. But we are going to get a lot of change out of this Congress if we can keep our eye on the ball and stop worrying about whether we characterize each other in some way or another and keep thinking about what's good for the American people.
Every day I try to get up and think about not what somebody characterizes my action as, but whether what I do will or will not help to improve the lives of most Americans. That is the only ultimate test by which any of us should be judged.
Thank you very much.
Photograph During his news conference yesterday, President Clinton sought to discourage new worries about inflation and urged the Federal Reserve not to raise interest rates. (Jose R. Lopez/The New York Times)

CREDIT MARKETS; Producer Price Rise Jars Treasury Market

Author: FUERBRINGER, JONATHAN

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]13 May 1993: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429040748?accountid=11752

Abstract: In the end, the 10-year auction went fairly well, with an average yield of 5.96 percent, the lowest since the Treasury began regular auctions in 1978. But that was not enough to lift the cloud created by the inflation report or to offset concerns about today's $8.25 billion auction of 30-year bonds. That auction could be undermined by the hangover of yesterday's inflation report and the release of the Consumer Price Index today. The April report on retail sales could also give traders headaches on the final day of the Treasury's quarterly auction series. "When the market was rallying earlier this year, people expected that the rate of inflation would be lower," Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Securities, said. "I think the market has realized that inflation won't be lower, but I do not think that it has realized that it will be higher." "10-Year Treausry Notes" tracks average yields at quarterly autions, in percent, since the beginning of 1989. (Source: Treasury Department); "Freddie Mac Yields" tracks average weekly yields on Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corporation 30-year and 15-year participation certificates since February. Yields track changes in fixed-rate mortgages. (Source: Federal Home Loan Mortgage Corp.)

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=CREDIT+MARKETS%3B+Producer+Price+Rise+Jars+Treasury+Market&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-05-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=FUERBRINGER%2C+JONATHAN&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: There was a rude awakening for the Treasury market yesterday: The April jump in producer price inflation was much higher than expected and signaled that there is less hope now for a new rally based on declining inflation.
The metaphorical slap in the face pushed prices lower, with the 30-year bond down 19/32 point and the yield up to 6.85 percent, from 6.81 percent Tuesday. The prices of two-year to five-year notes fell much less, however, because inflation, which erodes the value of fixed-income investments, has less impact on shorter-term securities.
The selling, which came in the morning, might have actually helped the Treasury's auction of $10.76 billion of 10-year notes because the price of that issue also fell ahead of the 1 P.M. bidding deadline, making it more attractive to dealers.
In the end, the 10-year auction went fairly well, with an average yield of 5.96 percent, the lowest since the Treasury began regular auctions in 1978. But that was not enough to lift the cloud created by the inflation report or to offset concerns about today's $8.25 billion auction of 30-year bonds. That auction could be undermined by the hangover of yesterday's inflation report and the release of the Consumer Price Index today. The April report on retail sales could also give traders headaches on the final day of the Treasury's quarterly auction series.
The bad news for traders and investors was in the six-tenths of one percentage point increase in the Producer Price Index for finished goods. Some analysts dismissed the April increase as an aberration, in part because of the weather-induced 44.7 percent increase in the prices of fresh and dried vegetables.
Some traders said if the figures had been taken as a new sign of rising inflation and not just an aberration, the selloff would have been steeper.
But this is the third out of the first four months of the year that the index has been higher than expectations because of aberrations and "these aberrations are adding up to a higher P.P.I. number," Michael H. Strauss, chief economist at Yamaichi International, said. "Bond investors should take a look at it."
If future reports make it clearer that inflation will be higher this year, there will be more upward pressure on interest rates. The C.P.I. rose 3 percent last year. But those who think it will be higher this year say the increase will be small.
Traders were able to dismiss the jump in food prices. But they had trouble with the more than 1 percent increase, after seasonal adjustment, in the price of passenger cars. "The car prices are real to me," one trader said.
"When the market was rallying earlier this year, people expected that the rate of inflation would be lower," Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Securities, said. "I think the market has realized that inflation won't be lower, but I do not think that it has realized that it will be higher."
However, some traders and forecasters, including those at Merrill Lynch, still expect inflation to decline, despite yesterday's report. Four Themes
Inflation is one of the four themes that have been influencing trading recently. And because traders can quickly turn from positive to negative, the bond market has been locked in a narrow trading range for two months.
The others are the pace of economic growth, President Clinton's handling of the economy and the deficit, and technical factors, which include the strong demand for Treasury securities because portfolio managers still have a lot of cash to invest.
The inflation theme had been a worry but was put to rest temporarily by the small increases in the producer and consumer price indexes in March. That confidence was unsettled a bit yesterday.
On growth, the data have been relatively positive for the market as it shows that the economy continues to appear to be moving ahead at a slow pace that would not put any upward pressure on prices. But this could change later in the year. Deficit Doubts
The recent setbacks to President Clinton on his economic program have raised some doubts about the outlook for significant deficit reduction, although it was his promise of such reduction that fueled the rally earlier this year.
The fate of today's 30-year bond auction is likely to hinge on the C.P.I. report and retail sales reports for April. A jump of two-tenths of one percentage point in the C.P.I. and a 1.2 percent rise in retail sales, which are the consensus forecasts, would settle inflationary jitters and help the auction. Stronger inflation and stronger sales would cause trouble.
The success of yesterday's 10-year note auction was reflected in trading afterward, when the yield in the when-issued market fell to 5.92 percent, from the 5.96 average at the auction.
The 10-year notes are the same as the 6.25 percent issue that was originally sold in February and matures in February 2003. The Treasury reopened the issue because there was a severe shortage of the notes available for trading, which left open the possibility that the issue could be manipulated.
The reopening policy was set after the Treasury bidding scandal at Salomon Brothers in 1991. The 30-year bond sale will also be a reopening of the 7.125 percent issue sold in February and maturing in 2023.

A New Rush Into Latin America

Author: Nash, Nathaniel C

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 Apr 1993: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429026304?accountid=11752

Abstract: "Latin America is our highest strategic priority and we do not see a more compelling investment opportunity anywhere in the world," said Ken Ross, chief spokesman for the corporation's international division. "We've had explosive growth in the region." NBC and Fox have announced plans for Latin American cable television networks. China's state-owned steel group has acquired Peru's state iron company. The NEC Corporation has entered a telecommunications joint venture with Argentina's largest industrial concern, Perez Companc. Mitsubishi has won a $150 million contract to build a power-generation plant for Chile's state copper company. And Hilton is building a luxury hotel in Punta del Este, the posh Uruguayan resort. Mr. [David Mulford] argues that Latin America has a number of other attractions. "We have a common historical religious and social heritage with Latin America, and it is now made up almost exclusively of democratic governments," he said. "They have much more sophisticated market economies than the new states of the former Soviet Union and Eastern Europe. "It's a market of almost 600 million," he said, referring to an estimate for the population in 15 years. That would even put Europe in the shade.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=A+New+Rush+Into+Latin+America&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-04-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Nash%2C+Nathaniel+C&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: EVEN as much of the world dissolves into a single, faceless market, some things just won't happen. Dunkin' Donuts would not try to sell baguettes to Parisians. Benihana would not open hot-dog stands in the United States. Pepsico, surely, would not sell tacos to the Mexicans.
Think again on that last one. Pepsico, which owns the Taco Bell chain, saw the fattening wallets of Mexico's younger generation, splashed some extra hot sauce onto its tacos and headed across the border.
Call it Pepsi's Latin invasion. And it's not just tacos, and not just Mexico. Last month the company announced a $750 million, five-year assault in Mexico, including plans to buy interests in big bottlers, buy distribution routes and advertise heavily. The company is opening Pizza Hut, Taco Bell and KFC franchises throughout Latin America, and selling Fritos, Ruffles and Doritos chips as far south as Tierra del Fuego. And it will soon announce that later this year it will spend tens of millions of dollars to sponsor Michael Jackson on an eight-city tour of Latin America.
"Latin America is our highest strategic priority and we do not see a more compelling investment opportunity anywhere in the world," said Ken Ross, chief spokesman for the corporation's international division. "We've had explosive growth in the region."
Pepsico has company. Led by the consumer-products, technology and service sectors, businesses in the United States, Japan and Europe have quadrupled their investments in Latin America in the past three years. Nearly $100 billion was invested in the region from 1990 through 1992, primarily in Mexico, Chile, Argentina, Venezuela and Brazil, up from $24 billion for the previous three years.
A few examples: Wal-Mart, Kmart, Sears, J. C. Penney, Price Club and other retailers have entered Mexico in the last year or stepped up operations there. Anheuser-Busch is buying a $477 million stake in Mexico's largest brewery. Ford has opened a modern axle factory in Argentina. Digital Equipment has teamed with Chile's largest software producer to sell its machines across Latin America. A small California disk-drive maker, Memotech, is moving its manufacturing operations to Chile.
NBC and Fox have announced plans for Latin American cable television networks. China's state-owned steel group has acquired Peru's state iron company. The NEC Corporation has entered a telecommunications joint venture with Argentina's largest industrial concern, Perez Companc. Mitsubishi has won a $150 million contract to build a power-generation plant for Chile's state copper company. And Hilton is building a luxury hotel in Punta del Este, the posh Uruguayan resort.
In addition, investors seeking profits in Latin America's volatile emerging stock markets have poured billions into the Buenos Aires, Santiago, Sao Paulo, Caracas and Mexico City stock exchanges. A Surging Population
The rush into Latin America is based on an idea that only a few years ago would have seemed unthinkable -- that this market of 470 million people from Mexico to Argentina (including the Caribbean) could be one of the more stable growth investments of the closing years of this century and beyond.
Some economists and government officials are arguing that now is the time for the United States to alter longstanding economic policies that favor Europe and Asia in favor of one that promotes closer ties in the Americas, arguing that Latin America's booming markets can be a critical source of economic strength to American business.
"If you are the new President of the United States and your top priority is restoring domestic economic growth, then Latin America is your best customer," said David Mulford, former Under Secretary of the Treasury for International Affairs under Presidents Bush and Reagan and now vice chairman of First Boston. "It is returning to strength at a time when Europe and Japan are in recession, and you should be very much aware of the fact that Latin America is contributing to Main Street America, creating jobs and economic activity."
Indeed, since the region's collective nadir in 1989, most Latin American nations have significantly increased their growth and lowered their inflation rates. Only Brazil and Peru lack a healthy rate of growth, and only Brazil currently suffers hyperinflation, although several other countries have rates above 25 percent.
Mr. Mulford argues that Latin America has a number of other attractions. "We have a common historical religious and social heritage with Latin America, and it is now made up almost exclusively of democratic governments," he said. "They have much more sophisticated market economies than the new states of the former Soviet Union and Eastern Europe. "It's a market of almost 600 million," he said, referring to an estimate for the population in 15 years. That would even put Europe in the shade.
He added that the United States already accounts for 57 percent of Latin American imports, compared with only 11 percent of Japan's and 22 percent of Europe's. The Risks
That is not to say that Latin America is not still a risky investment.
To keep investment dollars coming, many of its central banks have kept real interest rates high, a drag on growth. Much of the pent-up consumer demand from the 1980's was satiated in 1991 and 1992, dimming the outlook for this year. There is pressure on governments to devalue their currencies to make exports more competitive, but such devaluations would quickly lift inflation.
In December, the International Monetary Fund lowered its growth projections for the region, to 2 percent in 1993, from 3.9 percent. The main reasons were a continued recession in Brazil and slower than expected growth in Mexico and Argentina as high trade deficits robbed local industry of production and jobs.
And politics could change the picture for the worse. This is a year of presidential or other important elections in Chile, Bolivia, Mexico, Paraguay, Brazil, Argentina and Venezuela. The elections tend to put more pressure on governments to increase spending, which can lead to higher rates of inflation.
Moreover, memories of a troubled past are still fresh. The democratic regimes that now govern all Central and South American countries except French Guiana are still fragile -- susceptible to military coup attempts, as was seen last year in Venezuela, and leaders who seize extraordinary powers, as has Peru's President, Alberto K. Fujimori. Social unrest, the product of entrenched poverty and guerrilla and drug-trade violence, threatens the political order in Brazil, Colombia, Peru and Venezuela. Remember the 1970's
The recent surge in interest in Latin America is not the first.
In the 1970's, banks and international lending organizations lent hundreds of billions of dollars in the region, primarily to governments and state-owned businesses. But the money went to cover budget deficits and finance projects that became plagued by bureaucratic tangles, corruption and mismanagement. Foreign companies followed the lead of the lenders and set up shop in Latin America, but frequently encountered attempts at nationalization, high inflation and policies favoring local companies.
The investment climate here has changed radically since then. Now, much more of the lending is to privately owned local industrial groups for specific projects. And foreign investors are being much more cautious, often refusing to commit unless they can find prestigious local partners to share the risk.
The economic approaches recently embraced by most Latin governments -- emphasizing open markets, low inflation and balanced budgets -- have enabled them to strike agreements with commercial banks to reduce once-onerous debt levels. That has opened the door to the international markets and new borrowing both by companies and governments, reversing a history of defaulting on debt payments that left Latin America a pariah throughout the 1980's.
In 1991 and 1992, for example, Latin America raised an average of $12 billion each year in bond and stock offerings in the international markets, compared with an annual average of less than $1 billion throughout most of the 1980's.
Still, corporate managers say they are moving much more cautiously than in the past. William Rhodes, the Citicorp vice chairman who heads negotiations with countries over reorganizing their commercial bank debt, said the most important thing to businesses is the assurance that the new economic thinking will last.
Investors, he said, are waiting longer than they did in the 1970's before jumping in. "Corporate executives want to have the feeling that the continuity is there," he said, "that after two years of reform, the change is permanent, not a fleeting thing." Five Favorites
Economists and bankers generally agree that the most attractive Latin American countries for investment are these:
* Mexico, the top choice, because of proximity to the United States and the chance of a free-trade agreement with Washington, plus a huge domestic market and economic reforms.
* Chile, the second choice, with the fastest-growing and perhaps most open economy; the smallness of its market and distance from the United States are slight disadvantages.
* Argentina, third, because of the vast changes in the past two years in a country that was prone to hyperinflation and military government; but investors are worried about an overvalued peso and continued government corruption.
* Venezuela, fourth, with vast oil reserves and open markets; but two attempted coups against President Carlos Andres Perez have worried investors.
* Brazil, fifth, for its sheer size, industrial strength and natural resources; but 1,200 percent inflation and a lack of a coherent economic policy have investors essentially in a holding pattern.
The cautious new invasion of Latin America is not led by heavy industry, nor by the historical attraction of exploiting low-cost labor and abundant natural resources. Rather, it tends to be a tapping of parts of the local market that sorely need development.
Throughout Latin America there is an acute shortage of banking services, computerization, telecommunications, shopping centers, supermarkets, hotels, insurance and a host of other services. Thus, many of the corporate players are in consumer-products, technology and services.
"Most people think computer sales can only grow more or less at the rate of overall economic growth, but that is just wrong for less developed countries such as in Latin America," said Richard O. Martorana, president of I.B.M. in Argentina. "In these markets, the demand for such services often far exceeds growth." The Move Into Chile
Chile is a good example of heightened investment by consumer-product and technology companies. Pepsico late last year announced a $100 million investment program, buying the country's largest bottler and snack-food concerns, and opening KFC's and Pizza Huts in Santiago. In late 1991, Digital Equipment teamed up with Chile's largest software manufacturer, Sonda, adapting its hardware to accommodate Sonda software and marketing it in 14 Latin countries. It is now the largest Latin computer company.
Also, Sonda recently joined Memotech International of Milpitas, Calif., which found the costs of making hard disk drives prohibitive in California. Memotech now plans to start manufacturing in Chile in July, not only for the Latin American market but for Europe and the United States as well.
Foreign investment in mining, fishing, energy and other natural resources is also strong. In Chile, for example, the Phelps Dodge Corporation and the Sumitomo Mining and Metals Company announced last November a $1.5 billion mining project for copper and gold. And in Venezuela last month, the Exxon Corporation, the Mitsubishi Corporation and Royal Dutch Shell signed a $3 billion joint venture agreement with the state gas company to develop natural gas.
As for Pepsico, the attraction of Latin America seems obvious. "It's a very young population and a growing one," Mr. Ross said. "The markers are all favorable." CHANGING THE PERSPECTIVE IN WASHINGTON
BUENOS AIRES
IN Washington, it is generally acknowledged that United States policy toward Latin America has historically involved little more than worrying about containment of Communism and the promotion of democracy.
As a strategic economic market, Latin America has been hardly a blip on the political radar in Washington, with the exception of Mexico and the occasions when governments defaulted on debt payments in the 1980's, threatening the stability of the international banking system.
But if Latin America continues to show growth as it has in the past two years, to control inflation and to maintain stability, it may have to be considered in a different light.
Some are arguing that Washington should take a lead in this, if for no other reason than safeguarding the United States' own commercial interests.
Policy recommendations generally have two goals. One is to insure that the region, historically prone to protectionism, remains open to American business by keeping tariffs low and helping governments in modernizing their capital markets, banking systems and regulation of industry -- in effect, integrating them more into the world financial markets.
Don E. Newquist, chairman of the International Trade Commission, has recommended that the United States use the hope of bilateral free trade pacts as a prod. He has suggested that such an accord be signed with Chile even before the one with Mexico, arguing that it would send a signal to other Latin countries that democratic governments promoting open markets will be rewarded.
David Mulford, former Under Secretary of the Treasury for International Affairs and now vice chairman of First Boston, says the Clinton Administration should continue many of the programs begun under President Bush's Initiative for the Americas.
Among other things, these would create investment funds to help the development of small businesses, to study ways of reducing regulation and to institute changes in the judicial process to insure equal protection for foreign investors.
Chart "Latin America Roars Back": Map of Latin American indicating past problems and current conditions that have made or will make those countries more attractive to foreign businesses. (pg. 6)
Illustration "Slower Inflation, Faster Growth," lists vital statistics of nine of the largest Latin American economies, including inflation and Gross Domestic Product growth, for 1989 and 1992 (Sources: Inter-American Development Banki; national governments0 (pg. 6)

CREDIT MARKETS; Inflation News Heartens the Bond Market

Author: FUERBRINGER, JONATHAN

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 Apr 1993: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/429027677?accountid=11752

Abstract: The selloff in the market since early March had been sparked by sharp jumps in the Producer Price Index and the Consumer Price index in January and February and a surprisingly large jump in jobs over the first quarter of the year. The data did not portend a surge in inflation but indicated that if the pace continued, inflation would not decline below last year's level, which some forecasters and investors were betting on, and that it would likely be a little higher. Traders said part of yesterday's bond market surge came from buyers who had previously sold Treasury securities short because they expected the core inflation rate to be higher. When their speculative bet proved wrong, they had to buy back the securities quickly to limit their losses. That added to the sharp rise in prices and the steep decline in yields, said Andrew S. Brenner, a trader at Nomura Securities International. "I think people understand that inflation is not going to be horrible," said Nancy Kimelman, the chief economist for Technical Data in Boston. "But they understand that it is not going to be 2.5 percent either and that keeps us confined in a trading range."

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=CREDIT+MARKETS%3B+Inflation+News+Heartens+the+Bond+Market&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1993-04-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=FUERBRINGER%2C+JONATHAN&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: After several weeks of nervousness and a heavy selloff in the bond market, traders and investors got some reassuring news on inflation yesterday and drove the yields on Treasury bonds and notes sharply lower.
Prices of bonds and notes spiked higher in the morning after the Labor Department reported that the core producer inflation rate, which excludes food and energy prices, rose just a tenth of 1 percent in March, well below the rate in February and January and below market expectations.
The negligible increase in the core inflation rate allowed the market to ignore what probably would have been a very disturbing four-tenths of 1 percent increase in the overall Producer Price Index for finished goods.
Prices surged again later in the day when the the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alan Greenspan, offered reassuring comments on inflation, saying that the economy was nearing price stability.
By the end of a shortened trading day ahead of the Good Friday holiday today, the price of the 30-year bond was up 110/32 , while its yield plunged to 6.85 percent, down from 6.95 on Wednesday.
The price of the 10-year Treasury note, another longer-term issue that is sensitive to inflation, was up 24/32 as its yield fell to 5.96 percent, from 6.06 percent the day before.
The selloff in the market since early March had been sparked by sharp jumps in the Producer Price Index and the Consumer Price index in January and February and a surprisingly large jump in jobs over the first quarter of the year. The data did not portend a surge in inflation but indicated that if the pace continued, inflation would not decline below last year's level, which some forecasters and investors were betting on, and that it would likely be a little higher.
Given how far the bond market had rallied, that was enough of a reason to sell until the outlook was clearer.
But while yesterday's inflation report was positive, the course of the market from here is still uncertain. The Consumer Price Index for March will be released today, when the market is closed. If the consumer price increase is not in line with the small rise in producer prices, much of the market's good feeling of yesterday would be lost.
Traders said part of yesterday's bond market surge came from buyers who had previously sold Treasury securities short because they expected the core inflation rate to be higher. When their speculative bet proved wrong, they had to buy back the securities quickly to limit their losses. That added to the sharp rise in prices and the steep decline in yields, said Andrew S. Brenner, a trader at Nomura Securities International.
Edward Yardeni, the chief economist at C. J. Lawrence, said the report on March producer prices -- combined with the recent declines in the prices of commodities like lumber and copper -- provides a signal that inflation will decline this year.
Yesterday, Mr. Yardeni reaffirmed his forecast of a 2 percent increase in the Consumer Price Index, compared with 2.9 percent in 1992. He said such a small increase would mean that the yield on the 30-year bond should move toward 6.5 percent or lower, which is below the 16-year closing low of 6.72 percent reached on March 8.
But other analysts are not as sanguine. While the March producer price data provided a signal that inflation is not rising as fast as it was in the first two months of the year, they still forecast that prices will rise at the same or a slightly faster pace this year. And that means bond yields will remain around their current levels, but in a trading range that will preclude a breakthrough to new lows. Yields Are Locked
"I think people understand that inflation is not going to be horrible," said Nancy Kimelman, the chief economist for Technical Data in Boston. "But they understand that it is not going to be 2.5 percent either and that keeps us confined in a trading range."
Robert Giordano, the director of economic research at Goldman, Sachs & Company, said he expected data later in the year to show the inflation rate slowing to around last year's level. He said such data would send bond yields to new lows.
"But whether or not this is the start of that is another question," he added, saying that for now he believes that yields are locked in a trading range.
The bond market's rally yesterday was also aided by other data, including a small decline in initial jobless benefits claims and weak March store sales, which indicate that the economy is growing at a slower pace than at the nearly 5 percent clip at the end of last year. The slower growth means there is little chance that prices will be pushed higher by the demand of consumers or the exhausting of production capacity.
