O.E.C.D. Forecasts Slow U.S. Recovery From Recession

Author: Greenhouse, Steven

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]26 Nov 1991: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/428263725?accountid=11752

Abstract: Discussing energy efficiency, the report noted that energy prices were far lower in the United States than in most other industrial countries and that the United States consumed far more energy per capita than most other nations. Gasoline in the United States sells for less than one-third the price in France, Italy and the Netherlands, largely because of those countries' high energy taxes, which seek to encourage conservation. The organization praised the Bush Administration's banking proposals, which call for allowing unrestricted interstate branch banking and common ownership of commercial banks and investment banks. The report said the proposed changes would increase the efficiency of the financial sector as a whole by increasing competition, and should pose little risk to the stability of the financial system. Stance on Deposit Insurance

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=O.E.C.D.+Forecasts+Slow+U.S.+Recovery+From+Recession&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1991-11-26&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Greenhouse%2C+Steven&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development said today that the United States economy would recover far more slowly from its recent recession than it has from most other recessions of the last three decades.
The Paris-based organization predicted that the economy would grow by 1.4 percent in the second half of this year and by 2.5 percent next year, which compares with an average growth of 6 percent in the four quarters immediately after the last seven recessions.
In its annual report on the American economy, the organization said the expansion phase of the cycle is expected to be muted compared with those of typical recoveries.
The report said a shallow recession was likely to be followed by a weak recovery because not much spending was forgone in the downturn to fuel the upturn.
The organization, comprising 24 industrial nations, projected that by the end of 1993 the United States unemployment rate would fall to 5.75 percent (it was 6.8 percent in October) and the inflation rate would drop to 2.9 percent (from the 3.9 percent predicted for this year).
The organization said United States exports would continue to expand, but more slowly than in recent years. It forecast that exports of goods and services would grow by 4.7 percent this year and 7.1 percent next year, compared with double-digit jumps in 1988 and 1989.
The increases in exports will help stabilize the United States' current-account deficit at about $60 billion, the organization forecast, compared with annual deficits of more than $100 billion from 1985 through 1989.
It said the major components of private-sector demand were expected to rebound, but it added that the excessive supply of commercial real estate from the late 1980's would dampen activity in this sector for some years.
The organization praised the Bush Administration's banking proposals, which call for allowing unrestricted interstate branch banking and common ownership of commercial banks and investment banks. The report said the proposed changes would increase the efficiency of the financial sector as a whole by increasing competition, and should pose little risk to the stability of the financial system. Stance on Deposit Insurance
The organization questioned proposals to limit deposit insurance coverage, saying depositors might not be able to impose effective discipline on bankers. But proposals to increase capital requirements for banks were praised as a possible aid in restoring market discipline by insuring that the owners of a bank have a large stake in its financial health.
Discussing energy efficiency, the report noted that energy prices were far lower in the United States than in most other industrial countries and that the United States consumed far more energy per capita than most other nations. Gasoline in the United States sells for less than one-third the price in France, Italy and the Netherlands, largely because of those countries' high energy taxes, which seek to encourage conservation.
Raising energy taxes would be consistent with both environmental goals and enhanced energy security, the report said.

Business Scene; The Case Against Lower Inflation

Author: Uchitelle, Louis

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 Aug 1991: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/428156378?accountid=11752

Abstract: Some inflation, then, "greases the wheels of the economy" for manufacturers, and also for wage earners, said Alan Blinder, a Princeton University economist. Rising prices for gasoline or haircuts means that a $400-a-week wage can lose purchasing power without having to be cut from $400. With zero inflation, the same loss in purchasing power requires a wage cut and "people scream about cuts in their nominal wages," Professor Blinder noted. Still, the case for pushing down the inflation rate to zero, or nearly zero, attracts many followers. They acknowledge, for example, that a 4 percent inflation rate would be no more damaging to the economy than a 1 percent annual rise -- if the 4 percent rate remained steady and predictable. But 4 percent inflation is, by definition, hard to maintain, they say, and is therefore unpredictable. For that reason, the anti-inflationists prefer to aim for zero inflation, which they say is easier to maintain than 4 percent. "Price-level stability, popularly called zero inflation, is superior to inflation-rate stability," W. Lee Hoskins, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland, wrote recently. It is this behavior that demonstrates the problem in pushing inflation down too hard: Falling employment levels can prolong a recession or make a recovery very weak. "To achieve zero inflation," Paul R. Krugman, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, said in a recent article, "the Fed would probably have to engineer a recession that would keep the average rate of unemployment at 7.5 percent for the next five years."

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Business+Scene%3B+The+Case+Against+Lower+Inflation&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1991-08-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Uchitelle%2C+Louis&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: FOR the first time since the late 1980's, inflation is clearly in decline. The most popular inflation measure, the Consumer Price Index, has fallen by more than a percentage point, to an annual rate of less than 3 percent, in the first seven months of 1991. Various other measures also show inflation to be edging down. And that raises the question, should the Government use its powers to push the inflation rate even lower? The consensus, for the moment, is no.
Economists offer three main reasons. First, trying to reduce inflation from its already relatively low level would come at a high cost in more unemployment and recession. Second, inflation is not a problem as long as prices rise each year by the same predictable percentage. When the inflation rate jumps around, the uncertainty about what prices will do next is disruptive. Or, as William Niskanen, chairman of the Cato Institute, put it: "A stable 4 percent inflation rate is better than an unstable zero rate that might soon rise."
Finally, pushing down the average inflation rate would be much harder on manufacturers than on service companies. Medical care fees, for example, are still rising at an 8.6 percent annual rate, but the General Electric Company could not make a recent price increase stick for its appliances and backtracked as sales fell. If the Government were to succeed in reducing the average inflation rate in this situation, doctors would probably still raise their fees, although not by as much as before, but manufacturers would have to cut prices. For them, the Government's strategy would be deflationary.
"You kill off profits when you do this, and with profits, new investment also goes, and that reduces potential economic growth," said Peter L. Bernstein, an economic consultant. "You never get the economy out of the woods."
The inflation-fighting process explains why manufacturers bear the brunt. The Government's main agent in fighting inflation is the Federal Reserve, which tries to keep interest rates high enough so that people will be discouraged from financing new refrigerators, cars or homes. The recession that began last July, with its weak demand and falling production, was a byproduct of this anti-inflation effort. But the impact is always uneven.
The demand for refrigerators, cars or houses, and therefore their prices, is much more affected by interest rates on loans than are medical fees or the prices of other services. Whatever the cost of borrowing, medical fees tend to get paid, although they rise. Health insurance, Medicaid and Medicare sustain the demand.
Some inflation, then, "greases the wheels of the economy" for manufacturers, and also for wage earners, said Alan Blinder, a Princeton University economist. Rising prices for gasoline or haircuts means that a $400-a-week wage can lose purchasing power without having to be cut from $400. With zero inflation, the same loss in purchasing power requires a wage cut and "people scream about cuts in their nominal wages," Professor Blinder noted.
Still, the case for pushing down the inflation rate to zero, or nearly zero, attracts many followers. They acknowledge, for example, that a 4 percent inflation rate would be no more damaging to the economy than a 1 percent annual rise -- if the 4 percent rate remained steady and predictable. But 4 percent inflation is, by definition, hard to maintain, they say, and is therefore unpredictable. For that reason, the anti-inflationists prefer to aim for zero inflation, which they say is easier to maintain than 4 percent. "Price-level stability, popularly called zero inflation, is superior to inflation-rate stability," W. Lee Hoskins, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland, wrote recently.
Those who invest in Treasury securities and corporate bonds also tend to favor zero inflation, because inflation reduces the purchasing power of the money they have tied up in these securities. And then there is the argument that downward pressure on inflation forces companies to be more efficient and productive. If General Electric cannot raise refrigerator prices, it tries to preserve profit margins in the face of rising labor costs through automation, layoffs and new procedures.
Manufacturers generally respond more quickly than service companies to the anti-inflation prod, but lately the service sector, too, seems to be pushing for efficiencies, mostly through layoffs, as obtaining price increases becomes more difficult.
It is this behavior that demonstrates the problem in pushing inflation down too hard: Falling employment levels can prolong a recession or make a recovery very weak. "To achieve zero inflation," Paul R. Krugman, an economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, said in a recent article, "the Fed would probably have to engineer a recession that would keep the average rate of unemployment at 7.5 percent for the next five years."

In Canada, the Tempo Picks Up

Author: Farnsworth, Clyde H

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]29 July 1991: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/428112261?accountid=11752

Abstract: As might be expected, the national government in Ottawa waxes even more enthusiastic. "I'm very encouraged," said Finance Minister Donald Mazankowski, adding that the Canadian recovery "looks pretty solid." "At least for now there is a fury in the land against the Prime Minister," said Keith Spicer, a former political science professor and broadcaster. Mr. Spicer, the chairman of the Citizens' Forum on Canada's Future, appointed and financed by the Government, made the statement in his preface to the forum's report, published in late June. "The Bank of Canada has been given a free rein to squeeze inflation out of the system here," said Ernie Stokes, director of Canadian services for the WEFA Group, a research and consulting firm in Bala-Cynwyd, Pa. "The whole direction of policies here is much tougher against inflation."

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=In+Canada%2C+the+Tempo+Picks+Up&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1991-07-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Farnsworth%2C+Clyde+H&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Special to The New York Times
The Canadian economy is stirring again after a recession, far longer and deeper than the one in the United States, cut output by billions of dollars and sent unemployment to the highest levels since the early 1980's.
Like the United States, Canada has benefited from cuts in interest rates, which appear to be helping the housing and auto markets. Canada's economy is only a tenth the size of the United States' and Americans buy three-quarters of everything Canada exports, so it remains highly dependent on what happens south of the border.
Government indexes of leading indicators, used on both sides of the border to predict activity, are now pointing up. The Canadian index has just posted its first gain in 14 months, and most forecasting services expect further advances. 'At a Turning Pint'
"I think we're at a turning point, though we may be bouncing along the bottom for the next three or four months," said Warren Jestin, chief economist in Toronto for the Bank of Nova Scotia.
His feelings were echoed by Purdy Crawford, chief executive of Imasco Ltd., a consumer goods and services holding company that owns drug stores, variety stores, tobacco companies and the Canada Trust bank, as well as the Hardee's fast-food chain in the United States.
Mr. Crawford said he had noticed a "quickening of the tempo of business" in the various company operations in Canada, especially in the last few weeks.
But serious problems and questions do remain, including the federal debt, high unemployment, and the direction of long-term interest rates. And while economic concerns have for now brushed aside the burning issues linked to Quebec's drive for greater sovereignty or independence, they are still lurking.
Prominent economists say there are solid signs that the ice may finally be cracking. They point to strength in exports, rising investment in the energy industry and a surge in provincial government spending in Ontario. That province accounts for nearly half of the nation's economic output.
As might be expected, the national government in Ottawa waxes even more enthusiastic. "I'm very encouraged," said Finance Minister Donald Mazankowski, adding that the Canadian recovery "looks pretty solid."
Especially heartening for the Government are the upward revisions in economic growth rates earlier this year, signaling that the economy was not as weak as first indicated.
Yet the severity of the recession has spurred great popular discontent against the Progressive Conservative Government of Prime Minister Brian Mulroney, who has led the country for seven years.
Poll results show an overwhelming number of Canadians want Mr. Mulroney to resign, even though his mandate, renewed in 1988 elections, runs until 1993.
Last summer, before interest rates started falling here, the polls showed the Prime Minister's popularity, in terms of his percentage of supporters, had fallen below the prime rate.
The prime was about 15 percent then. It is now around 9.75 percent, compared with 8.5 percent in the United States.
"At least for now there is a fury in the land against the Prime Minister," said Keith Spicer, a former political science professor and broadcaster. Mr. Spicer, the chairman of the Citizens' Forum on Canada's Future, appointed and financed by the Government, made the statement in his preface to the forum's report, published in late June.
While economic prospects have improved, the recovery here, as in the United States, still seems a bit anemic and fitful. As a result, in neither country does business or consumer confidence yet correspond with the more promising statistics.
"The momentum for new growth is already here in Canada, but it could run out of steam if the U.S. doesn't really move," cautioned Patrick Grady, a partner in Global Economics, an Ottawa consulting firm.
In both the United States and Canada, debt burdens weigh heavily on governments and consumers. The Bank of Montreal could have spoken for both countries when it noted in its recent Business Review that debt "will not only inhibit further expenditures but keep long-term interest rates higher than would otherwise be the case, further slowing activity."
Unemployment is far higher here than in the United States and is a source of deep anxiety. The unemployment rate for June was 10.5 percent, matching a six-year high, and 3.5 percentage points above the American rate.
Taxes are also much higher, causing thousands of Canadian shoppers to bolt across the border for bargains in the northern United States. High Interest Rates
The cost of money is another preoccupation. Real interest rates after adjustment for inflation are still considered lofty in the United States, even after steep declines in the last 12 months. But they are even higher in Canada, a result of deliberate Government policy.
"The Bank of Canada has been given a free rein to squeeze inflation out of the system here," said Ernie Stokes, director of Canadian services for the WEFA Group, a research and consulting firm in Bala-Cynwyd, Pa. "The whole direction of policies here is much tougher against inflation."
Mr. Stokes, whose office is in Toronto, and others note that the health of the banking and credit system has not been a factor in the monetary equation here, as in the United States, allowing the Canadian authorities greater freedom to tighten credit.
To some, the Government's high interest-rate policy is a problem because it has raised the Canadian dollar from 70 American cents in the mid-1980's to about 87 cents now. Many economists and exporters say that appreciation is damaging this country's competitiveness. Currency May Decline
With growing expectations that Government inflation targets of 5 percent by the end of this year and 3 percent by the end of 1992 will be met, the Canadian dollar could move lower, some economists say.
Then there is the cloud over Quebec's future. If the French-speaking province makes foreign investors nervous. they might pull their money out of Canada. That could endanger the recovery.
The Mulroney Government expects to introduce new constitutional proposals this fall to try to keep Quebec in the federation while meeting the province's demand for recognition as a "distinct society."
All sides agree it will be a delicate balancing act.
Photograph Finance Minister Donald Mazankowski, left, said that the Canadian recovery "looks pretty solid." His optimism is shared by Warren Jestin, chief economist in Toronto for the Bank of Nova Scotia. (Canadian Press; The Bank of Nova Scotia)

Subject: ECONOMIC CONDITIONS AND TRENDS; RECESSION AND DEPRESSION

People: FARNSWORTH, CLYDE H MAZANKOWSKI, DONALD (MIN) JESTIN, WARREN

Company / organization: Name: Bank of Nova Scotia; Ticker: T.BNS; NAICS: 522110; DUNS: 20-770-5989

The New Intellectuals at the Fed

Author: Nasar, Sylvia

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 June 1991: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/428100837?accountid=11752

Abstract: The ways of the Fed often appear arcane. But to the economists, bankers and stock and bond traders who track the secretive board's every twist and turn, last July's deliberations provided a glimpse of the ideas and attitudes that are shaping a broad new intellectual consensus about the Fed's goals: making inflation-fighting the top priority. The idea that the Fed could let inflation creep up a little in order to bring down unemployment was a policy staple of the 1960's. The tandem rise of inflation and unemployment in the 1970's killed that thought. "Not only do I think there is no long-term trade-off between inflation and growth, but I think there is no short-term trade-off either," said Mr. [David W. Mullins Jr.]. "If we drove down short-term interest rates aggressively now, we would drive up long-term rates, making the recession longer." A more basic issue concerns how much the Fed should give up its discretion over setting its goals and strategies. Reflecting a consensus in academia, a growing minority within the Fed, including Mr. [W. Lee Hoskins], believes that monetary policy should be conducted as much as possible according to rules. "Rules let the public judge whether the Fed is doing what it says it is. That increases credibility," said Art Rolnick, research director at the Minneapolis Fed.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=The+New+Intellectuals+at+the+Fed&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1991-06-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Nasar%2C+Sylvia&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Last July, when the Federal Reserve Board's powerful Federal Open Market Committee met to decide the central bank's next move in managing the nation's money supply, a new member, Fed governor David W. Mullins Jr., did not hesitate to speak up.
On the eve of the meeting, which is always conducted in strict secrecy and in as formal a manner as a Japanese Kabuki play, there were signs that the nation's banks were becoming unusually reluctant to make loans to business. The worry was that credit-starved companies would be forced to slash spending, weakening the already-sluggish economy. The F.O.M.C. had to decide whether to nudge interest rates down, a move that would loosen credit and might keep the economy from slowing more. The risk was that easier credit might fan inflation fears and push up long-term interest rates.
Mr. Mullins, by his own account, waded right in. He argued that lowering the Federal funds rate, the rate that banks pay for overnight loans, would not amount to a shift toward easier money. The banks' credit squeeze was having the same effect on the economy as if the Fed were tightening the money supply. To keep money supply growth on its intended course, he maintained, the Fed needed to loosen up a bit. His argument helped persuade the committee. The funds rate was lowered by a quarter of a point.
The ways of the Fed often appear arcane. But to the economists, bankers and stock and bond traders who track the secretive board's every twist and turn, last July's deliberations provided a glimpse of the ideas and attitudes that are shaping a broad new intellectual consensus about the Fed's goals: making inflation-fighting the top priority.
To be sure, Alan Greenspan, the Fed chairman, is the dominant figure at the institution. But the Fed is far from the monolith it sometimes appears and Mr. Greenspan, more than most chairmen in the past, encourages other F.O.M.C. members to air their views. Those inside and outside the Fed frequently mention three people on the 19-member committee whose intellectual arguments and pursuasive skills have become key to forging the central bank's philosophical underpinnings -- and broad policy.
Besides Mr. Mullins, a Harvard Business School finance whiz and former Treasury official who has been nominated by the Bush Administration to become the Fed's vice chairman, they are: W. Lee Hoskins, a Milton Friedman disciple who heads the Cleveland Federal Reserve Bank and frequently dissents from majority decisions, and Wayne D. Angell, a Fed governor and contrarian who has taken stands that have earned him first the label of a dove, later that of a hawk.
Mr. Mullins, who is 45, and Mr. Hoskins, who is 50, were children in the prosperous, low-inflation Eisenhower era and came of age in the restless 1960's. Mr. Angell, who is 61, was raised in the Depression and Dust Bowl years on a Kansas farm and was a wheat farmer and state legislator. He taught economics at a Baptist college before becoming a central banker.
At the Fed, the new consensus generally favors the kind of steady-as-she-goes, hard-money policies that central bankers pursued back in the Eisenhower and Kennedy years, when inflation rarely crept over 2 percent and home mortgage rates stayed south of 6 percent.
"I don't think there are broad philosophical differences at the Fed," Mr. Mullins said. Debates mostly concern assessments of the economy and how best to implement monetary policy, not goals or the long-run course that has been set.
The new consensus, heavily influenced by recent academic research and modern theories of finance and rational expectations, is a far cry from the one that dominated the 1970's, when the Fed was supposed to smooth out the ups and downs of the American economy, and began to crumble in the 80's. In practical terms, that policy meant slamming on the brakes by raising interest rates when the economy boomed and inflation took off and hitting the monetary accelerator by lowering interest rates when the economy started to slump and unemployment rose.
In the new consensus, inflation is Enemy No. 1. "There is widespread agreement that stable prices are at all times the goal of monetary policy," said H. Erich Heinemann, chief economist at Ladenburg, Thalmann, a brokerage. Some credit the outspokenness of Mr. Hoskins and Mr. Angell on price stability for the fact that most F.O.M.C. members, including Mr. Mullins, now support legislation mandating that Fed policy pursue near-zero inflation.
The consensus also embraces the idea that "monetary policy should focus on maximizing long-term economic growth," as Mr. Angell puts it. It holds that economic growth comes from an anti-inflation policy.
The Fed's change in thinking is being crafted within the purview of the F.O.M.C., which consists of Mr. Greenspan and the six other Fed governors, plus the presidents of the 12 regional Federal Reserve banks, who share four votes.
Influential members, notably Mr. Mullins, Mr. Angell and Mr. Hoskins, shape the consensus through presentations at the meetings. They give speeches, have their staffs conduct research and write scholarly papers, write papers of their own and circulate comments on colleagues' work. The three are also considered adept at interacting with their colleagues both in the office and socially.
The idea that the Fed could let inflation creep up a little in order to bring down unemployment was a policy staple of the 1960's. The tandem rise of inflation and unemployment in the 1970's killed that thought. "Not only do I think there is no long-term trade-off between inflation and growth, but I think there is no short-term trade-off either," said Mr. Mullins. "If we drove down short-term interest rates aggressively now, we would drive up long-term rates, making the recession longer."
That inflation is ultimately fueled by excessive money growth is a monetarist notion that is now all but gospel within the Fed. Mr. Mullins, Mr. Hoskins and Mr. Angell -- as well as Mr. Greenspan -- all agree that the Fed should gradually lower the growth of M2, the broad measure of the money supply, to roughly the same rate as the growth of the economy's productive capacity.
Matching deeds to words, the Fed has nudged down its targets for money supply growth since the mid-1980's. Actual growth in the past four years has averaged half the pace of the prior four and month-to-month zigs and zags have shrunk sharply. "The focus on M2 targets is indicative of more systematic policies and less fine-tuning than in the past," said John B. Taylor of the President's Council of Economic Advisors.
The big test, of course, is whether inflation and long-term interest rates come down as the recession ends. Fed officials hope that will be the payoff of the past four years' policies. At the moment, long-term interest rates still reflect investors' worries that inflation will stay stuck at around the 4 percent rate of the past few years.
If in the past Fed policy debates could be framed in terms of monetary doves pitting themselves against monetary hawks, in the Greenspan Fed the doves are gone. Now, it's various breeds of anti-inflation hawk.
In one debate, monetarists like Mr. Hoskins argue that monetary policy should not be geared to the dollar's foreign-exchange value. Others, like Gary Stern, president of the Minneapolis Fed, favor fixed exchange rates, which means coordination of monetary policy with other countries.
A more basic issue concerns how much the Fed should give up its discretion over setting its goals and strategies. Reflecting a consensus in academia, a growing minority within the Fed, including Mr. Hoskins, believes that monetary policy should be conducted as much as possible according to rules. "Rules let the public judge whether the Fed is doing what it says it is. That increases credibility," said Art Rolnick, research director at the Minneapolis Fed.
But there is dissent. Mr. Mullins, for example, does not believe that any simple rule can work for long. Gerald Corrigan, president of the New York Fed and as such the most powerful of the regional bank presidents, believes that future crises are unpredictable and that the Fed cannot afford to limit its options.
Photograph Wielding influence, from left: Wayne D. Angell (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times), W. Lee Hoskins (Barney Taxel for The New York Times); David W. Mullins Jr. of the Federal Open Market Committee (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times) (pg. 1); Wayne D. Angell; W. Lee Hoskins; David W. Mullins Jr. (pg. 6)
Graph "Inflation Settles Down ...," tracks percent change in the consumer price index, excluding food and energy, 1960-1990 and detail of changes by month for latest 12 months (Source: Department of Labor); "... As the Fed Squeezes Money Growth," tracks percent change in M2, a measure of the amount of money in the United States economy, 1960-1990 (Source: Federal Reserve) (pg. 6)

Subject: CREDIT; UNITED STATES ECONOMY; PRICES

Location: UNITED STATES

People: NASAR, SYLVIA ANGELL, WAYNE HOSKINS, W LEE MULLINS, DAVID W JR GREENSPAN, ALAN (CHMN)

Company / organization: Name: Federal Reserve Board; NAICS: 921130; SIC: 9300

Title: The New Intellectuals at the Fed

Country of publication: United States, New York, N.Y.

Publication subject: General Interest Periodicals--United States

ISSN: 03624331

CODEN: NYTIAO

BUSINESS DIARY/SEPTEMBER 9-14

Author: Myerson, Allen R

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]16 Sep 1990: A.2.

Mr. Greenspan urged House Banking Committee members on Thursday to limit their plans for higher deposit insurance premiums. Full text: LEAD:
THE ECONOMY

Preventing Another Banking Crisis

On one side of the tables sat lawmakers terrified that they would be blamed for another Federal bailout. On the other sat the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alan Greenspan, afraid that Congress would hurt the banks more than help them.

THE ECONOMY

Preventing Another Banking Crisis

On one side of the tables sat lawmakers terrified that they would be blamed for another Federal bailout. On the other sat the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alan Greenspan, afraid that Congress would hurt the banks more than help them. Mr. Greenspan urged House Banking Committee members on Thursday to limit their plans for higher deposit insurance premiums. The General Accounting Office had warned two days before that bank failures might deplete the Federal insurance fund. Better to cushion losses by having banks bolster their capital than by making them contribute more to the fund, Mr. Greenspan said. Some analysts agree, saying that as much as risky lending and a weak economy have buffeted the banks, they remain far more healthy than the savings and loans. Arthur P. Soter, an analyst at Morgan Stanley, said politicians risk compounding the banks' problems: ''You keep raising these insurance payments,'' he said, ''and ultimately you're going to undermine their safety and soundness.''

More Pressure on Oil Prices

Word that the Bush Administration might dip into strategic oil reserves failed to keep prices from rising last week. Energy Secretary James D. Watkins said on Thursday that shortages might require the release of some supplies. But on Friday came news that Iraqi troops stormed the French Embassy in Kuwait. Traders who saw an increasing threat of war bid oil up to $31.76 a barrel on Friday, for a gain of $1.72 on the week.

Inflation Protection

This time, some economists say, higher oil prices might not ignite rapid inflation. Last week's report that producer prices rose 1.3 percent in August was troubling, but no surprise. Higher energy prices were to blame; without food and energy, wholesale inflation rose just three-tenths of 1 percent. Declines in retail sales and industrial production had some economists wondering whether producers will be able to pass on their higher energy costs. In the 1970's, a roaring economy turned an oil crisis into high, persistent inflation. ''This time, you have a very, very weak level of economic activity,'' said Steven R. Ricchiuto, chief economist at Barclays de Zoete Wedd Securities. Others fear that several categories of services - health care, education - might keep consumer prices rising even if wholesale prices settle down. As Lawrence Krohn, an economist at Shearson Lehman Hutton, said, ''The overall inflation picture is not nearly so bright as the core P.P.I. picture alone.''

Philadelphia Finances

A statue of Benjamin Franklin still looks out on Philadelphia from a perch on City Hall, but his city has cast aside his thrifty ways. After running deficits since 1988, the city failed last week to arrange $375 million in temporary financing to stave off default. As city and state politicians feud, Philadelphia faces drastic cuts in most services, and insolvency by the end of the year.

In Brief

The New York Stock Exchange approved limited after-hours trading for the first time in its history, initiating a program intended to establish 24-hour activity by the year 2000.

The Senate plans to vote next week on a bill to require that auto makers achieve an average efficiency of 40 miles per gallon by the year 2001. The Administration says the bill would force the auto makers to sell smaller, less safe cars. But many lawmakers say the fuel savings are essential.

COMPANIES

Re-regulating Cable Television

The House of Representatives voted unanimously last week to restore some regulation and competition to cable television. During the Reagan era, viewers complained that cable companies rapidly raised their charges, exploiting their local monopolies and exclusive rights to popular programs. The legislation gives the Federal Communications Commission power to roll back rates, and bans programming contracts that prevent competition. Advisers to President Bush have threatened a veto.

S.& L. Deal Doesn't Pass Muster

Federal regulators blocked the $3 billion sale of the Columbia Savings and Loan Association's ''junk bond'' portfolio to a Canadian investment partnership that planned to have Columbia lend 90 percent of the price. The Office of Thrift Supervision said Columbia could get a better, preferably all-cash, deal.

In Brief

ARCO agreed to pay $287 million last week to settle a 13-year lawsuit over royalties on North Slope oil owned by the state of Alaska. Other oil companies face a trial next year.

New York City Council President Andrew Stein has proclaimed Sept. 26 Accountant Appreciation Day. ''New York City is composed of caring, giving people,'' he said, with accountants among them.

INTERNATIONAL

Yeltsin Seizes the Lead

He was at the vanguard of reform; now he is at the rear. President Mikhail S. Gorbachev's endorsement on Tuesday of a plan for free markets in 500 days came so grudgingly and late that he can claim little credit or capacity to lead. He was pulled both ways. His own economic adviser, Stanislav S. Shatalin, led the group that drew up the plan. But Mr. Gorbachev's Prime Minister proposed a more cautious course, limiting private ownership and maintaining state control of prices. The same day that Mr. Gorbachev sided with Mr. Shatalin, Boris N. Yeltsin, the President of the Russian Republic, regained the initiative by rushing the program through his Parliament. The vote: 251 to 1. ''Yeltsin just forced the issue,'' said Joe Tragert, an economist at the Planecon consulting firm. And Mr. Gorbachev has little choice but to go along.

Cigarette Shortage

With cigarette shortages provoking riots and demonstrations, the Soviets are sending in RJR Nabisco and Philip Morris. The companies agreed on Thursday to ship 34 billion cigarettes, which represents more than a month's supply. The need is so urgent that both producers might transport some loads by air freight. Marlboro cigarettes, from Philip Morris, are virtually legal tender in the Soviet Union; just ask any cabby whether he wants American cigarettes or rubles.

The Soviet Share of the Pie

It took truckers from Finland, wine from Hungary and a contractor from London to bring American pizza to Moscow. Pizza Hut, a unit of Pepsico Inc., opened its first two restaurants in the Soviet Union on Tuesday with an investment of $10 million. The chain's executives say they actually receive 60 percent of their food and service supplies from Soviet sources. They also say they will be able to use this approach across the Soviet Union. The McDonald's Corporation spent more than $50 million to arrange the production of its own beef patties, milk and buns.

Building Trade Ties With Mexico

As economic alliances coalesce in Europe and Asia, President Bush is rushing to cement a regional trade agreement of his own. And what better time than an energy crisis to snuggle up to Mexico, which sells about 30 percent of its oil to the United States? Administration officials said last week that the President will ask Congress to let him negotiate a broad free-trade agreement with Mexico, like the one between the United States and Canada. President Carlos Salinas de Gortari of Mexico has already cut some tariffs from 100 percent to 10 percent - effectively reversing his country's historic opposition to free trade. The Mexicans have pledged to produce more oil, and American companies expect to sell them more oil equipment.

Japanese Film Deficit

They might not buy American cars, but American movies are always welcome. The Japanese remain the largest overseas market for United States films. Fashions and fads in Japan tend to absorb everyone or no one, so movies already certified as hits in the United States do best there. Tom Cruise, and Indiana Jones, are national heroes, and the popularity of ''Gone With the Wind'' lingers, with many Japanese products adopting antebellum themes. American film producers say the Japanese market is still young. But they are confident that better promotion and more theaters and screens will attract far more viewers. Kyoko Hirano, director of the Japan Society film series in New York, says Japanese films in the United States have a much more limited audience: ''The Japanese import 100 Hollywood films and the Americans import just one.''

Correction: September 23, 1990, Sunday, Late Edition - Final
A report on the Business Diary page last Sunday about Philadelphia's finances misidentified the statue on top of City Hall. It is of William Penn, the city's founder, not of Benjamin Franklin.

World Markets; Where to Gain From the Dollar's Loss

Author: Fuerbringer, Jonathan

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]24 June 1990: A.12.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427684910?accountid=11752

Abstract: This view of a weakening dollar gives investors a fairly attractive opportunity. Some analysts now expect the Tokyo stock market, as measured by the Nikkei 225 Index, to rally to between 35,000 and 40,000 by the end of the year, a remarkable rebound from the 30 percent decline in the first months of this year. At 40,000, the Nikkei would be at an all-time high. Those who feel the United States economy is slowing while West Germany's and Japan's continue to grow expect the dollar to fall some against marks, yen and other currencies. With moderating growth in Japan and low inflation, and with stronger growth in West Germany, these economies are more attractive than a slowing United States with inflation stubbornly holding around 5 percent.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=World+Markets%3B+Where+to+Gain+From+the+Dollar%27s+Loss&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1990-06-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.12&amp;au=Fuerbringer%2C+Jonathan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: While the dollar has been relatively tame this year, it has still raised the returns of some Americans who invested away from Wall Street.
While the dollar has been relatively tame this year, it has still raised the returns of some Americans who invested away from Wall Street.
And if forecasters who think the dollar will decline later this year are right, this could be a good time to invest in stock markets abroad - in Britain, West Germany and Japan - that look as though they have a chance to rally in the second half of the year. A weaker dollar later this year would give investors more for their money when stronger pounds, yen, marks or other foreign currencies are turned back into dollars.
In the first half of 1990, this exchange-rate gain - because of a weakening dollar - has accounted for most or all of the profits from several European stock markets and has mitigated the losses in others.
A 9.5 percent rise in the Swiss franc against the dollar accounted for most of the 11.9 percent gain in dollar terms from the Swiss stock market through last week, according to the Financial Times World Indicies. The market is up only 2.2 percent this year. This is the first time in two years that Americans investing in Switzerland have received foreign-exchange gains. In 1988, a 10 percent gain in the market was turned into a 7 percent loss because of a rise in the dollar. Last year, a much smaller rise in the dollar chipped a 23.7 percent gain in the stock market down to 21.4 percent. The investor in Britain also has been helped this year by the recent weakening of the dollar against the pound. Through last week, the London market was down 2 percent for the year. But with a 7.5 percent gain against the dollar, the dollar investor has more than a 5 percent gain. In Spain, a 4 percent fall in the market is cut to 1 percent when the stronger peseta is turned into dollars.
In the case of Japan, the reverse has occurred. A stronger dollar has made the sharp losses in Tokyo even more painful because the yen now buys fewer dollars. The Tokyo market, measured by the Financial Times index, fell 21 percent through last week, but the foreign-exchange factor increased this loss to 26.5 percent in dollars.
Unfortunately, forecasters are divided on what direction the dollar will take the rest of this year. But it looks as though the variables favor a weaker dollar, although there is no expectation of a collapse.
Those who feel the United States economy is slowing while West Germany's and Japan's continue to grow expect the dollar to fall some against marks, yen and other currencies. With moderating growth in Japan and low inflation, and with stronger growth in West Germany, these economies are more attractive than a slowing United States with inflation stubbornly holding around 5 percent.
If the Federal Reserve Board decides in the next few months to lower interest rates because the economy is growing too slowly, the dollar's decline could quicken. Analysts already expect the West German Bundesbank to raise interest rates later this year to curb any inflationary spurt from the economic union of the two Germanys. This would make the mark more attractive to investors.
The dollar, which now commands 1.6745 marks, could drop to 1.50. Now worth 154.85 yen, it could fall into the 140's by some estimates. The pound, although it is at $1.7335 and its high for the year, could move even higher if the euphoria over a British move to join the European Monetary System continues.
This view of a weakening dollar gives investors a fairly attractive opportunity. Some analysts now expect the Tokyo stock market, as measured by the Nikkei 225 Index, to rally to between 35,000 and 40,000 by the end of the year, a remarkable rebound from the 30 percent decline in the first months of this year. At 40,000, the Nikkei would be at an all-time high.
The market in Germany, which has fallen about 10 percent since reaching a record high at the end of March, does have the potential to recoup or even move higher if the jitters about monetary union subside after the two German economies are unified in July. This major European market has offered the smallest boost from foreign-exchange rates so far this year.

U.S. FACES STRAINS FROM GLOBAL JUMP IN INTEREST RATES

Author: UCHITELLE, LOUIS

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]25 Jan 1990: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427508749?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''It makes people nervous,'' said Nigel Gault, senior economist at DRI-McGraw-Hill, a research organization. ''There had been an expectation that interest rates would continue to slide down, and suddenly people have woken up to the idea that maybe they won't.'' Some American economists argue that the Bundesbank and the Bank of Japan are overreacting. They contend that inflation is not all that big a problem and they predict that the two central banks will back down soon on interest rates, which are now unusually close to American rates. (Yields on 10-year government notes range from 8.3 percent in the United States to 6.3 percent in Japan.) ''Our current view is that Japan has a case of inflation jitters,'' said Nicholas Sargen, the chief of bond research at Salomon Brothers. ''We can't identify what has changed so much in Japan in recent weeks to trigger such a jump in rates.'' Some policy makers at the Federal Reserve show signs of concern that the economy might be rebounding. Two of the central bank's governors, Manuel Johnson and Wayne Angell, have said they see little likelihood of a recession ahead. And Robert L. Parry, president of the Frederal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, said yesterday that the West Germans and Japanese are only partly responsible for the rise in long-term American rates. ''It seems to me that a prime additional suspect putting pressure on rates is heightened inflationary concerns in the United States,'' he said.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+FACES+STRAINS+FROM+GLOBAL+JUMP+IN+INTEREST+RATES&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1990-01-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=UCHITELLE%2C+LOUIS&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: Interest rates in Japan and West Germany are suddenly surging, in the process dragging up American rates at what most economists say is just the wrong moment for the nation's fragile economy.
Interest rates in Japan and West Germany are suddenly surging, in the process dragging up American rates at what most economists say is just the wrong moment for the nation's fragile economy.
Rarely has the global economy forced itself so vividly on the United States. West Germany and Japan, currently the engines propelling the world's economic growth, are trying to curb inflation. But their chief tool, higher long-term interest rates, is spilling over into the weak American economy and that could be a hammer blow, further slowing growth.
''This is a terribly inconvenient time to have rates pulled up,'' said Lawrence Veit, an economist at Brown Brothers Harriman & Company.
Free Flow of Money

Experts say the huge Federal deficit has left the American economy hostage to a continual flow of investment funds from abroad.
In today's international economy, with so few legal or practical obstacles to the flow of money across national boundaries, borrowers in this country must match the higher interest rates being offered abroad. Otherwise, savers throughout the world will pursue higher returns by buying the bonds issued in Japan and West Germany.
Although the market's spontaneous forces, not the Federal Reserve, have pushed up long-term rates, the central bank is nevertheless on the spot. The Federal Reserve could stand pat, allowing the market flows to take their course. But the higher interest rates could well nudge the vulnerable American economy into recession.
Higher interest rates have spread throughout the money markets. In the United States, mortgage rates have rebounded in the last month, just when the housing industry is weak. Many corporations, already heavily in debt, are facing higher monthly payments, which would further cut into their profits. Car loans might be pushed up as the higher rates force a General Motors or a Beneficial Finance to pay more for the money they lend to consumers.
'It Makes People Nervous'

As in any market, expectations are often self-fulfilling. Lenders who expect interest rates to rise will demand a higher rate before committing funds.
''It makes people nervous,'' said Nigel Gault, senior economist at DRI-McGraw-Hill, a research organization. ''There had been an expectation that interest rates would continue to slide down, and suddenly people have woken up to the idea that maybe they won't.''
A month ago, with the rate on 30-year Treasury bonds at 7.84 percent, no mainstream American economist was predicting its rise to 8.4 percent or forecasting that 30-year mortgage rates would be back to 10 percent after being closer to 9 percent in December. But the steps taken by West Germany and Japan made that chain of events inevitable, economists now say.
This is the first time in more than a decade that American economic growth is significantly less vigorous than in West Germany and Japan. With the robust growth, prices in Germany and Japan have climbed at rates deemed alarming in those inflation-resistant nations.
The West German inflation rate almost tripled last year, to 3 percent, and the prospect for 1990 is for more strong demand. Eastern Europeans are pouring into West Germany at the rate of 2,000 a day, adding to the national demand for goods.
Boom Times in West Germany

What is more, a capital spending boom in Europe has drawn heavily on German products, and the country's exports are up strongly. And now the metal workers union, seeking a greater share of the profits, is demanding what amounts to a 15 percent wage increase. The union represents more than 10 percent of the nation's organized labor.
In such a situation, the Bundesbank pushed up interest rates, the classic central bank strategy for curbing an expansion that threatens to increase inflation. The higher rates are intended to raise the cost of the loans that finance spending by consumers and businesses.
Similarly, the inflation rate in Japan more than doubled last year, to 2.5 percent, and workers are seeking a 6 percent wage increase in nationwide negotiations now under way. Rising land prices also were a problem, and, inexplicably, the yen weakened a bit against the dollar, perhaps because of the uncertain outcome of the national election next month. In this situation, the Bank of Japan on Dec. 25 raised its key discount rate by half a percentage point. The move helped to push up 10-year notes to more than 6.3 percent, from 5.5 percent.
Some American economists argue that the Bundesbank and the Bank of Japan are overreacting. They contend that inflation is not all that big a problem and they predict that the two central banks will back down soon on interest rates, which are now unusually close to American rates. (Yields on 10-year government notes range from 8.3 percent in the United States to 6.3 percent in Japan.) ''Our current view is that Japan has a case of inflation jitters,'' said Nicholas Sargen, the chief of bond research at Salomon Brothers. ''We can't identify what has changed so much in Japan in recent weeks to trigger such a jump in rates.''
A Different Viewpoint

A few economists in this country hold that the rising rates abroad are fortuitous, coming at a time when inflation again threatens the American economy.
These economists argue that recent data - particularly an upturn in industrial production and in the Producer Price Index, an important inflation measure - indicate that the long slowdown in American economic growth ended late last year. As a result, they say, the annual inflation rate, which declined from about 5.5 percent in early 1989 to 4.5 percent by year-end, will be on the rise again by springtime. So, in their view, the higher long-term interest rates forced on the nation by the West Germans and the Japanese will help to arrest the inflation.
Some policy makers at the Federal Reserve show signs of concern that the economy might be rebounding. Two of the central bank's governors, Manuel Johnson and Wayne Angell, have said they see little likelihood of a recession ahead. And Robert L. Parry, president of the Frederal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, said yesterday that the West Germans and Japanese are only partly responsible for the rise in long-term American rates. ''It seems to me that a prime additional suspect putting pressure on rates is heightened inflationary concerns in the United States,'' he said.
Awaiting the G.N.P. Report

But it remains uncertain whether the economy is getting a second wind. Government data measuring developments in recent weeks are still too sparse to tell. The first major assessment will come on Friday, when the Commerce Department releases its initial estimate of what happened to the gross national product in the fourth quarter.
Most economists guess that the G.N.P., which measures the total value of the nation's goods and services, grew by less than 1 percent from September through December. Growth of 1 percent or more would feed the economic-rebound viewpoint while a growth rate of less than five-tenths of one percent would be viewed as evidence that the economy remains very weak.
Whatever the state of the economy, the mechanism through which rising interest rates abroad affect American rates is causing a problem of its own: the increased competition for funds. The United States Treasury in recent years has counted on Japanese buyers to purchase roughly one-third of the long-term securities that the Government issues periodically to finance its budget deficit.
The next major auction of these securities is scheduled for early February, and the question is whether American rates will be high enough to attract not only Japanese investors, but Americans as well. Their alternative is to purchase Japanese or European securities. Salomon, in fact, recommended to clients this week that they consider this possibility.
Looking Abroad to Invest

''For the first time in two years, we have told customers that the Japanese bond market may be attractive to U.S. investors,'' Mr. Sargen said. If they agree, then rates on the securities auctioned in early February will have to rise above their current levels to draw investors.
But more than just rates influence investors' decisions. Foreign investors might not purchase Treasuries, for example, if they believe that the dollar will decline in value after they make the purchases. Currently, the dollar is just beginning to fall against the yen and has been losing ground since last fall against the mark. On the other hand, the Japanese might invest here for political reasons, because their Government encourages them to do so.
Right now, neither Japanese nor American nor German investors are buying many bonds, as the selloffs in all three marketplaces in recent days indicate. Most investors are holding their funds in cash, waiting to see what will happen, Mr. Sargen said.

Subject: STOCKS AND BONDS; BOND PRICES AND TRADING VOLUME; UNITED STATES ECONOMY

People: Sargen, Nicholas UCHITELLE, LOUIS

Europe Taking a Lead in Growth

Author: STEVEN PROKESCH, Special to The New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]15 Jan 1990: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427531403?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''It's very important for the world economy and the United States that Europe in conjunction with Japan should exhibit stronger economic growth,'' said George A. Magnus, chief international economist at Warburg Securities in London. ''To a degree, Europe is fulfilling a locomotive role as the U.S. economy slows down.'' ''There's a lot of dynamism in Europe,'' said Norbert A. Walter, chief economist at Deutsche Bank, West Germany's largest bank. ''Nineteen ninety-two and the Eastern Europe reforms have created the perception around the globe that Europe is on the move, that there is a renaissance in Europe.'' Heavy investment from companies based outside the community is one big reason this growth is so strong. ''Enticed by both the opportunities and the fears that outsiders may be prevented from exploiting them, companies from outside the European Community - especially the Japanese, the Swedes, the Finns and the Australians - are continuing to pour money into the community,'' said Martin Waldenstrom, president of Booz Allen Acquisition Services, a unit of Booz Allen & Hamilton, the consulting firm.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Europe+Taking+a+Lead+in+Growth&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1990-01-15&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=STEVEN+PROKESCH%2C+Special+to+The+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: The benefits already flowing from the European Community's continuing effort to create a single market by 1992 and the changes in Eastern Europe are prompting economists to predict that Western Europe's economic growth will exceed that of the United States in the 1990's.
The benefits already flowing from the European Community's continuing effort to create a single market by 1992 and the changes in Eastern Europe are prompting economists to predict that Western Europe's economic growth will exceed that of the United States in the 1990's.
For much of the 1980's, the fast-growing American economy was the envy of Western Europeans, who seemed to think that the high unemployment and sluggish growth plaguing most of their nations were insurmountable problems.
No longer. Last year, the economy of all Western European nations outpaced that of the United States. It grew 3.5 percent, while the United States economy grew about 3 percent.
Economists expect growth in Western Europe to continue to outpace that in the United States for at least two years and possibly much longer. While annual growth in the United States is widely expected to average 2.5 percent in the 1990's, many economists expect Western Europe to grow by 3 to 3.5 percent a year.
'A Locomotive Role'

''It's very important for the world economy and the United States that Europe in conjunction with Japan should exhibit stronger economic growth,'' said George A. Magnus, chief international economist at Warburg Securities in London. ''To a degree, Europe is fulfilling a locomotive role as the U.S. economy slows down.''
With the 12 nations of the European Community moving ahead with their program to create a barrier-free common market by 1992 and with Eastern Europe moving toward democracy and market-based economies, there is a palpable excitement in Europe - a feeling that the Age of Europe may be dawning.
''There's a lot of dynamism in Europe,'' said Norbert A. Walter, chief economist at Deutsche Bank, West Germany's largest bank. ''Nineteen ninety-two and the Eastern Europe reforms have created the perception around the globe that Europe is on the move, that there is a renaissance in Europe.''
For a couple of years there have been signs that optimism is replacing the pessimism that had long gripped Europe. But with unemployment in Europe remaining high and inflation inching upward, many economists still feared that the expansion would lose steam.
About the only major nation where that has happened is Britain, whose Government's use of high interest rates to combat an inflation rate of 7.7 percent has brought growth nearly to a standstill.
With West Germany and other major European countries maintaining a relatively tight monetary policy, most economists are now cautiously optimistic that inflation, which hit a plateau in the second half of last year, will remain under control. Annual inflation in the European Community could average 4 percent between 1990 and 1995, down from 5.4 percent last year, said Paul Cockle, chief European economist at DRI/McGraw-Hill, the economic consulting firm.
Reflecting the cheerful new outlook, economists have managed to find a virtue in Western Europe's high unemployment rate, which has been falling only slowly. The rate -now about 9 percent - means Western Europe does not face the kinds of limits on growth confronting the United States, where unemployment is 5.3 percent, they say.
In a turnaround from just a year ago, some economists say it is possible that unemployment could fall to 6 or 7 percent by the mid-1990's. But any sizable reduction will require sustained economic growth of 3 to 4 percent, they say.
Boom in Capital Spending

One big reason for the current optimism is the capital-spending boom that has been under way in the European Community for several years. ''By helping to make sure that demand does not outstrip supply, the buoyant investment is an important factor in a continued noninflationary expansion,'' said David A. Currie, an economist at the London Business School.
While the spending boom has been slowing, it shows no signs of petering out. ''We had expected a greater moderation than there has been,'' Mr. Cockle said.
This spending, economists say, is being fueled by a combination of excitement and fear that the 1992 program has created.
Some companies are spending heavily to exploit the opportunities for expansion that they think the single market will offer them. Others are doing so to defend themselves against the increased competition they expect.
Investment in machinery and equipment has been growing at 9 percent annually in West Germany, even more in France and more than 20 percent in Spain and Portugal, Dr. Walter of Deutsche Bank noted.
Money From Outside

Heavy investment from companies based outside the community is one big reason this growth is so strong. ''Enticed by both the opportunities and the fears that outsiders may be prevented from exploiting them, companies from outside the European Community - especially the Japanese, the Swedes, the Finns and the Australians - are continuing to pour money into the community,'' said Martin Waldenstrom, president of Booz Allen Acquisition Services, a unit of Booz Allen & Hamilton, the consulting firm.
Mr. Waldenstrom, who recently completed a study for the European Community, said American direct investment in the community, which soared in the mid-1980's, has declined since its peak in 1987.
The European Commission, the community's executive branch, estimated earlier that the 1992 program could add as much as one percentage point to the community's medium-term economic growth.
The surprise is that the rise has come so soon. Given the heavy capital spending, some economists now say that 1992 has already added a total of two to three percentage points over the last three years to the European Community's economic growth and that the total extra growth from the 1992 program could exceed the commission's prediction.
'Clear Change' in Behavior

''There has been a clear change in European economic behavior,'' Robert Z. Lawrence, a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution in Washington, said of the new optimism.
While it may be too early to pronounce Communism dead in Eastern Europe, the widespread hope is that the leadership role that Western Europe would play in rebuilding Eastern Europe will take up where 1992 leaves off in keeping Western Europe's expansion rolling along.
West Germany, already the economic locomotive of Western Europe (its gross national product represents 25 percent of the European Community's), is expected to reap the greatest benefit from any rebuilding of Eastern Europe. Mr. Walter predicted that the portion of West Germany's exports going to Eastern European countries could soar to 15 percent of its total within 10 years, from 5 percent now.
While such forecasts may be too optimistic, the flood of immigrants from the East is already giving a boost to West Germany's economy. The influx of 1.3 million ethnic Germans from Eastern Europe and the Soviet Union in the last three years is adding one-quarter to three-quarters of a percentage point to West Germany's gross national product, economists figure.
Help for Labor Shortage

Most of the immigrants are younger people who are in their prime spending years and who can help alleviate the shortage of skilled workers in West German industry. In addition, it is already clear that the immigration will force the Government to increase social spending to house the new arrivals.
To keep West Germany's inflation, now about 3 percent, under control, the Bundesbank, the country's central bank, is expected to continue to pursue its tight monetary policy. But economists do not expect it to slow the economy much. They note that besides the stimulus from the immigration, a sizable tax cut went into effect on Jan. 1.
With many economists expecting this tight monetary policy and looser fiscal policy to continue well into the 1990's, the result could be a higher value for the West German mark and an increase in imports that would stimulate the economies of West Germany's trading partners.
''What the capitalist economies could never persuade the West Germans to do, the demise of Communism is achieving - a more expansive policy,'' said Mr. Lawrence of Brookings. Implications for the U.S. What does all this mean for the United States?
Exports to Western Europe will undoubtedly continue to rise. A surge in such exports - to an estimated $100 billion last year from $61.6 billion in 1986 - has largely eliminated America's trade deficit with Western Europe.
Most economists note that this amount of exports is still tiny compared with the size of the $5 trillion American economy, and will not spell the difference between growth and recession.
The growing attractiveness of Europe - both East and West - as a place to invest could pose some threats to the United States. For one thing, the United States may find it increasingly difficult to attract the foreign capital it needs to finance its big trade and budget deficits.
'Pressure on the Dollar'

As a result, ''it may end up putting a lot of downward pressure on the dollar,'' said Charles R. Bean, an economist at the London School of Economics. That, he said, could translate into higher inflation and a drop in the standard of living for Americans.
If disarmament continues, reducing the importance of United States military power, American influence in the world could continue to slip because of its weakening economic muscle. The back seat America seems to be taking in the West's response to the events in Eastern Europe reflects this decline, political analysts and economists say.

Economic Scene; Business Cycle's New Stability

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]05 Jan 1990: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427505998?accountid=11752

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Economic+Scene%3B+Business+Cycle%27s+New+Stability&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1990-01-05&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: IS the business cycle dead? The United States economy has now completed seven straight years of expansion, the longest in its peacetime history. Most economists foresee no recession in 1990 or 1991, and 1992 will be another Presidential election year, when the odds tip strongly against recession. Japan, West Germany and most of the other capitalist countries of Europe and Asia are also on a long upward roll, with no end in sight.
IS the business cycle dead? The United States economy has now completed seven straight years of expansion, the longest in its peacetime history. Most economists foresee no recession in 1990 or 1991, and 1992 will be another Presidential election year, when the odds tip strongly against recession. Japan, West Germany and most of the other capitalist countries of Europe and Asia are also on a long upward roll, with no end in sight. It is time to consider whether the capitalist system has experienced a fundamental change in its stability, as important in its own way as the decay of the Communist system.
To be sure, plenty of people worry that the long spell of stable growth may still end with a bang, conceivably this year. Ravi Batra's latest best seller is called ''Surviving the Great Depression of 1990.'' But the crash of October 1987, and the market plunge of Friday, Oct. 13, 1989, have come and gone without stopping the expansion of the real economy.
Those economists who have been warning for years that the United States is bound to suffer a ''hard landing'' actually demonstrate how resilient the American economy is. In saying just that, Lawrence Summers of Harvard, who was Michael S. Dukakis's chief economic adviser in the 1988 campaign, warns that the wolf could still come. Writing in The Financial Times this week, Professor Summers said: ''In the end, the wolf ate the boy. It would be a mistake to bet too strongly on the American economy in general or the dollar in particular over the next few years.''
This is not a partisan position. Speaking at a news conference in Atlanta last week, Michael J. Boskin, President Bush's chief economic adviser, foresaw moderate growth in 1990 but warned that the United States faces the danger ''not of a heart attack, but cancer'' - a gradual wearing away of its economic strength from inadequate savings and continuous dependence on foreign savings to finance domestic budget deficits, trade deficits and private investment.
Nevertheless, why has the danger of an economic ''heart attack'' ebbed? And why have those economists who expected a collapse of the real economy erred, year after year, despite the instability of financial markets and the dollar?
One source of error, common since the development of national income accounts and the heavy focus on the national economy, has been to see the United States economy as ''closed.'' In this view, the principal determinants of national income are domestic consumption, private domestic investment and government spending - with net exports a small tail incapable of wagging a very big dog.
In the postwar years the United States has increasingly become an ''open economy,'' as international flows of goods and services - and, far more important, capital - have grown to shape the national economy, thus far toward greater real stability.
The shortage of private domestic savings, aggravated by the Federal budget deficit, has been covered by capital inflow from abroad, thereby sustaining business investment and keeping the American economy growing. Of course, this has meant the transfer of United States assets to foreign hands. That disturbs many nationalists, but the immediate consequences for American growth in output, income and employment have been positive. Cutting off that foreign capital inflow now would drive United States interest rates through the roof.
A team of economists at Salomon Brothers, headed by David G. Shulman, contends that the liberalization of financial markets has, paradoxically, increased the volatility of securities and currency markets but has improved the stability of the real economy. The Salomon team concludes that this trend will continue and that the economic climate in the coming decade will still favor financial assets over real assets or cash.
Deregulation, according to this analysis, has enhanced United States growth prospects, ''even though it has not repealed the business cycle.'' Financial market liberalization acts as a ''shock absorber'' for the real economy, with price substituting for quantity in rationing credits. And deregulation means greater labor and capital mobility, attracting resources from weak sectors or regions to strong or growing sectors.
Meanwhile, the Federal Reserve holds to a cautious policy, accepting modest growth in exchange for lower inflation. The Salomon economists predict that disinflation will pave the way for lower interest rates, aided by lower Federal deficits resulting from the scaling back of military spending.
Similarly, Thomas W. Synnott 3d of U.S. Trust predicts that with a smaller Federal deficit relative to G.N.P. and lower inflation, long-term real interest rates on Treasury securities ''could easily move down from the current 3 1/2 to 4 percent range to 1 1/2 to 2 percent, their historic norm.''
Such a cut in the cost of capital would spur the industrial and public investment so urgently needed to enhance United States international competitiveness - a major goal for the 1990's.

Argentina's Chief to Try Charm on U.S.

Author: SHIRLEY CHRISTIAN, Special to The New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 Sep 1989: A.20.

Abstract: ''Fundamentally, what we want to accomplish is a more fluid insertion of Argentina in the international field,'' he said.
