Full text: LEAD: In two and a half months in the presidency, Carlos Saul Menem has woven a kind of magic over the Argentines.
In two and a half months in the presidency, Carlos Saul Menem has woven a kind of magic over the Argentines.
He has played soccer in a packed stadium alongside one of the world's greatest players, Diego Maradona. He has kept up his end of a basketball game with men half his age and two feet taller. He has elegantly danced the tango and the zamba, the folk dance of the Argentine gauchos, or cowboys, on prime-time television.
In his 20-hour days, he has also tamed Argentina's soaring inflation, refilled the Central Bank with dollars and forged an alliance with private enterprise and capitalism that would have troubled the founder of his political party, Juan D. Peron.
Now he hopes to weave some of that magic abroad as he sets out Saturday night for the United States on a mission to convince foreign officials, bankers and investors that Argentina is a serious country and has stopped living, in his words, ''from party to party.''
Mr. Menem said in an interview that on this trip he wants to transmit ''live and direct'' the news that Argentina's finances are improving dramatically, that he is pushing ahead with efforts to sell off or dismantle Government-owned companies and that the doors are open to foreign investors. Inflation Down Sharply
''Fundamentally, what we want to accomplish is a more fluid insertion of Argentina in the international field,'' he said. ''It is well known that we are coming out of a very conflictive situation, with the image of a decadent Argentina, I would say, a chaotic Argentina without future.''
In July, the month in which Mr. Menem succeeded Raul Alfonsin in the presidency, Argentine inflation almost hit 200 percent, just for the month. On an annualized basis, that was equal to any known world record.
The soaring inflation, attributed to huge deficits by state companies and tax evasion in the last months of the Alfonsin presidency, was accompanied by a drop in the foreign reserves to less than $100 million, next to nothing for a country of 32 million people.
Mr. Menem, bringing respected businessmen and free-market economists into his Government, reduced inflation to 38 percent in August through negotiated wage and price accords. It is expected to fall to 7 or 8 percent this month. Reserves have recovered to almost $2 billion, Mr. Menem said today.
But important tests lie ahead in terms of how quickly the Government can move with plans to turn over state companies to private ownership or management and whether it can come out of the unofficial wage and price controls without new inflation or a recession.
The one-week visit, which is to include a meeting with President Bush in Washington on Wednesday, a speech to the United Nations General Assembly on Monday, and meetings with several European foreign ministers in New York, will be the first ever made to the United States by a sitting President from the Justicialist Party, better known as Peronism. Lifting the Shadows
Mr. Menem, who was in the United States several times before he was elected President, said this visit ''will end an old and stupid legend'' about the supposed anti-Americanism of Peronism and ''lift all shadows from relations between the United States and Argentina.''
General Peron, after first coming to power in 1946, created a kind of populist socialism in Argentina, under which the state became the primary motor of the economy and created a vast network of programs and services for the working class that went further than most social welfare systems, including things like direct gifts to children and vacation hotels for laborers and their families.
Mr. Menem, whose partnership with capitalism has pleased many of those who did not vote for him and dismayed many of those who did, denies he is rejecting Peronism's traditional identification with social justice. He now talks of a ''popular free market economy'' as his goal. He says that means ''private enterprise and free trade, with social justice incorporated.''

New Argentine Leaders Move on Economic Crisis

Author: SHIRLEY CHRISTIAN, Special to The New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]10 July 1989: A.6.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427283723?accountid=11752

Abstract: LEAD: As the pomp and ceremony of the presidential inauguration continued into its second day, the Government of President Carlos Saul Menem today announced a plan to attack Argentina's hyperinflation and begin economic recovery.The new Government halved the value of the country's currency against the United States dollar and decreed sharp increases in charges for public services to halt runaway inflation, Economy Minister Miguel As the pomp and ceremony of the presidential inauguration continued into its second day, the Government of President Carlos Saul Menem today announced a plan to attack Argentina's hyperinflation and begin economic recovery.The new Government halved the value of the country's currency against the United States dollar and decreed sharp increases in charges for public services to halt runaway inflation, Economy Minister Miguel Roig announced in a television address.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=New+Argentine+Leaders+Move+on+Economic+Crisis&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1989-07-10&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.6&amp;au=SHIRLEY+CHRISTIAN%2C+Special+to+The+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: As the pomp and ceremony of the presidential inauguration continued into its second day, the Government of President Carlos Saul Menem today announced a plan to attack Argentina's hyperinflation and begin economic recovery.The new Government halved the value of the country's currency against the United States dollar and decreed sharp increases in charges for public services to halt runaway inflation, Economy Minister Miguel
As the pomp and ceremony of the presidential inauguration continued into its second day, the Government of President Carlos Saul Menem today announced a plan to attack Argentina's hyperinflation and begin economic recovery.The new Government halved the value of the country's currency against the United States dollar and decreed sharp increases in charges for public services to halt runaway inflation, Economy Minister Miguel Roig announced in a television address.
With factories closing, foreign reserves almost gone and merchants raising prices 100 percent at a time, Mr. Menem said this was the worst crisis Argentina had ever known and warned that even tougher times lay ahead. In May, after the elections, 15 people died in riots and looting touched off by rising prices.
In his inaugural speech Saturday, Mr. Menem indicated that his Peronist Party was even willing to abandon its tradition of heavy state influence in the economy in hopes of rebuilding it. The official inflation figure was 114.5 percent for the month of June alone. #90-Day Price Freeze Aides to Mr. Roig, a retired business executive, said measures to take effect this week would be limited to trying to slow inflation, ease its effect on wage earners and resume tax collections.
Such questions as management of the $60-billion foreign debt and a promised structural overhaul of deficit-ridden state companies would be left for later, the aides said.
A key part of the package of measures is the freezing of prices for 90 days, a decision that caused dissension within the new Government and produced the withdrawal of Mr. Menem's first appointee for Secretary of Commerce before he could be sworn in. Price controls have a long history of failure in Argentina.
Alberto Albamonte of the Union of the Democratic Center, a party identified with free-market economics, had accepted the Commerce post only to renounce it Friday after losing his argument against price controls.
As details of this debate leaked out last week, merchants began doubling and tripling prices. A merchants group said the cost of bread rose 263 percent. Doubling of Exchange Rate
The foreign exchange rate was set at 650 australs to the dollar, more than double the current official rate and slightly ahead of the black market rate. With this the Government hopes to channel more hard currency into the Central Bank and convince some leading exporters to liquidate crops that they have been holding off the international market because of the gap between the official and black-market exchange rates. The Government hopes to begin to replenish public coffers quickly because of taxes on exports.
The Government was also expected to order that all workers be given a one-time payment of the equivalent of $14 to $20 to help ease the pain of inflation. State workers will also receive increases in wages slightly exceeding the June inflation figure.
Increases of 650 percent were also announced in the prices of gas and electricity.

Economic Scene; Moment of Truth On the Budget

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]24 Feb 1989: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/427072757?accountid=11752

Abstract: Why did the January numbers deal such a sharp blow to the markets? There had been general anticipation that the inflation rate would tick upward this year. ''The only thing new,'' as George Perry of the Brookings Institution said yesterday, ''is that the news is actually happening.'' To be sure, the January report was a bit worse than anticipated. Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, called the rise ''disturbing'' and said it was ''a shade worse'' than he had expected. Particularly disturbing was the related report that weekly earnings jumped by six-tenths of 1 percent in January, after adjusting for inflation and seasonal factors. Without adjustment for inflation, weekly earnings leaped 1.2 percent in January, which translates into an annual rate of 14.4 percent. Instead, Mr. Greenspan has been inching toward tightness while trying, in his deliberately vague, nonconfrontational style, to coax both the Administration and Congress to take stronger action on the budget deficit. This is a tough, maybe hopeless, job, particularly as Mr. [Bush] has stuck to his campaign promises of ''no new taxes,'' a ''strong'' military program and some ''kinder, gentler'' social programs, while putting the overall budget into a ''flexible freeze'' with no increases in Government expenditures above the assumed rate of inflation.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Economic+Scene%3B+Moment+of+Truth+On+the+Budget&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1989-02-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: WITH the six-tenths of 1 percent increase in the Consumer Price Index for January, 7.2 percent at an annual rate, the moment of truth on budgetary policy is fast approaching for the Administration. And the markets clearly sense that the test is coming.
WITH the six-tenths of 1 percent increase in the Consumer Price Index for January, 7.2 percent at an annual rate, the moment of truth on budgetary policy is fast approaching for the Administration. And the markets clearly sense that the test is coming.
Why did the January numbers deal such a sharp blow to the markets? There had been general anticipation that the inflation rate would tick upward this year. ''The only thing new,'' as George Perry of the Brookings Institution said yesterday, ''is that the news is actually happening.''
To be sure, the January report was a bit worse than anticipated. Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, called the rise ''disturbing'' and said it was ''a shade worse'' than he had expected. Particularly disturbing was the related report that weekly earnings jumped by six-tenths of 1 percent in January, after adjusting for inflation and seasonal factors. Without adjustment for inflation, weekly earnings leaped 1.2 percent in January, which translates into an annual rate of 14.4 percent.
That seems highly improbable. Nevertheless, that 1.2 percent jump was due to a rise of six-tenths of 1 percent in both hourly pay and weekly work hours, and the tightness of the labor force would keep the heat under wages and prices if the recent pace of economic expansion continues.
What the markets most fear is a clash between the Administration's budget policy, built on the belief that strong economic growth can and must be sustained, and the Federal Reserve's monetary policy, aimed at preventing inflation.
Mr. Bush has been seeking to play down any conflict between himself and Mr. Greenspan, saying there is nothing the press loves to cook up more than than a fight between the White House and the Fed. The President maintains that any differences with Mr. Greenspan are slight. For his part, the Fed chairman is certainly not spoiling for a fight. In Congressional testimony this week, Mr. Greenspan was at pains to avoid a conflict with the Administration, to praise it wherever he could but not to deny that the Fed was likely to nudge interest rates higher.
Mr. Greenspan would like to avoid a clash between fiscal and monetary policy, if it is avoidable; he has no desire to thrust the economy into recession as a cure for existing or anticipated inflation. Unlike his predecessor, Paul A. Volcker, who had to stop a raging inflation in its tracks, Mr. Greenspan has no ''mandate'' to attack.
Instead, Mr. Greenspan has been inching toward tightness while trying, in his deliberately vague, nonconfrontational style, to coax both the Administration and Congress to take stronger action on the budget deficit. This is a tough, maybe hopeless, job, particularly as Mr. Bush has stuck to his campaign promises of ''no new taxes,'' a ''strong'' military program and some ''kinder, gentler'' social programs, while putting the overall budget into a ''flexible freeze'' with no increases in Government expenditures above the assumed rate of inflation.
That rate was assumed in the Reagan budget and Bush revisions to be only about 3 1/2 percent in 1989 and 1990. The Administration budget, with its optimism on inflation, also assumes steady economic growth at a rate of about 3 1/4 percent, not allowing for inflation. But as the news on inflation unfolds, and interest rates move higher, the markets increasingly find the Administration's budget and economic scenario incredible. The markets would particularly fear a rerun of the fiscal and monetary policies of the late 1960's and 1970's, which eventually sent inflation soaring.
As James J. O'Leary, economic consultant of the United States Trust Company, put it, ''The sharp escalation of inflation and interest rates during the decade ending in 1980-1981 had a lasting and revolutionary effect on investor psychology and upon the money and capital markets.''
And he added that the ''negative spread'' that developed between the cost of funds - interest on deposits - at the savings and loan institutions and the yield on their fixed-rate, long-term investments proved ruinous and lies at the base of the $100 billion-plus disaster that the savings institutions, and the American taxpayers, are facing.
Inflation and soaring interest rates also hurt the life insurance industry, long the steady and primary provider of long-term fixed-rate loans to business, through corporate bonds and commercial mortgages. This helped to create the conditions that gave rise to the ''junk bond'' market.
The Fed's job is to prevent a recurrence of the disastrous combination of higher inflation and higher interest rates. Mr. Greenspan has indicated that he intends to do the job by tightening money and raising interest rates as necessary. However, the Fed has held back from raising the discount rate, for reasons that remain murky but appear to be part of the effort to avoid conflict with the Administration.
All the same, the Fed chairman has continued a moderately restrictive policy and has made clear that, in his view, if the Fed does not arrest inflation by raising interest rates, the financial markets will take alarm and raise interest rates much higher, with far worse consequences for the economy.
The markets themselves are sending out the same warning to the Administration - and to Congress, which seems incapable of acting without Presidential leadership. That is why the moment of budgetary truth is approaching for Mr. Bush.

Iranian Plays Down Revenge

Author: YOUSSEF M. IBRAHIM, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 July 1988: 1.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426893062?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''We suggest that the United States has some additional crimes stored away for Iran, and that is why we do not push for any revenge,'' he said. ''Wise people understand why we do not take revenge.'' ''Compensation is not enough for what happened,'' he said. The hojatolislam said Iran wanted the United States ''to say they are guilty.'' Earlier this week, as Hojatolislam [Hashemi Rafsanjani] sought to encourage international condemnation of the United States and sympathy for Iran, Ayatollah Montazeri, in a message read on the Teheran radio, asked Ayatollah Khomeini to order ''revolutionary forces and resistance cells inside and outside the country to target America's material, political, economic and military interests.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Iranian+Plays+Down+Revenge&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1988-07-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1.1&amp;au=YOUSSEF+M.+IBRAHIM%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: The Speaker of Iran's Parliament said today that his nation would ''not push for any revenge'' against the United States for shooting down a civilian jet carrying 290 people last Sunday.
The Speaker of Iran's Parliament said today that his nation would ''not push for any revenge'' against the United States for shooting down a civilian jet carrying 290 people last Sunday.
The Speaker, Hojatolislam Hashemi Rafsanjani, who is also commander of the armed forces, told thousands of worshipers at Teheran University that further military confrontation with the United States was unwise.
''We suggest that the United States has some additional crimes stored away for Iran, and that is why we do not push for any revenge,'' he said. ''Wise people understand why we do not take revenge.''
Hojatolislam Rafsanjani told a crowd of several thousand who had gathered in an open-air theater that Iran should wait and see what the United Nations Security Council does on Tuesday, when it is scheduled to discuss the downing of the jet. Iran may be satisfied, he said, ''if they give us justice and condemn the warmonger and tell the United States to leave the Persian Gulf.''
The statement seemed to indicate that Iran would rely on diplomatic pressure and not violence to avenge the downing of the Iran Air jet by the American warship Vincennes.
As this country of 46 million continues to focus on the loss of the plane and the confrontation with the United States, Teheran's leaders appear to be trying to use the episode to reduce the Western armada in the Persian Gulf, which is containing Iranian military activities but leaving Iraq free to attack Iranian oil installations.
Iran's primary goal, its leaders say, is to reduce the high profile assumed by the United States.
These themes, often hinted at lately in interviews and various statements by Iranian officials, were fleshed out today in the speech by Hojatolislam Rafsanjani.
Hojatolislam Rafsanjani seemed to imply that Iran is not interested in Washington's talk of compensation. He said the victims of the incident ''did not ask for compensation.''
''Compensation is not enough for what happened,'' he said. The hojatolislam said Iran wanted the United States ''to say they are guilty.''
An element in this uncharacteristically toned-down Iranian attitude seems to be the exhaustion felt by the Iranian population after eight years of inconclusive war with Iraq.
For example, foreign diplomats here say fewer and fewer people show up at rallies to curse Iran's enemies. Heavy Economic Toll
The war has also imposed punishing economic penalties, which can be seen in dilapidated public buildings, neglected transportation, and high inflation.
Western diplomats say Iran's courting of the world press to report on its grief in the aftermath of the downing of the jet shows the degree to which the faction led by Hojatolislam Rafsanjani is anxious to find a pragmatic way to end Iran's military predicament.
But Hojatolislam Rafsanjani took pains to deny that there were differences among senior leaders of the Iranian Government. He said the seeming contradictions between his views an those of Ayatollah Hussein Ali Montazeri, the designated successor of Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini, were all part of a complementary policy debate. Montazeri Pushes Hard Line
Ayatollah Montazeri, who is considered Hojatolislam Rafsanjani's leading rival for power, has called for attacks against American interests and property in response to the downing of the jet.
Earlier this week, as Hojatolislam Rafsanjani sought to encourage international condemnation of the United States and sympathy for Iran, Ayatollah Montazeri, in a message read on the Teheran radio, asked Ayatollah Khomeini to order ''revolutionary forces and resistance cells inside and outside the country to target America's material, political, economic and military interests.''
Ayatollah Khomeini's response, according to Westerners who follow Iranian politics, appeared to be a rebuff to Ayatollah Montazeri.
Westerners who study Iranian politics said before the plane was shot down that Iran's radical attitudes had diminished considerably in the last several months, and that many leaders seemed to be giving priority to economic changes and to a search for a way to end the war.

Ailing Mexico Economy Will Be a Focus of Talks

Author: LARRY ROHTER, Special to the New York Times

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Ailing+Mexico+Economy+Will+Be+a+Focus+of+Talks&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1988-02-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=LARRY+ROHTER%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: As President Miguel de la Madrid and President Reagan prepare for their last scheduled official meeting, Mexico is struggling to emerge from its worst economic crisis in half a century and hoping the United States will play a key role in helping it recover.
As President Miguel de la Madrid and President Reagan prepare for their last scheduled official meeting, Mexico is struggling to emerge from its worst economic crisis in half a century and hoping the United States will play a key role in helping it recover.
Officials of both Governments said the leaders, each in his final year in office, would spend a substantial portion of their four hours together on Saturday discussing economic matters. The meeting will be in the Pacific Coast resort of Mazatlan.
The issues expected to be atop the agenda are Mexico's foreign debt, including a novel Mexican plan to issue bonds backed by the United States Treasury, and bilateral trade.
Narcotics trafficking, Central America, border and immigration questions and other subjects will also be addressed. But Mr. de la Madrid comes to the meeting under enormous domestic criticism and pressure to stem the economic decline he inherited when he took office in December 1982. Inflation and Debt Soar
In many respects, the economic problems have deepened. Inflation is at an all-time high of 177 percent, and a wage and price indexing program, scheduled to take effect March 1, remains untested. The foreign debt of more than $105 billion is the second-largest in the developing world, real wages have declined by half during the 1980's and net economic growth has been negative during the last two years.
Increasingly, Mr. de la Madrid and his Cabinet have been looking to the rapidly growing export sector, one of the economy's few bright spots, to counterbalance the depression in the domestic market. The key market for exports is the United States, which is Mexico's biggest foreign investor and its largest trading partner. The United States accounts for more than 60 percent of both Mexican imports and exports.
Prospects for growth in bilateral trade have been bolstered by a trade consultation accord signed by the two nations in November. That document calls for the two countries to consult on trade in goods including steel and electronics; American officials said agreements on textiles and telecommunications might be signed this weekend.
But some additional fine-tuning is likely to be required on the innovative bond plan Mexico announced in December, officials said. Under that proposal, Mexico would use $2 billion in zero-coupon bonds purchased from the United States to buy back several billion dollars of its debt, at a discount of up to 50 percent, from commercial banks.
After the plan was announced, the Securities and Exchange Commission made recommendations that would limit the tax and accounting benefits available to banks taking part in the program. Mexican officials said they planned to raise that issue in the talks on Saturday and would seek further Treasury department backing for the bonds, which will be auctioned later this month.
A Mexican diplomat, who asked not to be identified, said the Mexican Government intended to point out that the bond plan is an idea that needs further lobbying, to persuade more American banks to participate. Common Market Concept
There have also been indications that Mr. Reagan will pursue a favorite project, the North American Common Market. Last fall, the United States and Canada negotiated a free-trade agreement that must be ratified by the legislative bodies of both countries. In his State of the Union Message last month, Mr. Reagan described free trade as a concept he was ''determined to expand'' and said he looked forward to the day it would move ''south as well as north.''
Mexican officials, however, have shied away from such proposals, which are politically explosive here. Both Mr. de la Madrid and Carlos Salinas de Gortari, the former Minister of Budget and Planning who is expected to succeed him as President Dec. 1, have said such an arrangement would be both inappropriate and impractical.
''I am not in favor of such a proposal because I believe that through the General Agreement on Trade and Tariffs we have a multilateral way to deal with our neighbors,'' Mr. Salinas said last month. ''There is such a different level between the United States and Mexico that I don't believe such a common market would provide an advantage to either country.''
An American official said Mr. Reagan did not expect immediate progress on the matter. Instead, he said, the President is ''laying a marker for the future, expressing a vision and a long-term goal.''
It is also expected that the United States will once again call for Mexico to loosen its rules and procedures governing foreign investment, which Washington views as cumbersome and unclear.
For its part, ''Mexico will once again manifest its preoccupation with protectionist tendencies in the United States,'' the Mexican diplomat said.

U.S. and Mexico: Trade Barriers Fall

Author: CLYDE H. FARNSWORTH, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 Jan 1988: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426721104?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''What we're seeing is a maturing of the bilateral trading relationship,'' said one American negotiator, B. Timothy Bennett, a Deputy Assistant United States Trade Representative. ''Both sides recognize the need to make mutually beneficial concessions for their mutual advantage.'' Integrated Market Foreseen ''Both the United States and Mexico now recognize that they share a common destiny,'' said Robert E. Herzstein, a former senior Carter Administration trade official, now a partner at the Washington law firm of Arnold & Porter. ''Our relations with Mexico should be at the very top of our international agenda,'' he added. ''The threat,'' notes Geoffrey Kean, partner of Perception International, an economic intelligence service based in Ridgefield, Conn., ''is higher immigration, which affects us directly, if there is an economic downturn in Mexico. The opportunity is that a better economy in Mexico means both countries will benefit.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+and+Mexico%3A+Trade+Barriers+Fall&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1988-01-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=CLYDE+H.+FARNSWORTH%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: Mars chocolate bars, Miller Lite beer, Avon products and WestPoint Pepperell towels are among American products that will become available in Mexico for the first time after a spate of unusual agreements between the two countries.
Mars chocolate bars, Miller Lite beer, Avon products and WestPoint Pepperell towels are among American products that will become available in Mexico for the first time after a spate of unusual agreements between the two countries.
For its part, the United States will import more Mexican steel bars, cotton trousers and men's shirts.
Both countries, recognizing their deepening relationship, have suddenly embarked on a significant trade liberalizing course highlighted by the trade and monetary accords reached over the last two weeks.
''What we're seeing is a maturing of the bilateral trading relationship,'' said one American negotiator, B. Timothy Bennett, a Deputy Assistant United States Trade Representative. ''Both sides recognize the need to make mutually beneficial concessions for their mutual advantage.'' Integrated Market Foreseen
At this stage the goals are not as ambitious as the provisions in the Canada-United States free trade agreement signed last Jan. 2 that would almost eliminate trade and investment barriers between the two nations.
Still, the gradual, pragmatic lowering of trade fences at the Rio Grande is recognized as an achievement of potentially great importance. At some time, perhaps early in the next century, it could lead to a North American Common Market.
In general terms President Reagan has called for an integrated market from the Arctic to the Yucatan. A Reagan trade official, who asked not to be identified, recalled the President asking one Cabinet meeting: ''If the Europeans can create a Common Market, why can't we? '' Treasury Takes a Role
To support what has been a relatively new policy of the Mexican Government in favor of trade liberalization, the Treasury, under a previously announced plan to sell bonds to Mexico, has effectively become a partner in debt relief with commercial banks and Mexico.
The complex transaction might help extinguish as much as one-fifth of Mexico's outstanding debt and could free Mexico to spend an additional $1 billion on imports, much of which could go to American producers, analysts said.
The United States is Mexico's biggest foreign supplier. Mexico, with three times the population of Canada, is the fifth-largest customer of the United States after Canada, Japan, West Germany and Taiwan.
According to many analysts, these elements of a broad Mexican-American framework are shaping up: With a large labor force and plentiful natural resources including oil, Mexico would help the United States with its global competitiveness problems. The United States, with its capital and technology, would help Mexico realize its development ambitions. 'A Common Destiny'
''Both the United States and Mexico now recognize that they share a common destiny,'' said Robert E. Herzstein, a former senior Carter Administration trade official, now a partner at the Washington law firm of Arnold & Porter. ''Our relations with Mexico should be at the very top of our international agenda,'' he added.
Echoing this sentiment, Felix G. Rohatyn, partner of the investment firm of Lazard Freres & Company, has said, ''I believe that over the next 10 years our relationship with Mexico will be even more important to our vital interests than with either the Soviet Union or Japan.''
For years the United States had sought closer trade links with Mexico, but had been rebuffed by Mexico's political leadership, which was entrenched in policies of trade nationalism. These policies enforced high tariffs and large domestic subsidies to encourage domestic production as a substitute for imports. Old Policies Reassessed
The debt crisis and recognition of the need for greater economic efficiency brought a dramatic reassessment of the old policies - a process that is expected to be accelerated by the new presidential standard bearer, Carlos Salinas de Gortari, the Harvard-educated Minister of Budget and Planning who is expected to succeed President Miguel de la Madrid later this year.
In August 1986 Mexico signed the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, the world's free-trade charter, and since then has reduced tariffs and dismantled other trade controls.
Last November the United States and Mexico agreed to establish their first dispute-settlement mechanisms for trade and investment and set a framework for pursuing liberalization in textiles, steel, agriculture, investments, technology transfer, electronic products, services, patents, copyrights and other ''intellectual property.''
In addition to increasing efficiency, Mexican authorities see reduced trade barriers as an anti-inflation weapon. Anticipation of lower prices from cheaper imports was one of the factors behind a forecast last Wednesday by Mexican Finance Secretary Gustavo Petricioli that the monthly rate of inflation would slow to 1 percent to 2 percent by the end of the year. By March, monthly inflation is expected to fall to 8 percent, from the current rate of 10 percent.
After petroleum, Mexico's two most important exports to the United States are textiles and steel - products involved in two of the recent agreements with the United States.
In steel, American negotiators agreed to a 12 percent increase in Mexico's allowable steel shipments to the United States, in return for a steel tariff reduction by Mexico to 20 percent, from 38 percent, and certain other liberalizing measures. In other action, a limit was set at 6,300 tons on imports of Mexican steel wire products, compared with imports last year of 11,000 tons.
Under a second pact, textile and apparel exports from Mexico to the United States would grow by 6 percent annually over the next four years, compared with a 50 percent increase in 1987.
In addition, Mexico is permitted much faster expansion of shipments here than the 1 percent to 3 percent annual growth that is the goal of similar accords with the major textile producers in the Far East.
But American textile and apparel makers also won greater access to the Mexican market as a result of Mexico's commitments to cut tariffs on yarn and fabrics and to permit the United States to sell up to $240 million of apparel and finished products, such as shirts, towels and pajamas, in Mexico. Previously, this market had been closed to all foreign suppliers.
Mexico also agreed to eliminate or significantly reduce its import restrictions on beer, wine, distilled spirits, agricultural seeds, confectionary chocolate, lotions and face creams, linoleum and other floor coverings, air deflectors for autos and more than 30 other products. 'Mutual Benefits' Emphasized
In announcing the agreements, the United States Trade Representative, Clayton K. Yeutter, emphasized the ''mutual benefits.''
''I am pleased by the cooperative spirit that has been present between our two nations throughout these consultations,'' he said.
Reaction in the private sector in the United States was mixed. Lynne Strang, an official of the Distilled Spirits Council of the United States, said that the agreements ''can't do anything but good.''
But some analysts see Mexico as both a threat and an opportunity.
''The threat,'' notes Geoffrey Kean, partner of Perception International, an economic intelligence service based in Ridgefield, Conn., ''is higher immigration, which affects us directly, if there is an economic downturn in Mexico. The opportunity is that a better economy in Mexico means both countries will benefit.''

PARADISE LOST?; When It's Every Country for Itself, Pity the Markets

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]18 Oct 1987: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426637990?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''Up to now, so many investors and the Administration have been living in what can only be described as a fool's paradise,'' said Geoffrey Bell, an international investment adviser. ''This was based on hopes that the trade deficit would soon begin to show modest but significant improvement, and this would keep money flowing to the United States.'' To be sure, Mr. [James A. Baker] added, not everything was right with the world. The United States wasn't getting the cooperation it had counted on from West Germany, he said, charging that four increases in an important German interest rate since July had violated ''the spirit of our recent consultations.'' He seemed to be warning that the dollar may have to decline further, presumably hurting other countries' exports. The Germans insist that ''the markets'' are lifting rates and, besides, Government and central-bank policy must be aimed at nipping incipient inflation in the bud. The problems of the United States, they insist, are of its own making. Despite this year's drop in the budget deficit, their belief - one that is shared by many economists and investors - is that little or no progress will be made in the coming two years, because the underlying deficit is rising and President Reagan refuses to accept tax increases or cuts in military spending.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=PARADISE+LOST%3F%3B+When+It%27s+Every+Country+for+Itself%2C+Pity+the+Markets&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1987-10-18&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: IS the world economy beginning to fall apart?
IS the world economy beginning to fall apart?
Wall Street, plunging into a downturn that many analysts and investors feared was the end of the big bull market of the last five years, behaved last week as though the center would not hold.
The drop for the week in the Dow Jones industrial average of 235.48 points, or 9.49 percent, was a reaction to a gloomy trade report that was only one in series of symptoms of the precarious condition of the international economic system.
''Up to now, so many investors and the Administration have been living in what can only be described as a fool's paradise,'' said Geoffrey Bell, an international investment adviser. ''This was based on hopes that the trade deficit would soon begin to show modest but significant improvement, and this would keep money flowing to the United States.''
The August trade deficit of $15.7 billion, or $188 billion at an annual rate, dashed these hopes and led to a far-reaching fallout.
The prospect of a trade imbalance well above last year's $160 billion raised anxieties that Congress, concerned about the loss of American markets and jobs, would enact a protectionist trade bill, thus intensifying world inflationary pressures.
The trade outlook also brought the dollar under heavy selling pressure from private traders - pressure that the Federal Reserve and foreign central banks sought to contain by buying dollars.
That drove up interest rates, as the markets and banks anticipated that a falling dollar would heighten inflationary forces in the United States.
Rising interest rates are feared like poison by both the stock and bond markets, since capital values tend to drop as rates rise. The bond market, less noticed by the general public, also plunged with the yield on the 30-year Treasury bond, a bellwether for other bonds, landing above 10 percent.
Finally, the interest rate rise threatened problems for the national economy and was a reminder of smoking volcano in the world economy. At home, the prospect of slower spending by consumers and businesses raised worries about a recession in 1988, which most economists have not expected and which the Administration fears could cost the Republicans the White House in 1988.
Abroad, rising interest rates jeopardized the ability of the Latin American and other third world debtors to pay their bills, menacing the liquidity of some big American banks. Dispute With Germans
In what looked like one more symptom of the precariousness of it all, Treasury Secretary James A. Baker 3d held a White House news conference to offer official reassurance. The Federal Reserve's decision not to raise the discount rate since its half-point hike on Sept. 4, he said, showed the Fed thought anxieties about inflation were overblown.
He also pointed to the economy's five years of steady growth, the low unemployment rate and relatively low interest rates.
He said the Federal budget deficit was getting better; at a little over $150 billion this year it would be $70 billion smaller than the record $221 billion of last year. And the trade deficit, he said, was also improving, in volume terms, if not in current dollars.
To be sure, Mr. Baker added, not everything was right with the world. The United States wasn't getting the cooperation it had counted on from West Germany, he said, charging that four increases in an important German interest rate since July had violated ''the spirit of our recent consultations.'' He seemed to be warning that the dollar may have to decline further, presumably hurting other countries' exports.
The effort of the United States to stabilize the dollar without accepting its trade deficit as irreparable, and without thrusting its economy into recession, depends on the cooperation of the other industrial countries in holding their interest rates down and accelerating growth.
Mr. Baker is relatively satisfied, if not ecstatic, about the performance of the Japanese but bitterly unhappy with the West Germans. In the last three months, while the United States' Federal funds rate (which banks charge one another for short-term loans) has been gone up 1.1 percentage points, the Japanese rate has gone up only one-tenth of 1 point and the West German rate has risen a full percentage point. The German economy continues to languish, with unemployment at nearly 9 percent.
The Germans insist that ''the markets'' are lifting rates and, besides, Government and central-bank policy must be aimed at nipping incipient inflation in the bud. The problems of the United States, they insist, are of its own making. Despite this year's drop in the budget deficit, their belief - one that is shared by many economists and investors - is that little or no progress will be made in the coming two years, because the underlying deficit is rising and President Reagan refuses to accept tax increases or cuts in military spending.
It now begins to look as though the Administration sold itself a bill of goods on the cooperative arrangements worked out at various meetings of the Group of Seven industrial countries during the past year. The financial markets seem to recognize that where policies for spurring growth, opening up trade, holding down interest rates and stabilizing exchange rates are concerned, it's every country for itself and the devil take the hindmost. In these circumstances, the plunge in Wall Street -by 95 points on Wednesday, 57 points on Thursday and an all-time record of over 100 points on Friday - did not seem irrational.
When the kissing stops among the allies, the money stops flowing on Wall Street and foreign capital stops flowing into dollars. That is the great danger that hangs over the American and the world economy - a danger that will take deeper and more genuine cooperative action to avert.

Gold Called Aid to New Economic Order

Author: PETER T. KILBORN, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]13 Oct 1987: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426647125?accountid=11752

Abstract: While still far from complete, economists contend that the new order has begun to yield some tangible results. ''I think something good has happened,'' said Ralph C. Bryant, economist at the Brookings Institution and formerly a top official of the Federal Reserve System. ''The process of economic policy-making is a little better than it was.'' ''We didn't have it before,'' he added. ''We have it now. Is it nirvana? Never was intended to be.'' ''I just regard it as a joke,'' said Robert V. Roosa, partner in Brown Brothers Harriman & Company, the banking firm. And Lawrence H. Summers, an economist at Harvard, said, ''The gold thing, that's crazy, crazy.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Gold+Called+Aid+to+New+Economic+Order&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1987-10-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=PETER+T.+KILBORN%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: Despite the turbulence in the financial markets and the barbs of skeptics, many economists find that the United States and other leading countries are making headway in a fit-and-start effort to regain control of the world economy.
Despite the turbulence in the financial markets and the barbs of skeptics, many economists find that the United States and other leading countries are making headway in a fit-and-start effort to regain control of the world economy.
Some 16 years ago the major nations gave up their battle to keep the dollar's value tied to gold and abandoned their old system. But they have since decided that the wide-open markets and often frenzied exchange rates that took gold's place have not served them very well either. As a result, they have been devising a new order that most likely will resurrect gold, although probably not to a role as powerful as before.
The new arrangement - two years in the making - is based on negotiation, compromise and the use of mutually accepted indicators of each country's economic performance. The aim is to anticipate and thwart the kinds of jolts to the world economy that arose in recent years -inflation, the yawning American trade and budget deficits, the loan-payment struggles of the developing countries and the volatile dollar.
While still far from complete, economists contend that the new order has begun to yield some tangible results. ''I think something good has happened,'' said Ralph C. Bryant, economist at the Brookings Institution and formerly a top official of the Federal Reserve System. ''The process of economic policy-making is a little better than it was.''
He cites the stability of the dollar since last February, when economists were predicting a further decline after a yearlong fall. Although many economists are still predicting that the dollar will drop, the seven leading countries can say they have had some success. They are members of the so-called Group of Seven, consisting of the United States, Japan, West Germany, France, Britain, Italy and Canada.
Having agreed last February that the dollar's value stay put, they spent billions of their own currencies to buy dollars from the market and keep its price up in the face of heavy selling pressure. As a result, the dollar has remained stable in relation to the German mark, and has slipped only about 7 percent against the Japanese yen, compared with double-digit declines the previous year.
There have been other achievements. Economists cite Japan's lowering of interest rates twice last year, in tandem with similar actions by the Federal Reserve. Japan, they say, has also been stimulating domestic spending, as it had promised its partners.
And for their part, President Reagan and Treasury Secretary James A. Baker 3d, the principal proponent of the new order, have been promising to bring the United States budget deficits under control.
The 1987 deficit is down at least $60 billion from last year's record $221 billion, and last month the President, although reluctantly, signed a law helping to keep the deficit down in 1988. That was largely the result of domestic pressures but Mr. Baker said that, in part, it reflected promises the United States made to the other six countries.
The new approach to policy-making, Mr. Baker said in an interview, ''provides us with a political mechanism that was badly needed in order to improve the coordination of economic policy among the major trading nations of the world.''
''We didn't have it before,'' he added. ''We have it now. Is it nirvana? Never was intended to be.''
The United States still runs big budget and trade deficits, and Japan and Germany in recent weeks have been letting interest rates rise, to the fury of the Reagan Administration, because their increases have contributed to interest rate increases in the United States and to the turmoil in the American stock and bond markets.
But, Mr. Baker said, ''Are we considerably better off in terms of our chances of obtaining better coordination as a consequence of it? Yes we are.''
And Mr. Baker added that he thought that bringing gold back would make the system even better. During a speech here at the end of September, he proposed that the seven countries use commodity prices to gauge how their economies are performing, especially regarding inflation. One of the commodities would be gold.
The remark brought disbelief to many economists, but there was nothing idle about it. ''The President went for it like a duck for a June bug,'' Mr. Baker said when asked how the White House responded to the mention of gold.
Although Mr. Baker is not asking for a return to a gold-based system, some proponents of such a system -such as Robert A. Mundell, an economics professor at Columbia University, and Jude Wanniski, a supply-side lobbyist - see his proposal as an important step in that direction. More Cooperative Approach
Whether or not gold will have a formal role in the new system, the leading democracies are well along in constructing a more cooperative approach. In a sense, they are looking back, seeking to restore the sort of world economic order that followed the Bretton Woods, N.H., conference in 1944 that set up the postwar economic system, including the establishment of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank.
The value of the dollar was tied to gold and the value of other currencies was tied to the dollar. Countries were expected to maintain the values of their respective currencies by using dollars to buy and sell them. This was practical as long as each country's economy remained stable in relation to the others. If it did not, it was expected to devalue or revalue its currency, with agreement from its trading partners. The object was to prevent inflation and maintain steady growth.
The role of the United States was to pay gold to buy dollars from foreign governments that amassed the American currency as a result of their efforts to support its price. But that system broke down when high inflation in the United States caused speculators to dump dollars, exerting huge pressures on the nation's gold reserves. In a dramatic Sunday night television broadcast in August 1971, President Nixon closed the ''gold window'' and the system collapsed.
Although gold has lost its role, the concept of coordinating their economic policies has re-emerged as a basic ingredient of the economic philosophy of the seven nations. Growth With Low Inflation
Some economists say there are special reasons why the new approach shows signs of working. First, they say there is little pressure for radical policy changes because the economies of the seven countries have been growing with relatively low inflation for several years. Also, with the exception of Italy, all have like-minded conservative governments.
''Countries have been talking together for a long time,'' said Gottfried Haberler, economist at the American Enterprise Institute, ''and this has intensified. But I think some skepticism is still in order.''
The earlier hands-off policy of benign neglect reached its zenith with the soaring dollar of the first half of the 1980's. It began to crumble as Congress began demanding protectionist legislation because the strong dollar was making American goods less competitive and causing a loss of jobs.
To avert a trade war, in September 1985, Mr. Baker and Paul A. Volcker, then chairman of the Federal Reserve, asked the finance ministers and central bankers of Japan, West Germany, France and Britain to the Plaza Hotel in New York, where they declared they stood ready to push the dollar's value down. Since then, the dollar has declined by about 40 percent against other major currencies. Agreement on Indicators
From there, a new policy began taking shape. Two other countries, Italy and Canada, were brought aboard. At the Tokyo economic summit conference in May 1986, the group agreed to devise a set of indicators to measure their economies and to serve as an objective means of determining how their policies should be coordinated.
At subsequent meetings they established ranges within which their currencies would be allowed to rise and fall before the governments began buying and selling currencies to keep them within the zone.
More fundamentally, they negotiated changes in domestic policies, including the commitment to reduce the American budget deficits.
None of these initiatives has met with unequivocal success. Germany and Japan have resisted American appeals for changes in interest rates more often than they have gone along. Germany has lagged far behind its partners in fitting its economy into the goals set by the others. Even agreement on an acceptable set of indicators is still incomplete.
Mr. Baker now would like to see gold become part of that package. Gold, he said, can be effective in helping countries spot inflation - the price of gold was near rock bottom at $340 an ounce in the summer of 1986 when American inflation was only 1 percent, and now, with inflation around 5 percent, it is about $460. Avoiding a Delusion
Gold, Mr. Baker said, by enabling countries to measure the value of their respective currencies against something other than other currencies, helps avoid the delusion that occurs when inflation is rising everywhere while all currencies still seem stable.
But there are many doubters. John Kenneth Galbraith, the Harvard economist, said Mr. Baker's speech last month was a ''marvelous exercise in fantasy and obfuscation.''
''I just regard it as a joke,'' said Robert V. Roosa, partner in Brown Brothers Harriman & Company, the banking firm. And Lawrence H. Summers, an economist at Harvard, said, ''The gold thing, that's crazy, crazy.''
''I take it as mildly meaningful,'' said Stephen H. Axilrod, a former senior Fed official and now vice chairman of Nikko Securities Company International in New York. ''I think people are looking for a more objective indicator of inflationary tendencies, and every once in a while, gold tends to be that.''

Full text: LEAD: Following is an excerpt from President Reagan's address today to the annual meetings of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund:
Following is an excerpt from President Reagan's address today to the annual meetings of the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund:
There is, of course, the trade deficit - something of justifiable concern both in this country and abroad. Corrections are necessary, and there are strong signs that corrections are under way.
It is vital, however, that policy makers not be stampeded into self-destructive action. There has been a chorus of American politicans playing to the fears of working people, singing the song of protectionism and charging that, as a result of the trade deficit, jobs will go overseas, unemployment will rise, and the United States will be deindustrialized.
It sounds good as part of a political campaign speech, but as an old Virginia lawyer once told his hometown jury, ''tain't so.'' Unemployment has declined in the United States by 40 percent since late 1982, even as our trade deficit has grown. In Japan and Germany, countries with large trade surpluses, unemployment has gone up. Rise in Real Wages
And a long-term analysis shows us holding our own in manufacturing jobs. Importantly, from the end of 1982 to the present, during a time of large trade deficits, manufacturing jobs in the United States grew by more than a million. Furthermore, real wages in manufacturing, which declined by 7 percent from 1977 to 1981, increased by 2 1/2 percent from 1981 to 1986.
The trade deficit is symptomatic of structural problems that we as managers need to address. Self-destructive protectionism, however, is definitely not the answer. I pledge to you that any protectionist legislation reaching my desk is going to be returned to the Congress with veto on the cover.
Part of the answer lies here at home. As I noted at the economic summit in Venice last June, it is imperative that the United States consistently reduce its Federal deficit spending. Today, I will sign a bill that reinstates our deficit reduction targets as part of an extension of the borrowing authority of the United States Government. This was not an easy decision. On one hand was the responsibility to preserve our 200-year history of meeting our obligations and maintaining credibility and reliability to our own citizens and to the world. Political Debate
On the other hand was the political debate being waged between those who favor either raising taxes or cutting defense - or both - and those of us committed to further reductions in domestic spending, reductions that will bring down the deficit and keep our economy strong.
As I said, it was a tough decision. It should be seen as a signal that America is not backing down from its responsibilities. But having made this decision, I call on the surplus countries to do the same - to find the political gumption to stimulate their economies without reigniting the fires of inflation.
It must be recognized that the health of the world economy does not hinge solely on U.S. budget policy. As U.S. budget and trade deficits decline, other countries must pick up the slack, particularly on imports from developing countries.

Economic Scene; The Dollar And Inflation

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]04 Sep 1987: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426596345?accountid=11752

Abstract: CLIMBING interest rates, triggered by the declining dollar and worries about mounting inflation, have soured the mood on Wall Street. Full text: LEAD: CLIMBING interest rates, triggered by the declining dollar and worries about mounting inflation, have soured the mood on Wall Street. Some analysts have even started to write obituaries for the big bull market of the past five years. ''I don't think this is temporary,'' said Sung Wohn Sohn, chief economist at the Norwest Corporation, the Minneapolis-based bank holding company.
CLIMBING interest rates, triggered by the declining dollar and worries about mounting inflation, have soured the mood on Wall Street. Some analysts have even started to write obituaries for the big bull market of the past five years. ''I don't think this is temporary,'' said Sung Wohn Sohn, chief economist at the Norwest Corporation, the Minneapolis-based bank holding company. ''I certainly think we're in a bear market.''
Needing to defend the dollar and check inflation while keeping the expansion going, the Federal Reserve (under its new chairman, Alan Greenspan) is up against a tricky problem: If it does too little to tighten money and credit, it could fan inflationary expectations; but if it does too much, sending interest rates up sharply, it could put the securities markets and the economy into a nosedive.
A steep drop in the United States securities markets and a fall in the economy, hurting the outlook for corporate profits, could cause foreign investors to cut their holdings of American equities, bonds and other assets. Not only would this be bad news for the markets but also it would mean a severe shortfall in the savings needed to finance the Federal budget deficit and the trade deficit. This would mean another downward shove in the value of the dollar and another upward twist in interest rates.
It will do no good to try to minimize the danger of the nation's growing dependency on foreign capital. Earlier this year, before he became Fed chairman, Mr. Greenspan warned his clients of the danger of a slackening of foreign investment in United States assets, which appeared to be taking place in the first quarter. But the foreign capital inflow picked up again in the second quarter, bolstering the stock market, even as the Fed gradually tightened money, sending interest rates higher. With the dollar weakening after the poor trade figures for the second quarter were announced, worries about a foreign pullback from dollar investments have intensified. In a recent analysis, David Hale, chief economist of Kemper Financial Services in Chicago, warned that concerns about renewed dollar depreciation, if they cause foreign investors to cut their holdings, could have ''a very adverse effect on share prices'' because foreign capital has played a major role in pushing up the price-earnings ratios of American stocks in the face of rising bond yields this year.
Foreign investors, according to Mr. Hale, have been expected to purchase more than $40 billion in American equities this year, compared with $18 billion last year and only $3 billion to $4 billion a year during the early 1980's. He puts the current United States price/earnings ratio at 17, which appears expensive in relation to Treasury bond yields of more than 9 percent, but he maintains that this is not expensive compared with Japan's P/E ratios of 60 and bond yields of 5 percent.
''But not even the Japanese will be able to sustain U.S. equity multiples at high levels if inflation expectations rise sharply and U.S. bond yields rise into double-digit territory,'' Mr. Hale said.
The anxieties about inflation and the dollar seem bound to dominate Fed policy now. A further tightening of money runs the risk of slowing the economy. But the risks of failing to defend the dollar and head off inflation would probably be much bigger. In driving foreign capital out of the country, the upward wrench to interest rates would be far greater if inflationary expectations got out of hand.
With the world economy growing sluggishly and foreigners still accumulating dollars as a result of the American trade deficit - it ran at an annual rate of nearly $160 billion in the first half - foreign investment in the United States is unlikely to plummet unless a market panic explodes out of fear that American monetary policy has gone haywire.
Foreigners want a secure place for their capital; a safe and sane United States monetary policy has provided security, and Mr. Greenspan appears determined to continue it. The problem is worsened by a Federal budgetary policy that makes this country more dependent on capital inflow.
Sustained American economic growth has made possible continued growth of the world economy, though at a slow rate. The export-dependent economies of Japan, the Pacific Rim nations, West Germany and others have been kept afloat chiefly by America's trade deficits, although with painful economic and political strains on the United States.
As the growing American deficits have sustained foreign expansion, so has the willingness of foreigners to invest here made possible the continued growth and stability of the United States - and of their own economies - and they know it.
Despite tensions between the United States and its trading partners, foreign governments have not threatened to stem the flow of capital to this country or to replace the dollar as the world's key currency. On the contrary, they realize that their own fate is bound up with the continued key role of the dollar and continued expansion of the American economy. It would take monetary irresponsibility beyond anything yet experienced to wreck the confidence in America that has supported the emergence of a more closely integrated world economy.

Subject: CREDIT; UNITED STATES ECONOMY; INTEREST (MONEY); PRICES

Economic Scene; The Pressures On Inflation

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 Aug 1987: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426585626?accountid=11752

Abstract: Lawrence A. Veit of Brown Brothers Harriman & Company concludes that, at best, the industrial nations of the capitalist world will ''muddle through a few more quarters of deficient growth until some combination of more stimulative policies and underlying demand reignites the engines of expansion.'' The combined output of the non-OPEC developing countries is projected to grow at an annual rate of 4.5 percent both this year and next. But, because of extreme differences in the ''inertial'' inflation among the developing countries, and their wide spread from stringency to laxity in fiscal and monetary policies, their expected rates of inflation differ widely. They are: the direct effect on prices of imported goods such as autos and consumer electronics; an ''umbrella'' effect on American-made goods where competing import prices have risen; higher business costs for American companies resulting from more expensive purchases abroad; rising precious metals and commodities prices stemming from the increased purchasing power of strong-currency countries, and a strengthened American industrial sector, as the lower dollar, by stimulating exports, adds to demand-side pressures and makes it easier for businesses to pass on price increases.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Economic+Scene%3B+The+Pressures+On+Inflation&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1987-08-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: OIL prices tumbled this week, pushed down by the market's pessimism about the world's ability to absorb current output levels of crude and refined products. In addition, world demand for commodities is far from booming. But within a pattern of reasonable balance, individual countries, whether developing or developed, are capable of generating inflation.
OIL prices tumbled this week, pushed down by the market's pessimism about the world's ability to absorb current output levels of crude and refined products. In addition, world demand for commodities is far from booming. But within a pattern of reasonable balance, individual countries, whether developing or developed, are capable of generating inflation.
Lawrence A. Veit of Brown Brothers Harriman & Company concludes that, at best, the industrial nations of the capitalist world will ''muddle through a few more quarters of deficient growth until some combination of more stimulative policies and underlying demand reignites the engines of expansion.''
The Brown Brothers Harriman forecast puts the growth of real gross national product for the member nations of the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development as a whole at 2.5 percent this year and 3.1 percent next year, with the predicted rate of inflation averaging 3.4 percent in 1987 and 4 percent in 1988.
The gradual advance of the industrial countries would mean only modest growth for the members of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, whose combined real G.N.P. is forecast to rise by 2.5 percent in 1987 and 3 percent in 1988.
The combined output of the non-OPEC developing countries is projected to grow at an annual rate of 4.5 percent both this year and next. But, because of extreme differences in the ''inertial'' inflation among the developing countries, and their wide spread from stringency to laxity in fiscal and monetary policies, their expected rates of inflation differ widely.
Mexico, whose consumer price index rose by 57.8 percent in 1985 and 84.5 percent in 1986, is expected, according to Mr. Veit, to hit 110 percent in 1987 and 120 percent in 1988. Argentina, whose inflation was reined in to 81.9 percent in 1986, is forecast to climb again to a 125 percent rate in 1987 and 1988. Brazil, with inflation of 166.2 percent last year, is expected to climb to a 300 percent rate in 1987 and 1988.
While the spread in inflation rates among O.E.C.D. countries is much narrower, wide differences are still exhibited, depending mainly on national economic policies. In 1986, the lowest inflation was in West Germany, where consumer prices dropped by two-tenths of 1 percent, and the highest was in Australia, where prices rose 9.1 percent. The overall O.E.C.D. average increase in the consumer price index was just 2.7 percent, with the United States near the bottom, at just 1.1 percent.
The Veit analysis puts the O.E.C.D. inflation average for 1988 at 4 percent, with the United States forecast to rise by 4.5 percent. Some American economists, such as those at Salomon Brothers, are now putting it as high as 6.5 percent.
The argument that the United States rate will shoot higher starts with the lagging effects of a cheaper dollar. Allen Sinai, chief economist of Shearson Lehman Brothers, names five effects of a lower dollar on United States inflation.
They are: the direct effect on prices of imported goods such as autos and consumer electronics; an ''umbrella'' effect on American-made goods where competing import prices have risen; higher business costs for American companies resulting from more expensive purchases abroad; rising precious metals and commodities prices stemming from the increased purchasing power of strong-currency countries, and a strengthened American industrial sector, as the lower dollar, by stimulating exports, adds to demand-side pressures and makes it easier for businesses to pass on price increases.
The sharp fall in the dollar this week exacerbated worries of higher domestic inflation ahead. How far the dollar falls will depend to a degree on how the Group of Five major industrial countries acts to carry out the currency agreements reached in Paris in February through central bank interventions and coordinated macroeconomic policies.
The most important determinant of the dollar's future value may be actions on the United States budget deficit. But here the outlook is gloomy. The Congressional Budget Office this week predicted that the deficit would ease this year but grow again to nearly $200 billion next year unless Congress and the President make good their promises to stanch the red ink. They are still stalemated and prospects of a significant compromise are not bright.
Another major cause for anticipation of higher United States inflation is the expectation of a catch-up of wages to faster-rising prices. Consumer prices rose at an annual rate of 5.4 percent in the first half of this year, but wages rose by only 2.2 percent, so living standards went down. A new study by Olivier Jean Blanchard for the Brookings Institution made the case for some catch-up, showing that 40 percent of the upward adjustment of wages to rising prices is complete after one year and 65 percent after two years.
But, in specific cases and time periods, there is no iron law binding wages to prices, or vice versa. The bargaining power of corporations versus organized and unorganized labor will determine the speed and degree of the wage rise. For instance, a battle between labor and management, regarded as likely to have national significance, is now being waged between the International Paper Company and the combined forces of the United Paperworkers International Union at a strikebound mill in Jay, Me. As a result of conflicts over wages and company demands for givebacks, the mill has been closed since June 16.
That strike typifies the battle building in the United States over stepped-up inflation and who benefits or suffers from it.
Correction: August 25, 1987, Tuesday, Late City Final Edition
