Beyond Volcker, Beyond Greenspan

Author: Advisers., HERBERT STEIN; Herbert Stein, a senior fellow at the American Enterprise Institute for Public-Policy Research, was chairman of President Richard M. Nixon's Council of Economic

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]07 June 1987: A.29.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426520218?accountid=11752

Abstract: This bias in the Fed's ''all-things-considered'' policy was a main cause of the inflation of the 1970's. That Mr. [Paul A. Volcker], who was chairman for eight years, resisted it is a tribute to him. His resistance was fortified by his experience with the weak anti-inflationary policies of the 1970's while he was at the Treasury and the Federal Reserve Bank of New York. Alan Greenspan, whom President Reagan has nominated as the new chairman, is probably as much ''against'' inflation as Mr. Volcker. Like Mr. Volcker, he has had experience with the difficulties of checking inflation once it has gathered steam. But he is to enter office in an environment quite different from the one that surrounded Mr. Volcker's entrance. A test of the Fed's priorities awaits Mr. Greenspan. We hear a great deal about two goals of monetary policy -to fight inflation and to ''defend the dollar'' - that is, to stabilize the dollar's exchange rate at the existing level.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Beyond+Volcker%2C+Beyond+Greenspan%3A+%5BOP-ED%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1987-06-07&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.29&amp;au=Advisers.%2C+HERBERT+STEIN%3BHerbert+Stein%2C+a+senior+fellow+at+the+American+Enterprise+Institute+for+Public-Policy+Research%2C+was+chairman+of+President+Richard+M.+Nixon%27s+Council+of+Economic&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: Paul A. Volcker deserves all the honors a grateful nation can bestow upon him, mainly for his battle against inflation.
Paul A. Volcker deserves all the honors a grateful nation can bestow upon him, mainly for his battle against inflation.
It does not belittle Mr. Volcker's achievements to say that they did not include a solution of the problems of inflation and monetary policy.
The country is left with no guidance for his successor except that he should be like Mr. Volcker. Presumably, this does not mean that he should be 6 feet 7 inches tall. It means that his policy should be like Mr. Volcker's. But what was Mr. Volcker's policy?
The Federal Reserve's policy throughout its history has been to look at all the nation's objectives, all facts and forecasts and make decisions that it would be prepared to change in a week or a month. Until he writes a book telling us something different, that was also Mr. Volcker's policy.
There are two problems with the Fed's institutional policy.
First, it leads to great uncertainty. Even in the financial markets, which had so much confidence in Mr. Volcker, there was always enormous uncertainty about what he would do next and what the consequences would be. Such uncertainty infects and injures the economy outside ''the markets.''
Second, and more important, this policy contains an inflationary bias. When the Federal Reserve considers anew each month relative priorities of employment, economic growth, the dollar's exchange rate, third world debt, the troubled banks and inflation, there is a strong tendency for the control of inflation to sink to the bottom of the list.
The effects of monetary policy on inflation tend to come later than the effects on employment and output. Short-run interests are overwhelmingly in favor of a policy of expansion, even at the risk of inflation - at least until the inflation is upon us in a devastating degree.
This bias is probably stronger in the public and the politicians than in the Federal Reserve, but for all the talk of independence the Fed cannot be indifferent to the opinions of the public and the politicians.
This bias in the Fed's ''all-things-considered'' policy was a main cause of the inflation of the 1970's. That Mr. Volcker, who was chairman for eight years, resisted it is a tribute to him. His resistance was fortified by his experience with the weak anti-inflationary policies of the 1970's while he was at the Treasury and the Federal Reserve Bank of New York.
But we must remember that he took office after a decade of rising inflation that had made the public and the politicians extraordinarily willing to accept the costs of an anti-inflationary policy.
Alan Greenspan, whom President Reagan has nominated as the new chairman, is probably as much ''against'' inflation as Mr. Volcker. Like Mr. Volcker, he has had experience with the difficulties of checking inflation once it has gathered steam. But he is to enter office in an environment quite different from the one that surrounded Mr. Volcker's entrance.
Six years have passed since America last suffered double-digit inflation. The public no longer thinks of inflation as its No. 1 problem. The chairman will soon be working with a President who will not be elected on a promise to make control of inflation a top priority.
Sometime in his term, Mr. Greenspan will almost certainly encounter a recession accompanied by rising unemployment. Can we be confident that in this new environment he will be as resistant as Mr. Volcker was to the advocates of expansionary and inflationary policy as a way out of recession? And if we are confident about Mr. Greenspan, can we be confident of his successor?
The country needs greater assurance of price stability than the prospect that the Fed chairman will be against inflation. Before Mr. Volcker, the chairmen under whom we got the inflation were also against inflation. We need assurances that control of inflation will continue to be the first and dominant priority of the Federal Reserve.
This proposition implies, of course, that unemployment, growth, levels of particular prices and the solvency of particular financial institutions are at most subordinate objectives of monetary policy, except insofar as they are promoted by price level stability.
This proposition needs to be clearly stated, publicly debated and endorsed by the political leadership, possibly in the form of an act of Congress declaring it to be the primary mission of the Federal Reserve to stabilize the price level.
A test of the Fed's priorities awaits Mr. Greenspan. We hear a great deal about two goals of monetary policy -to fight inflation and to ''defend the dollar'' - that is, to stabilize the dollar's exchange rate at the existing level.
These two goals are talked about as if they are twins, but in fact they are consistent with each other only on occasion and by coincidence. For example, to have tried to stabilize the dollar from 1981 to 1985 - to keep it from rising - would have defeated the effort to get inflation down.
The Federal Reserve would have had to buy foreign currencies, which would have increased the supply of dollars and raised the level of prices in the United States. Preventing inflation and stabilizing the dollar may be consistent objectives today.
But it is at least highly possible that defending the dollar by monetary means will require an otherwise unnecessary deflation here. The Fed should keep its eye on the main thing - inflation - and make clear that it is not committed to stabilizing the prices of foreign currencies. Those prices are no more the proper objects of American monetary policy than are the prices of I.B.M. stock and soybeans.

EUROPE AND JAPAN SHOW NEW DECLINE IN THEIR ECONOMIES

Author: PAUL LEWIS, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]22 Feb 1987: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426402042?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''Playing with fire'' is how Karl Otto Pohl, president of the West German central bank, described United States policy shortly before this weekend's meeting began. ''Blackmail'' was the word that Jacques Delors, president of the European Community's Commission, used recently to describe the American approach. ''Continued appreciation of key non-dollar currencies is now clearly slowing down these countries' growth,'' said J. Paul Horne, European economic analyst with the big United States brokerage house of Smith Barney, Harris Upham & Company in Paris. 'Shotgun Cooperation' Referring to the pressure from the United States that Western Europe and Japan take steps to bolster their domestic economies, Stephen Marris of the Institute for International Economics in Washington said, ''There is a chance 1987 could turn out the year of shotgun economic cooperation.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=EUROPE+AND+JAPAN+SHOW+NEW+DECLINE+IN+THEIR+ECONOMIES&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1987-02-22&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=PAUL+LEWIS%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: LEAD: As finance ministers from seven major industrial nations hold talks here this weekend on improving their economic performance and stabilizing currency values, economic growth in Europe and Japan is showing new weakness, largely as a result of the dollar's recent fall, economic figures show.
As finance ministers from seven major industrial nations hold talks here this weekend on improving their economic performance and stabilizing currency values, economic growth in Europe and Japan is showing new weakness, largely as a result of the dollar's recent fall, economic figures show.
The recent rise in the value of European currencies and the yen is raising the prices of European and Japanese exports and reducing sales on which the countries depend heavily for economic growth. This has caused their economic preformance to falter.
Higher oil prices that followed an accord by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries in December are also depressing growth. Faster Growth and Imports
The Reagan Administration hopes the weaker economic trend will encourage these countries to agree this weekend on action to bolster their economies, officials report. The opening of the talks here today was surrounded in secrecy, and a communique is not expected until late Sunday. [ Page 13. ] The possibility of a further dollar decline has encouraged both Japan and West Germany to cut their interest rates more than they had appeared likely to do. West Germany is also considering advancing tax cuts planned for early next year, and Japan has announced a public works program, measures that would strengthen their domestic economies.
Faster growth in Europe and Japan would halt the appreciation of their currencies by drawing in more imports and provide the United States with the new export markets it needs to reduce its trade deficit, and could head off mounting protectionist pressure in Congress. Political Resentment
But this approach by the United States - implicitly threatening Europe and Japan with a futher dollar devaluation if they do not expand their economies faster - is also stirring political resentment, suggesting that agreement on international monetary reform will be difficult to achieve.
''Playing with fire'' is how Karl Otto Pohl, president of the West German central bank, described United States policy shortly before this weekend's meeting began. ''Blackmail'' was the word that Jacques Delors, president of the European Community's Commission, used recently to describe the American approach.
The most dramatic sign of Europe's weakening growth has occurred in West Germany, normally the Continent's economic powerhouse.
Total West German output dropped four-tenths of 1 percent during the final quarter of last year, the Government announced last week. The Government has revised downward its growth forecast for this year from 3 percent to 2.5 percent, but many private forecasters believe 2 percent is a more likely outcome without a change of policy.
Unemployment rose last month, and earlier this week the Berlin Economic Research Institute predicted that output would fall 1 percent in the first quarter of this year.
The French Government has also reduced its growth forecast for this year, from 2.8 percent to 2.3 percent.While French industrial production grew by 1.5 percent during 1986 as a whole, it began slackening at the end of the year, with a fall of 5.7 percent at annual rates in the final three months.
Italy presents a similar picture. Total economic output rose 1.5 percent at annual rates during the first nine months of 1986. But it weakened sharply in the third quarter of last year, dropping 3.2 percent at annual rates. Industrial production was down 7.1 percent at annual rates in the last three months of 1986.
The latest survey of European business confidence published by the Commission of the European Community in Brussels showed a decline last December in all major countries except Britain.
In Japan, overall economic growth is holding up, averaging 2.3 percent at annual rates in the third quarter of last year, compared with 2.6 percent during the first nine months. But industrial production declined 2.4 percent in the final quarter.
''Continued appreciation of key non-dollar currencies is now clearly slowing down these countries' growth,'' said J. Paul Horne, European economic analyst with the big United States brokerage house of Smith Barney, Harris Upham & Company in Paris. 'Shotgun Cooperation'
Referring to the pressure from the United States that Western Europe and Japan take steps to bolster their domestic economies, Stephen Marris of the Institute for International Economics in Washington said, ''There is a chance 1987 could turn out the year of shotgun economic cooperation.''
But West Germany and Japan remain reluctant to put at risk the progress they have made in reducing inflation and cutting their budget deficits by adopting more stimulatory policies now. As a result, many economists and policy makers see the United States approach as risky. They fear a further decline in the value of the dollar against their currencies could get out of hand, forcing the Federal Reserve Board to raise American interest rates to break its fall and push the American economy back into recession.
''Germany and Japan are being frightened into cooperation but that may mean the economic news has to get worse before it gets better,'' warned Christopher Johnson, economic adviser at Lloyds Bank in London.
In West Germany, particularly, United States policy is resented as an attempt to make the rest of the world pay the cost of the Reagan Administration's inability to reduce the Federal budget deficit, which is seen as a major cause of America's poor trade preformance.

OUR UNEMPLOYMENT QUANDARY

Author: Condon, James C

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]30 Nov 1986: A.4.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/426322870?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''We are now the crack labor market,'' says Orley Ashenfelter, a labor economist at Princeton University. ''Everyone now turns to the United States to find out how to manage a labor market. That's a real reversal from the '60's and most of the '70's when we envied Europe's low unemployment rates.'' Another legacy of the 1982 recession appears to be a doubling of the ranks of workers so discouraged about job prospects that they have ceased looking for work and have dropped out of the labor market all together. WHY does American unemployment seem to be stuck at what used to be an unacceptable level? ''There are plenty of things to point a finger at,'' says Marvin H. Kosters an economist with the American Enterprise Institute in Washington, ''but there just is no good way of explaining the rise.'' It was widely supposed in the late 1970's that the Phillips curve, which plots on a graph the trade-off between inflation and unemployment, had been discredited. Looking at the United States' experience over two and a half decades, it is easy to see why. During the 1960's the theory seemed to work: when unemployment fell, inflation rose, though not by much.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=OUR+UNEMPLOYMENT+QUANDARY%3A+%5BTEXT%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1986-11-30&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.4&amp;au=Condon%2C+James+C&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: They're closing down the textile mill across the railroad tracks Foreman said these jobs are going boys and they ain't coming back. - Bruce Springsteen THOSE lyrics, from a song written in 1983, might just as easily have referred to a steel mill or an automobile assembly line or a chemical plant. As the United States economy endured the wrenching recession of 1981-82 that wrung inflation from the system, the jobless rate surged to 10.7 percent, the highest level since the Great Depression, when a quarter of the work force was idled.
Since the end of 1982, the unemployment rate has declined to around 7 percent. But the recovery is now four years old: in the 1975-80 expansion, the unemployment rate fell steadily to below 6 percent. This time, the rate has been stalled stubbornly for a year.
Perhaps an even more troubling picture that emerges from a graph of the United States unemployment rate plotted back to 1890 is that the trend line is rising. The wild swings that characterized the early part of the century are gone, but the drift since the 1950's is clearly upward.
Most economists point out that the experience of Western Europe is even worse. Even West Germany, the economic powerhouse of Europe, has an unemployment rate higher than that of the United States. Britain, which had three percent unemployment in the 1970's, cannot seem to get its rate below 12 percent today.
Japan's unemployment rate looks lower, but part of that difference is due to peciliarities in the way Japan counts its unemployed. And recently, even Japan's rate has begun to creep steadily upward.
''We are now the crack labor market,'' says Orley Ashenfelter, a labor economist at Princeton University. ''Everyone now turns to the United States to find out how to manage a labor market. That's a real reversal from the '60's and most of the '70's when we envied Europe's low unemployment rates.''
Still, it is difficult to take comfort in the greater misfortune of workers in Western Europe's economies. And the raw number of unemployed in the United States masks some distressing situations.
For example, nearly 15 percent of the unemployed have been without work for more than six months, double the rate in 1973 when the economy was at the peak of its Vietnam war expansion. In addition, people who have lost jobs are still squeezing the new entrants - people returning to the labor force and young people seeking first jobs - out of the market.
Another legacy of the 1982 recession appears to be a doubling of the ranks of workers so discouraged about job prospects that they have ceased looking for work and have dropped out of the labor market all together. WHY does American unemployment seem to be stuck at what used to be an unacceptable level? ''There are plenty of things to point a finger at,'' says Marvin H. Kosters an economist with the American Enterprise Institute in Washington, ''but there just is no good way of explaining the rise.''
Unemployment insurance, purportedly a seductive cushion for some unemployed, is blamed for part of the higher rate of joblessness. Rising participation in the labor force by women and young people surely is part of the reason. In the last five years it seems an inesacapable conclusion that some American jobs - especially in manufacturing - have left these shores permanently as the strong dollar allowed foreign companies to win a larger share of the United States market.
Structural changes in the economy, which made it difficult to match jobs and workers, also may have contributed. And many experts say that the nation is now more willing to use macroeconomic policies to control inflation rather than reduce unemployment.
It was widely supposed in the late 1970's that the Phillips curve, which plots on a graph the trade-off between inflation and unemployment, had been discredited. Looking at the United States' experience over two and a half decades, it is easy to see why. During the 1960's the theory seemed to work: when unemployment fell, inflation rose, though not by much.
Then the relationship seemed to fall apart: In the late 1960's and 1970's, the economy, overheated by the Vietnam War and rising social spending, experienced its first postwar bout of accelerating, not simply higher, inflation. The oil- and food-price shocks of 1973-74, and the severe oil-price increase in 1978-79 only made matters worse. The Phillips curve began to crawl in all sorts of directions.
The recessions of the early 1980's seem to have restored some validity to the curve -inflation has been cut to 2 percent and continues there at the price of a substantial unemployment. Thus policy now seems bound by a familiar constraint: Is it worth risking an increase in the inflation rate to push the unemployment rate lower?
Or, as some economists predict, will the demographic changes that contributed to the problem ultimately provide part of the solution. As women's participation in the labor-force more closely matches the over-all rate in the United States, their participation rate should climb more slowly. That slowdown should ease unemployment as fewer new workers try to find jobs. And, by the turn of the century, the first of the baby boomers will be preparing for retirement, not for entry into the work force.

THINKING IN UNISON AT THE SUMMIT

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 May 1986: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425881870?accountid=11752

Abstract: In their political statement, the seven condemned Libya as a sponsor of terrorism, a stipulation demanded by the United States, Britain and Canada. The statement also called for measures against terrorists and their sponsors, including a ban on arms sales, limits on the size of diplomatic missions, improved extradition procedures, stricter immigration and visa requirements and the ''closest possible'' police and security agency cooperation to combat terrorism. But the statement fell short of endorsing the use of military force, such as the United States raids on Libya, and did not mention specific economic action against Libya - two things the United States would have welcomed. Japan and France were unenthusiastic about the terrorism statement, but Secretary of State George P. Shultz, said it amounted to a message for Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi, the Libyan leader: ''You've had it, pal.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=THINKING+IN+UNISON+AT+THE+SUMMIT&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1986-05-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: THE seven major industrial democracies found much to agree upon last week at their summit in Tokyo, and they managed, as summiteers will, to finesse sharp differences. The statements they produced on world politics and economics were specific, but not without carefully cut loopholes and omissions, allowing each head of state to report home that the meeting had been a victory for, variously, the United States, Canada, Britain, France, Germany, Italy and Japan.
In their political statement, the seven condemned Libya as a sponsor of terrorism, a stipulation demanded by the United States, Britain and Canada. The statement also called for measures against terrorists and their sponsors, including a ban on arms sales, limits on the size of diplomatic missions, improved extradition procedures, stricter immigration and visa requirements and the ''closest possible'' police and security agency cooperation to combat terrorism. But the statement fell short of endorsing the use of military force, such as the United States raids on Libya, and did not mention specific economic action against Libya - two things the United States would have welcomed. Japan and France were unenthusiastic about the terrorism statement, but Secretary of State George P. Shultz, said it amounted to a message for Col. Muammar el-Qaddafi, the Libyan leader: ''You've had it, pal.''
The statement also chided the Soviet Union for delaying the release of information on the nuclear accident in the Ukraine; praised the United States for its ''negotiating efforts'' on arms control, and called on Moscow to ''negotiate positively.''
The economic agreements were broad and ambitious, but, finally, statements of intention that were not binding and not enforceable, except by self-discipline. They prescribed how the seven countries should cooperate to avoid disruption in any of their economies. The aim of the principal agreement was to reduce sharp currency variations through the newly formed Group of Seven, the chief financial officals of each of the summit nations, who are to meet at least once a year. Instead of trying to deal with currency fluctuation through governmental buying and selling, the group is to monitor the basic economic policy and performance of each country - inflation, interest rates, growth, unemployment, deficits, trade balance - and recommend change when the actions of one country threaten to disrupt the others. (Managing the float, page 2.) On trade restrictions, and particularly those affecting agriculture, the seven agreed there were problems but set no date for dealing with them.
In Japan, Prime Minister Yasuhiro Nakasone came under criticism because of the summit's failure to meet his nation's concerns, notably by acting to stem the rise of the yen's value against the dollar. Partly as a result, his plan to call elections soon for both houses of parliament was jeopardized.

WAKING UP TO THE GLUT ECONOMY

Author: Williams, Winston

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]08 Dec 1985: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425733573?accountid=11752

Abstract: ''Everybody felt that the growth rate of metals would continue and they went searching the continents for new mines,'' says David Williamson, a metals analyst in the London office of Shearson Lehman Brothers. ''So all this new production came on stream in 1981 and walked right into a recession. Massive inventories developed,'' he continued. ''But once you're committed to opening up new mines and smelters that cost billions, you have to continue to operate them even if there's a surplus.'' In the United States, where debt-bloated balance sheets have made businesses unusually vulnerable to gluts, many companies have begun to grapple with the problem of overabundance. The Ogden Corporation, following a pattern that is becoming increasingly familiar, sold its collection of industrial operations - including its Avondale shipyard, an aluminum smelter, and a rail car assembler - to managers in a leveraged buy-out. It has redeployed its assets into businesses that turn solid municipal waste into energy and that provide management and maintenance services for large buildings like New York City's new convention center. General Electric's chief, John F. Welch, is saying he wants to move to a ''leaner, flatter'' structure to deal effectively with ''an intensified competitive world, a slower growth world.'' He is eliminating an entire layer of senior management and increasing the duties of other top executives. BUT corporate America's most frequent response to the glut problem has been to appeal for protection. American car manufacturers, steel makers and textile spinners all have applied for - and received - some protection in the last five years. And there seems to be no end to the pleas for relief. Petitions to Washington for import relief are up 22 percent this year, according to International Trade Commission figures. In the 1980-84 period, they increased 44 percent. ''We've cut taxes. We've had a boom. I don't know what else we can do,'' said Mr. [Thomas G. Moore], the Adminstration's advisor. ''There are problems for some firms and some individuals. But from the point of view of society as a whole, if you try to prop up these prices, it's going to hurt. We're fortunate in a sense because these gluts are keeping prices under control.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=WAKING+UP+TO+THE+GLUT+ECONOMY&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1985-12-08&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Williams%2C+Winston&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: IN London, an eerie silence hangs over the tin trading ring at the Metal Exchange; trading has been halted because there are simply not enough buyers for the vast quantities of tin that producers in faraway Malaysia keep turning out. In America's farm belt, more than a million bushels of this year's record corn crop went directly into storage, piling up on top of last year's surplus. In corporate America, such blue chips as General Motors, Warner-Lambert and Standard Oil of Ohio are reeling from lackluster demand for their products. They have been forced to cut prices, lay off thousands of workers and write off billions of dollars in redundant production capacity.
All over the globe, in developed and developing nations alike, producers in a broad spectrum of industries are turning out more than consumers can buy, creating a new world economy - a glut economy.
Overabundance has replaced the chronic shortages of the 1970's. There are rising stockpiles of raw materials, underutilized and mothballed factories and vast pools of idle labor. Prices are weak or falling, not ratcheting out of control in an inflationary spiral. And protectionism is gaining in popularity as a quick way to bar some of the outpouring of new goods.
''Every protectionist bill in Congress represents a glut,'' says Edward Yardeni, chief economist for Prudential Bache Securities.
Even in the United States, whose economy has been growing faster than those of most other advanced countries, the glut is taking a heavy toll. Edward Denison, a Brookings Institution economist who studies growth, estimates that this country has ''the largest reserve of unused production capacity since the 30's.'' His figures show that the drop in demand during the last recession, in the early 80's, was so great that even a relatively strong, three-year-long recovery has not taken up the slack - and is not likely to in 1986.
The glut has already produced widespread problems -among builders who flooded the market with too much office space and too many condominiums; among manufacturers of personal computers, cars and color televisions who also overproduced, and among executives who borrowed heavily to expand their businesses in the hope that higher sales would justify the risks.
But the new glut economy has its bright side. The changed environment, some say, presents an opportunity for economic growth without the nightmare of volatile inflation - and the chance for consumers to stop racing after products whose prices keep rising from one week to the next.
However they view it, most economists and businessmen agree that the economy is undergoing a major change, far more significant than its usual tendency to swing between shortages and gluts as the business cycle plays itself out. ''This is definitely not an ordinary period,'' said Alan Greenspan, former chairman of the Council of Economic Advisors and president of Townsend-Greenspan, an economic consulting firm.
Despite the sea change, governments of the industrial nations are still directing their economic policies as if inflation and overly rapid economic growth were still the greatest dangers.
But the suddenness of the shift has forced business leaders to desperately shave costs and overhead to remain competitive. ''After years of growth and years of expansion the climate for business in the United States has changed dramatically,'' said Ralph Ablon, chairman of the Ogden Corporation, which has shed most of its manufacturing operations and moved into services. ''The adaptation to the new environment is going to be very painful,'' he added.
Some economists argue that the failure of government policymakers in the industrialized countries to switch gears to soak up the glut is making the adjustment more painful than neccessary - not only for business but for millions of unemployed workers.
Attempts here and abroad to keep inflation under control by restricting credit and carefully controlling government spending have created a situation that economists fear weakens growth and extends the duration of the glut economy.
''The combination of forces pointing to low world inflation also points to low economic growth,'' said the Morgan Guaranty Trust Company in its December economic bulletin. ''The policy makers should hardly stand idle before this troubling prospect. What is needed is a cautious swing - away from the inflation-fighting preoccupations of the last several years - in favor of expansionary policies for the future.''
That swing is not evident yet, particularly in Western Europe and Japan. According to the Morgan Guaranty study, the lack of growth in those countries has totally offset whatever stimulus the United States provided. In the industrial countries as a whole, growth has averaged 2.2 percent a year since 1979, compared with 3.6 percent in the 1970's and 5.7 percent in the 1960's. IN part, the changed environment is a result of the 1970's, when skyrocketing inflation forced businesses to build and expand as if the shortages and the pressures on prices would never end.
''In the 70's, there was this idea that we were going to run out of everything. The Club of Rome was saying there was going to be mass starvation,'' said Thomas G. Moore, a member of President Reagan's Council of Economic Advisors. Investors then went overboard, he contends: ''When the worldwide inflation stopped, people found themselves with a lot more capacity than was warranted by the market.''
In the United States, that realization came slowly. It took the effects of the strong dollar, which were felt initially in surging imports last year, and the slowing of economic growth to less than 4 percent in the first three quarters of this year from 1984's booming 6.8 percent to drive home the fact that excess production had become a serious problem.
Nowhere is it more serious than in the third world. As the glut forces down prices, those debt-strapped nations continue to pump out their products anyway, hoping to raise the same income with more output.
South Korea and Taiwan are heavily into color television manufacturing. Petrochemical plants and refineries dot Saudi Arabia and other oil producing countries. China has expanded its textile industry. Yugoslavia is preparing to export cars. Argentina has doubled its corn and wheat acreage. Brazilian shoes have become ubiquitous in North America, and its growing steel output is another constant American worry.
The overcapacity, however, is most acute in raw materials. In some countries production has accelerated to compensate for the 15 percent drop in metals prices during the last year and to finance the importation of necessities. Copper prices, for example, have plunged from $1.50 a pound in 1980 to about 62 cents a pound now. In that period Chile, a major producer, has increased its production by more than 80 percent .
''Everybody felt that the growth rate of metals would continue and they went searching the continents for new mines,'' says David Williamson, a metals analyst in the London office of Shearson Lehman Brothers. ''So all this new production came on stream in 1981 and walked right into a recession. Massive inventories developed,'' he continued. ''But once you're committed to opening up new mines and smelters that cost billions, you have to continue to operate them even if there's a surplus.'' In the United States, where debt-bloated balance sheets have made businesses unusually vulnerable to gluts, many companies have begun to grapple with the problem of overabundance. The Ogden Corporation, following a pattern that is becoming increasingly familiar, sold its collection of industrial operations - including its Avondale shipyard, an aluminum smelter, and a rail car assembler - to managers in a leveraged buy-out. It has redeployed its assets into businesses that turn solid municipal waste into energy and that provide management and maintenance services for large buildings like New York City's new convention center.
''We made up in our minds several years ago that we wanted to be a total services company,'' because the obstacles to becoming competitive in manufacturing were so forbidding says Ogden's Mr. Ablon.
Companies that remain in manufacturing are having to cut prices to move their products. Chrysler and American Motors are again offering rebates ranging from $400 to $1,000 a car. General Motors renewed its offer of cut-rate financing after imports walked away with nearly one-third of the automobile market in November.
Similar price cutting is raging in other industries. The airline industry - with far more seats than travelers are willing to pay full fare for - is in the midst of a nasty price war that may get worse before it gets better because of all the new planes and seating capacity set to join the fleets in coming months.
Delta Airlines, whose profits have been declining recently, will make substantial additions to its fleet next year, despite the heated fare competition. ''Growth is necessary to protect your traffic base. Our competitors are all growing and adding new spokes and new hubs,'' said Robert W. Coggin, a Delta marketing executive. ''If everyone else is growing, the status quo is not acceptable.''
Not all the companies involved in price wars have the staying power of Delta and the automobile companies. Many are top heavy with debt. American corporations owe debts totalling $1.56 billion with annual real increases of 8.6 percent in the last two years.
Economists worry that many of the most debt-strapped companies will end up in bankruptcy after an extended period of weak profits. ''Excess debt creation has many of the characteristics of intoxication,'' warned Mr. Greenspan. ''The early stages are heady and expansionary. But the hangover is inevitable and unpleasant.''
He is worried that massive failures of marginal companies and farms will create widespread financial hardships that are ''no longer politcally acceptable.'' Such a crisis would coax the government into a series of case-by-case bailouts that may be insignificant in themselves but would be inflationary in the aggregate, he says.
Not all of business is waiting for a bailout, though. A phenomenal wave of cost-cutting is washing across corporate America. Companies are paring down and writing off marginal assets as never before. Amerada Hess announced last week that it would take a $430 million after-tax write-off to squeeze its refining, marketing and tanker operations down to more appropriate sizes.
Santa Fe Industries has put 650,000 acres of timberland in Texas and Louisiana up for sale in a deal that would include processing plants for plywood and particle boards. The markets for both products have been battered by imports from Canada and the weakness in homebuilding.
Delta Airlines is putting an increasing number of employees on part-time schedules to maintain flexibility and control over labor costs. Indeed, labor experts contend that the use of part-time labor, especially in unskilled jobs, represents one of the megatrends of the 1980's. Consequently, they say, the seven percent unemployment rate understates the glut of available labor.
Sohio is cutting back on its workforce also, taking a $1.15 billion after-tax charge to slim down its copper mining operation and to finance the early retirement of part of its work force.
General Electric's chief, John F. Welch, is saying he wants to move to a ''leaner, flatter'' structure to deal effectively with ''an intensified competitive world, a slower growth world.'' He is eliminating an entire layer of senior management and increasing the duties of other top executives. BUT corporate America's most frequent response to the glut problem has been to appeal for protection. American car manufacturers, steel makers and textile spinners all have applied for - and received - some protection in the last five years. And there seems to be no end to the pleas for relief. Petitions to Washington for import relief are up 22 percent this year, according to International Trade Commission figures. In the 1980-84 period, they increased 44 percent.
Last week, the Commerce Department, in a preliminary finding, ruled that Japanese semi-conductors, the silicon circuits used in computers and other electronic products, had been dumped in the United States at unfair prices. The United States also ended an imbroglio with the European Community over imported steel. The Government banned European steel for a week while quotas for 1986 were negotiated.
But the Reagan Administration has generally opposed protectionism as an adequate response to the glutted world. For several years, government policy-makers, here and abroad, have been in a quandary, fearing that if they stimulated growth to soak up the gluts, they would set off another price spiral. So far, there has been little agreement on policy prescriptions and the steps to reduce the gluts have been tentative so far.
But many economists saw September's meeting in New York of the finance ministers of France, Britain, Germany, Japan and the United States as a first step toward a coordinated approach. The ministers agreed to work in concert, intervening in foreign markets to drive down the value of the dollar. A weaker dollar, theoretically, would lead to lower interest rates in the United States and throughout the world, sparking more economic activity and eating into the gluts.
That agreement was followed in October by a promise by the industrial nations to lend billions of dollars more to the developing nations to promote economic growth, in return for concessions that would increase the role of market forces in their economies. But many economists say both moves will not amount to much unless the industrial powers shake off their own fears of growth and inflation.
The debate among economists in the United States illustrates why settling on a policy that addresses the glut will not be an easy task. In fact, there is no unanimity on whether any action is even desirable.
''We've cut taxes. We've had a boom. I don't know what else we can do,'' said Mr. Moore, the Adminstration's advisor. ''There are problems for some firms and some individuals. But from the point of view of society as a whole, if you try to prop up these prices, it's going to hurt. We're fortunate in a sense because these gluts are keeping prices under control.''
The argument rages along ideological grounds, with more liberal economists generally calling for aggressively lowering interest rates to stimulate consumer demand and investment, and to lower the cost of servicing the deficit.
''We're like a Latin American country. We're getting into a situation where borrowing to pay the interest on the deficit is causing the deficit,'' said Kenneth Arrow, a Stanford economist. He would bring interest rates down by tightening fiscal policy - cutting defense, water resources programs and farm subsidies as well as raising taxes.
Proponents of leaving American government policy as it now stands point to the forced dieting of bloated companies as yet another benefit of slow growth and gluts. That, along with an inflation rate of less than 3 percent this year, is the main argument for keeping things as they are.
But pro-growth forces remain unconvinced. ''There's never been any doubt that with enough excess capacity you could lick inflation,'' said Mr. Denison, ''But that way you waste a lot of resources and create a lot of misery among people who are unemployed.''

Subject: ECONOMIC CONDITIONS AND TRENDS; PRODUCTION; SHORTAGES

People: WILLIAMS, WINSTON

Company / organization: Name: General Motors Corp; Ticker: GM; NAICS: 336399, 336111, 333415; DUNS: 00-535-6613; Name: Ogden Corp; Ticker: OG; NAICS: 551114; SIC: 3731, 5093; DUNS: 00-132-8053; Name: Delta Air Lines Inc; Ticker: DAL; NAICS: 481111; DUNS: 00-692-4872

ECONOMIC SCENE; Protectionism And U.S. Jobs

Author: Hershey, Robert D, Jr

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 Sep 1985: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425522279?accountid=11752

Abstract: American jobs, so the argument goes, are thereby being ''exported'' to Far East and other low-cost producers whose products are pouring in at unprecedented rates that not only threaten United States prosperity but, combined with poor sales of American goods overseas, undermine the nation's very ability to survive in a ferociously competitive world. While the strong dollar has diverted American purchasing power to foreign products and may thus appear at first glance to penalize domestic production and jobs, that is far from the whole story, asserts Alan Greenspan, a New York economist and sometime adviser to Republican politicians. ''The heavy demand for United States dollars has also left interest rates lower than they otherwise would have been and has clearly contributed to a lower rate of inflation,'' Mr. Greenspan told Congress earlier this year. ''These in turn have reduced the sense of instability and risk associated with capital investment. It is thus unclear on balance how much of a job loss, if any, the strong dollar has produced.'' Responding to assertions by some that the strong dollar may have cost the economy as many as two million jobs over the last five years, Mr. [Henry C. Wallich] said that ''it is difficult to conclude that overall United States employment and output have been unduly restrained'' by the trade deficit and the rising dollar. He added, ''In the absence of the policies that have led to the capital inflow and the strong dollar, while losses of jobs from these sources would have been less, so probably would have been the creation of new jobs.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=ECONOMIC+SCENE%3B+Protectionism+And+U.S.+Jobs&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1985-09-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Hershey%2C+Robert+D%2C+Jr&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: IN the din of protectionist special pleading by such battered industries as shoes, steel and apparel, many people have come to believe that the nation's economic policies have caused a wholesale loss of American jobs.
The basic mechanism is this: The budget deficit drives up interest rates, high interest rates raise the international value of the dollar and this in turn produces a trade deficit that could reach another record this year of $150 billion.
American jobs, so the argument goes, are thereby being ''exported'' to Far East and other low-cost producers whose products are pouring in at unprecedented rates that not only threaten United States prosperity but, combined with poor sales of American goods overseas, undermine the nation's very ability to survive in a ferociously competitive world.
In this kind of atmosphere, it is perhaps not surprising that members of Congress have produced more than 300 bills that in one way or another offer protection for some segment of American industry. But while it is true that some jobs have been lost -overseas shoe producers now take a whopping 80 percent of the United States market, up from 50 percent five years ago - it is important not to mistake the admitted hardship of an often clamorous few for a national catastrophe.
In fact, at least in employment terms, it can be argued that the twin trade and budget deficits result in more jobs for Americans, not fewer.
First of all, while the strong dollar has undoubtedly resulted in lost sales of American agricultural and other products, exports have done far better than is generally believed. They actually rose last year by 8.7 percent from the level of 1983.
But what about imports, which climbed 26.4 percent last year? Doesn't the fact that imports rose three times as much as exports prove that there was an overall American job loss? In a word, no.
Although there is as yet no convincing data either way - and maybe never will be - one can plausibly argue that, in the aggregate, there has been no loss at all. For this, one does not even need to rely on official labor market data, which last week showed that the nation's unemployment rate fell in August to 6.9 percent, the lowest in more than five years, while employment was rising to a new record. This result is consistent with the thesis, but for various reasons, including possible seasonal aberrations, not in itself persuasive.
The main reasons for thinking the country might even be gaining jobs rather than losing them from the deficits begin to emerge when one looks at the overall dynamics of what happens in the real world when trade expands. For example, large numbers of workers are needed to unload ships carrying imported products and to transport, package, distribute, sell and service these goods. There is no denying that this adds to employment, though nobody knows by how much.
Then there is the inflation-suppressing effect of imported goods, with American consumers benefiting not only from lower foreign prices but from the fact that this competition prevents domestic companies producing such goods from raising their prices. Lower inflation implies a lowering of interest rates, which tends to spur economic growth.
While the strong dollar has diverted American purchasing power to foreign products and may thus appear at first glance to penalize domestic production and jobs, that is far from the whole story, asserts Alan Greenspan, a New York economist and sometime adviser to Republican politicians. ''The heavy demand for United States dollars has also left interest rates lower than they otherwise would have been and has clearly contributed to a lower rate of inflation,'' Mr. Greenspan told Congress earlier this year. ''These in turn have reduced the sense of instability and risk associated with capital investment. It is thus unclear on balance how much of a job loss, if any, the strong dollar has produced.''
And Mr. Greenspan has some distinguished, nonpartisan company in Henry C. Wallich, a member of the Federal Reserve Board.
Responding to assertions by some that the strong dollar may have cost the economy as many as two million jobs over the last five years, Mr. Wallich said that ''it is difficult to conclude that overall United States employment and output have been unduly restrained'' by the trade deficit and the rising dollar. He added, ''In the absence of the policies that have led to the capital inflow and the strong dollar, while losses of jobs from these sources would have been less, so probably would have been the creation of new jobs.''
Job growth, in fact, has been little short of spectacular. Employment now stands at 108.9 million, eight million more than in 1980. All of Western Europe showed practically no growth at all during this period. To be sure, employment in goods-producing industries, which are usually thought most affected by foreign competition, has fallen as a percentage of total employment. Employment elsewhere, however, has expanded more.
The main message in this for Congress is that protectionism will not save jobs, except in the most parochial and shortsighted terms. The best jobs bill would be to cut the Federal budget.

GROWTH AND INFLATION DEBATED

Author: Quint, Michael

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 Mar 1985: D.7.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425359588?accountid=11752

Abstract: Mr. [Lawrence A. Kudlow] warned that if Administation officials put pressure on the Fed into compromising its anti-inflation policy, the result would be higher rates in the future. ''A dose of restraint by the Fed in the first half of this year will keep inflationary expectations low and open the door to lower interest rates in the second half,'' he said. ''But a dose of ease in the first half will lead to significantly higher interest rates in the second half,'' Mr. Kudlow said. Too much stimulation early this year will bring higher rates as credit demands rise and investors anticipate an upturn in inflation and a subsequent vigorous Federal Reserve move to tighten policy. Although economists are convinced that inflation is a development that follows from periods of excessive money supply and economic growth, Mr. [Paul A. Volcker] said, the Fed ''has no preconceived notion as to just how rapid'' economic growth can be ''without straining our resources or giving rise to price pressures.'' The Fed's forecast for 1985 of about 3 3/4 percent inflation and the same amount of real growth are not targets which the Fed tries to achieve, Mr. Volcker said.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=GROWTH+AND+INFLATION+DEBATED&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1985-03-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.7&amp;au=Quint%2C+Michael&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: One of the thorniest issues facing economists - the relationship between money supply growth, the economy and inflation - is a matter of great debate among policy makers in the Federal Reserve Board and the Reagan Administration, who want both growth and low inflation.
''The fundamental question is how fast can the economy grow without creating pressure for more inflation,'' Dana B. Johnson, an economist at the Northern Trust Company of Chicago, said. ''The Fed is always going to be more inflation-conscious than the body politic, but when the body politic changes, the Fed must make some adjustments,'' he added.
Since 1984 was a year of fast growth without high inflation, Mr. Johnson and other analysts agreed, the Fed has been having a harder time reminding the public and politicians about the dangers of inflation. The Fed chairman, Paul A. Volcker, is still perceived by investors and economists as a strong inflation fighter, but, in his Congressional testimony last month, he indicated that he is bending a little to accommodate the Reagan Administration's desire for fast economic growth.
Tolerate Some Growth

In past years, for example, the Federal Reserve's commitment to reducing inflation has often included statements about the need to gradually reduce money supply growth year after year. In 1985, however, Mr. Volcker said, the central bank may tolerate money supply growth in the upper part of the 4 percent to 7 percent growth target, up from 5.2 percent growth in 1984.
Mr. Volcker said faster growth could be warranted by a slowdown in velocity, or the rate at which money is turned over. But some analysts worry that faster money supply growth is not consistent with the Fed's objective of gradually reducing inflation and money supply growth.
The rise in 30-year Treasury bond yields to 11.68 percent, from about 11 percent at the end of January, cannot be attributed to fear among investors that faster money supply growth this year will bring more inflation. But the rise in bond yields so far above 11 percent, even though consumer prices have increased at a rate of 4 percent or less for the last three years, is a sign that many investors are still worried that inflation will rise again.
Further Signs

Another sign of the Fed's willingness to adjust its anti-inflationary monetary policy statements was the special attachment to Mr. Volcker's Congressional testimony titled ''Targeting Real Growth.''
Although economists are convinced that inflation is a development that follows from periods of excessive money supply and economic growth, Mr. Volcker said, the Fed ''has no preconceived notion as to just how rapid'' economic growth can be ''without straining our resources or giving rise to price pressures.'' The Fed's forecast for 1985 of about 3 3/4 percent inflation and the same amount of real growth are not targets which the Fed tries to achieve, Mr. Volcker said.
''The commentary about real growth is a sign of the vitriolic debate between the Fed on one side and the White House and Treasury on the other,'' Lawrence A. Kudlow, president of Lawrence Kudlow Associates, said. ''The White House accused Volcker of undermining their fast growth policies by tightening in 1984. Now, Volcker is trying to allay their fears by saying that the Fed does not base its policy on targets for economic growth.''
Economic Growth

While the Fed may say it does not target economic growth, many economists are convinced that the Fed does play close attention to the rate of economic growth. When the Fed reacts to excessive economic growth it may say its actions are a response to excessive growth in money supply or credit, they said.
''I think that intuitively the Fed has some notion of what is too fast or too slow,'' Thomas Thomson, chief economist at Crocker National Bank, said. While Federal Reserve Governors Preston Martin and Martha Seger have suggested that real growth of 5 percent may be sustainable, Mr. Thomson said, ''I think the sustainable non-inflationary growth rate is considerably less,'' or about 3 percent.
Mr. Kudlow warned that if Administation officials put pressure on the Fed into compromising its anti-inflation policy, the result would be higher rates in the future. ''A dose of restraint by the Fed in the first half of this year will keep inflationary expectations low and open the door to lower interest rates in the second half,'' he said. ''But a dose of ease in the first half will lead to significantly higher interest rates in the second half,'' Mr. Kudlow said. Too much stimulation early this year will bring higher rates as credit demands rise and investors anticipate an upturn in inflation and a subsequent vigorous Federal Reserve move to tighten policy.

WARNING ISSUED BY I.M.F. ON THE DOLLAR

Author: Special to The New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]13 Sep 1984: D.16.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425189156?accountid=11752

Abstract: The record level to which the dollar has risen is ''unsustainable'' and potentially ''detrimental'' to the United States and the rest of the world, the International Monetary Fund said today in its annual report. The I.M.F. also noted that, largely following patterns set by the United States, the average of short-term interest rates in the major industrial countries rose to 9 3/4 percent in the first half of 1984. The rates had fallen to 9 1/4 percent in the first half of 1983, from a peak of some 15 percent during 1981. ''The recent rise in interest rates,'' the report said, ''has been strongest and most persistent in the United States, where both short-term and long-term yields in the spring of 1984 reached levels roughly two percentage points higher than in the early months of 1983.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=WARNING+ISSUED+BY+I.M.F.+ON+THE+DOLLAR&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1984-09-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.16&amp;au=Special+to+The+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The record level to which the dollar has risen is ''unsustainable'' and potentially ''detrimental'' to the United States and the rest of the world, the International Monetary Fund said today in its annual report.
The report appeared to presage another spirited debate about the dollar and Washington's policies when the fund's 147 member countries meet here later this month. The joint annual meeting of the fund and the World Bank starts Sept. 24.
The report also repeated earlier I.M.F. criticism of the large United States budget deficit, but went a step further by warning of some of the perils of the high dollar.
The 203-page report noted that at around midyear, when the study was being drafted, the dollar had risen to about 15 percent above its average value for the period 1973 to 1982 in relation to the Japanese yen and the British pound. It was up 40 percent over the West German mark and nearly 50 percent over the French franc.
In recent days the dollar has reached record levels against the pound, the franc and the Italian lira and an 11 1/2-year high against the mark.
Impact of the Deficit
The I.M.F. document, whose key passages are negotiated by governments and represent a consensus of the membership, said the United States budget deficit, by intensifying competition for savings, contributes to high interest rates, which in turn increases the value of the dollar.
The Reagan Administration does not endorse this causal relationship. It argues that the dollar's popularity is spurred by low inflation, high growth of the American economy and profitability of American investments.
The report cited the dollar's strength as ''an important factor'' contributing to the deterioration in the American trade accounts - to an estimated $130 billion merchandise trade deficit this year.
The I.M.F. study said the trade deficit ''could have disquieting implications for the international allocation of world private saving,'' by drawing in savings to the United States from the rest of the world to finance the deficit.
Easing of Debt Crisis
At the same time, the international agency, which has more than $30 billion of short-term credits outstanding to more than 40 countries, said that the trade deficit had contributed to an easing of the world debt crisis. The huge American appetite for imports has spurred economic activity abroad and has especially helped many third world countries improve their cash position and pay their debts, the I.M.F. report notes.
The I.M.F. also noted that, largely following patterns set by the United States, the average of short-term interest rates in the major industrial countries rose to 9 3/4 percent in the first half of 1984. The rates had fallen to 9 1/4 percent in the first half of 1983, from a peak of some 15 percent during 1981. ''The recent rise in interest rates,'' the report said, ''has been strongest and most persistent in the United States, where both short-term and long-term yields in the spring of 1984 reached levels roughly two percentage points higher than in the early months of 1983.''

LEFTISTS POSE LITTLE THREAT TO BOLIVIA'S GOVERNMENT

Author: Simons, Marlise

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]12 Aug 1984: A.3.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425165405?accountid=11752

Abstract: Successive American administrations continued to act as if Bolivia were indeed vulnerable. The United States built up the weak military with training and materiel. From 1972 to 1976, it sent $47 million of military aid and outfitted five army regiments, bolstering an institution that was adding to the record of coups and countercoups and which eventually assumed a major role in the international narcotics traffic. Some Bolivian officers were even cited as criminals in Miami. Bolivia thus joined the list of Latin countries where the United States has been embarrassed by officers it chose as political allies. In Guatemala, Chile and Argentina, among other places, American support in the 1960's helped promote military regimes that rejected United States influence in the 1970's. In Bolivia's case, Washington suspended all military and most civilian aid in 1980 and called home its Ambassador. Confidence was restored in 1982 when Hernan Siles Zuazo became the first freely elected President in 19 years. Since then, Bolivia has received close to $230 million, including $7 million for the military and $5 million for drug enforcement, the linchpin of American policy. Last week, after long delay, the Government sent police and military forces into four cocaine-producing provinces. Heritage of Dictatorship The new democratic Government has been preoccupied with formidable obstacles. In the past year, it had to cope with near-bankruptcy, 1,000 percent inflation, general strikes, food riots, drought, floods, three coup plots and the kidnapping.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=LEFTISTS+POSE+LITTLE+THREAT+TO+BOLIVIA%27S+GOVERNMENT&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1984-08-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.3&amp;au=Simons%2C+Marlise&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Almost daily, the United States Embassy here hears rumors predicting that Bolivia is about to fall into the hands of international communism. Alarm is spread by local politicians and businessmen and repeated by Latin American military envoys who find the current experiment in civilian democracy disturbing. Echoing these views, the daily newspaper El Diario complained recently that the United States was ''looking on with indifference'' while ''the communists are advancing as much as the drug traffickers.''
But American diplomats say they have found no substance to reports that scores of Libyans, Cubans, Nicaraguans and other unfriendly folk are preparing revolution. On June 30, the American Ambassador, Edwin Corr, even played a key role in blocking a military coup. Dissident army officers and narcotics policemen kidnapped the President, claiming they wanted to ''clean out the Communists.''
The Reagan Administration finds itself in an unusual role: Despite its strong commitment to fighting communism, in Bolivia it is supporting a left-of-center Government that includes two Communist Ministers and other officials who have been political exiles in Cuba and Eastern Europe. Washington apparently sees the elected Government as its main hope of combating Bolivia's large-scale cocaine trafficking. During previous regimes, ''top military leaders were up to their ears in the drug trade,'' a United States diplomat said, ''and several drug rings had protection from the armed forces.'' The power that enabled the military to become involved in narcotics was, to a good measure, an unintended result of United States policies. When Ernesto ''Che'' Guevara decided that Bolivia was ripe for a Cuban-style revolution, he drew Washington's attention to this poor region in the Andes. Before he was killed here in 1967, Mr. Guevara learned that he was wrong.
But successive American administrations continued to act as if Bolivia were indeed vulnerable. The United States built up the weak military with training and materiel. From 1972 to 1976, it sent $47 million of military aid and outfitted five army regiments, bolstering an institution that was adding to the record of coups and countercoups and which eventually assumed a major role in the international narcotics traffic. Some Bolivian officers were even cited as criminals in Miami. Bolivia thus joined the list of Latin countries where the United States has been embarrassed by officers it chose as political allies. In Guatemala, Chile and Argentina, among other places, American support in the 1960's helped promote military regimes that rejected United States influence in the 1970's. In Bolivia's case, Washington suspended all military and most civilian aid in 1980 and called home its Ambassador. Confidence was restored in 1982 when Hernan Siles Zuazo became the first freely elected President in 19 years. Since then, Bolivia has received close to $230 million, including $7 million for the military and $5 million for drug enforcement, the linchpin of American policy. Last week, after long delay, the Government sent police and military forces into four cocaine-producing provinces. Heritage of Dictatorship The new democratic Government has been preoccupied with formidable obstacles. In the past year, it had to cope with near-bankruptcy, 1,000 percent inflation, general strikes, food riots, drought, floods, three coup plots and the kidnapping. Years of dictatorship prevented new leadership from developing. The three most important civilians, now in their 70's, were already leaders in the 1952 revolution. The three, the President, labor leader Juan Lechin and opposition leader Victor Paz Estenssoro have since become enemies. President Siles once confided that he allied himself with the Communists to use them against Mr. Lechin, who has already ordered four general strikes against Mr. Siles this year, including one just four days after the President's abduction. The ruling coalition is also weakened by infighting among its four left-of-center parties and other leftists. American diplomats say there is no contradiction in supporting the Siles Government. ''There are a lot of people out of step here with the Reagan Administration,'' one said, ''but some act as a moderating influence on the President. We would gain nothing by pressing against the Communists here.'' It may be too early to say, but Washington's policy in Bolivia suggests that lessons may have been drawn from Central America, where repression of leftist opponents of the establishment ignited a violent guerrilla movement.

Summary of Meetings Held By Seven Nations Since 1975

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]08 June 1984: A.11.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425119371?accountid=11752

Abstract: Versailles, 1982 - Monetary stability and the Reagan Adminstration's call for economic sanctions against the Soviet Union were the two leading issues. The United States faced criticism about large budget deficits and relatively high interest rates. A study of the need for government intervention in currency markets was initiated, but the conferees later disagreed over who was to blame for continuing monetary disorder. Williamsburg, Va., 1983 - President [Reagan] managed to win a show of political solidarity on nuclear issues, but on key economic issues only broad goals, rather than specific actions, were formulated. The industrialized nations agreed to maintain economic recovery, to reduce inflation, interest rates and unemployment, and to roll back protectionism, but left without concrete policies. Despite their pledges, interest rates, especially in the United States, have risen substantially and the average unemployment rose last year to 8.3 percent, compared with 8 percent in 1982.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Summary+of+Meetings+Held+By+Seven+Nations+Since+1975%3A+%5Bsummary%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1984-06-08&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.11&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Rambouillet, France, 1975 - At this first gathering, which came during one of the worst business slumps since the end of World War II, participants declined to return to fixed exchange rates, but agreed to counteract disorderly market conditions. Although the need for economic cooperation was acknowledged, there was sharp division over individual actions, which generated some bitter debate.
Dorado Beach, P.R., 1976 - Inflation was the main issue, with a prominent theme the need to increase jobs without stimulating inflation. Even so, unemployment rose to 15 million over the next 12 months. There was also an agreement to consider a multinational credit facility to help developing nations.
London, 1977 - The focus was on recovery and the conferees committed themselves to a 5 percent growth rate for the year - a goal that West Germany and Japan failed to meet. Promises for a comprehensive energy program also went unfulfilled.
Bonn, 1978 - The Europeans were critical of the weak American dollar, of high inflation in the United States and of what was perceived as the apparent lack of an energy policy in Washington. A clearly articulated, diverse set of goals was formulated for each country. West Germany, Canada and France agreed to economic stimulation; the United States to reduced dependence on foreign oil; Britain and Italy to reduced inflation, and Japan to a cut in exports.
Tokyo, 1979 - Energy issues dominated the agenda when the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries announced a 50 percent increase in oil prices the day before the meeting. Participants agreed to place limits on oil imports, and the United States said it would let domestic oil prices rise to international levels to induce energy conservation. Interest in the oil market faded, however, as supplies increased.
Venice, 1980 - Energy remained the main issue, with the industrialized nations agreeing to develop alternate sources. Inflation was also called a priority. The meeting was plagued by political tension, with the conferees sharply divided over relations with the Soviet Union after the invasion of Afghanistan.
Ottawa, 1981 - This meeting was the first summit experience for many of the participating heads of government, including President Reagan, who sought to keep a relatively low profile. The nations agreed to substantive North-South discussions, but fell out over the level of aid to the less developed nations.
Versailles, 1982 - Monetary stability and the Reagan Adminstration's call for economic sanctions against the Soviet Union were the two leading issues. The United States faced criticism about large budget deficits and relatively high interest rates. A study of the need for government intervention in currency markets was initiated, but the conferees later disagreed over who was to blame for continuing monetary disorder.
Williamsburg, Va., 1983 - President Reagan managed to win a show of political solidarity on nuclear issues, but on key economic issues only broad goals, rather than specific actions, were formulated. The industrialized nations agreed to maintain economic recovery, to reduce inflation, interest rates and unemployment, and to roll back protectionism, but left without concrete policies. Despite their pledges, interest rates, especially in the United States, have risen substantially and the average unemployment rose last year to 8.3 percent, compared with 8 percent in 1982.
