RESULTS OF 1983 CONFERENCE

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]29 May 1984: D.7.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425062628?accountid=11752

Abstract: At Williamsburg the heads of state declared, ''We renew our commitment to reduce structural budget deficits, in particular, by limiting the growth of expenditures.'' The budget deficits of the summit countries are little changed from their levels of a year ago, but most of the deficits are largely cyclical - made up mostly of government spending (partly for unemployment benefits) to tide them through the recession. Others, however, see a discouraging trend in support of the ''fair trade'' alternative to free trade. To many Europeans, said Sven W. Arndt, international economist at the American Enterprise Institute, fair trade means protecting a country's share of the world export market. To Americans, he said, it means forcing other countries to remove government subsidies and other aid to exporting industries. The leaders also promised ''closer consultations on policies affecting exchange markets,'' and since Williamsburg there has been evidence of closer cooperation among monetary authorities of the seven countries.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=RESULTS+OF+1983+CONFERENCE%3A+%5BANALYSIS%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1984-05-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.7&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Here, according to economists, is how seven major industrial democracies performed in trying to fulfill the commitments they made at their Williamsburg, Va., economic summit conference, held May 28-30, 1983:
Recovery
Last year the leaders of the United States, Britain, West Germany, France, Italy, Japan and Canada pledged themselves to economic growth, a painless commitment because recovery was well under way in the United States and stirring elsewhere.
The economies of the seven summit countries declined by an average of two-tenths of 1 percent in 1982, then grew 2.5 percent in 1983 and are expected to grow even faster, about 4.5 percent, this year, according to the International Monetary Fund.
The overall figures, however, camouflage weaknesses in major countries such as France and Italy and throughout Latin America.
Partly because of protectionism and imbalances in the recovery, says Howard J. Howe, an international economist at Wharton Econometrics, the growth of both world trade and the overall world economy so far lags the pace of other recent recoveries.
Economists attribute much of this year's expansion to the power of the United States economy, especially strong American consumer demand and the high- valued dollar.
Budget Deficits
At Williamsburg the heads of state declared, ''We renew our commitment to reduce structural budget deficits, in particular, by limiting the growth of expenditures.'' The budget deficits of the summit countries are little changed from their levels of a year ago, but most of the deficits are largely cyclical - made up mostly of government spending (partly for unemployment benefits) to tide them through the recession.
With the American economy completely recovered from the last recession, however, most of its deficit is structural, the result of increases in Federal spending (mostly for the military and interest payments on the national debt) and decreases in the growth of government revenues, the consequence of three years of individual and business tax reductions.
As a percentage of the gross national product, the American deficit last year was 5.4 percent, only slightly higher than those of the six other countries, but it has grown far faster than the others. In addition, the United States has a lower national savings rate than those of all summit countries but Italy, which means that the country is left with proportionately less to finance the deficit and to invest in industry.
Protection
The summit leaders pledged themselves to reverse the spread of protectionism as the world recovery grew. But all the countries continue to maintain a wide variety of restraints on imports and to provide numerous incentives for their exporters. The United States, which some economists credit with having adhered to this commitment better than many of the other nations, nevertheless decided to curb imports of Japanese automobiles for a fourth year and to stiffen restraints on imports of textiles and steel.
The United States realized some success in efforts to encourage Japan to relax its barriers to American goods. ''While proceeding with its usual glacial slowness, Japan has nevertheless made some progress,'' said Richard W. Roberts, president of the National Foreign Trade Council in New York, an association of export-oriented American companies.
Others, however, see a discouraging trend in support of the ''fair trade'' alternative to free trade. To many Europeans, said Sven W. Arndt, international economist at the American Enterprise Institute, fair trade means protecting a country's share of the world export market. To Americans, he said, it means forcing other countries to remove government subsidies and other aid to exporting industries.
And to many countries, fair trade means protecting young, fragile industries from foreign competition until they get established.
Unemployment For the seven countries, the unemployment rate averaged less than 4 percent from the mid-1960's to the mid-1970's, according to the I.M.F., and the American rate was usually the highest. At Williamsburg the leaders pledged to reduce unemployment, but the average rose last year, to 8.3 percent from 1982's 8 percent. Despite the world recovery, forecasts show the average sliding this year to only 7.7 percent, the present level in the United States.
The average would be closer to 10 percent were it not for Japan's exceptionally low rate of 2.6 percent last year. Europe has been far less successful than the United States and Japan in fostering new, often technology-based, industries to provide jobs for workers displaced by fading, older industries.
Interest Rates
The Williamsburg communique sought lower rates, but American interest rates, in particular, have risen substantially since then. Now, for the first time in 15 years, they are higher than British interest rates. The prime rate of American banks has risen to 12.5 percent from 10.5 percent; the equivalent British rate is now 10 percent.
More sensitive rates on three-month certificates of deposit show a similar trend, according to Citicorp Information Services. The average American rate for the second quarter climbed to 11 percent from 8.8 percent. The rates have declined in Japan, Britain, France and Italy. Rates on German certificates have risen, but to only 6 percent from 5.4 percent.
Inflation
Average annual price increases for the seven countries were only 4.7 percent last year, 2 full percentage points below the rate in 1982. Britain, with inflation of 20 percent in 1980, recorded a rate last year of just 5.1 percent. A broad measure of inflation, the G.N.P. deflator, showed an average inflation rate in the United States last year of 4.1 percent, less than half the level of 1980.
The European countries brought inflation down partly by imposing severe restraints on the growth of government spending and partly by curbing the growth of their money supplies. In the United States, government spending rose, but other forces - such as the Federal Reserve Board's tight monetary policy and the strong dollar - helped tame inflation.
Currencies
The summit countries promised ''greater stability of exchange rates.'' By various estimates, the American dollar remains 20 percent to 30 percent overvalued, but the volatile activity in foreign-exchange rates has subsided a bit over the past year, economists say. European currencies, particularly, are far better synchronized now than a year ago.
The leaders also promised ''closer consultations on policies affecting exchange markets,'' and since Williamsburg there has been evidence of closer cooperation among monetary authorities of the seven countries.
The United States, which for years had practiced a free-market policy of ''benign neglect'' of turbulence in the currency markets has modified that position over the past year.
Third World
After the Williamsburg conference, the Reagan Administration won a difficult struggle in Congress to increase the American contribution toward a general increase in the money that the International Monetary Fund uses to help developing countries of the third world manage their debts.
The growth of the world economy, with the accompanying growth in trade, expanded markets in the West for the developing countries. For example, Brazil, the biggest of the debtor countries, increased its exports 24 percent from the first quarter of 1983 to the first quarter of 1984.
But with the rise in American interest rates, to which the debtor countries' payments are tied, the foreign debts of the developing countries that are not oil producers grew from $612 billion in 1982 to $669 billion in 1993. With this year's interest rate increases, economists say, the debts now total about $700 billion.

AN INTERVIEW WITH REUBIN ASKEW, EX-GOVERNOR OF FLORIDA

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]03 Jan 1984: D.14.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/425731038?accountid=11752

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=AN+INTERVIEW+WITH+REUBIN+ASKEW%2C+EX-GOVERNOR+OF+FLORIDA%3A+%5BTEXT%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1984-01-03&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.14&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Following are key questions and answers from an interview with Reubin Askew by William E. Farrell:
ASKEW. I think the most important problem, domestically, is getting control of the deficits, because the implications of not controlling the deficits are staggering. There's a structural aspect to these deficits that will continue to enlarge, and which will make difficult extended economic recovery, will contribute toward higher real interest rates in real terms. And will have its impact subsequently upon the housing industry, which will be critically important for the recovery of the country.
Now those deficits also have a very serious impact upon our external economic relations. As long as the deficits stay high and those interest rates in real terms stay high we're going to continue to see dollars coming in, capital coming into this country, seeking not only a profit from the yield, but this also being the safest state in the world for the investment of private capital. Now what this is going to do, it's going to keep the dollar very strong, it's going to make it difficult for us to be as competitive as we could otherwise be, based on price and quality, because it just makes our goods more expensive and makes our competitor's weaker currencies cheaper.
And then so much of the other countries, the industrialized democracies, losing that capital because of the United States will also impede their own recovery. And then the difficulty of the non-oil-producing third world nations to pay for their oil bill, because if their currencies get weaker they have to pay for those oil bills in dollars. It becomes critically important in terms of their own recovery. And that makes difficult their recovery and the more difficulty they have the less ability they have to service even the interest level on the debt service. Against the Grain on Trade
A. As far as the United States is concerned on the question of trade, I am going against the grain of the others, because I think it's critically important for our country to have policies that not only vigorously fight unfair trade practices, but that also positively work toward keeping our markets open. Otherwise we're going to find ourselves trying to insulate ourselves from areas where we're not competitive, and then in many instances retarding our own movement toward competitiveness. But then in addition we'll find markets closed to us overseas as a result of closing ours, and they'll be closed to us in areas where we are the most competitive.
Agriculture, for instance, will be the first victim of our turning inward. Of course we've got to be talking more about expanding exports abroad in nonagricultural as well as agricultural items where certainly we know that immediately now we're very competitive in those areas.
So all of these are problems that the solution to which at times is going to be elusive, but we know the key ingredients that triggers all of them is combating the deficits. How to Reduce the Deficit
A. Well, I think that for all the reasons I've set forth plus others, I think it's a very critical problem. I'm not one of those that believes the structural aspect of it is a myth. I believe it's real. Which means that the deficits are not self-correcting. Even with a substantial recovery we're not going to clear the deficits. So it's going to require strategy. A twofold strategy of both increasing revenue or preventing the loss of revenue now projected to be lost, and in addition, some reductions of spending.
And on the tax side, obviously I was against it from the very beginning because I don't believe that we were calculated to really see the economic spending list that was supposed to come as a result of it. It was supposed to produce this big pool of savings, and Americans are saving less now then they were the year Ronald Reagan became President. We were supposed to have substantial investment. That's not taking place. And the jobs aren't taking place to the extent that was envisioned. So the justification for the big cut simply hasn't transpired, and yet there's also been substantial loss of that to the Treasury.
So we're going to have to take some action on the top side. I doubt seriously, while I have been willing to talk about reinstating some of the tax cuts, I don't think, frankly, that it's going to happen. I believe also that it's that the tax has lost some of its progressive nature. I think that the corporate income tax, the changes have substantially reduced its overall contribution. But what I believe is feasible: first of all, I would support postponing of indexing of income tax brackets. He Explains Indexing
A. In 1985, we're going into a law that would say that when inflation goes up, your income tax brackets are adjusted commensurately downward. Hypothetically if you have a 6 percent inflation factor, the income tax bracket should come down 6 percent.
What I am suggesting is that it should be deferring this while they have a structural aspect of the deficit so at least they can get in it to reduce the deficit. However, because I believe that there is substantial validity in having the index in income bracket, I'm generally supportive of it. But what I'd like to see us do is I'd like to defer the first couple of percent of that so that for example instead of adjusting the income tax bracket down 6 percent, we would defer first 2 percent and adjust to only 4, which means you wouldn't have revenue loss that you otherwise envision to lose under the present revenue projections that continue to project an ever increasing amount of deficit. On the other hand, I think you also have to do the thing with the cost of living adjustments and all entitlement programs such as Social Security. Changes in Benefit Programs
A. I would start cutting the defense budget. It is increasing this year some 33 percent while your conventional forces are increasing somewhere around 8.5 percent. I would opt for a smaller figure, particularly on the strategic where so much of the money is located. Figures on Military Cuts
A. Oh, yes. I would not go forward with any further commitments on MX. I would prefer instead an alternative program of upgrading the Minuteman 3 missiles that presently contain the new warheads and put the new guidance system that would go in an MX, you put it on a Minuteman III. So you would have upgraded your Minuteman 3 and the fact that you achieve the same mission as the MX, which would save a significant amount of money and not only that the final deployment dates would be quicker.
I would not go forward with any further B-1's. I would opt more for the expanded use of cruise missiles on B-52's and then the technology on the stealth bomber. I would not continue production of any type of neutron bombs now. We have got to get control of the Defense Department budget.
One of the most serious problems facing the next President is the gap between what is anticipated that we require to achieve the the present missions before the Department of Defense and money in sight to try to fund them. We need to be much tougher on cost overruns; we need to do a better job of procurement. Growth in the Budget
A. As I view the budget, I believe we can do all that's necessary for us at 5 percent growth adjusted to actual inflation. And I must say to you that I would have to be convinced that the 5 percent is necessary and I would be willing to go above it if I was shown that it's necessary now.
And I also feel that we ought to go into a voluntary and affordable program of national service for young people that would seek to eventually take over the annual quota for the volunteer Army and as fast as you could possibly effect it.
A. I'm talking about voluntary national service for men and women with a military and a nonmilitary program of national service. But I'm speaking now principally on the military side of it because I believe that we're looking at spending too much money on the entry level for the volunteer Army in order to compete with private enterprise. We're getting the entry level now because of the economic downturn. Presently our problem is retention. But entry level also will become a problem as economic opportunities improve. And rather than continue to keep escalating the level of the entry, we need to start projecting a lower level pay and couple that with educational benefits, which is in effect a way of deferred compensation and we can save substantial money from the Department of Defense's budget.
Now on the nonmilitary side, I would be talking about expanding the Peace Corps, expanding the Youth Conservation Corps, existing programs that have proven to be successful. Tensions With Soviet Union
A. I think you have to recognize that our problems with the Soviet Union haven't been by accident. It's a different country, they think differently, it's a different culture, not a democratic country, it's an authoritarian country.
But in spite of all of this, I believe there is enough commonality of interests between our two nations to seek affirmatively more ways in which we can get along, and to be looking for ways in which to locate the commonality of interest to, hopefully, try to reduce the tensions so that the other efforts that we make would, frankly, seem less necessary.
The excessive rhetoric of the President, I think is a good example. I think it's just unnecessary. I think his excessive rhetoric has been counterproductive in terms of any hope of trying to come together on improving our relationships. Now some of the things he's said I completely agree with, but the excess of which he goes to say it, I think really is unnecessary.
As far as arms control, I think it's the paramount issue of our times. And what you have to realize is that no arms control agreement is going to keep the peace. It's lessening of tensions that reduce the likelihood of conflict, and a reduction of tensions also increases the likelihood of some kind of arms control agreement.
And how you reach the arms control agreement, there are different approaches. This President ran on a platform that he was going to immediately come forth with his options; he didn't do that. It became clear that what he was really talking about was to try to build up enough leverage to an arms control race to create the bargaining chips to go in and be able to get what he wanted. Value of Nuclear Freeze
A. Well, what I would do first of all is that I would recognize that the nuclear freeze itself, I think, as a negotiating strategy, will not produce the type of agreement that even his opponents want. The nuclear freeze movement I applaud, because I think without it I don't think the President would have ever gotten off dead center. But I think that we would have to be willing to be more challenging in the proposals we've made and then be more consistent, frankly, in the proposals.
We've made proposals knowing full well that the Soviet Union wouldn't accept them. They've made proposals knowing we wouldn't accept them. I think one of the first things that you would want to consider, even though technically it would have to be worked out carefully, is to go in and combine both the Start talks and the I.N.F. talks because you reach a point where, with the Soviet Union, it's of little consolation where they can be reached with intermediate missiles and the long-range missiles. They're both missiles.
In fact, they have less warning time with the intermediate missile than they do the long-range missile. So you have a lot more bargaining chips on the table. Change in Strategy Noted
Furthermore, something that had not been pointed out much, that the President adopted in that strategy, is that for the first time he's been willing to talk about aggregate destructive capability; that is, talking about the bombers and the missiles at the same time. They've been wanting to only talk about the missiles. Now one thing that that group recommended, and that I fully support, which I think he should have, and that is to put a ceiling on the number of warheads and the total destructive capability of all of our systems together. Just stop them where they are now in terms of capability and number.
Now this isn't freeze in the sense the others are talking about. This would allow modernization from within all during the time that you're reducing the numbers.
What we need to be doing is reducing the number, don't go any higher, try to bring the talks together and at the same time try to get rid of any destabilizing systems and try to reduce the number of destabilizing systems, which is one of my big complaints about the MX. Policies in Middle East
A. I think it's a mistake.
A. That's right. I think, like almost everyone else, I supported the initial United States participation in the multinational peacekeeping force to insure the orderly withdrawal of the P.L.O. from Beirut. We got that accomplished, we had an objective, we left.
We came back because of the exigencies of the moment because of the tragedies in the Palestinian camps.
Once we went back in, in my opinion, the President was incumbent under the War Powers Act to define the policy that then existed. And he didn't do that, and I think the Congress made a serious mistake that they ought to reconsider when they come back, ever extending the time, and I said so at the time. The President should have the pressure within the 60 plus 90 extension, or the 30 days extension to 90 days, to state what our mission is.
Because what is our mission there? View of What Should Be Done
A. What I'm saying is that that we ought phase out our presence in favor of a United Nations peacekeeping force of neutral nations.
See, the United States and the Soviet Union have a stake in the outcome. The British and the French have had interests in that area. Bring in others that will help stabilize the situation militarily but will not be perceived as taking one side or another, as the different factions attempt to get together on some form of power sharing. And that's really what it's about.
But we should not look toward Lebanon in a vacuum. We've still got to look to it in an overall context of Middle East negotiations. We should not be getting drawn into a war with Syria, and that's essentially what we're doing. Nor should we ever have placed Americans, fighting marines that aren't given a chance to be fighting marines, where their only mission there is to keep from getting killed.
Now I suggested way back that they ought to immediately start moving all the people who weren't otherwise on duty immediately off the airport and putting them on the ships and ferrying them back and forth. We may get to a point where you eventually might want to do that.
In other words, we need to be exploring ways to avoid getting drawn into a war, in ways that are more productive toward a reconciliation, as much as can be expected of the Lebanese situation, and understanding, too, it's always going to spill over into the Middle East conflict. Camp David Accords
A. Well, I believe, in retrospect, the President's initiative really was not consistent with Camp David. I know that, in all due respect to President Carter, he said it was, but one of the underlying, I think, understandings at Camp David was we weren't going to try to so impose so many different conditions that we would be perceived as forcing one side or the other as opposed to trying to create the atmosphere that would permit the negotiations between the parties themselves.
I think, in a sense, they have got to try to come to grips with it. But right now, you could be seeing some really basic shifts in the Middle East. You could really be seeing more of a split, you know, within the Arab side because you've always had these differences within a lot of these so-called Moslem nations, some of which, like Iran, that was a Persian nation and not Arabic. View on Central America
A. Well, the President is finally starting to take a tougher stand in El Salvador on supplying arms to that Government. I still don't think he's managed to convince them in terms of trying to reduce the killing by both government, both uniform and nonuniform security forces.
A. In El Salvador for the moment because that's the only place we're really military supplying for the moment. We're doing some things in Honduras that's common interest. But I think that until the El Salvadors realize that we are willing to go so far as to pull all military systems out from them and be prepared to do so, will they make the changes that without which they're not going to get the active support of their own people to really stabilize that government. So that's one situation.
And I believe a lot of the problem comes from without. I believe that the Communist threat is real in Central America. But I also notice it's against a backdrop - a backdrop of poverty, of political repression and economic injustice, you know, and if that area is worth our fighting for, it ought to be worth our spending some time with long-term goals economically.
In the case of Nicaragua, I don't think we should be supporting a mpvement whose purpose is to overthrow the Government of Nicaragua. I think that it will be counterproductive. I don't think we ought to be having 5,000 ground troops in Honduras. Again I think that draws fire. I think however the President was justified in going into Grenada because it was a difficult situation. I think the Americans were in fear. I think that there was anarchy. Possibility on Vice President
A. No. I'm not interested in the Vice Presidency. McGovern offered me that in 1972 along with others before the list grew long. The challenge to me is in the Presidency. So I think the strength of our candidacy has been a record of my willing to take risks over period of 25 years in public service plus that risk, even though risk-taking kept me with public support and that allowed me to do things and I think again the record speaks for itself.
So, it's not going to be just enough to try to understand the world of the 1980's, which I do, because I've been out negotiating for this country during the '80's as the chief trade negotiator. I know the country's got to adjust to the change in the nature of its competition. That's the big challenge facing this country economically. It has to live within its means. It can do that. It's just that there have to be national goals that engage the American people, the sense of fairness that they understand, feel a part of, plans and visions for the country and how you go about doing it, it's going to be free enough to do it.

O.E.C.D. GLOOMY ON EUROPE

Author: Lewis, Paul

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]22 Dec 1983: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424837821?accountid=11752

Abstract: In the United States, the speed and pattern of the current recovery are broadly similar to past recoveries and much faster than elsewhere, Kjell Andersen, acting head of the O.E.C.D.'s Economics and Statistics Department, said at a news conference today. Much of this lopsidedness can be explained by expansionist monetary policy in the United States and a lack of market flexibility in Europe, the O.E.C.D. said. While Britain's economy should grow by about 2.25 percent in 1984 and the German and Italian economies by 2 percent each, the O.E.C.D. forecasts stagnation for France and many smaller European countries. As a result, unemployment will remain unchanged in Britain, at 11.5 percent, while it will rise in West Germany, France and Italy. This weak European performance means the recovery in the non-Communist world as a whole will remain disappointingly slow, the O.E.C.D. says, running at about half the 5 to 7 percent rate typical of similar phases of earlier recoveries. Overall growth in the 24 O.E.C.D. countries will average 3.25 percent next year, compared with an expected 2.25 percent this year. And unemployment will stabilize at 9 percent of the total work force.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=O.E.C.D.+GLOOMY+ON+EUROPE%3A+%5BSTATISTICS%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1983-12-22&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Lewis%2C+Paul&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Strong economic recoveries next year in the United States and Japan will not be sufficient to bring an equivalent resurgence in Western Europe, the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development said today.
The forecast was contained in The Economic Outlook, a semiannual publication of the O.E.C.D. The group, made up of 24 non-Communist industrial nations, publishes economic analyses that are widely regarded as among the most comprehensive and authoritative.
In the United States, the speed and pattern of the current recovery are broadly similar to past recoveries and much faster than elsewhere, Kjell Andersen, acting head of the O.E.C.D.'s Economics and Statistics Department, said at a news conference today. Much of this lopsidedness can be explained by expansionist monetary policy in the United States and a lack of market flexibility in Europe, the O.E.C.D. said.
The United States recovery should continue strong next year, the forecast said, despite the likelihood that a big Federal budget deficit will keep interest rates high.
Faster Growth Rate The O.E.C.D. predicts that the American economy will grow at a 5 percent rate during 1984, after adjustments for inflation, or slightly faster than the 4.5 percent rate the Administration is forecasting and which the industrial group had forecast in its last report.
Prices are expected to rise by about 5.25 percent, an acceleration on this year's expected 4 percent inflation rate. But unemployment should fall, to 8 percent during 1984 as a whole before dropping further to 7.75 percent in the first half of 1985. The 1983 annual rate was put at 9.5 percent.
For Japan, the O.E.C.D. predicts a 4 percent growth rate in 1984, up from 3 percent this year. As usual, Japanese inflation will remain very low, averaging only about 1.5 percent this year and next.
But the economies of Western Europe, in marked contrast, will hardly grow at all next year, and unemployment is expected to continue rising.
Stagnation Seen
On average, the O.E.C.D. sees the European economies expanding by a mere 1.5 percent, compared with only 1 percent this year. As a result, unemployment that averaged 10.5 percent in 1983 is likely to creep up to 11.25 percent next year and 12 percent during the first half of 1985.
While Britain's economy should grow by about 2.25 percent in 1984 and the German and Italian economies by 2 percent each, the O.E.C.D. forecasts stagnation for France and many smaller European countries. As a result, unemployment will remain unchanged in Britain, at 11.5 percent, while it will rise in West Germany, France and Italy.
This weak European performance means the recovery in the non-Communist world as a whole will remain disappointingly slow, the O.E.C.D. says, running at about half the 5 to 7 percent rate typical of similar phases of earlier recoveries. Overall growth in the 24 O.E.C.D. countries will average 3.25 percent next year, compared with an expected 2.25 percent this year. And unemployment will stabilize at 9 percent of the total work force.
Deficit Warning Repeated
The O.E.C.D. warns, as it has done regularly in past forecasts, that the huge United States budget deficit, together with the high interest rates and the strong dollar it causes, remains a threat to America's own economic recovery and to the upturn in the rest of the world.
Next year, the O.E.C.D. expects the United States balance of payments deficit on current accounts to nearly double, from $43 billion to a new record of $82 billion, because the dollar's strength prices American exports out of foreign markets at the same time as it lowers the prices of imports into the United States.
But the O.E.C.D. fears that at some point the budget deficit's size will undermine confidence in the dollar, halting the inflow of foreign funds now financing the country's payments deficit and forcing the United States to take drastic deflationary action.
Nevertheless, at the moment, the O.E.C.D. welcomes America's expansionary fiscal policy as part of the reason why its economy is recovering so rapidly, while the big payments deficit this recovery creates is giving a major lift to Europe and Japan by increasing the demand in the United States for their exports.
But the United States economy is also performing better than Western European economies because it is more flexible, Dr. Andersen said, citing more functional markets, more flexible wage patterns and better profitability. As a result, employment is rising rapidly in the United States, generating stronger consumer demand.
European Rigidity
By contrast, the O.E.C.D. says, European economies continue to be hampered by rigidity. It also notes that most governments are still following restrictive fiscal policies aimed at reducing budget deficits and fighting off inflation.
Over the last year or so, the O.E.C.D. Secretariat has frequently hinted that West Germany, Britain and other European countries where inflation has been reduced could join the United States in giving a boost to world recovery by carefully stimulating their economies.
The latest Economic Outlook contains no trace of such thinking, however. Now the O.E.C.D. Secretariat emphasizes the need for European governments to make their economies more flexible and market-oriented as the necessary precondition for faster economic growth.

ISSUE AND DEBATE; MAJOR QUESTION IN ELECTIONS: BLAME FOR STATE OF ECONOMY

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]29 Oct 1982: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424441131?accountid=11752

Abstract: Was the situation that critical? Certainly the American economy had performed worse in the 1970's than in the 1960's or the 1950's, if judged by inflation, unemployment or productivity growth. An analysis by John L. Palmer and Isabel V. Sawhill of the Urban Institute shows that inflation, measured as the percentage increase percent in the 1960's and 112 percent in the 1970's. The rate of economic growth in the 1970's, though about one-third slower than in the 1960's, was almost as rapid as in the 1950's. ''Real'' gross national product, total output adjusted for inflation, rose by 36 percent in the 1970's, as against 49 percent in the 1960's and 38 percent in the 1950's. Part of the reason for the slowdown may have been the economy's absorption of a greater proportion of the population into the labor force, as a growing number of women and of youngsters born in the postwar baby boom sought jobs. Total employment in the 1970's rose 27 percent as against 20 percent in the 1960's and 11 percent in the 1950's. By the same token, unemployment ran higher in the 1970's, averaging 6.2 percent, as against 4.8 percent in the 1960's and 4.5 percent in the 1950's.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=ISSUE+AND+DEBATE%3B+MAJOR+QUESTION+IN+ELECTIONS%3A+BLAME+FOR+STATE+OF+ECONOMY&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-10-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Who is to blame for the country's economic problems? That is an important national issue in Tuesday's elections. By Leonard Silk
President Reagan and his supporters contend that the Republican Administratin inherited the problems and has taken bold and decisive actions to solve them.
The Democrats maintain that the policies adopted by the Administration and supported by the Republicans in Congress are at fault for the depth of the recession. If those policies are continued, the Democrats argue, the nation will face economic disaster.
What follows is an attempt to sort out the various assertions and counterassertions and to put the matter in perspective.
The Background
Mr. Reagan certainly inherited from earlier administrations a serious problem: how to achieve steady economic growth, price stability and low unemployment simultaneously.
Unemployment and inflation seemed to alternate, in the early postwar period, like opposite ends of a see-saw. But after the mid-1960's, the average levels of both inflation and unemployment moved upward together, from one business cycle to the next, in a new economic disease that came to be called "stagflation," a combination of economic stagnation and inflation.
Successive Administrations pursued policies aimed either at fighting stagnation and inflation.
Characteristically, Democratic administrations, with heavier support from labor, put greater stress on reducing unemployment, while Republican administrations, with a larger constituency among businessmen and the well-to-do, focused more on stopping inflation. Thus, the Eisenhower Administration underwent three recessions from 1953 through 1960. Richard M. Nixon, who lost to John F. Kennedy in 1960, felt that it was the third Eisenhower recession that cost him the White House on his first try. The average unemployment rate in 1960 was 5.5 percent. The rate of inflation that year was 1.5 percent.
President Kennedy had pledged ''to get the country moving again.'' When Congress resisted increasing public spending to stimulate the economy, he turned to tax reduction. He was assassinated before his in 1964, and the economy surged.
By the end of 1965 the unemployment rate had fallen to 4 percent, then regarded as equivalent to full employment. The Vietnam War buildup was beginning, and inflation loomed, since a rapid growth in Government and private spending would not call forth enough additional production to meet the demand. Johnson Resisted Tax Increase
Mr. Johnson resisted a tax increase for more than two years, partly to avoid a major debate on the costs of the Vietnam War, partly to insure against Congressional rejection of his Great Society program.
His refusal to choose between the guns of Vietnam and the butter of the Great Society set the economy on an inflationary course that still persists. But in the final year of the Johnson Administration, 1968, inflation was still moderate by present standards: 4.7 percent. And the unemployment rate was only 3.6 percent. President Nixon started out by trying to rein in the economy gradually, depending chiefly on slow monetary growth, but the rise in unemployment to 6.2 percent, together with the rise of inflation to 6.1 percent in 1969, alarmed him, as did Republican losses in the midterm elections of 1970.
He was determined to prevent the economy from costing him the White House in 1972, as he thought it had in 1960. On Aug. 15, 1971, he announced his new economic program, which included a freeze on wages and prices, devaluation of the dollar, a cut in taxes to stimulate business and, with the help of a cooperative Federal Reserve Board under the chairmanship of Arthur F. Burns, rapid monetary growth.
The economy boomed, prices were stable under the controls and Mr. Nixon was re-elected. But after the election, when wage and price controls were lifted, rapid inflation broke out.
At the same time, a world boom added to inflationary pressures on commodity prices. In October 1973 the Arab members of the on the United States. Oil prices soared, and the Consumer Price Index climbed to double-digit levels. Preoccupied by Watergate, President Nixon never made a strong response to the energy threat. In 1974, unemployment averaged 5.6 percent, and inflation reached 12.2 percent.
Gerald R. Ford, who became President after Mr. Nixon's resignation in August 1974, tried to bring inflation under control by fiscal and monetary tightening, and in 1975, under the impact of the deepest recession in 35 years, unemployment averaged 8.5 percent. Inflation fell to 7 percent.
In 1976, an election year, Mr. Ford was hesitant to try to pump up the economy. For the year, unemployment averaged 7.7 percent, but inflation came down to 4.8 percent. Jobless Rate Brought Down
Jimmy Carter, on taking office in January 1977, made a reduction in monetary policy, he brought the jobless rate down to 5.8 percent in 1979.
But one consequence of that course was a rise in the inflation rate. Consumer prices climbed by 6.8 percent in 1977, by 9 percent in 1978 and then, in 1979, under the additional impact of the second oil shock, by 13.3 percent, higher even than the rate in 1974.
To fight inflation and defend the dollar, then under heavy pressure in international markets, Mr. Carter, in July 1979, named Paul A. Volcker, the president of the New York Federal Reserve Bank, who was regarded as a strong anti-inflationist, to the chairmanship of the Federal Reserve Board. Mr. Volcker, in the fall of 1979, proclaimed that he would pursue a doctrine of ''operational monetarism,'' focusing on controlling the money supply rather than interest rates.
Strict monetary control aimed at bringing down inflation put the economy into recession. In 1980, an election year, the jobless rate summer, the decline in unemployment did not come soon enough to help Mr. Carter's electoral prospects. The unemployment rate was still at 7.4 percent when Ronald Reagan assumed the Presidency.
But the inflation rate, despite tight money and interest rates that rose to then-record levels, came down very slowly to 12.4 percent in 1980 from 13.3 percent in 1979. Fighting 'Economic Dunkirk'
Mr. Reagan came to office calling for a radically different policy to rescue the economy from what his aides called ''an economic Dunkirk.''
Was the situation that critical? Certainly the American economy had performed worse in the 1970's than in the 1960's or the 1950's, if judged by inflation, unemployment or productivity growth. An analysis by John L. Palmer and Isabel V. Sawhill of the Urban Institute shows that inflation, measured as the percentage increase percent in the 1960's and 112 percent in the 1970's.
The rate of economic growth in the 1970's, though about one-third slower than in the 1960's, was almost as rapid as in the 1950's. ''Real'' gross national product, total output adjusted for inflation, rose by 36 percent in the 1970's, as against 49 percent in the 1960's and 38 percent in the 1950's.
Productivity, measured as output per hour in the private business sector, slowed markedly in the 1970's; it rose by just 15 percent in the decade as against 33 percent in the 1960's and 32 percent in the 1950's.
Part of the reason for the slowdown may have been the economy's absorption of a greater proportion of the population into the labor force, as a growing number of women and of youngsters born in the postwar baby boom sought jobs. Total employment in the 1970's rose 27 percent as against 20 percent in the 1960's and 11 percent in the 1950's. By the same token, unemployment ran higher in the 1970's, averaging 6.2 percent, as against 4.8 percent in the 1960's and 4.5 percent in the 1950's.
But with more people employed and bringing home income, the American standard of living, defined as the percentage increase in per capita income, after adjustment for inflation and taxes, rose by 23 percent in the 1970's, slower than the 35 percent gain in the 1960's but faster than the 15 percent gain in the 1950's. The Reagan Record
President Reagan's chief claim for the success of his program is that it has stopped inflation. Indeed, the Consumer Price Index rose by just two-tenths of 1 percent in September, making the annual rate of increase in the first nine months of 1982 4.8 percent, the same as in 1976, the final year of the Ford Administration.
But the unemployment rate has climbed to 10.1 percent, the highest To what extent is the Reagan economic program responsible for the decline in inflation and for the recession and the rise in unemployment?
The rate of inflation had been coming down before Mr. Reagan took office, as a result of the worldwide oil glut, falling commodity prices and the recession of 1979-80. By the second quarter of 1981, the rate of price increase was down to 9.8 percent.
But the steep Reagan recession certainly shoved the inflation rate down a lot faster. The Reagan Administration did not set out deliberately to cause a recession to check inflation. On the contrary, its original economic program was intended to attack both stagnation and inflation by a combination of massive ''supply side'' tax cuts, to spur economic growth, and support for a restrictive Federal Reserve policy of slowing the growth of the money supply, to check inflation.
But this two-pronged attack on unemployment and inflation did not Sharply rising interest rates in the first half of 1981 thrust the economy into recession so hard on the heels of the Carter recession as to seem almost like a relapse.
The slump has pushed down not only inflation but also, to a lesser extent, interest rates, raising hopes of an economic recovery in 1983. Thus far, however, signs of the recovery are scarce.
The outlook for business capital spending remains ''dismal,'' a description used by Norman Robertson, chief economist of the Mellon Bank of Pittsburgh. Profits in steel, oil and many basic industries are down. The manufacturing industry is operating at less than 70 percent of capacity.
Businesses are still struggling to bring down their inventories, but thus far declining sales have prevented a cleanup of surplus stocks. Worries Over Business The most worrisome aspects of the present slump are the high rate of business failures and the strained balance sheets, with high debt ratios, of many corporations.
Many banks are carrying heavy amounts of ''problem'' loans, and the world financial system is burdened by an enormous volume of national debt, much of it owed to private banks.
The stock and bond markets have surged forward in response to lower interest rates. However, uncertainties remain over whether the Federal Reserve means to push interest rates still lower by accommodating growing Government and private debt, or whether, as Mr. Volcker has insisted, it means to go on fighting inflation by holding to its fundamental course of restricting monetary growth.
Anxieties in the markets still focus on the Federal budget deficits. Returns for the fiscal year 1982, ended Oct. 1, show a record deficit of $110.7 billion. The prospective deficit for 1983 is estimated at $155 billion by the Congressional Budget Office. And the estimates that the 1984 deficit could exceeed $200 billion.
Financing such Government deficits in the face of rising credit demands of business and consumers, once recovery begins, could prevent strong and steady economic growth in the years ahead.
The Administration's newly appointed chief economic adviser, Martin Feldstein of Harvard, says he finds it ''not unreasonable'' that the economy will grow by 2.9 to 3.9 percent in 1983, the private consensus of economists. But that rate of growth would still imply an unemployment rate of about 9 percent next year. The Reagan Solutions
Mr. Reagan has been campaigning for Republican candidates by urging voters to ''stay the course.'' It is not entirely clear which course he means, since the President, over the opposition of his supply-side supporters, backed Congressional legislation to increase taxes by $99 billion over the next three years, to narrow the budget deficit. Basically, however, Mr. Reagan has held to his original course. He strongly opposes rescinding the third installment of the 1981 tax cut, which would reduce personal income taxes by 10 percent next July 1. He is holding out for a change in the tax law, already enacted, that would reduce personal income tax rates, beginning in 1984, to offset the effect of inflation.
Mr. Reagan is also resisting efforts to curb the military buildup, scheduled to add 7 percent or more annually to the military budget, after inflation. And he still wishes to scale back social expenditures, provoking Democratic charges of ''unfairness'' in his program.
A report by Mr. Reagan's advisory committee on Social Security, headed by Alan Greenspan, is due by the end of the year, but, whatever its recommendations, the prospect of significant cuts in the politically sensitive areas of Social Security and Medicare is dubious. without either rescinding the next income tax cut or raising other taxes, which Mr. Reagan said would take ''a palace coup,'' the likelihood remains for big budget deficits for at least the next few years. And these will create continuing problems for the Federal Reserve and the financial system. Democratic Solutions
The Democrats contend that if the Administration stays on this fiscal-monetary course, it could drive the economy into another depression.
Seeking to respond to the Republican charge that they have no alternative to the Reagan course, some Democrats have sought to put together a new program aimed at regenerating national economic growth by an approach different from the Administration's supply-side tax cutting.
A Democratic Congressional group headed by Representative Timothy development, increasing training programs for workers, rebuilding public works such as roads and bridges and investing more in energy resources. The Wirth group would also establish a council of public and private officials to build ''broad support for national initiatives.''
Other Democrats are widely dispersed along a political spectrum ranging from old liberals, laborites and New Dealers to Southern conservatives, the ''boll weevils'' who on economic policy have been closer to Mr. Reagan than to mainstream Democrats.
Despite their differences, however, most of the Democrats favor social programs of benefit to their constituencies among organized labor, the ''working poor'' and the aged or ailing poor, welfare recipients, blacks and other minorities. The party has offered no answer to the question of how to finance Social Security.
By and large, the Democrats support growth in military budgets but would trim the rate of increase advocated by Mr. Reagan. rigidly monetarist and as a prime cause, along with mounting deficits, of high interest rates, which have hurt the housing, auto and many other industries and cost millions of workers their jobs. Analysis
The chronic problem of how to arrest inflation and reduce unemployment at the same time has yet to be solved by the Reagan Administration. Inflation moved the electorate toward the Republicans in 1980; unemployment in 1982 appears to be exerting a polar tug toward the Democrats.
The greater interdependence of the world economy has made it more difficult than ever for a single nation, even the United States, to act alone to attack one problem to the exclusion of the other. In using tight money and high interest rates to stop inflation, the Administration caused unemployment to rise not only at home but also abroad, as other nations raised interest rates to protect their currencies. High rates in the United States also made the dollar so dear as to hurt American exports, endangering major industries, wiping out jobs and increasing pressures for protectionism. High unemployment worldwide endangers the entire economic and monetary system.
Likewise, for a single nation to try to attack unemployment simply by stimulating internal demand can also be perilous, as the Carter Administration found in 1979 and the Mitterrand Government in France and the Trudeau Government in Canada have since learned. Solving unemployment and inflation together will require greater international policy coordination.
It will also require each nation to do more than periodically expand demand, then contract it, tighten money, then loosen it, or set the money supply on automatic pilot, no matter what happens to interest rates and the economy. Specific policy tools are needed to reduce structural unemployment, restrain inflationary pressures from labor, cartels or other interest groups and encourage productivity growth.
The political process in the United States has not produced a diverse and balanced program for dealing with the complex economic problems facing all modern industrial societies.
Until it does, the political pendulum between fighting unemployment and fighting inflation seems likely to go on swinging awhile longer.

O.P. EXPECTS DATA ON U.S. INFLATION, DUE TODAY, TO SUPPORT REAGAN CLAIM

Author: ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr., Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]26 Oct 1982: A.26.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424446144?accountid=11752

Abstract: Economists have defined inflation in various ways and have identified different types such as ''cost-push'' and ''demand-pull.'' It is probably best defined, however, as a sustained rise in the general price level. This means that while the relative prices of goods may frequently change - energy, for example, may go up while longdistance phone calls go down - the average price of all goods must rise to create inflation. Moreover, many of those who did acknowledge progress tended to complain that the gains had been made at unacceptably high cost because of soaring unemployment. Mr. [Reagan] has disputed this, declaring it to be the ''most cynical form of demagoguery.'' Inflation, not inflation-fighting, causes unemployment, Mr. Reagan says. There's little doubt that the high unemployment rate makes people uncomfortable about their general economic situation. ''When you have high unemployment,'' Ann K. Lower of the Consumer Federation of America said, ''the fact that something comes down a couple of pennies doesn't matter much.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=O.P.+EXPECTS+DATA+ON+U.S.+INFLATION%2C+DUE+TODAY%2C+TO+SUPPORT+REAGAN+CLAIM&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-10-26&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.26&amp;au=ROBERT+D.+HERSHEY+Jr.%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: When the Reagan Administration took office 21 months ago, one domestic problem loomed above all others: Inflation had reached alarming, virtually unprecedented peacetime levels.
Warnings were sounded about the social breakdown that has accompanied extremely high inflation in other countries while tens of millions of ordinary Americans watched supermarket and other prices that seemed to rise with each successive purchase.
The situation is vastly different today. Double-digit unemployment is now identified as the top problem and inflation, which dropped from an annual rate of 12.4 percent two years ago to about 6 percent this year, ranks well down the list of public concerns.
President Reagan has been citing this inflation decline as evidence that his economic program is starting to work. And when the September consumer price figures are announced on Tuesday, for the last time before the Congressional election, they will immediately become part of the last-minute political rhetoric.
In the time since the days two years ago when alarmists were predicting runaway inflation on the South American model, the United States seems to have been more successful in cutting inflation than people have realized. Polls show that relatively few people believe there has been a substantial abatement of price increases, much to the chagrin of an Administration, which has been pointing to gains in the struggle with prices. Credit 'Has Been Lost'
Much of the credit ''has been lost,'' laments Robert F. Steeves, deputy director of the White House Office of Consumer Affairs. There are several possible explanations for the apparent discrepancy between the facts and the perceptions of consumers. Perhaps the most popular measuring rod itself, the Consumer Price Index, is not accurately reflecting market conditions. The figures for September are to be reported Tuesday.
Another possible cause is that prices of some major items, such as used cars, school tuition, utilities and medical care, have continued to rise sharply. At the same time, the prices of some items such as clothing have risen scarcely at all, but few realize it because these purchases are often put off in a recession.
Psychological factors, however, are probably the most important. High unemployment and smaller raises make consumers uneasy, specialists note, and therefore they feel they cannot afford things even though their actual buying power may have risen.
Some, because of previous inflation, have switched their buying patterns from, say, steak to hamburger and have come to regard this as a more or less permanent decline in their standard of living caused by inflation. It is also natural for consumers who remember paying 35 cents a gallon for gasoline 10 years ago to disregard a decline from $1.40 to $1.30. Long Memories a Factor
According to Patrick C. Jackman, the branch chief for the Consumer Price Index at the Labor Department, this long consumer memory is significant because ''the same factors that pushed up prices most have been the factors showing the most moderation.''
Another explanation of why surveys show consumers still skeptical about progress is that ''most people probably give fairly flip answers'' to poll takers, Mr. Jackman says.
Economists have defined inflation in various ways and have identified different types such as ''cost-push'' and ''demand-pull.'' It is probably best defined, however, as a sustained rise in the general price level. This means that while the relative prices of goods may frequently change - energy, for example, may go up while longdistance phone calls go down - the average price of all goods must rise to create inflation.
Thus, economists say, one must be careful not to confuse particular big price increases with inflation itself. The Consumer Price Index is the most popular of several yardsticks for measuring inflation in the United States. It measures changes in the weighted average of a fixed sample of consumer goods.
Like most indexes, it has substantial flaws. One is that the market ''basket'' it tracks is only rarely revised, with the result that the index includes the prices of things that people may buy much less than they did in 1972-73, the data used when the latest revision was made in 1977. Quirk in Housing Index
The biggest current complaint is that the index overstates the cost of home ownership by heavy weighting of mortgage interest rates. Mortgage rates, however, are not a good proxy for housing costs because of the infrequency with which houses are purchased.
The Labor Department in January will amend the index by substituting a rental equivalence measure that will reduce the home ownership weighting by about one-third.
Because of the interest-rate distortions, according to Jerry J. Jasinowski, chief economist for the National Association of Manufacturers, the index overstated the actual inflation rate from the end of 1979 to the end of 1981 by more than 3 percentage points.
What the index records is of great importance because of its role not only in recording higher prices but also in transmitting them throughout the economy. Some 44 Federal benefit programs are directly linked to it, including Social Security, with its 36 million beneficiaries. Relative Factors in Index
There are seven major categories in the index with the following relative importance: housing, 46 percent; transportation, 19 percent; food, 17 percent; medical care and apparel, 5 percent each; entertainment and other goods and services, 4 percent each.
Individual items have shown sharp and not unusual variations in the last year. Professional medical services, for example, rose 15.3 percent while school books and supplies rose 14.6 percent and used cars rose 14.1 percent. Fats and oils, a food component, showed the biggest decline, 4 percent, followed by declines of 3.2 percent for gasoline, 2.2 percent for fuel oil, coal and bottled gas and 1.5 percent for ''other apparel.''
It is a matter of debate how much credit the Reagan Administration can claim for these declines, but experts point out that the strongest factors affecting the prices of food and energy have been bountiful harvests and a worldwide recession.
With the vast number of goods and services in the American market and the frequent price changes of so many of them, it is not surprising that even the most diligent consumer often loses his bearings.
Then there are items sold at discounts from the regular price, there are prices that do not make much impression because they are paid by health or other insurers, and there are things like college tuition that go up only occasionally. A Labor Department official says the tuition component will climb ''substantially'' in the report Tuesday. Difference Hard to Grasp
Mr. Jackman says it is also hard for people to grasp the distinction between lower inflation rates and lower prices. ''Prices are still going up but at a much slower rate,'' he observes.
The Administration thus seems to have made relatively little headway in persuading voters that inflation has been substantially reduced. A New York Times/CBS News Poll last month showed that twothirds of those questioned thought prices were rising as fast as or faster than a year earlier.
Moreover, many of those who did acknowledge progress tended to complain that the gains had been made at unacceptably high cost because of soaring unemployment. Mr. Reagan has disputed this, declaring it to be the ''most cynical form of demagoguery.'' Inflation, not inflation-fighting, causes unemployment, Mr. Reagan says.
But there's little doubt that the high unemployment rate makes people uncomfortable about their general economic situation. ''When you have high unemployment,'' Ann K. Lower of the Consumer Federation of America said, ''the fact that something comes down a couple of pennies doesn't matter much.''

REAGAN AND O'NEILL EXCHANGE CHARGES OVER THE ECONOMY

Author: STEVEN R. WEISMAN, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]24 Oct 1982: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424449021?accountid=11752

Abstract: While Mr. O'[Neill Jr.] said the current recession was ''not only stalled'' but ''starting to slide backwards,'' Mr. [Reagan Ranches] said, ''We aren't out of the woods yet, but we're getting there.'' As he has many times in recent weeks, the President cited the recent drop in inflation and interest rates, as well as the ''surge of investor confidence we've witnessed'' in the stock and bond markets. He also said that ''real wages,'' retail sales, housing starts and car sales were all ''heading up again.'' The Speaker said Mr. Reagan spoke ''easily'' of unemployment without realizing ''the horror that lies behind those figures.'' He said that, as a Presidential candidate in 1980, Mr. Reagan promised to protect Social Security benefits but then acted as if he had ''never made such a promise.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=REAGAN+AND+O%27NEILL+EXCHANGE+CHARGES+OVER+THE+ECONOMY&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-10-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=STEVEN+R.+WEISMAN%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: President Reagan and the Speaker of the House, Thomas P. O'Neill Jr., exchanged accusations on the economy today as the off-year election campaign went into its final 10 days.
Sounding their principal themes in a campaign that is viewed by both sides as a referendum on the Reagan Administration's economic policies, Mr. Reagan charged the Democrats with proffering ''fairy tales'' and Mr. O'Neill said the Administration had deliberately thrown people out of work.
Mr. Reagan used his weekly five-minute radio broadcast to denounce what he said were ''six big myths'' put forward by the Democrats. Specifically, he denied that military spending increases and the three-year tax cut had contributed to the Federal deficit and that his Administration had cut social programs.
Says Most Can Find Jobs
He also said that most of those unemployed were able to find jobs within two months. ''Bad as current unemployment is, in most individual cases it's a temporary problem,'' Mr. Reagan said.
Mr. O'Neill, in a radio broadcast taped in Boston, charged that Mr. Reagan's ''bold and confident promises of 1981 have become the tragedy and the excuses of 1982.'' He said the Administration had thrown ''millions of people out of work as part of a deliberate plan to slow down inflation.''
Just as Mr. Reagan appealed for patience, Mr. O'Neill sounded the principal theme of the Democrats this fall: that the Administration's policies were unfair. Equality of Sacrifice Urged
''I have found that the American people are willing to make tremendous sacrifices for their country, on one condition,'' Mr. O'Neill said. ''They want the sacrifices to be shared equally by everyone.'' He said the Administration's budget cuts, tax cuts and anti-inflation policies had benefited the wealthy and hurt the poor.
While Mr. O'Neill said the current recession was ''not only stalled'' but ''starting to slide backwards,'' Mr. Reagan said, ''We aren't out of the woods yet, but we're getting there.''
As he has many times in recent weeks, the President cited the recent drop in inflation and interest rates, as well as the ''surge of investor confidence we've witnessed'' in the stock and bond markets. He also said that ''real wages,'' retail sales, housing starts and car sales were all ''heading up again.''
But the bulk of Mr. Reagan's speech was defensive in nature. In many respects it went so far as to seem in contradiction to what many of his aides acknowledge publicly and privately.
For example, Mr. Reagan said that ''myth No. 1'' was that ''increases in defense spending in recent years are one of the main causes of projected Federal deficits.''
''The fact is that the defense share of the Federal budget and the gross national product has been shrinking sharply over the last 20 years,'' he said. Agency Figures 11% Growth
The Office of Management and Budget has said that the Administration's plan to spend $1,500 billion over five years on the military constitutes a growth rate, after inflation is taken into account, of 11 percent from the base that existed in 1980.
Mr. Reagan also termed as ''a whopper'' the assertion that the tax cut was a cause of big deficits. ''That is just not true,'' he said, adding that it held tax rates even ''to keep the hard-pressed American taxpayer from being bled even drier through further hikes and the bracket creep caused by inflation.''
The tax cut enacted last year was calculated as diminishing Federal tax revenues by $750 billion over five years. Another myth, Mr. Reagan said, was ''the charge that this Administration has slashed Federal social spending and caused a lot of human hardship.'' He added, ''Well, that sounds bad. But is it true? The answer is no.'' He said the social spending budget had gone up. Cuts Were Offset
The Administration in the last two years has eliminated or cut scores of programs and tightened eligibility in such areas as public service jobs, school aid, student loans, food stamps, welfare, Medicaid and subsidized housing. The overall social program budget has gone up because these cuts were offset by Social Security, Medicare, veterans' benefits and other programs that were not cut as much.
Mr. Reagan said the other myths were the suggestion that the unemployed faced a permanant lack of jobs, the idea that ''America's best days are behind us,'' and what he called ''the biggest and cruelest myth of all, the doom-peddling argument that there is no end in sight for this bitter recession.''
While the President delivered his address live from Camp David, Md., shortly after noon today, Mr. O'Neill taped his Friday night for broadcast after 1 P.M. For the Speaker, whose aides acknowledge that he lacks Mr. Reagan's broadcasting abilities, the speech was a first. In the past, other members of Congress have responded to Mr. Reagan's weekly radio talks.
Mr. O'Neill said the Administration's program was ''not working because the program is not fair - and, just as important, because the people themselves know it is not fair.''
''It is not fair to throw millions of people out of work as part of a deliberate plan to slow down inflation,'' Mr. O'Neill said. ''It is not fair to give huge tax breaks to the wealthy while denying the basics of life to those who need them. It is not fair to spend trillions on the Pentagon and, in the same breath, threaten to cut people's Social Security checks.''
Taking his cue from the principal Republican campaign theme, Mr. O'Neill said, ''Stay the course? That is unfair. America needs a change.'' He called on voters to send ''the clearest possible message on Nov. 2'' to that effect.
The Speaker said Mr. Reagan spoke ''easily'' of unemployment without realizing ''the horror that lies behind those figures.'' He said that, as a Presidential candidate in 1980, Mr. Reagan promised to protect Social Security benefits but then acted as if he had ''never made such a promise.''
Mr. O'Neill said, ''We must reform the Social Security system fairly and in a way that honors our commitment to America's elderly.''
Meanwhile, a spokesman for the Speaker disputed Mr. Reagan's statement that most of those unemployed were only temporarily without jobs. Christopher Matthews, the Speaker's administrative assistant, said the Bureau of Labor Statistics reported earlier this month that the average duration of unemployment was 16.6 weeks, a rise from 13 weeks a year ago. ---- Tent Cities Erected By The Associated Press
Members of church, labor and community action groups began setting up tent cities yesterday in Lansing, Mich., St. Louis, Columbus, Ohio, and New Orleans in a Depression-style protest of President Reagan's economic policies. Organizers said that at least 30 more ''Reagan Ranches,'' as they called them, were to be set up in other cities by Election Day.
Much of the activity was organized by the Association of Community Organizations for Reform Now, which says it has 60,000 low-@ and moderate-income members in 26 states.

Subject: ELECTIONS; UNITED STATES POLITICS AND GOVERNMENT; ELECTION ISSUES; GOVERNMENT SPENDING; BUDGETS AND BUDGETING; UNITED STATES ECONOMY; DEMONSTRATIONS AND RIOTS

Location: UNITED STATES LANSING (MICH) ST LOUIS (MO) COLUMBUS (OHIO) NEW ORLEANS (LA)

People: Jr, Neill Ranches, Reagan REAGAN, RONALD WILSON (PRES)

IN THE SHADOW OF THE DOLLAR

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]25 Aug 1982: A.22.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424392980?accountid=11752

Abstract: Until the late 1970's it was tough for any trucker to obtain new route authority in either country. Then, just as the recession hit hard, deregulation in the United States opened the door to Canadian as well as U.S. companies. U.S. competitors protested that they had a tougher time in Canada than Canadians did in the United States. With thousands of teamsters out of work, the Interstate Commerce Commission took the complaints seriously. Such views are simplistic. Canadian stagflation predated the United States interest rate explosion. U.S.-owned businesses are no more exploitative than native concerns operating under Canadian law. But the Trudeau Government has shamelessly seized on resentment of U.S. influence. It is forcing energy companies to the south to sell their assets to Canadians at bargain rates. Its Foreign Investment Review Agency unfairly hobbles U.S. citizens who want to make direct investments.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=IN+THE+SHADOW+OF+THE+DOLLAR%3A+%5BEDITORIAL%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-08-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.22&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Squeezing Trucks in Canada
Congress is annoyed. United States truckers, it says, don't get a fair shake in Canada. So it has declared a moratorium on new licenses for Canadian truckers until Canada changes its discriminatory ways. That, of course, is not the end of the matter. Manitoba has already retaliated with a moratorium on route licenses for U.S. truckers. Other provinces, which under Canadian law control truck routes, will surely follow suit.
It is hard, at this point, to say who is right. But it's clear that both countries lose from interference in open trade and investment. Self-interest should be motive enough for Canadians to curb growing protectionism. And this country, for its part, should recognize Canadians' frustrations in living in the shadow of an economic giant. Disputes like this are more likely settled by compromise than by chauvinist posturing.
Canadian and U.S. truckers have long shared rights to transport goods across the border. The controversy over new licenses follows from deregulation on the U.S. side, and recession on both.
Until the late 1970's it was tough for any trucker to obtain new route authority in either country. Then, just as the recession hit hard, deregulation in the United States opened the door to Canadian as well as U.S. companies. U.S. competitors protested that they had a tougher time in Canada than Canadians did in the United States. With thousands of teamsters out of work, the Interstate Commerce Commission took the complaints seriously.
The commission suspended route applications by Canadians pending an investigation of the charges. Now Congress has formalized the deal: no more licenses for two years. The suspension may be lifted only if the President affirms that Canada has had a change of heart.
If this were an isolated incident it would be possible to ignore. The cost to consumers from a loss of international competition is probably modest. But the dispute reflects a general souring of economic relations that could mean substantial losses for both countries. The Canadian economy is in trouble, with unemployment and inflation at record highs. Many Canadians blame Washington. If only United States interest rates were lower and its business less exploitative, they say, life would be rosy.
Such views are simplistic. Canadian stagflation predated the United States interest rate explosion. U.S.-owned businesses are no more exploitative than native concerns operating under Canadian law. But the Trudeau Government has shamelessly seized on resentment of U.S. influence. It is forcing energy companies to the south to sell their assets to Canadians at bargain rates. Its Foreign Investment Review Agency unfairly hobbles U.S. citizens who want to make direct investments.
The urge to bite back is tempting - and should be resisted. First, any move to limit Canadian investment or shut out Canadian business hurts us as much as it hurts them. More important, it is unlikely to prompt Canadians to mend their ways. Protectionism only breeds more protectionism.
The better approach is quiet diplomacy, with a goal, in the case of trucking, of guaranteeing U.S. companies an equal shot at new route authority. More generally, the goal should be a softening of Canada's harsh restrictions on direct United States investment.
There is little chance the Trudeau Government will repeal anti-United States laws, but it might be persuaded to interpret them more liberally. Far from the television cameras, diplomacy may yet show Canadians where their real interests lie.

Subject: TRUCKS AND TRUCKING INDUSTRY; LAW AND LEGISLATION; UNITED STATE INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS; FOREIGN INVESTMENTS; Licenses; Deregulation
