IN THE NATION; REAGANOMICS, R.I.P.

Author: Wicker, Tom

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]23 July 1982: A.23.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424376864?accountid=11752

Abstract: Whatever the 1.7 percent second-quarter growth rate means, therefore, the [Paul Volcker] testimony assures that any recovery during the rest of the year, and perhaps next, will be weak and inadequate. As the economist Sidney Weintraub commented recently in the New Leader, ''So long as we rely on monetary policy for an inflation antidote we will persistently suffer either slow growth or recession.'' And monetary restraint is Mr. [Reagan]'s only antidote for inflation. That is the only part of his economic program that has been even moderately successful; Mr. Volcker went so far as to say that ''the inflationary tide has turned in a fundamental way.'' But he added the Catch-22 that to increase the money supply to aid recovery would only rekindle inflation - which might be true, if no other action were taken, but only makes Sidney Weintraub's point more dramatically. Besides, revised statistics suggest that inflation has not declined as much as was believed earlier in the year. I wrote in this space upon passage of his tax bill that such rhetoric confronted the President with a new problem - ''whether the recovery program can produce recovery, or at least what can plausibly be described as recovery.'' A year later, after fair testing, it's self-evident that it hasn't and won't.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=IN+THE+NATION%3B+REAGANOMICS%2C+R.I.P.%3A+%5BOP-ED%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-07-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.23&amp;au=Wicker%2C+Tom&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: he first anniversary of Congressional passage of President Reagan's three-stage tax reduction bill is just a week away, so this seems a good time to pronounce it a solid, ringing failure.
Economists within and without the Reagan Administration may differ as to whether the 1.7 percent second-quarter increase in gross national product means that the recession continues for a little while or quite a while. Supply-siders may debate whether the Reagan tax cut was big enough or fast enough to meet doctrinal criteria.
But the heart of the matter is that the predictions that stampeded Congress last year have not come true. Remember?
* A major, guaranteed reduction in marginal tax rates over a threeyear period would cause people - particularly the rich, who benefited most -to save and invest more, bringing on a boom in production and productivity.
* Monetary restraint, promised by the President and enforced by the Federal Reserve Board, would bring down inflation without the pain of a recession. In both cases, went the refrain, if people only knew what to expect, and had confidence that their expectations would be met, the problems of stagflation could be solved. And virtually no one save the slothful poor and the parasitic bureaucrats would feel any pain.
Scarcely one year later, unemployment is running at more than 9 percent - and nearly twice that for blacks. Newly released figures from the Labor Department show that one of every five American workers was unemployed at some time during 1981. Nearly three million were out of work all of that year, not including those so discouraged that they quit hunting for jobs.
Although G.N.P. did show a slight upturn for the second quarter of 1982, most of the gain was suspect - an increase in motor vehicle inventories, not sales - and did not necessarily suggest real economic growth. The Commerce Department's revised figures, moreover, showed that the declines in G.N.P. in the fourth quarter of last year and the first quarter of this year were sharper than originally reported; the recession was worse than thought at the time, making the 1.7 percent increase for the second quarter even less impressive.
Interest rates, meanwhile, have risen to levels that even Paul Volcker, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, called ''extraordinarily high.'' Perhaps the major reason for those rates is the huge federal deficits now impending as a result of the Reagan tax cut and the Reagan defense spend-ing boom, both dumped on a depressed economy and a budget already strained by runaway entitlement programs.
Mr. Volcker, testifying to the Senate Banking Committee, said the Federal Reserve would not increase its target rates for monetary growth this year and that the best it could do for next year would be to keep them the same - not least because the Fed has ''a strong sense that considerably more remains to be done to bring the deficit under control.''
Whatever the 1.7 percent second-quarter growth rate means, therefore, the Volcker testimony assures that any recovery during the rest of the year, and perhaps next, will be weak and inadequate. As the economist Sidney Weintraub commented recently in the New Leader, ''So long as we rely on monetary policy for an inflation antidote we will persistently suffer either slow growth or recession.'' And monetary restraint is Mr. Reagan's only antidote for inflation.
That is the only part of his economic program that has been even moderately successful; Mr. Volcker went so far as to say that ''the inflationary tide has turned in a fundamental way.'' But he added the Catch-22 that to increase the money supply to aid recovery would only rekindle inflation - which might be true, if no other action were taken, but only makes Sidney Weintraub's point more dramatically. Besides, revised statistics suggest that inflation has not declined as much as was believed earlier in the year.
Even one of the basic rationales for the Reagan tax cut has been found to have been overstated, if not mistaken. Supply-siders said a drop in the personal savings rate to a five-year average of only 5.4 percent, and to only 5.2 percent in 1978 and 1979, required a cut in tax rates to encourage more savings and investment. In fact, revised figures now show, the personal savings rate was 6.1 percent in 1978 and 5.9 percent in 1979; the five-year average was 6 percent.
In the early stages of his Administration, Mr. Reagan claimed unequivocally that his economic program ''would achieve all the essential aims of recovery,'' that it would produce 4.5 percent annual growth after 1982 and a balanced budget in 1984.
But I wrote in this space upon passage of his tax bill that such rhetoric confronted the President with a new problem - ''whether the recovery program can produce recovery, or at least what can plausibly be described as recovery.'' A year later, after fair testing, it's self-evident that it hasn't and won't.

BENCHMARK BANK RATE RAISED AGAIN IN CANADA

Author: HENRY GINIGER, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]11 June 1982: D.5.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424365506?accountid=11752

Abstract: In Parliament, opposition Members accused the Liberal Government of causing ''economic ruin'' through its interest-rate policies. ''Canada is in a crisis,'' Nelson Riis, the finance critic of the New Democratic Party told Finance Minister Allan MacEachan in the House of Commons. ''And an extraordinary situation requires that extraordinary measures be taken.'' Mr. MacEachan today continued to blame United States interest rates for much of Canada's ills. References to ''prudent monetary and budgetary policies'' in the final communique from Versailles, he said, were meant to warn the United States ''that if the world is to get any opportunity to get out of this recession, the United States must take action to reduce its interest rates.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=BENCHMARK+BANK+RATE+RAISED+AGAIN+IN+CANADA&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-06-11&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.5&amp;au=HENRY+GINIGER%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Bank of Canada today raised its benchmark discount rate for the second time in two weeks in a thus-far unsuccessful effort to halt the slide in the value of the Canadian dollar.
On the heels of a half-point increase in its bank rate last week, to 15.87 percent, the central bank today raised the rate to 16.20 percent. But the dollar, which quickly fell below 80 cents American after last week's adjustment, has failed to respond. The currency closed today at 79.10 cents in Toronto and 79.17 in New York.
The major private banks responded to last week's increase by raising their prime lending rates by half a point, to 17 1/2 percent, and a further increase appeared probable for Friday. Sharp Criticism in Commons
In Parliament, opposition Members accused the Liberal Government of causing ''economic ruin'' through its interest-rate policies.
''Canada is in a crisis,'' Nelson Riis, the finance critic of the New Democratic Party told Finance Minister Allan MacEachan in the House of Commons. ''And an extraordinary situation requires that extraordinary measures be taken.''
The Government has consistently denied rumors that it intends to devalue the Canadian currency or impose exchange controls, actions that would support the dollar by curbing the flow of capital out of the country.
Canada, along with the other major industrial nations, has placed much of the blame for its deteriorating currency on high United States interest rates. At the summit conference in Versailles, France, over the weekend, the Canadian delegation sought in vain to win a commitment from the Reagan Administration to bring down United States interest rates. Countering High U.S. Rates
High American interest rates encourage foreign investors to sell their own currencies for dollars to take advantage of the high yields of dollar-denominated securities. The rise in the key Canadian bank rate is an attempt to counteract this by making the Canadian dollar more attractive as an investment currency.
Since last weekend's economic summit meeting, whose results Canadian officials called disappointing, Mr. MacEachan has faced sharp attacks in the House of Commons without being able to promise quick relief for the nation's economic problems.
Canada's unemployment rate currently stands at 10.2 percent, its annual rate of inflation has increased to 11.3 percent and a record number of business failures have been recorded in recent months.
High interest rates contribute to a recession by discouraging capital investment and installment buying because the financing is so costly.
Mr. MacEachan today continued to blame United States interest rates for much of Canada's ills. References to ''prudent monetary and budgetary policies'' in the final communique from Versailles, he said, were meant to warn the United States ''that if the world is to get any opportunity to get out of this recession, the United States must take action to reduce its interest rates.''

U.S. SIGNS A PACT IN PACIFIC WITH MARSHALL ISLANDS

Author: ROBERT TRUMBULL, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]01 June 1982: A.6.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424368908?accountid=11752

Abstract: The pact, concluded here on Sunday by President Amata Kabua of the Marshalls and Fred M. Zeder, President Reagan's representative, is subject to approval by a Marshalls plebiscite and by the United States Congress. The accord would establish a new political relationship between the Marshalls and the United States known as a ''free association.'' It would make the islands independent in domestic and foreign affairs. The United States would retain control over security. Pacts Due With 2 Other Groups He has set a deadline of Oct. 1 for approval by Congress, provided the Marshallese assent. If the accord is rejected in the plebiscite or if Congress fails to act by the deadline, the Marshalls Government will consider the islands ''an independent nation,'' he said.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+SIGNS+A+PACT+IN+PACIFIC+WITH+MARSHALL+ISLANDS&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-06-01&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.6&amp;au=ROBERT+TRUMBULL%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Marshall Islands, part of the American trust territory in the Pacific since World War II, have signed a provisional agreement with the United States, granting it military rights for 50 years.
The pact, concluded here on Sunday by President Amata Kabua of the Marshalls and Fred M. Zeder, President Reagan's representative, is subject to approval by a Marshalls plebiscite and by the United States Congress.
The accord, completed after 13 years of intermittent negotiations, also commits the United States to pay the Marshalls up to $1.5 billion over the 50-year period, Mr. Zeder said. The exact figure will depend on adjustments to cover inflation, he said.
The accord would establish a new political relationship between the Marshalls and the United States known as a ''free association.'' It would make the islands independent in domestic and foreign affairs. The United States would retain control over security. Pacts Due With 2 Other Groups
The United States is negotiating similar accords with the Palau Islands and with a third island group known as the Federated States of Micronesia, consisting of Ponape, Truk, Yap, Kosrae and other Caroline islands. A fourth group, the Northern Marianas, voted in 1975 to become a commonwealth of the United States, like Puerto Rico.
The people of the four island groups, numbering 130,000, are mostly Micronesians, with a few Polynesians. Nine major languages are spoken.
The islands were taken from Japan by American forces as a result of World War II. Parts of the Marshall Islands, the Palau Islands and the Northern Marianas were captured by the United States in fierce battles.
Since then, the islands have been administered by the Department of the Interior as a United Nations trusteeship known as the Trust Territory of the Pacific Islands. For some time, they have been selfgoverning in internal affairs.
The Marshalls accord includes payment of $150 million to the hundreds who have suffered ill health from radiation effects as a result of nuclear weapons testing at Bikini and Enewetak between 1946 and 1962. Also included are payments up to $9 million a year to landowners of Kwajalein, which has become a missile testing base.
Anton A. de Brum, the Marshalls' Foreign Minister, said the basic agreement on free association would be submitted to a plebiscite of the 31,000 Marshallese on Aug. 17 with international supervision.
He has set a deadline of Oct. 1 for approval by Congress, provided the Marshallese assent. If the accord is rejected in the plebiscite or if Congress fails to act by the deadline, the Marshalls Government will consider the islands ''an independent nation,'' he said.

A MISERY THAT MAKES IT HARD TO LOVE COMPANY

Author: Lewis, Paul

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]30 May 1982: A.3.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424340724?accountid=11752

Abstract: The five European Common Market countries at Versailles are also sorely divided. Britain last week reluctantly agreed to an $875 million rebate from the Common Market budget; it will contribute $1.6 billion more than it gets back. Trade disputes between Europe and the United States are breaking out again over steel and agriculture. And while all the participants at Versailles formally support Britain in its war with Argentina, they fear their trade with Latin America may suffer in consequence. But the leaders are likely to keep their disagreements under wraps. President [Francois Mitterrand] has prepared a modest, informal meeting confined to ''an exchange of reflections.'' Instead of a formal communique, the participants plan to issue a short ''declaration.'' At London in 1977, the more prosperous countries agreed to take more risks with inflation, expanding their economies in hopes of leading the world to recovery. They officially adopted this ''locomotive'' approach at Bonn in 1978, when Germany and Japan agreed to reflate if the United States would curb energy consumption. But the locomotives were derailed by inflation and Congress was slow to act on decontrolling oil and natural gas prices as a spur to conservation. At Tokyo in 1979, the leaders set energy-saving targets, but these were overtaken by recession after the second oil price shock. Last summer's meeting in Ottawa was noteworthy for its moderating impact on some of Mr. [Reagan]'s policies, especially toward the third world, and for establishing a working relationship between him and France's new Socialist President. The meetings have also been valuable in maintaining commitments to free trade and thus avoiding a protectionist upsurge that could have made a bad situation worse. Reagan-Style Priorities

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=A+MISERY+THAT+MAKES+IT+HARD+TO+LOVE+COMPANY&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-05-30&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.3&amp;au=Lewis%2C+Paul&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: PARIS Leaders of the West's largest economies will meet again this week at Versailles to try to recapture the prosperity that has eluded them since the big oil price increases of the 1970's. Again this year, they are trapped by concerted stagnation of their economies, with prospects for more of the same in 1983. Since their annual meetings began at Rambouillet, France in 1975, unemployment in the industrial democracies is up 40 percent to more than 25 million people and it is still rising.
Quarrels and doubts will abound when French President Francois Mitterrand welcomes President Reagan and the leaders of West Germany, Britain, Canada, Italy and Japan, plus Belgium as spokesman for smaller nations. The West Europeans, fearing prolonged damage from high American interest rates, see themselves as victims of President Reagan's tight budget and lenient tax policies. Japan's moves last week to open its markets to foreign goods and farm products were carefully timed to defuse resentment at Versailles of Tokyo's superior trading performance. But, as the United States special trade negotiator, William E. Brock, has warned, it will be a long time before increased Western sales in Japan can make up for the jobs lost to Japanese imports.
The five European Common Market countries at Versailles are also sorely divided. Britain last week reluctantly agreed to an $875 million rebate from the Common Market budget; it will contribute $1.6 billion more than it gets back. Trade disputes between Europe and the United States are breaking out again over steel and agriculture. And while all the participants at Versailles formally support Britain in its war with Argentina, they fear their trade with Latin America may suffer in consequence. But the leaders are likely to keep their disagreements under wraps. President Mitterrand has prepared a modest, informal meeting confined to ''an exchange of reflections.'' Instead of a formal communique, the participants plan to issue a short ''declaration.''
Their first economic summit in 1975 dealt with threats to the postwar economic rulebook under which they had prospered. Among the threats were the breakdown of the monetary system, with the dollar no longer convertible into gold, and the first big oil price increase. They agreed to curb disorderly currency markets, preparing for the decision the next year empowering the International Monetary Fund to exercise ''strict surveillance'' over Western exchange rates and economic policies. The agreement has been only partially observed, but it helped avert a ''war'' of currency devaluations aimed at increasing exports to pay oil bills.
At London in 1977, the more prosperous countries agreed to take more risks with inflation, expanding their economies in hopes of leading the world to recovery. They officially adopted this ''locomotive'' approach at Bonn in 1978, when Germany and Japan agreed to reflate if the United States would curb energy consumption. But the locomotives were derailed by inflation and Congress was slow to act on decontrolling oil and natural gas prices as a spur to conservation. At Tokyo in 1979, the leaders set energy-saving targets, but these were overtaken by recession after the second oil price shock. Last summer's meeting in Ottawa was noteworthy for its moderating impact on some of Mr. Reagan's policies, especially toward the third world, and for establishing a working relationship between him and France's new Socialist President. The meetings have also been valuable in maintaining commitments to free trade and thus avoiding a protectionist upsurge that could have made a bad situation worse. Reagan-Style Priorities
At Versailles, Western leaders are likely to return to some of the same issues they faced at their first meeting. The Reagan Administration says it cannot do anything about high interest rates, which it predicts will come down when the world realizes the United States has licked inflation. Washington offers to mitigate the deflationary impact of high rates by helping to stabilize the dollar against other currencies, to foster trade and investment.
In return, the Administration wants its economic partners to put real teeth into their 1975 promise to let the International Monetary Fund discipline their economic policies. The idea is to encourage the others to adopt Reagan-style priorities - against inflation and for free trade.
The proposal poses fewer difficulties for Britain, Germany and Japan, which already have similar priorities, than for France, which has been trying to reflate its way out of recession. But President Mitterrand's economic experiment is already in trouble with soaring inflation and a widening trade gap. Tighter I.M.F. surveillance might be welcomed by Mitterrand Government moderates who are calling for a new tack.
The Reagan Administration also hopes the Versailles meeting will mark a turning point in East-West economic relations. It is urging the leaders to recognize that they erred in providing financing for East European purchases of technology in the 1970's. The policy left Western banks loaded with loans that may never be repaid, while Eastern European resources were diverted to military spending. The Eastern European countries now are barely creditworthy. But as their position improves, they may go on another borrowing spree to finance planned development of Russia's vast natural gas reserves. The West may be more careful next time.

WORLD ECONOMY: PULLING APART

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]14 Feb 1982: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424301597?accountid=11752

Abstract: Elsewhere in the industrial world, high unemployment, combined with the threat to business employers, is intensifying pressures for protectionism. In the United States, for instance, the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation, with the support of General Motors, have already induced the Government to exact ''voluntary'' export quotas from the Japanese. Steel producers, dissatisfied with the protection accorded them by a ''trigger price mechanism,'' are seeking stronger safeguards by bringing anti-dumping charges against foreign competitors, mainly in Europe. ''We have to remind ourselves that there is simply no precedent in history,'' Mr. Clausen said, ''for the dynamic rate of change in the geographic spread of global output in our era, and in the structure and character of world trade.'' Neither in the United States nor in Europe is that shift of labor taking place smoothly or painlessly. In Europe, not only are jobs scarce for the young and unskilled, but also lost jobs in old-line industries are causing nations to send their surplus ''guest workers'' back to their own countries, such as Turkey or Greece. In America, the shrinking of job opportunities is also having a major impact on young people, blacks and other minorities, and provoking rising popular - or populist - resistance to immigration from such countries as Haiti or Mexico. And strained budgets are causing social and job programs to be cut, not increased.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=WORLD+ECONOMY%3A+PULLING+APART&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-02-14&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: IN 1982, the world economy, which has been growing increasingly interdependent for decades, is being subjected to the most powerful disintegrative pressures, both economic and political, of the postwar period. Foremost among these pressures are recession and rising unemployment. In the United States, Canada, Britain, West Germany and the other Western industrialized countries, the slump is expected to lift total unemployment to 28.5 million workers in the second half of this year. High youth unemployment is exacerbating radicalism in Western Europe.
Economists at the Paris-based Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development are projecting that, for 1982 as a whole, unemployment in the United States will average 9 percent; in Canada, 8 1/4 percent; in Britain, 12 percent; in West Germany, 6 percent; in France, 8 1/2 percent and in the rest of the O.E.C.D. countries, 10 1/4 percent. North American will have 10 3/4 million out of work, while 16 million Western Europeans will be jobless. These are the highest levels since World War II.
Only in Japan, thanks not only to its continued strong economic growth and export performance but also to its labor-management policies, is the jobless rate expected to be as low as 2 1/4 percent, only a little above its postwar average.
Elsewhere in the industrial world, high unemployment, combined with the threat to business employers, is intensifying pressures for protectionism. In the United States, for instance, the Ford Motor Company and the Chrysler Corporation, with the support of General Motors, have already induced the Government to exact ''voluntary'' export quotas from the Japanese. Steel producers, dissatisfied with the protection accorded them by a ''trigger price mechanism,'' are seeking stronger safeguards by bringing anti-dumping charges against foreign competitors, mainly in Europe.
This country's industries are far from alone, however, in the push for protection. The members of the European Common Market recently joined with the United States in a new Multifiber Agreement to set tight limits on the growth of exports of the major textile exporters, Hong Kong, Taiwan and South Korea. Whether individually or collect ively, openly or covertly, protectioni sm is growing, imposing strains both within the industrial world and between the developed and developing nations.
Moreover, strains between the so-called North and South - a vast oversimplification of the divisions within the world, regionally, politically and in stages of economic development - are being intensified by the shifting shares of global industrial production.
A.W. (Tom) Clausen, the former chairman of the Bank of America who has succeeded Robert S. McNamara as president of the World Bank, said in a recent speech in Tokyo that, even with a good recovery from the present recession in Western Europe and North America, the older industrialized countries were likely to account for less than half of total world production by 1990, compared with more than two-thirds in 1960. Newly industrialized countries, including Japan, are likely to increase their share of world output to 25 percent in 1990 fr om 8.3 percent in 19 60, he said.
''We have to remind ourselves that there is simply no precedent in history,'' Mr. Clausen said, ''for the dynamic rate of change in the geographic spread of global output in our era, and in the structure and character of world trade.''
In the current decade, world trade is likely to expand almost twice as rapidly as the growth in world output, with manufactured goods accounting for 80 percent of the increase. With exports of commodities growing more slowly, the newly industrialized countries will be driving for a bigger share of the markets for traditional manufactured goods in the older industrial countries. These countries, in turn - at least in an economist's frictionless world - would shift their production and employment to high-technology industries and to financial, communications, knowledge and other services.
But the world is by no means frictionless, and this ideal scenario depends on the highly industrialized countries' maintaining high rates of growth and employment - and also on labor-market and educational policies to shift workers out of contracting industries into expanding ones.
Neither in the United States nor in Europe is that shift of labor taking place smoothly or painlessly. In Europe, not only are jobs scarce for the young and unskilled, but also lost jobs in old-line industries are causing nations to send their surplus ''guest workers'' back to their own countries, such as Turkey or Greece. In America, the shrinking of job opportunities is also having a major impact on young people, blacks and other minorities, and provoking rising popular - or populist - resistance to immigration from such countries as Haiti or Mexico. And strained budgets are causing social and job programs to be cut, not increased.
Hard times exacerbate political antagonisms, which feed on economic conditions. The European Common Market, one of the great postwar achievements, is under intense strain, with Britain, France and West Germany pulling apart.
Differences between the United States and the West Europeans over how to deal with the crisis in Poland have imposed new strains on the North Atlantic alliance. The United States, which has levied economic sanctions on both the Soviet Union and Poland, is pressing West Germany to end its big natural gas pipeline deal with the Russians and urging the Germans and its other European allies to cut back on trade, especially in high technology, with the Soviet bloc.
But the West Europeans, with their economies far more heavily dependent on trade with the Communists, are resistant to American pressures. They caustically note that President Reagan lifted former President Jimmy Carter's curbs on grain sales to the Russians when American economic interests - and his own political interests - were involved.
The Europeans are also highly critical of Mr. Reagan's domestic economic policies. They blame his commitment to monetarism and huge supply-side tax cuts for causing high interest rates, which in turn they blame for disturbing world currency markets, and for forcing high interest rates upon them, thereby slowing world economic growth.
The United States rejects these charges, contending that high interest rates and sluggish growth throughout the industrialized world are a consequence of inflation and the efforts of all governments to check inflation, whether by tight fiscal policies, tight monetary policies, or both.
There may be some merit in both sides' arguments. Unquestionably, the current world slowdown, which is now more than two years old, stemmed from measures adopted by all the Western nations to prevent the second great oil shock of 1978-79 from worsening world inflation, as the first oil shock of 1973-74 had done.
The skyrocketing of oil prices through the 1970's has also created another serious source of world strain: the mountain of international debt, especially that of the oil-poor developing countries. By 1980 the total debt owed by the developing countries totaled $439 billion, of which $284 billion, or two-thirds, was owed to private lenders. The problems of debt repa yment, with the lending capacity of private institutions close to the ir limits, add another element of risk of disintegration to the trou bled world economy.
The Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, which exploited the tight markets and panic buying of oil importers, first in the wake of the Arab-Israeli war of 1973 and then of the Iranian revolution of 1978, is now faced with an oil glut. Pressures from other OPEC nations on Saudi Arabia to cut back its oil production are growing. But even a Saudi cutback of two million barrels a day, as Sheik Ahmed Zaki Yamani has suggested, might not end the glut, for the enormous increase in oil prices has resulted in a huge increase in production from non-OPEC sources and a cut in consumption by the major oil importers, weakening OPEC's grip on the world oil market.
Even with the heat off oil prices, however, and even in the midst of the recess ion, inflation in the industrialized countries is still a problem, al though rates vary widely, depending on national economicpolicies. In the United States, the price index used to deflate grossnational prod uct was 9 percent in 1981 and is expected to come down to 8 percent in 1982. Last year the inflation rates in Britain, France and It aly were 12 1/4 percent, 12 percent and 17 3/4 percent respectively, but only 4 percent in West Germany and 3 1/4 percent inJapan. Those widely differening rates of inflation will continue to breed instabi lity in foreign exchange rates, together with swinging trade surplus es and deficits. Japan is suspected in the West of having held d own the yen's rate below its market value to strengthen its already p owerful trade position.
Thus the world slump is embittering both economic and political relations all over the world: North-South, East-West, within the capitalist world and within the Communist world alike. Inflation hangs on, and the fear of worsening it is inhibiting national economic policies to stimulate recovery.

ALLIES REBUKING REAGAN SHARPLY ON BIG DEFICITS

Author: PAUL LEWIS, Special to the New York Times

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]13 Feb 1982: 1.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424304727?accountid=11752

Abstract: Although the Europeans and Japanese were ''completely unanimous,'' in the words of a French official present, in warning Mr. [Donald T. Regan] that another big United States budget deficit could ''push the world into a depression,'' their advice had been ignored. In a speech today in Bremen, West Germany, Dr. [Karl Otto Pohl] renewed the attack, warning that high United States defense spending, coupled with tax cuts, would produce a deficit that would ''either keep American interest rates high or raise inflation.'' Calling this prospect ''particularly disappointing,'' Dr. Pohl added: ''I cannot believe that the United States does not understand that it bears responsibility not only for its own economy, but also for the world economy.'' Hans Mast, chief economist at Credit Suisse in Zurich, sees ''no room'' for an appreciable cut in interest rates in the near future. ''It's not very satisfactory to see so much private savings being chanelled to the Government instead of going into productive private investment,'' he said. Budget Is a 'Major Calamity'

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=ALLIES+REBUKING+REAGAN+SHARPLY+ON+BIG+DEFICITS&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-02-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=1.1&amp;au=PAUL+LEWIS%2C+Special+to+the+New+York+Times&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: President Reagan's proposed budget has drawn highly unusual public attacks from West European finance ministers, central bank chiefs and many private economists, who assert that the big projected deficits will further depress their own economies.
President Reagan formally announced his budget last weekend, and since then Finance Minister Jacques Delors of France, Chancellor of the Exchequer Sir Geoffrey Howe of Britain, Finance Minister Willie de Clercq of Belgium and Karl Otto Pohl, president of the West German central bank, have all called it, in effect, a threat to the world's economic health. They have urged Mr. Reagan and Congress to reduce the $91.5 billion deficit projected for the fiscal year that begins Oct.1. Effects Already Being Fe lt
Indeed, the complex economic mechanism by which a big United States budget deficit tends to raise interest rates and unemployment in Western Europe and throughout the world is already starting to operate: Since President Reagan made his budget proposals, American interest rates have gone up and the value of the dollar, in turn, has risen sharply against other major currencies as investors have transferred funds into dollar investments.
Senior officials at the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development, along with many private European bankers and economists, say another large United States deficit, coming on top of this year's expected $99 billion deficit, will keep interest rates high throughout the world.
That, in turn, will choke off the mild economic upturn that the O.E.C.D. has predicted for its 24 member nations in the second half of this year, the officials said, raising unemployment beyond its current level of 9 percent, the highest since the Depression.
Even with a recovery, which could see European economic growth rates rise from zero in 1981 to about 2.5 percent by the end of 1982, unemployment would edge up to 10 percent in 1983, O.E.C.D. analysts said. And without such an upswing, the picture is even worse.
To be sure, past inflationary policies of European governments are now forcing them to keep interest rates high in an attempt to curb rising prices. But the governments find it advantageous to blame American policies for their own political and economic difficulties.
Helmut Schmidt's coalition Government in Germany is in serious difficulty, and the Chancellor has been forced to initiate a $4.5 billion economic stimulation program to combat unemployment, which has risen to 1.7 million workers, a 50 percent increase in the past year, the steepest rate of increase in Western Europe.
Bonn's overall rate, however, is still well below most other countries. The O.E.C.D. projects that West Germany will have an unemployment rate of 6 percent this year, compared with 9 percent in the United States, 8 1/4 percent in Canada, 12 percent in Britain, 9 percent in Italy, 8 1/2 percent in France and 2 1/4 percent in Japan. Altogether, the organization says, North America will have 10.75 million out of work, while 16 million Europeans will be jobless, the highest levels since World War II.
The latest polls in Britain show the governing Conservatives heading for defeat. And France's new Socialist Government has recently lost four byelections and faces a serious loss of backing from its trade union supporters. Past Support From Tories
In the past, Britain's Conservatives have voiced strong support for the Reagan Administration, many of whose policies parallel those of Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher. Nevertheless the Chancellor of the Excheq@uer, a ddressing the House of Commons Thursday, said it was ''important t hat our country and other countries help to make plain to the United States our concern about the level of their prospectivebudget defici t and its implications for interest rates around the world.''
The 10 members of the European Community had already made their views know to Washington, Sir Geoffrey added. In Brussels, Mr. de Clercq, who currently holds the presidency of the Community's Council of Finance Ministers, said in his criticism of the budget that continued high interest rates would ''increase the temptation for countries to go protectionist.''
European governments are particularly bitter about President Reagan's budget, officials say, because the finance ministers of Britain, France, Germany and Japan all urged Treasury Secretary Donald T. Regan to raise taxes and curb the deficit at the most recent of their regular private meetings, held in the Palace of Versailles three weeks ago. 'Advice Has Been Ignored'
Although the Europeans and Japanese were ''completely unanimous,'' in the words of a French official present, in warning Mr. Regan that another big United States budget deficit could ''push the world into a depression,'' their advice had been ignored.
In a speech today in Bremen, West Germany, Dr. Pohl renewed the attack, warning that high United States defense spending, coupled with tax cuts, would produce a deficit that would ''either keep American interest rates high or raise inflation.'' Calling this prospect ''particularly disappointing,'' Dr. Pohl added: ''I cannot believe that the United States does not understand that it bears responsibility not only for its own economy, but also for the world economy.''
Earlier this week, Mr. Delors told an international business conference here that high American interest rates were now ''the principal obstacle'' to economic recovery in Europe. I f Western nations could not agree on coordinated steps to bring rates down, he said, France would try to cut its interest ra tes unilaterally. He gave no further details. Effects on the Alliance
In addition, many European leaders now argue that the United States is weakening the Western alliance and undermining many of its own political and military objectives because its high rates are spreading recession around the world. ''How can we defend our alliance with the United States when critics say American policy is making us bankrupt?'' Mr. Delors asked.
Privately, European officials talk of ''a complete breakdown'' in monetary cooperation between Europe and the United States and say that trans-Atlantic economic relations are now worse than at any time since Aug. 15, 1971, when former President Richard M. Nixon unilaterally ended the dollar's convertibility into gold and slapped a 20 percent surcharge on United States imports.
''We have simply never before seen a United States Administration that displayed this degree of indifference to the effects of its action on its allies,'' a senior German official commented.
Similarly, Paul King, chief economist at Forex, the London-based exchange-rate forecasting service, commented, ''European governments are finally realizing the Reagan Administration doesn't care about the international consequences of its monetary policy.''
Hans Mast, chief economist at Credit Suisse in Zurich, sees ''no room'' for an appreciable cut in interest rates in the near future. ''It's not very satisfactory to see so much private savings being chanelled to the Government instead of going into productive private investment,'' he said. Budget Is a 'Major Calamity'
''The Reagan budget is a major calamity not just for the United States but for the West as a whole and even for the whole world,'' said David Watt, director of Britain's Royal Institute for International Affairs, in an article this morning in The Times of London.
One reason the Europeans are distressed is that oil and many other imported goods must be paid for in dollars, so their import costs rise when their currencies weaken against the dollar. Thus, they raise interest rates to make their currencies more attractive and prevent depreciation, but this tends to choke off new investment and delay economic recovery.
The dollar's latest surge in value is particularly frustrating for some European governments because it appears to have ended their recent concerted attempt to take the edge off their interest rates.
During the last days of Janaury, the Bank of England, the Bundesbank and the Dutch central bank pushed down several key interest rates by about half a percentage point and sold large su ms of dollars fr om their reserves to prevent their currencies from declining aga inst the dollar.
At that time, monetary officials admitted that this experiment in coordinated rate-cutting was unlikely to go much further unless the United States raised taxes to reduce the deficit, a move that President Reagan opposes. Mr. Reagan also opposes intervening on the foreign-exchange markets to hold the dollar down - a move that the Europeans advocate.

Subject: UNITED STATE INTERNATIONAL RELATIONS; UNITED STATES ECONOMY; BUDGETS AND BUDGETING; ECONOMIC CONDITIONS AND TRENDS

Location: FRANCE GREAT BRITAIN BELGIUM WEST GERMANY

People: Delors, Jacques Pohl, Karl Otto

Company: OECD

WHY INTEREST RATES ARE SO HIGH

Author: Arenson, Karen W

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 Feb 1982: D.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424297924?accountid=11752

Abstract: Furthermore, ''real'' interest rates - the interest rate after inflation is subtracted - have also continued to run at record levels. For example, long-term Government bonds recently sold at a record 14.56 percent. With inflation currently running at about 8 percent, that means the real interest rate on those bonds was roughly 6 percent. Historically, real interest rates have been in 2 to 3 percent range. Q. Let's take those three factors one at a time. How much power does the Fed really have to control interest rates? A. The Fed does not have direct control over interest rates. It can indirectly affect the money supply, but even there its influence is somewhat tenuous. Factors that can dilute the Fed's impact include how fast the economy is growing and how actively people are moving their money around. If money is turning over faster, for example, rates may tend to be a bit lower than a situation of slower turnover but the same size money supply. For example, after a year of 10 percent inflation, it would take $1.10 to equal the value of a dollar a year earlier. Someone lending money for that year, therefore, must get back $1.10 just to break even. Although anything greater than $1 represents an interest payment, it is only the amount above $1.10 that represent a ''real'' interest payment, after inflation is taken into account.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=WHY+INTEREST+RATES+ARE+SO+HIGH&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-02-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.1&amp;au=Arenson%2C+Karen+W&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: With long-term interest rates at record levels, many business and political leaders are predicting that the President will have to bail out of his economic program before it fully begins to take hold. Many critics contend that the projected Federal budget deficits will push rates still higher, depressing financial markets and the economy. A discussion of interest rates, and why they are so high, follows.
Q. Why are interest rates so important? A. The American economy has become increasingly dependent upon borrowing as a means of financing various purchases. Business uses loans to help pay for manufacturing and for new plant and equipment. Consumers rely on borrowing to pay for automobiles, homes and other goods.
Interest rates represent the cost of the borrowed money, which then becomes part of the cost of these items to the purchasers. If interest rates get too high, items become too expensive and purchasers start backing away. Mortgage rates, for example, must be taken into account in calculating monthly housing costs.
When rates get too high, people stop buying houses and automobiles. Business stops investing in new plant and equipment because the h igher initial costs make projects less profitable.
Q. But haven't interest rates risen before? A. Yes, but never to the present levels and never for so long a period. Even a decade ago, long-term interest rates for blue-chip corporate borrowers were only about 7 percent, about half of what they are today.
Furthermore, ''real'' interest rates - the interest rate after inflation is subtracted - have also continued to run at record levels. For example, long-term Government bonds recently sold at a record 14.56 percent. With inflation currently running at about 8 percent, that means the real interest rate on those bonds was roughly 6 percent. Historically, real interest rates have been in 2 to 3 percent range.
Q. Why do we have such high rates now? A. Economists have many different theories about what determines interest rates generally and why they are so high now. The three reasons mentioned most frequently are the Federal Reserve's restrictive monetary policy, continuing strong demand for credit and continued expectations for high inflation.
Q. Let's take those three factors one at a time. How much power does the Fed really have to control interest rates? A. The Fed does not have direct control over interest rates. It can indirectly affect the money supply, but even there its influence is somewhat tenuous. Factors that can dilute the Fed's impact include how fast the economy is growing and how actively people are moving their money around. If money is turning over faster, for example, rates may tend to be a bit lower than a situation of slower turnover but the same size money supply.
Having recognized this, however, many economists agree that if the Fed pursues a restrictive monetary policy, it will have an impact. In the short term, such a policy is likely to result in higher interest rates. But in the longer run the Fed could elicit the opposite result. If the growth in the money supply is slow enough for long enough, it may result in lower inflation and thus lead to lower interest rates. The only questions are how tight the monetary policy must be, and for how long, and what it will mean in terms of unemployment.
Q. How does the demand for money affect interest rates? A. In a slack economy, if business is at a standstill and consumers stop buying, lenders lower their interest rates - the price of the money -to entice borrowers. In a booming economy, th ere is more active bidding for the same pool of lendable funds, and that pushes interestrates up.
That is what concerns some of the Administration's critics. They maintain that the Government will have to borrow huge sums to finance the huge budget deficits, leaving business and consumers scrambling for what is left, which will push interest rates up. The Administration, however, responds that there will be enough money to go around, particularly if their tax incentive programs attract new savings dollars that will be available for borrowing.
Q. How about inflationary expectations? What exactly are they and how do they affect interest rates? A. Since inflation erodes the value of money, a lender wants to make sure that the interest paid on a loan covers both the amount eroded by inflation and enough above that for his profit. Thus, although people can not predict inflation rates exactly, their expectations about inflation affect how much interest they charge.
For example, after a year of 10 percent inflation, it would take $1.10 to equal the value of a dollar a year earlier. Someone lending money for that year, therefore, must get back $1.10 just to break even. Although anything greater than $1 represents an interest payment, it is only the amount above $1.10 that represent a ''real'' interest payment, after inflation is taken into account.
Q. How accurate are the lenders in estimating inflation when they calculate their interest charges? A. There is really no good way to say exactly. People have to rely on predictions and expectations. That is one reason interest rates are so volatile. People's expectations change almost daily, every time there is a new report about the economy or a new announcement about the Government's budget plans.
In recent years, lenders underestimated inflation and found they were losing money. Now, even though inflation has begun to fall, lenders are being more cautious. Although long-term interest rates fell three percentage points in November, concerns about budget deficits have reignited fears of higher inflation and higher interest rates, and long-term rates have climbed upward again.

Market Place; Common Stock And Inflation

Author: Metz, Robert

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 Jan 1982: D.8.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424282331?accountid=11752

Abstract: Computer Directions looked at all possible 25-year holding periods and discovered that in each such period gains in common stocks outpaced inflation. For the most recent 25-year period the average annual increase was 7.9 percent, well in excess of the inflation rate for the period of 5 percent. The Salomon Brothers bond index was up 3.2 percent a year in that same period. United States Government bonds advanced 2.8 percent annually and Treasury bills 5.4 percent annually, also in that recent 25-year period. During the most recent 10-year period ended in 1981, common stocks advanced at an annual rate of 6.4 percent. Th at compared with an increase of 8.6 percent annually in the Consu mer Price Index. The Salomon Brothers index, meantime, was up 3 pe rcent annually and United States Government bonds advanced 3.2 p ercent a year while Treasury bills gained 7.7 percent a year in t hat 10-year period.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Market+Place%3B+Common+Stock+And+Inflation&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1982-01-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.8&amp;au=Metz%2C+Robert&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: COMMON stocks work best. The longer you hold them, the more they outshine alte rnative securities. At least that's the thesis of a new study.
On the other hand, as a result of 10 years of rising oil prices, common shares have not served investors as well lately as in the past.
During the most recent 10-year period, inflation has eliminated gains in stocks on the average. Meanwhile, holders of longer-term, fixed-interest securities have been devastated by comparison.
These conclusions are from Computer Directions Advisors of Silver Spring, Md. Its study updates data originally published by Roger Ibbotson and Rex Sinquefield of the University of Chicago on investment results from 1926 through 1976.
Over that period, the two researchers found that common stocks advanced at a rate of 9.2 percent annually, assuming that all distributions were reinvested.
The current study indicates that common stocks continue to be a good long-term hedge against inflation. Emphasize the words long term. For example, over the entire 56-year period, total returns on common shares exceeded the rise in the Consumer Price Index by a ratio of 3 to 1. Still, the high rate of inflation during the last 10 years has taken its toll. The average annual gain for the full period eased one-tenth of a percentage point, as a result of the recent inflation, to 9.1 percent, the Computer Directions study found.
The picture is not so bright for recent intervals. For example, during the past five years common stocks showed an average annual return of 8 percent while the Consumer Price Index advanced by an average of 10.1 percent a year.
Even so, common stocks outpaced longer-term, fixed-income securities in that same five-year period. The Salomon Brothers highgrade corporate bond index declined by an average of 1.2 percent annually and United States Government bonds dropped by an average of six-tenths of 1 percent a year.
Three-month Treasury bills, meantime, proved to be superior even to stocks, almost holding their ground against inflation in posting an annual rise averaging 9.5 percent over the same five-year period.
Computer Directions did not say so, but the strong performance of Treasury bills is encouraging to investors in money market funds. Treasury bills are the bellwether for money market instruments, which usually offer somewhat higher returns.
Computer Directions looked at all possible 25-year holding periods and discovered that in each such period gains in common stocks outpaced inflation. For the most recent 25-year period the average annual increase was 7.9 percent, well in excess of the inflation rate for the period of 5 percent. The Salomon Brothers bond index was up 3.2 percent a year in that same period. United States Government bonds advanced 2.8 percent annually and Treasury bills 5.4 percent annually, also in that recent 25-year period.
During the most recent 10-year period ended in 1981, common stocks advanced at an annual rate of 6.4 percent. Th at compared with an increase of 8.6 percent annually in the Consu mer Price Index. The Salomon Brothers index, meantime, was up 3 pe rcent annually and United States Government bonds advanced 3.2 p ercent a year while Treasury bills gained 7.7 percent a year in t hat 10-year period.
Computer Directions notes that there were 47 possible 10-year holding periods over the 56 years and common stocks outpaced consumer prices in 41 of the 47 periods. All six lags occurred during the most recent 10-year holding periods.

A THREAT TO JOBS

Author: Rothschild, Emma

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]22 Nov 1981: A.25.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424230677?accountid=11752

Abstract: The forces of inflation and unemployment, in the [Reagan] view, confront each other at last. Eight percent unemployment, says his Council of Economic Advisers, "reflects the unfortunate but natural short-term consequences of unwinding the deeply rooted inflation that is embedded in the American economy." Once the unwinding is concluded, it suggests, a weaker but purer America will move forward again into the medium-term sunlight of "economic recovery." Since 1973, employment in America has increased rapidly - more rapidly than in other large industrial countries. But three-quarters of the new jobs created - 10 million positions - were in services, retail trade, and state and local government. New private-sector employment was further concentrated in health services, business services, and eating and drinking places (including fast-food restaurants). These "big three" labor-intensive industries generated almost five million new jobs between 1973 and 1980: more than the total employment in the automobil, steel, computer, electronic-components, aircraft, and petroleum industries combined. It is this structure of employment, these sources of new work, that Reagan policies may jeopardize. For three trends, each ominous for employment, are likely to coincide in the years ahead.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=A+THREAT+TO+JOBS%3A+%5BOP-ED%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-11-22&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.25&amp;au=Rothschild%2C+Emma&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: President Reagan greeted the autumn increase in unemployment with grim resolve - "I will not be deterred by temporary economic changes or short-term political expediency" - and with an affirmation of historic mission.
The forces of inflation and unemployment, in the Reagan view, confront each other at last. Eight percent unemployment, says his Council of Economic Advisers, "reflects the unfortunate but natural short-term consequences of unwinding the deeply rooted inflation that is embedded in the American economy." Once the unwinding is concluded, it suggests, a weaker but purer America will move forward again into the medium-term sunlight of "economic recovery."
This "Pilgrim's Progress" vision is familiar. But it is also deceptive. It obscures the real transformation of the work Americans do, the real dangers ahead, and the ways in which the Reagan policies could bring a far greater collapse in employment, above all in industries where jobs grew fastest in the 1970's.
Since 1973, employment in America has increased rapidly - more rapidly than in other large industrial countries. But three-quarters of the new jobs created - 10 million positions - were in services, retail trade, and state and local government. New private-sector employment was further concentrated in health services, business services, and eating and drinking places (including fast-food restaurants). These "big three" labor-intensive industries generated almost five million new jobs between 1973 and 1980: more than the total employment in the automobil, steel, computer, electronic-components, aircraft, and petroleum industries combined. It is this structure of employment, these sources of new work, that Reagan policies may jeopardize. For three trends, each ominous for employment, are likely to coincide in the years ahead.
The first is the recession itself, and the effects of restrictive monetary policies. Inflation is likely to remain high next year, particularly given the effects of tax cuts and increased military spending. It is hard to believe that the Republicans, faced with inflation plus unprecedented Federal-budget deficits, will soon abandon their hope of redemption through hard times. They are far more likely to maintain existing monetary policies, and even to tighten fiscal policies.
The second trend has to do with long-term developments in the new boom industries. Employment in the "big three" has been growing much slower in 1981 than it did from 1973 to 1980 - in eating and drinking places, the rate of growth this year has been a little more than 2 percent per year, compared to 8 percent per year between 1973 and 1980. The growth of demand for services and meals may be slowing down; the boom industries may be investing in processes that are less labor-intensive. A deep recession, moreover, might devastate small, recently established service businesses. Employment in trade and services increased rapidly in the 1920's but was far from immune to the recession of 1929 to 1932.
The third trend is the Administration's assault on nonmilitary public spending. This will have direct concsequences for employment. State and local government is no longer a source of new work: From April to October, this sector actually lost 350,000 jobs, on a seasonally adjusted basis.
The effects of spending cuts are also unlikely to be counteracted by eventual defense-related employment. Even at the time of the Vietnam War, military spending created less employment than other public spending. The new boom - buying research, accurate missiles, a communications system that would survive a nuclear war - would provide still fewer jobs, above all for unskilled and unemployed workers.
The indirect consequences of the Reagan cuts may be even more ominous. For the private health services, on which so much new employment depends, grew up in the interstices of public-health spending. Like certain business services that supply government, they will be hurt by cuts in public expenditure. Recent suggestions by Richard S. Schweiker, Secretary of Health and Human Services - to cut reimbursement of "ancillary hospital services," and to limit Medicaid payments for long-term care - could end the growth of jobs in the private hospitals and nursing homes so important in the 1970's boom.
The transformation of employment in the United States makes it essential to find ways of creating new jobs in industries where productivity is high; of creating jobs with possibilities for advancement and creativity; of increasing quality and efficiency in private and public services. These needs are obscured by an economic vision that looks only at the behemoths of overall inflation and overall unemployment. The chance of long-term growth in productive work may be lost for many more years to come - if the Reagan policies bring a collapse in employment in services, in government, and throughout the economy. --------------------------------------------------------------------- Emma Rothschild, author of "Paradise Lost: The Decline of the Auto-Industrial Ago," teaches in the Science, Technology and Society Program at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology.

Economic Scene; Urgent Search For a New Mix

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]20 Nov 1981: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424232460?accountid=11752

Abstract: Although it has been partly obscured by the fog of technical concepts and jargon, a conflict exists between the Administration and the Federal Reserve Board over how monetary policy should be conducted. Paul A. Volcker, chairman of the Fed, regards bringing down the rate of inflation as his top priority, and has been telling both the Administration and the public that he does not intend to relax restrictive monetary policy. He told a group of real estate brokers in Florida earlier this week that ''we can't effectively deal with your problems by pumping up the money supply.'' Even if this were to produce short-term relief, he said, it would only aggravate problems for the longer run. ''An increase in the rate of growth of money supply generated by the Federal Reserve in the face of unchanging money demands probably brings down short-term rates for some period ahead. But given the probably shortening lag between money and its economic effects, inflation will soon increase and expectations of inflation will increase even earlier. Long-term rates, therefore, might hardly come down at all in response to a Federal Reserve-induced acceleration of the money supply. An acceleration of inflation, moreover, would quickly compel the Federal Reserve to reverse its expansionary action.''

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Economic+Scene%3B+Urgent+Search+For+a+New+Mix&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-11-20&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: THE onset of a recession, which the Reagan Administration now expects to be severe, has focused national attention on the need for a new mix of economic policies that will check and reverse the slump without causing inflation and interest rates to soar again once the current recession ends. The American economy has been on a roller coaster. This is the second recession in two years, and if interest rates go shooting up again when the current slump ends, there could be a third recession close behind.
Although it has been partly obscured by the fog of technical concepts and jargon, a conflict exists between the Administration and the Federal Reserve Board over how monetary policy should be conducted. Paul A. Volcker, chairman of the Fed, regards bringing down the rate of inflation as his top priority, and has been telling both the Administration and the public that he does not intend to relax restrictive monetary policy. He told a group of real estate brokers in Florida earlier this week that ''we can't effectively deal with your problems by pumping up the money supply.'' Even if this were to produce short-term relief, he said, it would only aggravate problems for the longer run.
The Fed, which has been blamed for the current recession by focusing solely on the money supply and letting interest rates skyrocket, is now trying to find a way to a more stable monetary policy that will produce less economic instability.
Henry C. Wallich, a member of the Federal Reserve Board of Governors, says the cost has been paid most heavily by the thrift industry, housing builders, the auto industry and other manufacturers of durable goods, but it is now being borne by the entire economy.
Nevertheless, the Administration's monetarists, such as Beryl Sprinkel, the Under Secretary of the Treasury for Monetary Affairs, believe that it would be a mistake for the Fed to begin efforts to control interest rates rather than simply to keep the money supply growing within its present targets. The Administration is counting on the Fed, by feeding reserves to the banking system in a time of falling loan demands, to help bring down interest rates and turn the recession around.
While the Fed has not formally departed from that policy, it appears to be trying to avoid rigid commitments to targets that, by driving interest rates down too low in a slump or shooting them up too high in a recovery, will exacerbate the business cycle. Mr. Wallich, a former professor of economics at Yale, recently told a conference in Frankfurt, West Germany:
''An increase in the rate of growth of money supply generated by the Federal Reserve in the face of unchanging money demands probably brings down short-term rates for some period ahead. But given the probably shortening lag between money and its economic effects, inflation will soon increase and expectations of inflation will increase even earlier. Long-term rates, therefore, might hardly come down at all in response to a Federal Reserve-induced acceleration of the money supply. An acceleration of inflation, moreover, would quickly compel the Federal Reserve to reverse its expansionary action.''
Mr. Volcker has continued to press the Administration to close the yawning gap in the Federal budget as a means of reducing the problem of the Fed in managing money. He has suggested that the Administration needs to reconsider both its spending and tax programs as a means of slimming the deficit. As a result of the impact of the current recession, the budget deficit for the fiscal year 1982, which the Administration has estimated would be $44 billion, is now likely to double. Alan Greenspan, the former chief economic adviser to President Gerald R. Ford and president of his own economic consulting firm, estimates that the fiscal year 1982 deficit will be $84.3 billion. Even more disturbingly, however, his firm, Townsend-Greenspan, is estimating that the deficit will be $109.4 billion in the fiscal year 1983, presumably a year of economic recovery and growth.
If such deficit figures are realized, the Fed will face the thankless task of either accommodating the deficit and thereby breeding worse inflation or refusing to accommodate it and helping to drive interest rates even higher. In either case, interest rates are likely to soar once again, creating the probability of another plunge into recession.
For the economy to get back on a more stable course, with interest rates less volatile on a lower plateau, fiscal policy needs to carry more of the burden of checking inflation. This implies that either the amount of tax-cutting voted by Congress would have to be reduced, or budget expenditures, in both the military and civilian sectors, would have to be cut. However, after the experience of recent years, it appears increasingly unlikely that the job of stopping inflation and holding interest rates on a steady and lower course can be accomplished by monetary and fiscal policy alone. Inflation could be brought down by a continuing slump and climbing unemployment, but that course would be economically painful and politically suicidal.
To achieve steady and noninflationary growth, a better balance of fiscal and monetary policy will need to be reinforced by an incomes policy to restrain wages and prices. Tax cuts might be related to national policies for inducing more stable wage and price behavior by labor and management.
In addition, in a world in which the United States has been losing ground in productivity and innovation, an industrial policy that joins the talents of business, labor, academia and government would also appear to be required.

____________________________________________________________

Comment; EVIL EXPECTATIONS

Author: 3d, BAYARD F. POPE

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]27 Sep 1981: A.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424173735?accountid=11752

Abstract: Are we forever doomed to ''crippling interest rates'' and ''bulging deficits,'' as they say? I think not. First of al l, deficits are not themselves inflationary. The fiscal 1975 and 1976 deficits exceeded the projected 1982 deficit as a percent of gr oss national product, personal savings and Government spending (all the buzz ratios). Yet, in both years inflation moderated and rates fell. The second point is that there is nothing inflationary about interest rates falling to 13 or 14 percent. We have already built into current interest rates an inflation premium of 9 to 10 percent. (Indeed, this is widely perceived to be the underlying rate of inflation.) Whereas in the past lenders and investors have demanded a risk premium (or real underlying rate of return) of two percentage points, under the 13 to 14 percent scenario, their risk premium would be four percentage points. There was little concern in the past about low interests rates being inflationary, in fact, they were considered disinflationary. So what are we doing with rates at 17 to 20 percent? Producing more inflation, that's what.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Comment%3B+EVIL+EXPECTATIONS&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-09-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.2&amp;au=3d%2C+BAYARD+F.+POPE&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Y OU can't inhale and exhale at the same time. Put differently, today's inflation and high interest rates are the result of a head-on collision between a tight monetary policy and a stimulative fiscal policy.
Wall Street's reaction to this collision is unmistakable - indifference to economic developments, fear of Government crowding other borrowers out of the credit markets, increasing paranoia about the Federal deficit. And negative psychology has reached alarming proportions.
For example, if the Federal Reserve tightens, it will raise interest rates, which is bad for inflation and bonds, and will slow down the economy, which is bad for stocks. If the Fed loosens, the money supply will grow, which means the Fed will soon have to tighten, which will drive up interest rates, which is bad for bonds, and will slow down the economy, which is bad for stocks. What's more, real interest rates - interest rates minus the rate of inflation - are the highest they have been in 50 years.
Are we forever doomed to ''crippling interest rates'' and ''bulging deficits,'' as they say? I think not. First of al l, deficits are not themselves inflationary. The fiscal 1975 and 1976 deficits exceeded the projected 1982 deficit as a percent of gr oss national product, personal savings and Government spending (all the buzz ratios). Yet, in both years inflation moderated and rates fell.
Why? Because the Federal Reserve did not monetize the deficit. In fact, in 1976 it bought only 6 percent of the Federal debt. The Fed does this by injecting reserves into the banking system, and by lowering interest rates, thus stimulating the demand for credit and reviving the economy.
When the Fed expands bank reserves, it provides the raw material for the growth of the money supply. This leads to increases in aggregate demand and interest rates, which starts the cycle all over again.
From 1956 to 1974, the inflation impact of budget deficits would have been statistically too small to quantify if the debt had not been largely monetized. Currently, the Fed can purchase 14 percent of the fiscal 1982 deficit (a figure it has stayed below in three of the past six years) and still have the money supply growing within its projected targets of 2 1/2 percent to 5 1/2 percent. Admittedly, this type of rationale holds little sway with the financial markets.
The second point is that there is nothing inflationary about interest rates falling to 13 or 14 percent. We have already built into current interest rates an inflation premium of 9 to 10 percent. (Indeed, this is widely perceived to be the underlying rate of inflation.) Whereas in the past lenders and investors have demanded a risk premium (or real underlying rate of return) of two percentage points, under the 13 to 14 percent scenario, their risk premium would be four percentage points. There was little concern in the past about low interests rates being inflationary, in fact, they were considered disinflationary. So what are we doing with rates at 17 to 20 percent? Producing more inflation, that's what.
M Y third point is that high interest rates do not combat inflation, but are in fact one of the principle causes of it. They slow the economy down, which reduces tax receipts and increases transfer payments. The implications for an expanding deficit are obvious. High rates work their way into the Consumer Price Index largely through mortgage rates and the housing component. Social Security, Federal pensions and veterans benefits are all tied to this index. There goes the deficit again.
I am not one to indulge in all this agnostic mischief without suggesting a remedy. The problem, it would seem, is one of perception. The deficit itself is not inflationary. It is the expectation and perception of that deficit. Low interest rates are not inflationary, but the perception of what would follow low interest rates is inflationary. Inflationary psychology is itself inflation ary. Workers, lenders and manufacturers raise the price they charge for their goods and services to rotect themselves from erosion of their future purchasingpower.
No one came down from the mountain with the deficit etched in stone tablets. There is plenty of fat in the defense budget. Social Security has for too long avoided the ax that cut up the rest of the New Deal. There are alternatives. But the deficit is not our problem. Our problem is understanding what is inflationary and what is not. --------------------------------------------------------------------- Bayard F. Pope 3d is vice president and investment officer of First Bancorp of New Hampshire.
