Economic Scene

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]09 Sep 1981: D.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424188573?accountid=11752

Abstract: Vietnam kicked off the current inflation, and the [Reagan] miltary buildup in th e midst of huge tax cuts could give inflation another cruel twist. An analysis by James R. Capra in the current Quarterly Review of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York finds that the Reagan miltary build up, concentrated in procurement, ''will apparently far exceed the Vi etnam procurement increase.'' Wassily Leontief of New York University, who won the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Science for his analysis of interindustry flows, has warned that enormous new military outlays will concentrate scarce capital in the military area and ''will starve the rest of the economy of the investment it desperately requires to remain competitive in the tightening worldwide market.'' Nevertheless, increasing nuclear arsenals and delivery systems still gets top priority in American and Soviet military planning. In his study, ''Defense in the 1980's,'' William W. Kaufmann, a leading analyst at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, argues that expenditures on nuclear arms should get a lower priority in the Reagan buildup than additional highly mobile, conventional air, sea and land forces. He concludes that, with the nuclear forces already programmed, the United States will continue to have a powerful second-strike capability through the 1980's and that the probability of any use of nuclear weapons remains low, although it could worsen if the arms race accelerates.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Economic+Scene&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-09-09&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.2&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Falling stock and bond prices, persistently high interest rates, a widening budget gap and fears that worse inflation may lie ahead have forced President Reagan to reconsider his planned buildup in military spending.
Thus far the White House is talking about relatively small trims, amounting at most to $15 billion in each of the next three years. If fully carried out, those trims would still permit the Pentagon's budget to rise by $15 billion in 1982, $25 billion in 1983 and $42 billion in 1984. Beyond 1984, the costs of the military buildup would climb a lot faster - by $81 billion in 1985 and by $117.7 billion in 1986. Total miltary outlays in the next five years are scheduled to exceed $1.4 trillion.
But how is that huge outlay on the military to be financed within the context of tax cuts totaling, on the Administration's own figures, $750 billion in the next five years. The danger is that, instead of real economic growth making the miltary buildup and tax cuts compatible, the result will be worse inflation, which, with money held tight, will drive up interest rates and choke off the economy's real growth.
Vietnam kicked off the current inflation, and the Reagan miltary buildup in th e midst of huge tax cuts could give inflation another cruel twist. An analysis by James R. Capra in the current Quarterly Review of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York finds that the Reagan miltary build up, concentrated in procurement, ''will apparently far exceed the Vi etnam procurement increase.''
Where the Vietnam buildup lifted procurement authority (measured in constant 1982 dollars) from $42 billion in 1965 to $63 billion in 1967, but then dropped in each of the next three years, the Reagan military buildup would raise procurement authority from $41 billion in 1980 to nearly $70 billion in 1982 and on upward in the next three years to reach $100 billion in 1985.
The inflationary problem could now be somewhat easier than during Vietnam because of greater spare industrial capacity. However, the economy now has a much higher rate of inflation and greater inflationary expectations than during the Vietnam War.
Wassily Leontief of New York University, who won the Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Science for his analysis of interindustry flows, has warned that enormous new military outlays will concentrate scarce capital in the military area and ''will starve the rest of the economy of the investment it desperately requires to remain competitive in the tightening worldwide market.''
A nation's economic strength, balance and flexibility have become vital elements in both its domestic well-being and its strength abroad. But military planners seem to treat their goal as the maximizing of the level of miltary expenditures, regarding economic considerations as an unfortunate if necessary constraint.
The nuclear age seemingly downgraded the significance of a nation's economic capabilities to outlast and crush an economically weaker opponent, because what would count in a nuclear war that might last only a few days (conceivably only a few hours or minutes) were forces in being - nuclear bombs and missiles immune from attack and ready for instantaneous response to any threat. That concept of hair-trigger and short-lived war still dominates the military policy of both the United States and the Soviet Union.
But Vietnam was no hair-trigger war and, both by its incredible length and the failure of the United States to prevail, it signalized the importance, not only for this country but all other advanced industrial countries as well, of developing new strategies to protect or advance their interests.
Subsequent events, including the Petrodollar coup by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, the Iranian upheaval, and, on the Soviet side, Moscow's failure to subdue Afghanistan or even Poland, reinforce the lessons of Vietnam in demonstrating the constraints on a superpower; those constraints are political, social and economic as well as conventionally military.
Nevertheless, increasing nuclear arsenals and delivery systems still gets top priority in American and Soviet military planning. In his study, ''Defense in the 1980's,'' William W. Kaufmann, a leading analyst at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, argues that expenditures on nuclear arms should get a lower priority in the Reagan buildup than additional highly mobile, conventional air, sea and land forces. He concludes that, with the nuclear forces already programmed, the United States will continue to have a powerful second-strike capability through the 1980's and that the probability of any use of nuclear weapons remains low, although it could worsen if the arms race accelerates.
A rethinking of United States military strategy has become essential if the nation is to avoid exacerbating both its military and its economic problems. end [ TEXT OMITTED FROM SOURCE ]

THE INTEREST RATE ISSUE; Economic Analysis

Author: Silk, Leonard

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 July 1981: A.1.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424136561?accountid=11752

Abstract: Again, the Fisher theory of the link between inflation and interest rates is why Administration officials up to President [Reagan] are assuring foreign leaders that they expect American interest rates to come down, possibly by the end of this year. By how much? The Treasury Secretary said he did not want to be pinned down but ''I'd say by several points.'' How long is the Administration prepared to stick with this policy of trying to bring down interest rates by keeping money tight to squeeze inflation out of the system? Secretary [Donald T. Regan] said the policy had already been in effect for three months and ''sticking with it for another six months, that would be nine months of tight interest, would be really sticking with it; it's been a long time since we've done that in the United States.'' What might be the effect of this policy? The Europeans are growing increasingly concerned that it might have a more devastating effect on employment in their countries than on inflation. Secretary Regan acknowledged that President Francois Mitterrand of France had warned of the dire consequences that would develop if interest rates did not come down by the end of this year, and reported his saying that ''unemployment in France as well as in Germany and other countries in Europe was getting to a flash point, to a point where it might cause social upheaval.'' ''And,'' he said, ''they wanted to avoid that.'' Cost of Loans Increased

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=THE+INTEREST+RATE+ISSUE%3B+Economic+Analysis%3A+%5Banalysis%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-07-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Silk%2C+Leonard&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Interest rates, once considered a topic that appealed mostly to financiers, have moved to the top of the agenda of this summit conference of the leaders of the industrial world.
The reason for this dramatic change is the extraordinary height of interest rates in the United States, Japan and Western Europe and the danger they pose to the industrial world in rising unemployment and falling output.
For months now, the Europeans have singled out the United States, and particularly the monetary policies of the Reagan Administration, as the culprit behind the run-up in rates, and the resulting disruptions that high rates have caused their economies.
Today, Secretary of the Treasury Donald T. Regan quoted a striking statement on the subject by Chancellor Helmut Schmidt of West Germany. Chancellor Schmidt, Mr. Regan recounted, recently told a meeting of the finance and economic ministers that interest rates in his country, at 15 to 16 percent, were ''the highest rates of interest in Germany since the birth of Christ, as far as real interest rates are concerned.''
By the ''real'' rate of interest, Mr. Schmidt meant the difference between the nominal rate charged by lenders -such as the 15 to 16 percent rate he cited - and the rate of inflation. Since the consumer price index in West Germany was rising at an annual rate of 5.8 percent in the second quarter of 1981, this means that the real rate of interest there was roughly 10 percent.
Historically, economists have considered a real rate of interest of 2 or 3 percent - the real sacrifice that lenders make in letting borrowers use their savings - normal over the longer run. Thus a 10 percent real rate - and it is slightly higher than that in the United States today, with the prime rate at 20 1/2 percent and inflation under 10 percent - is about five times normal.
Advances in the real rate of these dimensions are highly unusual, whether or not Chancellor Schmidt is right in saying that the current level, at least in his country, is the highest since the Christian era began. The leading American monetary theorist, the late Irving Fisher of Yale University, contended that the real rate of interest was remarkably constant over time, with the nominal rate of interest moving up and down more or less in phase with the increase or decrease in the rate of price inflation.
This theory in fact underlies the Reagan Administration's explanation of why interest rates have climbed so high. It is blaming the inflation created by earlier administrations for the high interest rates.
Treasury Secretary Regan, asked at a news conference here what kind of justification President Reagan was offering the other leaders, replied: ''What he has told them is: look what he inherited when he came in, the rate of inflation, things of that nature and high interest rates. Remember interest rates were high when the President took office. So what he is telling them is that his policies - his fiscal policy, the monetary policy, particularly that of the Federal Reserve, our deregulation policy, and above all, our tax policy, these things are going to get inflation down.'' Position Called Disingenuous
Oddly enough, the President and his aides are stressing at this summit conference that it is the Federal Reserve that controls interest rates, not the Administration. This position is being taken here as disingenuous, since the Administration has been leaning hard on the Federal Reserve - sometimes even publicly - to keep money tight, no matter what the effect on interest rates.
Again, the Fisher theory of the link between inflation and interest rates is why Administration officials up to President Reagan are assuring foreign leaders that they expect American interest rates to come down, possibly by the end of this year. By how much? The Treasury Secretary said he did not want to be pinned down but ''I'd say by several points.''
How long is the Administration prepared to stick with this policy of trying to bring down interest rates by keeping money tight to squeeze inflation out of the system? Secretary Regan said the policy had already been in effect for three months and ''sticking with it for another six months, that would be nine months of tight interest, would be really sticking with it; it's been a long time since we've done that in the United States.''
What might be the effect of this policy? The Europeans are growing increasingly concerned that it might have a more devastating effect on employment in their countries than on inflation. Secretary Regan acknowledged that President Francois Mitterrand of France had warned of the dire consequences that would develop if interest rates did not come down by the end of this year, and reported his saying that ''unemployment in France as well as in Germany and other countries in Europe was getting to a flash point, to a point where it might cause social upheaval.'' ''And,'' he said, ''they wanted to avoid that.'' Cost of Loans Increased
High United States interest rates force other countries to raise their own rates to prevent investors from shipping their funds abroad to take advantage of the rates. In raising their own rates, however, the other countries raise the cost of loans to their own industries and consumers. The higher rates dissuade industry from investing in the plants and machinery that provide jobs and discourage consumer buying of all those products that are bought on credit - houses, automobiles and other durable goods.
The widespread rioting and arson that Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher has faced in recent weeks in Britain clearly underlies the anxiety over a tight money and high interest rate policy being forced upon other countries by the United States. Although the Americans here have sought to describe high interest rates as a common problem among the industrial countries - really a consequence of general inflation - the Europeans are seeking to link the high interest rates to the United States. What they are suggesting is that it is not inflation alone but the monetarist solution to inflation being pursued by the United States that has driven interest rates so high.

Business Forum; IT AIN'T NECESSARILY SO ABOUT HIGH RATES

Author: Wright, John Winthrop

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]21 June 1981: A.2.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424119249?accountid=11752

Abstract: Money and credit are the essential lubricants of our economic system. In the past 10 years, money supply has contracted by onethird relative to G.N.P. - far more than can be explained away as a result of faster check clearing and other modern contributions to the ''velocity'' of circulation. Finally, interest rates can be reduced safely and promptly. Adequate money and credit can be supplied, provided that there is selective control in the application of credit. The Fed has the power and the ability, without significantly increasing its bureaucracy, to limit credit, which contributes to inflationary consumer demand, and to increase substantially credit available for ''supply-side'' capital investment in the development of new sources of energy, in increased production of goods and services and in costreducing equipment and technology. Today, Eurodollars are coming to the United States for investment. When they arrive here, these Eurodollars inflate the domestic money supply accordingly because they then become deposits in American banks. For our Federal Reserve Board to counter this by restricting the current money supply correspondingly is simply to deprive Americans of money and credit in order to offset the supply of Eurodollars coming into the United States from foreign investors. --------------------------------------------------------------------- John Winthrop Wright is president of Wright Investors' Services, an investment management and advisory organization in Bridgeport, Conn.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=Business+Forum%3B+IT+AIN%27T+NECESSARILY+SO+ABOUT+HIGH+RATES&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-06-21&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.2&amp;au=Wright%2C+John+Winthrop&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: THE Reagan Administration, in conjunction with the Congress, has a mandate to increase productivity, improve the nation's standard of living, reduce wasteful Government spending and interference with the free enterprise system and stabilize monetary values. These objectives cannot be achieved unless there is a re-education of the Federal Reserve Board and the American public in the true causes of inflation and the effective control of money and credit.
There is no way that the waste of our manpower through unemployment can be reduced or the productivity of our workers increased while interest rates are significantly above 8 percent. Until interest rates are reduced to a level at which it is profitable for small, medium and large businesses tg borrow capital, unemployment will increase, Government expenses for unemployment and welfare will grow, while taxable revenues fall.
United States interest rates rose above the 8 percent level on a sustained peacetime basis for the first time in 1973. Since then, the prime rate has averaged 10 percent and long-term corporate bonds 9 percent, compared with 4 percent and 3 percent, respectively, for the preceding 25 postwar years. These rates, if continued, will destroy the American economic system because of the following effects:
* They take from the poor and add to the rich.
* They diminish the producers and expand the lenders.
* They inhibit and diminish long-term capital investment, productivity, research and development, production and development of new sources and efficient applications of energy.
* They do not encourage capital formation and its productive applications.
* They bankrupt small businesses, increasing the concentration of economic productive power, drastically reducing effective, competitive price cutting.
Our nation has been drastically misinformed and misled by officials who have made it an article of faith that inflation has been the result of excessive monetary growth; that high interest rates can cure inflation, and that because they say so, interest rates cannot safely be cut and Americans must suffer a decline in their standard of living.
Let me respond to these doctrines with the documented truth that gives a much different picture. Although inflation can be caused by excessive monetary growth, there has been no such excessive growth in the United States during the last decade. Instead, while the gross national product rose 37 percent in constaft dollars, the nation's money supply (M-1B) declined by 3 percent.
Money and credit are the essential lubricants of our economic system. In the past 10 years, money supply has contracted by onethird relative to G.N.P. - far more than can be explained away as a result of faster check clearing and other modern contributions to the ''velocity'' of circulation.
T IGHT money and high interest rates in the early 1970's spawned the prolific growth of Eurodollars, dollar credits and dollar deposits created by foreign-domiciled banks including the foreign branches of American multinational banks. Indeed, since they came on the scene, Eurodollars have grown to $550 billion, compared to about $400 billion for the nation's basic money supply.
By 1973, the huge upsurge of Eurodollars doubled the dollar value of world commodity prices, causing wordd-wide inflation and setting the stage for the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries. And the subsequent increase in the price of OPEC oil was more than 1,000 percent, while the G.N.P. price deflator rose only 76 percent.
In my opinion, the rise in the price of energy alone more than accounts for all of the United States inflation. The inflationary result of rising energy costs cannot be measured only in terms of its magnitude as a raw material component of G.N.P. Energy is also part of the cost of every other component and the cost of producing every component rises with the cost of energy.
High interest rates, instead of curing inflation, have stimulated it. They add to the costs of every business, to all forms of product distribution and to all types of consumer purchases. They are themselves a significant secondary cause of inflation. The interest rate rise in 1974 caused by the tight monetary policies of the Federal Reserve, created the second-greatest recession in this century. The 1974-75 recession increased welfare and unemployment costs and decreased Government revenues so much that our Federal deficit from 1975 through 1978 totaled $226 billion and became the greatest and most inflationary deficit in our history.
Finally, interest rates can be reduced safely and promptly. Adequate money and credit can be supplied, provided that there is selective control in the application of credit. The Fed has the power and the ability, without significantly increasing its bureaucracy, to limit credit, which contributes to inflationary consumer demand, and to increase substantially credit available for ''supply-side'' capital investment in the development of new sources of energy, in increased production of goods and services and in costreducing equipment and technology.
A system of variable reserve requirements related to the mix of bank loan portfolios can produce a communion of profit-making interest betweef our banks and our national economic objectives. What is lacking is simply the mental effort to devise the plan and the will to put it into action.
One more thing concerning Eurodollars. Although they are not obligations of the United States or of American domestic banks, they can and do buy goods and services for dollars anywhere in the world, including inside the United States. For nearly 10 years, Eurodollar growth has added to American import costs and thus to domestic inflation by depressing the foreign exchange value of the dollar. This was and is because foreigners, and American multinational corporations, can acquire dollars by borrowing them from foreigndomiciled banks instead of selling their foreign currencies in exchange for dollars.
Today, Eurodollars are coming to the United States for investment. When they arrive here, these Eurodollars inflate the domestic money supply accordingly because they then become deposits in American banks. For our Federal Reserve Board to counter this by restricting the current money supply correspondingly is simply to deprive Americans of money and credit in order to offset the supply of Eurodollars coming into the United States from foreign investors. --------------------------------------------------------------------- John Winthrop Wright is president of Wright Investors' Services, an investment management and advisory organization in Bridgeport, Conn.

U.S. TRADE IS IN SURPLUS BY $3.1 BILLION IN QUARTER

Author: AP

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]19 June 1981: D.13.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424118549?accountid=11752

Abstract: In the first three months of the year, the new report said, ''In the merchandise accounts, a large increase in exports, following several quarters of little change, more than offset an increase in imports associated with stepped-up U.S. business activity.'' In the services category, the report added, the United States account showed a decline in net income from direct investment by foreigners. However, that was more than offset by ''higher interest rates and a sustained high level of U.S. bank lending abroad.'' Foreign Official Assets Up United States official reserve assets increased $4.5 billion in the quarter, partly reflecting purchases of West German marks, the report said. Meanwhile, foreign official assets in the United States increased by $5.4 billion, ''due almost entirely'' to an increase in holdings by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=U.S.+TRADE+IS+IN+SURPLUS+BY+%243.1+BILLION+IN+QUARTER&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-06-19&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.13&amp;au=AP&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The nation showed a $3.1 billion firstquarter surplus in its foreign trade balance as measured by the broad current account of goods, services and other financial transactions, the Commerce Department reported today.
The new report also sharply revised last year's current-account figures, which now show a $3.7 billion surplus in 1980 rather than the $118 million reported in March. It was the biggest yearly surplus since the $4.4 billion of 1976, but it was no longer the first surplus since that year, as earlier reported. The Commerce Department now says a $1.4 billion surplus was posted in 1979 rather than the $705 million deficit the department has been citing.
Department officials indicated earlier that the balance would show a big surplus for the January-March quarter, contributing heavily to the unexpectedly rapid 8.6 percent annual rate of growth in the inflation-adjusted gross national product during those three months. What Figures Measure
The current-account figures, which are also adjusted for seasonal variations, measure a wide variety of financial transactions, including those involving goods, services, foreign aid, loans to foreigners, the movement of gold and such payments as pensions to Americans who live abroad.
In the first three months of the year, the new report said, ''In the merchandise accounts, a large increase in exports, following several quarters of little change, more than offset an increase in imports associated with stepped-up U.S. business activity.''
In the services category, the report added, the United States account showed a decline in net income from direct investment by foreigners. However, that was more than offset by ''higher interest rates and a sustained high level of U.S. bank lending abroad.'' Foreign Official Assets Up
United States official reserve assets increased $4.5 billion in the quarter, partly reflecting purchases of West German marks, the report said. Meanwhile, foreign official assets in the United States increased by $5.4 billion, ''due almost entirely'' to an increase in holdings by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries.
Over all, the merchandise trade deficit declined in the first quarter to $4.6 billion from $5.6 billion in the fourth quarter; the surplus in services receipts fell slightly to $9.2 billion from $9.3 billion, and the deficit in unilateral transfers, such as nonmilitary foreign aid and pension payments, declined to $1.5 billion from $2.3 billion.
The revision of 1980 figures was spread across the year, with the new statistics showing big surpluses in the final six months outweighing deficits in the previous two quarters.
The fourth-quarter surplus, originally reported at $687 million, is now said to have been $1.39 billion.

CURRENCY MARKETS; Dollar Posts Sharp Rise And Gold Prices Retreat

Author: The Associated Press

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]16 Apr 1981: D.16.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424086358?accountid=11752

Abstract: Consequently, investors shunned the West German mark, and the dollar ''did quite nicely,'' one dealer in New York said. Uncertainty about the future course of interest rates in the United States also played a role in the dollar's rise, dealers said. Interest rates in United States credit markets were up. Against the Japanese yen, the dollar rose to 216.88 yen, from 215.40. The dollar rose to 1.1985 Canadian dollars, from 1.1957. It also gained against the British pound, with sterling at $2.1513, compared with Tuesday's $2.1720. In London, the pound weakened against the United States currency to $2.1594, a decline of nearly 2 cents from Tuesday's 2.1788. It was sterling's lowest level against the dollar in London since April 8, 1980, when it traded at $2.15825.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=CURRENCY+MARKETS%3B+Dollar+Posts+Sharp+Rise+And+Gold+Prices+Retreat&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-04-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=D.16&amp;au=The+Associated+Press&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The value of the dollar climbed sharply against other key currencies in trading in Europe and the United States yesterday, largely in reaction to unfavorable economic developments in West Germany. Gold prices registered a decline.
In Bonn, the Government reported that wholesale prices were up, fueling fears of double-digit inflation. The Government also said its budget deficit in the first quarter rose 8 percent, more than had been allowed for the entire year.
Consequently, investors shunned the West German mark, and the dollar ''did quite nicely,'' one dealer in New York said. Uncertainty about the future course of interest rates in the United States also played a role in the dollar's rise, dealers said. Interest rates in United States credit markets were up.
The dollar ended the day in New York at 2.1895 marks, up from 2.1710 on Tuesday. In European trading, the dollar climbed to 2.1715 marks, from 2.1645 the previous day.
The dollar started the trading day by rising in Tokyo to 216.40 yen, from the previous session's closing rate of 215.70. As trading moved to Europe, the United States currency continued to gain ground against all major currencies.
As the trading day ended in New York, the dollar had registered these gains: 5.1613 French francs, up from 5.1250; 1.9970 Swiss francs, up from 1.9767.
Against the Japanese yen, the dollar rose to 216.88 yen, from 215.40. The dollar rose to 1.1985 Canadian dollars, from 1.1957. It also gained against the British pound, with sterling at $2.1513, compared with Tuesday's $2.1720. In London, the pound weakened against the United States currency to $2.1594, a decline of nearly 2 cents from Tuesday's 2.1788. It was sterling's lowest level against the dollar in London since April 8, 1980, when it traded at $2.15825.
Other late dollar rates in Europe yesterday, compared with Tuesday's close, were as follows: 2.1715 West German marks, up from 2.1645; 1.9822 Swiss francs, up from 1.9733; 5.1225 French francs, up from 5.1080; 2.40425 Dutch guilders, up from 2.3975, and 1,080.70 Italian lire, up from 1,078.15.
Gold prices were lower in Europe and New York. In Zurich, gold lost $5 to close at $477.50 a troy ounce. In London, gold fell to $475.75 an ounce, down $12.
In New York, gold for current delivery fell $9.30, to $473.90 an ounce on the Commodity Exchange Inc. As of 4 P.M. at Republic National Bank, gold was quoted at $474 an ounce, down $8.50.
Silver prices also fell. The price on the Comex eased to $11.12 an ounce, down 19 cents. In London it dropped to $11.15 an ounce, down 13 cents.

THE EDITORIAL NOTEBOOK; DIPLOMACY, TOO PAYS FOR INFLATION

Author: Passell, Peter

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]24 Mar 1981: A.18.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424059041?accountid=11752

Abstract: The tune sounds familiar, but the soprano seems to be singing the tenor's part. Only a few years back, it was the United States that complained that conservative financial policies in Western Europe were spoiling the West's prosperity. Now it is Europe, particularly West Germany, that blames the Continent's recession on stringent monetary policies in the United States. Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=THE+EDITORIAL+NOTEBOOK%3B+DIPLOMACY%2C+TOO+PAYS+FOR+INFLATION%3A+%5BEDITORIAL%5D&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1981-03-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.18&amp;au=Passell%2C+Peter&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The tune sounds familiar, but the soprano seems to be singing the tenor's part. Only a few years back, it was the United States that complained that conservative financial policies in Western Europe were spoiling the West's prosperity. Now it is Europe, particularly West Germany, that blames the Continent's recession on stringent monetary policies in the United States.
These complaints cannot be ignored. They reflect political tensions created by economic stagnation in Europe and the uncertain prospects for recovery - tensions that can further damage the Atlantic alliance. But giving the Europeans what they say they want would probably not solve their problems, and it would certainly complicate America's.
The West German economy is in trouble. Trouble, of course, is a relative term. By any reasonable measure, West German living standards are among the highest in the world. And neither inflation nor unemployment rates are as bad as those in the United States. But the perception that West Germany's economy can do no wrong is fading. Production grew very little in 1980; what little gain was made is expected to be lost in 1981. A Helping West Germany By Cheapening the Dollar Could Hurt Everyone once-prosperous foreign trade is in deficit, and the mighty D-mark has lost about one-fifth of its value relative to the dollar in just 15 months.
One obvious result of the fall from grace is discontent with the policies of Chancellor Helmut Schmidt. The West Germans, whose 12-digit catastrophe in the 1920's left them dreading inflation more than anything, will not risk big budget deficits to stimulate their economy. And they cannot lower their interest rates while America's remain high without risking a massive shift to dollar investments. That, in turn, would cause the mark to depreciate further, raising the cost of imports, especially oil. So Bonn and its partners in the European Monetary System want a coordinated lowering of interest rates, in America as well as in Europe.
But the Reagan Administration is in no mood to cooperate. It is determined to stick to a conservative monetary policy, lowering interest rates only gradually to break the psychology of inflation. Some American officials are privately skeptical, in any case, about the sincerity of Europe's request. They suspect that the main motive is to shift the blame for unemployment and high oil prices to Washington.
Besides, the allies too would benefit if Mr. Reagan succeeds in turning America's inflation rate downward. All would certainly suffer even more if he fails.
But two lessons can be found in this conflict. First, West Germany's main problem is oil imports. If worldwide demand for oil were lower, oil prices would decline, and so would Bonn's trade deficit. Energy conservation therefore remains a high priority for all the industrial democracies.
Second, there is an obvious international cost to using high interest rates as a bulwark against inflation. The United States feels obliged to keep them up, because it has yet to curb inflationary spending programs and because it has been unwilling to try to control wages and prices by more direct means.
It is a tangled policy web, woven in desperation. PETER PASSELL

SOME ADVICE FOR RONALD REAGAN

Author: Burns, Arthur F

Publication info: New York Times , Late Edition (East Coast); New York, N.Y. [New York, N.Y]16 Nov 1980: A.3.

https://search.proquest.com/docview/424015709?accountid=11752

Abstract: In the past, interest rates tended to respond to inflation with a considerable lag. More recently, in view of experience with inflation gained since the mid-1960's, interest rates have been reacting more promptly. Indeed, they are now apt to respond to fears of intensified inflation even before inflation as such intensifies. The sad fact is that in spite of the recession that we have just gone through, the basic rate of inflation is no lower now than it was before the recession started. From the standpoint of our inflation problem, we have had a wasted recession. T HE most basic requirement is to create general confidence that anti-inflation policies will henceforth be pursued resolutely, consistently, and as far as feasible in ways that encourage business innovation and investment. The Federal Reserve should not be expected to carry the main burden of fighting inflation. Fiscal and structural policies must also be enlisted in the battle.

Links: http://findit.library.jhu.edu/resolve?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Anytimes&amp;atitle=SOME+ADVICE+FOR+RONALD+REAGAN&amp;title=New+York+Times&amp;issn=03624331&amp;date=1980-11-16&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.3&amp;au=Burns%2C+Arthur+F&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=New+York+Times&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: N UMEROUS stirrings in our economy since July suggest that the recession in economic activity has come to an end. But the ending of recess ion, if that indeed has taken place, do es not necessarily implythat a sustainable economic recovery has star ted. There are many reasons for concern about the immediate econo mic future. Let me mention some of them.
First, the recovery since midsummer is virtually confined to the consumer trades and home building. There is as yet little or no evidence of a revival in the machinery industries, in the equipment trades or in business construction. A recovery that originates in the consumer sector and that fails to embrace business capital formation is inherently fragile.
Second, the outlook for the automobile industry remains cloudy. The new models are still being tested in the marketplace, and the prospect for substantial recovery has not been improved by the latest increases in automobile prices.
Third, the economies of other industrial countries have recently we akened, and some are actually experiencing recession. Meanwhile, the less deve loped countries are suffering greatly from the sharp increase in o il prices. Our exports, therefore, may soon turn sluggish.
Fourth, the war in the Middle East has created major new uncertainties about the availability of oil and its price. Fifth, and still more important for the near-term future, interest rates have again risen sharply since mid-June. Such an increase is a highly unusual development at this stage of the business cycle. If interest rates rise further, or even remain at their present level for more than a short period, our economic recovery may be endangered.
The rise in mortgage interest rates is already having an adverse effect on sales of both existing and new homes. The recovery of homebuilding has therefore become precarious. Sales prospects for automobiles have been further dimmed by the rise in interest rates. Business investments are also being adversely affected. Many corporations that had planned bond issues have postponed them. Much the same is true of various public agencies.
Furthermore, the rise in interest rates has been causing tremors in markets for equities and real estate as well as in the bond markets. The severe difficulties already faced by thrift institutions are multiplying, and their troubles could spread to industrial and commercial enterprises.
The recent rise of interest rates is obviously an unfavorable development. More frequently than not, bankers or the Federal Reserve authorities are now being blamed for it. The fault, however, is not theirs. The fundamental cause of the rise of interest rates is the renewed expectation of rapid inflation.
In the past, interest rates tended to respond to inflation with a considerable lag. More recently, in view of experience with inflation gained since the mid-1960's, interest rates have been reacting more promptly. Indeed, they are now apt to respond to fears of intensified inflation even before inflation as such intensifies.
The sad fact is that in spite of the recession that we have just gone through, the basic rate of inflation is no lower now than it was before the recession started. From the standpoint of our inflation problem, we have had a wasted recession.
In view of the difficulties and uncertainties that surround the economy, I do not think we can have a robust recovery in 1981. However, our longer range economic future could improve dramatically if we went to work seriously on the inflation problem. I trust that the Reagan administration will do precisely that even as it seeks to revive enterprise and diminish unemployment.
T HE most basic requirement is to create general confidence that anti-inflation policies will henceforth be pursued resolutely, consistently, and as far as feasible in ways that encourage business innovation and investment. The Federal Reserve should not be expected to carry the main burden of fighting inflation. Fiscal and structural policies must also be enlisted in the battle.
In addition to a firm monetary policy, I would emphasize the following principles at this juncture of history: First, encouragement of productivity-enhancing capital investments by lowering business taxes, and accompanying these and any other tax reductions with significant cuts in projected Federal spending.
Second, revision of the budget process so as to require a balanced budget unless a deficit is authorized by more than a simple majority - say, by two-thirds - of each House of Congress.
Third, reduction of restrictions on competition that are now imposed by Government; for example, introducing a youth differential for minimum wages in order to increase job opportunities for teengers, and rescinding or amending the Davis-Bacon Act, which serves to escalate construction costs.
Fourth, reform of regulations concerned with the environment, public health, and safety so as to insure that basic national objectives a re achieved at minimum feasible c ost.
Fifth, encouragement of labor-management councils in individual shops, offices and stores and throughout the country, so that workers and managers will jointly seek ways of improving productivity.
Sixth, more rapid decontrol of oil prices despite adverse shortrun effects on the price level, in the interest of achieving price stability over the longer run as well as regaining national energy independence.
I would add that no policy will work unless it establishes confidence that progress toward reasonably stable prices is being made and that this will in time restore our nation's economic health. The new administration not only faces the problem of finding the road to reasonable price stability, it must also inspire hope that the path it p ursu es, however, rocky, will lead to that goal and to a better future . --------------------------------------------------------------------- Arthur F. Burns was chairman of the Federal Reserve Board for eight years and is an adviser to Ronald Reagan.
